Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim



Chapter 1



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> All substitute names<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Since vows and oaths are sacral acts, the invocation of vow or oath is automatically the invocation of God’s Name. Just as God’s Name should not be invoked in vain, the people shied away from using the expressions “vow, making a vow” or “oath, swearing”. As explained in the next Note, one of the oblique references to a vow was the word <i>qorbān</i> and its substitute, as given in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>.</i> of vows<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Making a vow is taking on an obligation. There are three kinds of vows found in talmudic literature.<br>A person may vow to bring a sacrifice or to give a certain sum to the Temple. This is a relatively simple matter since (Mishnah <i>Qiddšin</i> 1:6) “speaking to Higher Powers is like delivering to a human”; i. e., a promise to God is as final as delivering the merchandise in a commercial transaction. Therefore, these vows are only a minor topic in the present tractate.<br>A person may make a vow to become a <i>nazir</i> for a certain time; then he is required to abstain from grapes and grape products as well as from becoming impure by the impurity of the dead, and he must let his hair grow. This kind of vow is discussed in Tractate <i>Nazir.</i><br>The main topic of Tractate <i>Nedarim</i> is “any vow or oath of prohibition to deprive oneself” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:14" href="/Numbers.30.14"><i>Num.</i> 30:14</a>), i. e., a vow in which a person for any reason prohibits to himself things which otherwise are permitted. One may also make a vow to forbid certain actions to oneself but the actions must be forbidden as things are forbidden. (Self-flagellation is forbidden in Judaism since inflicting any wound on oneself is forbidden except for medical purposes, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 8:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.8.4.1">Mishnah <i>Baba qama</i> 8:5</a>.) Since an animal which was dedicated as a sacrifice is forbidden for any profane use, it became general practice to say about anything of which one wanted to prohibit all use for himself “this is <i>qorbān</i> for me”, or simply <i>qorbān</i> “sacrifice”. Therefore, if in <i>Math.</i> 15:5 it is noted that a son can say to his parents that all he possesses should be <i>qorbān</i> for them; on the surface it is the height of mistreatment of the parents if the son prohibits them any use whatsoever of his property and even prohibits himself to help his parents in any way. The rabbinic answer would be that if the son goes off to fight as a guerilla against the Romans, or the Herodian government, cutting off all ties might save his parents’ property from confiscation. (Cf. <i>Peah</i> 1:1, Note 113, about the unpredictability of the duty to honor father and mother: “Somebody might serve his father fattened meat and go to hell; another might bind his father to the grindstone and go to paradise.”) The vow could also be in error, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.3.2.1">Mishnah 3:2</a>. Chapter 4 shows that a person forbidding his property to another can nevertheless take care of that other person. Cf. also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 9:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.9.1.8">Chapter 9, Note 38</a>.<br>{In <i>Math.</i> 15:5, Jesus complains that Pharisees declare a person free from prosecution (פָּטוּר) if he violates the commandment to honor and fear father and mother by maliciously forbidding his property to them. The pharisaic rule in question is that “a prohibition violated without an action is not prosecutable” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 63" href="/Sanhedrin.63">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i> 63a/b</a>). Speech is not counted as an action in this respect. The rule is opposed by R. Jehudah who in many cases represents older traditions (from his father’s teacher R. Eliezer.) The testimony of <i>Math</i>. shows that in this case, the majority opinion is the historic position. R. Jehudah’s position is that of the Qumran community in the Manual of Discipline and the Damascus Document.}</i> are like vows, of bans<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">“Ban” is the irrevocable gift of property to the Temple, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:28" href="/Leviticus.27.28"><i>Lev</i>. 27:28</a>.</i> like bans, of oaths<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">An oath is subject-related, in contrast to a vow which is object-related. If a person prohibits himself to eat any bread, that is an oath of deprivation. If he prohibits the use of a specified loaf, or even any loaf, on himself, that is a vow of deprivation.</i> like oaths, of <i>nazir vows</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">While this is also a vow of deprivation it follows its own rules, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:1-21" href="/Numbers.6.1-21"><i>Num</i>. 6:1–21</a>.</i> like <i>nazir</i> vows. If one says to another, I am vowed away from you<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the detailed explanation of what substitute names are. All expressions in this sentence are examples of נדר הנאה “a vow [to forbid] usufruct”, in which A tells B that all of B’s property is forbidden to A as if it were a sacrifice or sacred property and A’s property to B..</i>, I am separated from you, I am distanced from you, that I will not eat from yours, that I will not taste from yours, he is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">All of B’s property, not only his food.</i>. I am excommunicated from you, Rabbi Aqiba was stringent in that because of a doubt<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">R. Aqiba decided that, while the exact meaning of “excommunicated” in this context is unknown since the use of a judicial term by a private person is inappropriate, the most extensive interpretation must be adopted.</i>. As vows of the wicked, he vowed as <i>nazir,</i> a sacrifice, and an oath<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence is very elliptic. Since the <i>nazir</i> prohibits vines and all their products for himself and he needs atonement for his status (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:11" href="/Numbers.6.11"><i>Num</i>. 6:11</a>) one concludes (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:1:2-3:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.1.2-3.7"><i>Nedarim</i> 1:1</a>, 36d 1. 50; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 10a" href="/Nedarim.10a">Babli 10a</a>) that it is essentially sinful to deprive oneself from permitted enjoyments. It follows that just people do not make vows of deprivation or <i>nezirut</i>. They also do not make vows of sacrifices since such a vow implies that the person will be financially responsible until a correct sacrifice has been presented in the Temple. Responsible people offer free-will gifts in which they designate an animal as sacrifice; if anything should happen to that animal before it is sacrificed he is not obligated to offer a replacement.<br>Since the wicked do make vows and swear, any expression of vow or oath declared to be in the form used by the wicked has to be interpreted to imply a maximum obligation. Still, somebody who simply says that he makes a vow of the wicked is not obligated for anything unless some object on which the vow could fall was before him at that moment. For example, if a loaf of bread was before him and he said, this loaf is for me as a vow of the wicked, he is forbidden to eat that loaf. If a long-haired <i>nazir</i> was passing before him and he said, that is for me as a vow of the wicked, he is a <i>nazir</i> for the standard period of 30 days.</i>. As vows of the good ones, he did not say anything<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Since good people do not make vows nor do they swear.</i>. As their freewill gifts<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">If somebody said, this loaf of bread is to me as a free-will gift of a just person, he means that for him it is <i>qorbān</i> and therefore forbidden for all usufruct. Similarly, if a long-haired <i>nazir</i> was passing before him and he said, that is for me as a free-will gift of the just, he is a <i>nazir</i> for the standard period of 30 days.</i>, he made a vow of <i>nazir</i> and sacrifice.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “All substitute names of vows are like vows,” etc. It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:3" href="/Numbers.30.3"><i>Num.</i> 30:3</a>.</i> “Any person who vows,” why does the verse say “a vow”? From here that substitute names of vows are like vows. “Or he swears,” why does the verse say “an oath”? From here that substitute names of oaths are like oaths. “But any ban,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:28" href="/Leviticus.27.28"><i>Lev.</i> 27:28</a>.</i>” why does the verse say “which he bans”? From here that substitute names of bans are like bans. “A vow of <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:2" href="/Numbers.6.2"><i>Num.</i> 6:2</a>.</i>”, why does the verse say “to be a <i>nazir</i>”? From here that substitute names of <i>nazir</i> vows are like <i>nazir</i> vows. So far for Rebbi Aqiba who says that these are expressions of additions. <sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 8:1:12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.8.1.12"><i>Yebamot</i> 8:1, Note 72</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 27a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.27a">Babli <i>Avodah zarah</i> 27a</a> (and another 18 times without attribution). The quotes are from speeches of Laban and Joseph in <i>Gen</i>. which have no legal implications. This proves that the repetitions are a matter of style.</i> For Rebbi Ismael who said, these are double expressions in the normal style of the Torah, “going you went, desiring you desired, by stealing I was stolen”, from where? “<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:3" href="/Numbers.30.3"><i>Num.</i> 30:3</a>.</i> Any person who vows a vow to the Eternal or swears an oath to forbid a prohibition on himself shall not profane his word,” why does the verse say “he must fulfill anything coming out of his mouth”? From here that substitute names of vows are like vows and substitute names of oaths are like oaths<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The second half of the verse is clearly written for emphasis. It implies (a) that a vow is valid only if pronounced, not if only thought of and (b) that any speech which can be interpreted as a vow is a vow.<br>The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 3" href="/Nedarim.3">Babli, 3a/b</a>, quotes both the argument in the style of R. Aqiba and that of R. Ismael without mentioning any names.</i>. And from where that substitute names of bans are like bans? “A vow, a vow”<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">This is an application of the second hermeneutical rule of <i>gezerah šawah</i> “equal cut”. If it was established in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:3" href="/Numbers.30.3"><i>Num</i>. 30:3</a> that “vow” means “anything that implies a vow” and in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:2" href="/Leviticus.27.2"><i>Lev</i>. 27:2</a> any dedication to the Temple, including bans, is classified as “vow”, it follows that anything which implies a ban is a ban.</i>. Since “a vow” at one place means that substitute names of vows are like vows and substitute names of oaths are like oaths, “a vow” at the other place means that substitute names of bans are like bans. And from where that substitute names of being a <i>nazir</i> are like being a <i>nazir</i>? “A vow, a vow”<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Again this is an application of <i>gezerah šawah</i>, but this time the reference quote is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:2" href="/Numbers.6.2"><i>Num</i>. 6:2</a>, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:1:14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.1.14">Note 14</a>.</i>. Since “a vow” at one place means that substitute names of oaths are like oaths<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">This reference is odd since the argument is about vows, not oaths. One has to assume that the scribe left out the relevant portion of the sentence which should be identical to the one used in the preceding case.</i>, “a vow” at the other place means that substitute names of being a <i>nazir</i> are like being a <i>nazir</i>.
How does Rebbi Aqiba explain “he must fulfill anything coming out of his mouth”<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">If a vow cannot be fulfilled completely, it is not a vow.</i>? From here that if part of a vow is invalid, all of it is invalid. Does Rebbi Ismael not agree with this? Everything derives from there. It follows from there that substitute names of vows are like vows and substitute names of oaths<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">That conclusion could have been drawn even if the word כל “all, anything” were not written. Therefore, the clause admits two conclusions independent of one another.</i> are like oaths and it follows from there that if part of a vow is invalid, all of it is invalid. How does Rebbi Ismael explain “any person who vows a vow of <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:2" href="/Numbers.6.2"><i>Num.</i> 6:2</a>.</i>”? From there that a person can obligate himself as <i>nazir</i> while he currently is a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The pentateuchal state of <i>nazir</i> is always limited in time (when a Temple is in existence. Since a <i>nazir</i> is only permitted to drink wine after he has absolved the required Temple ritual, a person vowing to be a <i>nazir</i> today must remain in that state until the Temple is rebuilt and officiating Cohanim are found who have complete documentary proof of their priestly status going back to the priests officiating in the second Temple.) The prophetic state of <i>nazir</i>, as exemplified by Simson and Samuel, is unlimited but does not include a prohibition of the impurity of the dead. It is noted here that while a person is in the state of a <i>nazir</i> for a fixed period, he can undertake to be a <i>nazir</i> for an additional period, to begin after the Temple procedure for the current <i>nezirut</i> was performed. This statement is needed since the vow of <i>nazir</i> of an unincumbered person makes that person a <i>nazir</i> immediately upon pronouncing his vow.</i>. Does  Rebbi Aqiba not agree with this? He agrees and everything derives from there<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The same verse.</i>. He agrees that from there a person can obligate himself as <i>nazir</i> while he currently is a <i>nazir</i>.
Now estimates<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">A person who offers to pay to the Temple the monetary value of his person, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:1-8" href="/Leviticus.27.1-8"><i>Lev</i>. 27:1–8</a>, which in general is a fixed sum but becomes an estimate for poor persons.</i>, bans, exchanges<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Exchanging a dedicated animal for another, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:9-10" href="/Leviticus.27.9-10"><i>Lev</i>. 27:9–10</a>.</i>, and dedications<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Giving non-sacrificial animals or any other valuables to the Temple to cover its expenses, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:11-25" href="/Leviticus.27.11-25"><i>Lev</i>. 27:11–25</a>.</i> were in the same paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27" href="/Leviticus.27"><i>Lev</i>. 27</a>, where bans are treated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:28" href="/Leviticus.27.28">v. 28</a>. Why should bans be treated differently from all other categories of gifts?</i>, but we did not state ‘substitute names of estimates, substitute names of exchanges and dedications.’ But if these were stated, what would we state<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Recognized substitutes for vows, bans, <i>nazir</i> vows, and oaths are listed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>. The other dedications do not have recognized substitute names. The hypothetical names quoted in the sequel are built on the linguistic pattern of the accepted ones but either they make no sense or they have a sense very far from the intended one. Since they are not part of the current vocabulary as substitute names, they cannot be used.</i>? ‘<i>Araphin, ‘araṣin, ‘araqin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">“Disappearances”.</i>? <i>Temupha, temuna</i><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">“Picture”.</i>, <i>temuqa</i>? <i>Hegder, hegzer, hegres</i><sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The substitution of גּ for ק in this series shows that the pronunciation of <i>q</i> was close to that of Arabic ق.</i>. It is understandable following him who says, unspecified bans are for the support of the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">If bans are gifts to the Temple they are gifts to God and, as stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.1.1">Note 2</a>, they become absolute the moment the intention to give is declared. Therefore, substitute names which are in common use are as effective as the explicit mention of the word “ban”. But if an unspecified ban is intended for Cohanim (the majority opinion in Mishnah ‘<i>Arakhin</i> 8:6), the collection of the gift is a money transaction between Cohanim and the giver, subject to the rule that “the proof is on the claimant” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 3:8:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.3.8.2-8"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 3:9</a>, 21b 1. 70; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 46b" href="/Bava_Kamma.46b">Babli <i>Baba qama</i> 46b</a>). The Cohanim would have to prove that the putative giver in his use of the substitute words really did intend to refer to bans. This proof seems almost impossible. Therefore, substitute bans could not be like bans and the Mishnah here must be interpreted as supporting the opinion that unspecified bans are for the Temple.</i>. But according to him who says, unspecified bans are for the priests? Why did we not state ‘substitute names for heave’? But if it were stated, what would we state<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Recognized substitutes for vows, bans, <i>nazir</i> vows, and oaths are listed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>. The other dedications do not have recognized substitute names. The hypothetical names quoted in the sequel are built on the linguistic pattern of the accepted ones but either they make no sense or they have a sense very far from the intended one. Since they are not part of the current vocabulary as substitute names, they cannot be used.</i>? <i>Terupha</i><sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">“Medicine”.</i>, <i>teruṣa, teruqa</i>?
Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Eleazar disagree, for Rebbi Joḥanan said, one whips for prohibitions<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Somebody forbade something for himself without using the language either of vows or of oaths or any of the recognized substitute expressions. If he then breaks his own prohibition this falls under the biblical prohibition of “he shall not profane his word” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:3" href="/Numbers.30.3"><i>Num</i>. 30:3</a>). The question is whether this is a sin or a prosecutable offense. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:1:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.1.2-9"><i>Nazir</i> 1:1</a> (51a 1. 34), R. Joḥanan is quoted as holding that it is not a prosecutable offense. Accordingly, one has to read here “one does not whip”, and in the opinion of R. Eleazar “one does whip” since this is also required by the argument quoted later by R. Jacob bar Aḥa. (Alternatively one would have to switch names in that argument, but then the quote in <i>Nazir</i> would remain unexplained.)</i> and Rebbi Eleazar said, one does not whip<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Somebody forbade something for himself without using the language either of vows or of oaths or any of the recognized substitute expressions. If he then breaks his own prohibition this falls under the biblical prohibition of “he shall not profane his word” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:3" href="/Numbers.30.3"><i>Num</i>. 30:3</a>). The question is whether this is a sin or a prosecutable offense. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:1:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.1.2-9"><i>Nazir</i> 1:1</a> (51a 1. 34), R. Joḥanan is quoted as holding that it is not a prosecutable offense. Accordingly, one has to read here “one does not whip”, and in the opinion of R. Eleazar “one does whip” since this is also required by the argument quoted later by R. Jacob bar Aḥa. (Alternatively one would have to switch names in that argument, but then the quote in <i>Nazir</i> would remain unexplained.)</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, so did Rebbi Joḥanan answer Rebbi Eleazar: According to you, who says one does not whip on prohibitions, did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.4.1">Mishnah 4:4</a>: “If somebody has vowed not to have any usufruct from another and he goes to visit him (when he is sick), he may stand but not sit down and heal him (but not his animals).” He cannot sit down because he would derive usufruct from the other’s possessions. The question makes sense only if R. Eleazar holds that transgressing the prohibition is prosecutable.</i>: “If somebody has vowed not to have any usufruct from another and he goes to visit him,” let him not enter<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">If transgressing a private prohibition is not prosecutable, one can explain the Mishnah as referring to a prohibition of usufruct, not a formal vow or oath. But if it is prosecutable as a formal vow, why permit to enter since the person entering will find shelter there from the sun in summer and the rain in winter?</i>! Rebbi Jeremiah said, there is a difference there because of the ways of peace<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">“Ways of peace” are the obligations of interpersonal relationships necessary in a civilized society; in this case, visiting the sick.</i>. Rebbi Yose asked: If it is because of the ways of peace, should he not also be permitted in case of an oath? But we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 2:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.2.2.1">Mishnah 2:2</a>: “A vow that I shall not build a tabernacle, that I shall not take a <i>lulab</i>, that I will not wear phylacteries: vows are forbidden, oaths permitted, for one cannot swear to break religious obligations.” If somebody makes a vow that religious objects should be forbidden to him (as if they were dedicated sacrifices), he commits a twofold sin in making a frivolous vow and breaking biblical commandments, but what he did is done. But if he swears that he will not fulfill his religious obligations, the oath is invalid since, in the language of the Babli, “he already is under oath from Mount Sinai”, and a valid oath cannot be superseded by another oath. Since visiting the sick is a religious obligation, if the prohibition of usufruct is interpreted as an oath it should be nonexistent in the case of a visit to a sick person. No answer is given since practice follows R. Joḥanan.</i>: “Vows are forbidden, oaths permitted.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 1:4" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).1.4">Tosephta 1:4</a>, quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 12a" href="/Nedarim.12a">Babli 12a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 14a" href="/Nedarim.14a">14a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 20a" href="/Shevuot.20a"><i>Šebuot</i> 20a</a>.</i>“What is a prohibition? This loaf is for me as the day of my father’s death<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">If he keeps the anniversary of his father’s death as a private fast day. In the Babli sources (including the Tosephta) the reference is not to a loaf but to meat and wine, assuming the person had vowed not to eat meat and drink wine at the anniversary of his father’s death.</i>, as the day X was killed<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli: As the day Gedaliah ben Aḥiqam was killed (<a class="refLink" data-ref="II Kings 25:25" href="/II_Kings.25.25"><i>2K</i>. 25:25</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 41:2" href="/Jeremiah.41.2"><i>Jer</i>. 41:2</a>), which is a fast day by tradition, not by law, and which therefore needs a declaration to be a binding obligation. (In practice, fasting three consecutive years on a particular date creates the equivalent of a vow to fast.)</i>, as the day I saw Jerusalem in ruins; that is the prohibition the Torah spoke of<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">“A person who vows a vow to the Eternal or swears an oath to forbid a prohibition to deprive oneself” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:3" href="/Numbers.30.3"><i>Num</i>. 30:3</a>). “If in her husband’s house she vowed, or forbade a prohibition on herself by an oath.” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:11" href="/Numbers.30.11"><i>Num</i>. 30:11</a>). Since the prohibition can refer both to a vow (tied to a material object that can be vowed to sacrificial use) and an oath (not tied to any object), it creates a category by itself.</i>.” Rebbi Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan and Rav: Both say, only if he had made a vow about that day<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">If the person did not <i>earlier</i> make a vow that food on the anniversary of his father’s death should be forbidden to him like a dedicated sacrifice or had sworn to the same effect, the reference to that day has no effect. In the Babli, the person quoted is Samuel.</i>. Rebbi Yose asked: If only he had made a vow about that day, why a separate prohibition? He should say on that day<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Since the status of a prohibition is in doubt as to whether it is a vow or an oath (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.1.6">Note 44</a>), the reference given will determine its status. If the person fasts in consequence of a vow, the additional prohibition is a vow; if it refers to an oath, it is an oath. In no case is a separate category created. The only case in which one could speak of a separate category is if the additional prohibition is made together with the original one while the status is still undefined.</i>!
<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>baraita</i> similar to this one is quoted and explained in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 20a" href="/Shevuot.20a">Babli <i>Šebuot</i> 20a</a>.</i>“ ‘Prohibition’, is an oath. ‘Expression’<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:4" href="/Numbers.30.4"><i>Num.</i> 30:4</a>: “If she be married to a man and her vows are on her or the expression of her lips which she forbade on herself.” Since “expression” is opposed to “vows”, it must refer to oaths. Similarly, “to express with one’s lips” is used for “to swear an oath” in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:4" href="/Leviticus.5.4"><i>Lev.</i> 5:4</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 20a" href="/Shevuot.20a"><i>Šebuot</i> 20a</a>.</i>, is an oath. If you say prohibition is an oath, he is guilty for every single prohibition and for every single oath. If you say prohibition is<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that “not” has fallen out here. The two cases will be explained below.</i> a kind of oath, he is guilty for this separately and that separately.” “Prohibition is an oath”, and you say “if you say so”?<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">First one states that ‘prohibition’ is an expression of an oath. Then one argues ‘if it is an oath’, ‘if it is not an oath’. This contradicts the first statement.</i> Rebbi Eleazar said, these are two Tannaїm<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The first two sentences have one author, the last two a different one.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah said, it is from one Tanna<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>baraita,</i> as one would reasonably expect, is from one author. But the implicit verb in the first two sentences should not be read as “is” but as “may be”.</i>. If he said it in the language of a vow<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">That he forbids the object for himself.</i> you catch him in the language of a vow. If he said it in the language of an oath<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">That he forbids himself the use of the object. Since “prohibition” is applied to both vows and oaths, the meaning of the word is determined by the syntax of the sentence in which it is used; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.1.1">Note 4</a>.</i> you catch him in the language of an oath. If you say, prohibition is a kind of oath, he is guilty for every single prohibition and for every single oath<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Violating a vow is a prohibition which, if proved in court by two witnesses, might subject the perpetrator to punishment by whipping. Even if the violation was inadvertent, there never is a possibility of a sacrifice. But inadvertently violating an oath imposes on the perpetrator the duty to offer a reparation or a purification offering in the Temple, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:1-13" href="/Leviticus.5.1-13"><i>Lev.</i> 5:1–13</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, this comes only for five loaves<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Multiple guilt (multiple sacrifices) are possible only for multiple objects of prohibitions, not for repeated prohibitions of the same object.</i>. But for a single loaf, from the moment he mentioned “oath” for it, he made it a cadaver<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">If somebody made an oath to the effect that he would not eat a certain food, that food is forbidden to him as if it were cadaver meat, forbidden to any Jew.</i>. Furthermore, he may want to have oaths apply to prohibited items, but oaths cannot apply to prohibitited items<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 2:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.2.2.1">Mishnah 2:2</a>: “A vow that I shall not build a tabernacle, that I shall not take a <i>lulab</i>, that I will not wear phylacteries: vows are forbidden, oaths permitted, for one cannot swear to break religious obligations.” If somebody makes a vow that religious objects should be forbidden to him (as if they were dedicated sacrifices), he commits a twofold sin in making a frivolous vow and breaking biblical commandments, but what he did is done. But if he swears that he will not fulfill his religious obligations, the oath is invalid since, in the language of the Babli, “he already is under oath from Mount Sinai”, and a valid oath cannot be superseded by another oath. Since visiting the sick is a religious obligation, if the prohibition of usufruct is interpreted as an oath it should be nonexistent in the case of a visit to a sick person. No answer is given since practice follows R. Joḥanan.</i>. Rebbi Ḥananiah said, it applies even to a single loaf. As we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shevuot 2:2" href="/Tosefta_Shevuot.2.2">Tosephta <i>Šebuot</i> 2:4</a>, referred to in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 28b" href="/Shevuot.28b">Babli <i>Šebuot</i> 28b</a>.</i>, “in that the past is more stringent than the future, if he said I did not eat, I did not eat, he is guilty for every statement<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">If he swore falsely that he had not eaten, he committed the sin of a false oath. Since that oath does not forbid anything, each statement stands on its own and subjects him to the punishment for swearing falsely.</i>; I shall not eat, I shall not eat, he is guilty only once<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The first oath established the prohibition; the following oaths are futile, forbidden in the Third Commandment, but not triggering any obligation of sacrifice.</i>.” Rebbi Yudan said<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">He explains the prior statement “If you say prohibition is <i>not</i> a kind of oath, he is guilty for this separately and that separately.”</i>, only if he first mentioned “vow” and then mentioned “oath”. But if he mentioned oath and then vow, vows can apply to prohibitions but oaths cannot apply to prohibitions<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Since vows are subject related, if he forbade himself the use of a loaf, he may later declare the loaf to have the status of <i>qorbān,</i> to add the prohibition of sacrilege to the prohibition of eating. But if he first declared the loaf to be <i>qorbān,</i> it is automatically forbidden to him and no oath can increase the degree of prohibition. Therefore, the oath following the vow is a futile oath.</i>.
Rebbi Yose explained “to forbid”<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:3" href="/Numbers.30.3"><i>Num.</i> 30:3</a>, “to forbid a prohibition to himself”, which can be either a vow or an oath.</i>: That should be forbidden to me<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Even though no oath formula was mentioned, this is an oath since it is person-related.</i>; there should be a prohibition on it, it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Even though no vow was mentioned, this is a vow since it is object-related.</i>. An oath is on me, it is forbidden, there should be an oath on it, it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">The only problem is in the last statement, since an oath cannot be binding on a thing. R. Yose holds that an oath formula, used when a vow formula would have been appropriate, induces a vow since it cannot induce a valid oath. In the language of the Talmudim, the oath formula is a <i>handle</i> to introduce the vow.</i>. Rebbi Yudan said, in vows it is forbidden, in oaths it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that an oath formula applied to a thing is ineffective; it is as if it had not been said. This paragraph is discussed at length in the Commentary of R. Nissim Gerondi on the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 2b" href="/Nedarim.2b">Babli, 2b</a>.</i>. There is a prohibition on me, it is forbidden. There is a prohibition on it, it is forbidden. An oath is there on me, it is forbidden. There is an oath on (me) [it]<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that it must be read עליו instead of  עליin order to make sense.</i> is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">An oath directed to a thing is void and has no consequences.</i>.
“I am vowed away from you”. Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said, both of them are mutually forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the detailed explanation of what substitute names are. All expressions in this sentence are examples of נדר הנאה “a vow [to forbid] usufruct”, in which A tells B that all of B’s property is forbidden to A as if it were a sacrifice or sacred property and A’s property to B..</i>; as if [the other person] had said: and I from you<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">In the absence of a specific restriction. For example, if A would say to B: I am separated from you, I shall not eat at your place, then the vow falls only on the specific activity indicated. But in absence of such a qualification, a vow of separation is understood to go both ways as indicated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.1.9">Note 6</a>. The statement of R. Yose ben Ḥanina is quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 5a" href="/Nedarim.5a">Babli, 5a</a>.</i>. If he said, this loaf is vowed away from me and I from it, or I am vowed away from this loaf and it from me<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">While an inanimate object cannot be told anything, as long as the vow is object directed it is a valid vow, irrespective of the language.</i>. It does not make any difference whether he said “from me” in Aramaic (מינך) or in Hebrew (מימך), or he said “I am to you [forbidden]” or “I am on you [forbidden]”, “you are to me [forbidden]” or “you are on me [forbidden].”<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli disagrees in principle, 5a.</i><br><sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">This is from Halakhah 11:13. The Mishnah, quoted by R. Yose, speaks of a woman who wants to force a divorce by making a vow that sexual relations with any Jew shall be forbidden to her by saying "I am taken away from the Jews." The other expressions mentioned are equivalents of that statement.</i>It was stated: “I am jailed away from you, I am separated away from you”. Rebbi Jeremiah asked, why did one not state “taken away from”? Rebbi Yose said, that was stated at the end, “I am taken away from the Jews.”
“That I will not eat from yours, that I will not taste from yours.” Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Hoshaiah. One catches him because of a handle for <i>qorbān</i><sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Even if he did not spell out: <i>qorbān</i> that I shall not eat from yours. Since he used the language of vows, referring to the other person’s food instead of his person, he made it clear that he intended a vow.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked, if he said, I shall not eat from you, does one catch him because of a handle of an oath<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The answer is negative.</i>? Rebbi Yose said, people usually say “handle of an axe”, do they ever say “axe of a handle”<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:2:2-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.2.2-10"><i>Nazir</i> 1:2</a> (51b 1. 28), R. Yose notes that common usage requires the word “oath” to be at the beginning of the sentence, not at the end, to which R. Mattaniah adds the example of the handle of the axe to show that (in rabbinic Hebrew) word order counts. The translation of כולכה (in the parallel כולבא) as “axe” follows J. Levy’s Dictionary.</i>?
“I am excommunicated from you, Rabbi Aqiba was stringent in that because of a doubt.” To forbid all his possessions<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">R. Aqiba decided that, while the exact meaning of “excommunicated” in this context is unknown since the use of a judicial term by a private person is inappropriate, the most extensive interpretation must be adopted.</i>. As you say<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezra 10:8" href="/Ezra.10.8"><i>Ezra</i> 10:8</a>.</i>, “all his property should be banned and he should be separated from the community of the diaspora.” What do the rabbis do with this? An excommunication by the court is weightier.
“As vows of the wicked, he vowed as <i>nazir,</i> a sacrifice, and an oath.” <sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">This text is obviously corrupt. A consistent text is quoted by Rashba [R. Shelomo ibn Adrat, Barcelona (d. 1310)] in his Novellae to the Babli, 9a, <i>s. v.</i> Mishnah. However, one should be reluctant to consider this the original text; it might well be Rashba’s reconstruction (it replaces Yerushalmi או by Babli אי and is elliptic elsewhere). But since the text certainly captures the intent of the Yerushalmi, it is presented here with commentary (from edition Brno 1798, reprint New York 1961):<br>שְׁמוּאֵל אָמַר. לִצְדָדִין הִיא מַתְנִיא. אִי בְנָזִיר אִי בְקָרְבָּן אִי בִשְׁבוּעָה. רִבִּי זְעִירָה אָמַר. נָדַר בִּשְׁלָשְׁתָּן. אָמַר רִבִּי אָבִין. מָאן דְּבָעֵי מִיפְתָּר הָדָא רִבִּי זְעִירָא בְּמִי שֶׁהָיָה לְפָנָיו אֶשְׁכּוֹל אֶחָד וּבָא אֶחָד וְאָמַר. הֲרֵינִי נָזִיר מִמֶּנּוּ. הֲרֵי זֶה נָזִיר. בָּא אַחֵר וְאָמַר. הֲרֵי עָלַי כְקָרְבָּן. וּבָא אַחֵר וְאָמַר. הֲרֵי עָלַי כְּנִדְרֵי רְשָׁעִים. בָּא אַחֵר וְאָמַר. מַה שֶׁאָֽמְרוּ שְׁלָשְׁתָּן עָלַי. נִמְצָא זֶה נָדוּר בְּנָזִיר וּבְקָרְבָּן וּבִשְׁבוּעָה.<br>Samuel said, it is taught about several cases, either about the <i>nazir</i>, or a sacrifice, or an oath. Rebbi Ze'ira said, he made a vow in all three of them. Rebbi Abin said, one wants to explain that of Rebbi Ze'ira about a person who has before him a bunch of grapes. A person came and said, I will be a <i>nazir</i> from these<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">He makes a vow by referring to the bunch of grapes which will be forbidden to him. But since the status of <i>nazir</i> cannot be split, he is a <i>nazir</i> in all its aspects.</i>, he is a <i>nazir</i>. Another person came and said, these are for me <i>qorbän</i><sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">That bunch of grapes is forbidden to him as if it were a dedicated sacrifice.</i>; another person came and said, they are for me like vows of the wicked<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">As the Mishnah said, since the “wicked” are impulsive people who swear on every occasion, he is considered not only to have vowed but also to have sworn that he will not eat the grapes.</i>. Another person came and said, what these three said is on me; it turned out that this one vowed for <i>nazir</i>, <i>qorbän</i>, and oath.</i> Samuel said, the Mishnah speaks about several cases, either as <i>nazir,</i> or a sacrifice, or an oath. Rebbi Ze‘ira said, he is a <i>nazir</i> in all three of them. Rebbi Abin said, if somebody wants to explain that of Rebbi Ze‘ira, it is such: There was before him a bunch of grapes and another person came and said, there is an oath on me, then there is an oath on him. Another person came and said, that is <i>qorbān</i> for me, it is <i>qorbān</i> for him. Another person came and said, there is an oath on me, then there is an oath on him. Another person came and said, what these three said is on me, does it not turn out that this one is obligated for <i>qorbān</i> and oath?
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">As the Mishnah said, since the “wicked” are impulsive people who swear on every occasion, he is considered not only to have vowed but also to have sworn that he will not eat the grapes.</i>: “As to their free-will offerings, he did not say anything.” Does that mean that the wicked give free-will offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The formulation seems to imply that free-will offerings of the wicked do exist; otherwise how could one speak about them?</i>? Since he gave a free-will offering, he is not wicked. The Mishnah follows Rebbi Jehudah, since it was stated in the name of Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 5:4" href="/Ecclesiastes.5.4"><i>Eccl.</i> 5:4</a>. In all parallel sources, the attributions are switched between R. Jehudah and R. Meїr <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kohelet Rabbah 5:2" href="/Kohelet_Rabbah.5.2"><i>[Eccl. rabba</i>5(2</a>) a Yerushalmi source; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 9a" href="/Nedarim.9a">Babli <i>Nedarim</i> 9a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 2a" href="/Chullin.2a"><i>Ḥulin</i> 2a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Chullin 2:5" href="/Tosefta_Chullin.2.5">Tosephta <i>Ḥulin</i>2:17</a>]. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kohelet Rabbah 5:2" href="/Kohelet_Rabbah.5.2"><i>Eccl. rabba</i> 5(2</a>) is a shortened parallel to the entire paragraph. There, and in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Vayikra Rabbah 16:5" href="/Vayikra_Rabbah.16.5"><i>Lev. rabba</i> 16(5</a>), the next verse, “do not let your mouth make your flesh sin” is referred to people who pledge money for charity but do not redeem their pledges.</i>: “It is better that you should not make a vow than that you make a vow but do not pay,” better in any case is that you should not make a vow at all. Rebbi Meїr says, “it is better that you should not make a vow than that you make a vow but do not pay,” better in any case is that you should make a vow and pay. And so it says<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 76:12" href="/Psalms.76.12"><i>Ps.</i> 76:12</a>.</i>, “make vows and pay to your God.” How does one handle a free-will offering? He brings his sheep to the Temple courtyard and says, that one is an elevation offering<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">In this way he is sure to have taken on an open-ended financial obligation.</i>. Rebbi Abin said, Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 22a" href="/Nedarim.22a">Babli, 22a</a>, this is attributed to R. Yannai, in connection with his interpretation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 20:25" href="/Proverbs.20.25"><i>Prov.</i> 20:25</a>, and is rejected in practice.</i> used to open a door<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the topic of Chapter Nine. While it is written that the maker of a vow shall not profane his word, it is rabbinic tradition (contested by Samaritans, Karaites, and probably Sadducees) that while he cannot profane his word, the “heads of the tribes” addressed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30" href="/Numbers.30"><i>Num.</i> 30</a>, and in their stead one ordained rabbi or a court of three lay people, can free a person from the obligations of his vow. But such a ruling depends on the person making the vow repenting it. An argument which induces such repentance is called “door of regret”, פֶּתַח חֲרָטָה, or simply “door”.</i>: If you had known that one who makes a vow is called wicked, would you have vowed? Rebbi Yannai said, “it is a trap for a human to call ‘sanctified’ and afterwards to check out the vows,<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 20:25" href="/Proverbs.20.25"><i>Prov.</i> 20:25</a>.</i>” if one started to make vows, his account book<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Greek πίναξ, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 9:13:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.9.13.5"><i>Soṭah</i> Chapter 9, Note 220</a>.</i> is opened. Another explanation: “it is a trap for a human to call ‘sanctified’ and to be late to check out the vows,” if a person is in arrears with his vows, his account book is opened. It happened to one who said, I undertake to bring an elevation offering and he tarried to bring it. His ship sank at sea<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kohelet Rabbah 5:2" href="/Kohelet_Rabbah.5.2"><i>Eccl. rabba</i> 5(2</a>), the man perished in his ship.</i>.
“Like vows of the good ones, he did not say anything.” Does this mean that good people make vows<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The formulation seems to imply that free-will offerings of the wicked do exist; otherwise how could one speak about them?</i>? Since that one made a vow, he is not a good one. <sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Purification offerings cannot be voluntary; they are required either for an inadvertent sin or for purification from a state of impurity. A male who does not sin inadvertently and who does not suffer from an impure sickness has no way to fulfill the commandments relative to the purification offerings. A woman can always bring a purification offering after childbirth.</i> The Mishnah follows Rebbi Jehudah since it was stated in the name of Rebbi Jehudah, the ancient pious ones desired to bring a purification offering<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the paragraph, the text is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:5:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.5.2-4"><i>Nazir</i> 1:6</a>, 51c 1. 36.</i>, but the Omnipresent did not let a sin happen to them; so they made a vow of <i>nazir</i> in order to be able to bring a purification offering<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">One of the 3 prescribed animal sacrifices at the end of the <i>nazir</i> period; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:14" href="/Numbers.6.14"><i>Num.</i> 6:14</a>.</i>. Rebbi Simeon says, they became sinners because they made a vow of <i>nazir,</i> for it was said: “He shall atone for him for what he sinned about the person<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:11" href="/Numbers.6.11"><i>Num.</i> 6:11</a>. In <i>Sifry Num.</i> 30, R. Ismael points out that this verse is written about the <i>nazir</i> who became inadvertently impure in the impurity of the dead, who is in effect a sinner in respect to the dead person. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 10a" href="/Nedarim.10a">Babli, 10a</a>, accepts the argument of R. Simeon, which in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 1:1" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).1.1">Tosephta, 1:1</a>, is in the name of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel.</i>,” that one sinned against his own person because he barred himself from [drinking] wine. It turns out that that of Simeon the Just parallels Rebbi Simeon. As it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 9b" href="/Nedarim.9b">Babli 9b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:5:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.5.2-4"><i>Nazir</i> Yerushalmi 1:6</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 4b" href="/Nedarim.4b">Babli 4b</a>, <i>Sifry Num.</i> 22, <i>Num. rabba</i>10(20).</i>: Simeon the Just said, I never ate the reparation offering of a <i>nazir</i> except once. Once a man came to me from the South, I saw that he was reddish, with beautiful eyes and good looks, and his hair in nice rows of waves<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">A combination of images referring to David (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 17:42" href="/I_Samuel.17.42"><i>1S.</i> 17:42</a>) and the friend in the Song of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Song of Songs 5:11" href="/Song_of_Songs.5.11">Songs (5:11</a>).</i>. I said to him, my son, what induced you to cut off that beautiful hair? He said to me: Great man, I was a shephard in my village and I went to fill the water vessel with water when I saw my mirror image in the water and my instinct rushed over me and tried to lose me from the World<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">He realized how much money he could make as a male prostitute in a hellenized city but that he would lose the World to Come.</i>. I said to it, wicked! You are rushing me to something which is not yours; it is upon me to sanctify you to Heaven! I bent my head to him and said, my son, there should be many more in Israel who fulfill the Omnipresent’s will like you. About you the verse says<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:2" href="/Numbers.6.2"><i>Num.</i> 6:2</a>.</i>, “man or woman, if he clearly articulates vowing a vow of <i>nazir,</i> to be a <i>nazir</i> for the Eternal.” Rebbi Mana asked<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 9b" href="/Nedarim.9b">Babli 9b</a>.</i>: Why following Simeon the Just, even following Rebbi Simeon? Did Simeon the Just never eat a purification offering for suet<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">The standard purification offering is for the purification from an inadvertent sin which at least carries a penalty of extirpation by Divine decree, e. g., if somebody ate suet or blood inadvertently. In order to effect the purification, the Cohanim have to eat the sacrificial meat (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:19" href="/Leviticus.6.19"><i>Lev.</i> 6:19</a>). How can somebody called “the Just” refuse to purify people?</i>? Did Simeon the Just never eat a purification offering for blood? Simeon the Just holds that people make a vow while they are upset. Since they make the vow while they are upset, in the end, they wonder<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">They feel that they should not have made the vow. This becomes acute in particular in the case of the reparation offering, which is brought only in case of impurity of the <i>nazir,</i> who has to restart his entire time as <i>nazir</i> after his impurity has been repaired. Since the verse repeatedly requires that offerings in the Temple must be brought willingly (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:3" href="/Leviticus.1.3"><i>Lev.</i> 1:3</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:29" href="/Leviticus.22.29">22:29</a>), an offering brought unwillingly is of questionable validity.</i>. But if he wonders, his sacrifices become similar to one of those who slaughtered profane animals in the Temple courtyard. But this one made a well thought-out dedication, when his mouth and his thoughts were in unison<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Which alone makes the vow unquestionably valid.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody says <i>qônām</i><sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">“Sacrifice” in Phoenician. The other words have not been identified in any language. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.1.1">Note 1</a>.</i>, or <i>qônāḥ,</i> or <i>qônās,</i> these are substitute names for <i>qorbān. Ḥēreq, ḥērek, ḥēreph,</i> these are substitute names for ban. <i>Nāzîq, nāzîaḥ, pāzîaḥ,</i> these are substitute names for <i>nazir. Šĕbûtâ, šĕqûqâ,</i> or he made a vow of <i>Moy</i><sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">The first half of the name <i>Moyses</i> (Moses). The connection between Moses and oaths is given later, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.2.4">Note 122</a>. While the difference between vow and oath is important in rabbinic law, people in everyday speech do not make these fine distinctions. If the verb is an expression of making a vow but the noun is “oath”, the entire sentence has to be interpreted as being an oath.</i>, these are substitute names for an oath.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody says <i>qônām,</i> or <i>qônāḥ,</i>” etc. So far for himself. Can one dedicate to Heaven by saying <i>qônām</i><sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Can one dedicate property to the Temple using the expression <i>qônām</i> or any other of the recognized substitutes for <i>qorbān?</i></i>? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">There exists a Babylonian version of this <i>baraita,</i> <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 35a" href="/Nedarim.35a">Babli 35a</a> and in abbreviated form <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 4:6" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).4.6">Tosephta 4:6</a>. The Babylonian version differs from the Yerushalmi in that the lender asserts that he has only one spade which he needs for himself and only asserts that he has no spare and, most importantly, that the lying lender’s property is only forbidden to him during his lifetime but becomes the property of his heir while in the Yerushalmi the property becomes the Temple’s. Therefore, the position of the Babli is opposed to that of the Yerushalmi and every attempt to emend and explain the Yerushalmi in the light of the Babli falsifies the text.</i>: One said to another, lend me your spade<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">In modern Hebrew, קרדום is an axe. But in Mishnaic Hebrew it means “spade” as shown by the formulaic expression קרדום לחפור בו “a <i>qardom</i> used for digging.”</i>. He said, <i>qônām</i> the spade if I have it, <i>qônām</i> my property on me. If he has a spade, his property would be forbidden for this one “has no spade”<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">He made a conditional vow. The vow must refer to an object. If he has no spade, there is nothing the vow refers to, and the vow is nonexistent. But should he have a spade, the vow refers to an object and is valid.</i>. Rebbi Taḥlifa from Caesarea said, there is a difference, for he used the same expression to attach the spade and to attach his property<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">As described in detail now, the two vows are one and both refer to the spade.</i>. As you look at it, if the spade is sanctified, so is his property. If the spade is not sanctified, neither is his property. If he said, <i>qônām</i> the spade if I have it, and added <i>qônām</i> my property on me, if he owns a spade, his property is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">To himself, but not to any other person in the world.</i>. That is correct. Rebbi Joshua ben Ḥananiah said, if he had said “my property”<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Without repeating the word <i>qônām,</i> or any equivalent expression, there is no vow.</i>, it would be permitted for he stopped. That is correct. He only asserted that the person had no spade. Therefore, if he has a spade, his property is forbidden. That means that the spade was sanctified. That means that the property was sanctified<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joshua ben Ḥanania holds that the property is permanently forbidden to everyone, including the heirs, and must be surrendered to the Temple administrator. This proves that <i>qônām</i> is as valid for dedications as <i>qorbān.</i></i>. This means that a person may dedicate to Heaven using the expression <i>qônām.</i>
Rebbi Jeremiah asked: May a person bind himself by a word which has both a profane and a sacral meaning? Did we not state קוֹנָס? Its name is קונסה<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">Which word is intended is not quite clear. The early commentators vote for קְנָס “a fine”; then קוּנְסָה could be a stand-in for קְנָסָא. Another possibility would be קִינְסָא “a chip”. There is no problem in asserting that קוֹנָס is used only for vows. Note Arabic قّونّس “point of the helmet”.</i>. Did we not state שְּׁבוּתָה? Its name is שְׁפוּתָה<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">“Cooking”. It also could be a word שפתא <i>[Tanḥuma Re’e</i> (5) “a bench”.] This is another example to show that in Galilee, under the influence of Greek, β was /v/ and /v/ close to /f/.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the paragraph, the text is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:1:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.1.2-9"><i>Nazir</i> 1:1</a>, 51a 1. 52.</i> But did not Bar Qappara state <i>ḥeres</i><sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">In Mishnaic Hebrew the word usually means “potsherd”, which certainly is not coming close to the meaning “ban” for which it may be substituted according to Bar Qappara. In Biblical Hebrew, it may be “sun” or “scabies”.</i>? Rebbi Ze‘ira said, that is a name relating to the High One: <sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 9:7" href="/Job.9.7"><i>Job</i> 9:7</a>.</i>“If He commands the sun: would it not shine?”. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, these are Gentile words<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">The Yerushalmi is inconsistent in its attribution. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 1:1:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.1.1.2-9"><i>Nazir</i> 1:1</a> (and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 10a" href="/Nedarim.10a">Babli 10a</a>), R. Joḥanan is reported to hold that the substitutes are words from foreign languages whereas R. Simeon ben Laqish holds that they are artificial words created by the Sages to avoid using Hebrew sacral words. In the latter case it would be obvious that only officially sanctioned words may be used.</i>, like those Nabateans who say <i>khaspa</i> for <i>ḥaspa</i><sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">They cannot distinguish between ح and خ.</i><i>.</i> Rebbi Yose said, it is reasonable in other places, but in a place where the <i>nazir</i> is called <i>naziq</i><sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">Where ר is not a dental sound but close to a French /r/, which may be confounded with ق or غ. If the locals hear the equivalent of <i>nazir,</i> that is enough to establish the vow.</i>, do I say that a <i>nazir</i> of people with speech defects should not be a <i>nazir</i>?
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 10b" href="/Nedarim.10b">Babli, 10b</a>, has a different collection of substitutes for “oath”.</i>: šĕ<i>butâ, šĕquqâ</i>; Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: š<i>ĕbuqa,</i> š<i>ĕqu‘â, šĕqurâ.</i> “A vow”, when he made a vow by Moy, by the oath which Moses vowed, “and Moses accepted the curse.<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 2:21" href="/Exodus.2.21"><i>Ex.</i> 2:21</a>. “Moses swore to stay by the man,” not “Moses agreed to stay with the man,” cf. the Targumim.</i>” Rebbi Jonah asked, why do we not say, by the oath which Saul vowed, “Saul menaced the people with a curse<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 14:24" href="/I_Samuel.14.24"><i>1S.</i> 14:24</a>. Why is the invocation of Saul’s name not a form of oath. In the verse quoted, the meaning of “imposing a curse by an oath” is clear and undisputed. There is no answer, probably because religious fear was never connected with the name of Saul. It is noteworthy that all through proto-Mishnaic, Mishnaic, and Talmudic periods the name Moses is not used for living persons; there are a few appearences of a substitute name such as Maishe.</i>”? By my right hand, that is a vow, by my left hand, that is a vow<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 10b" href="/Nedarim.10b">Babli, 10b</a>, only “right hand” is accepted.</i>. Rebbi Mattaniah said, for it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Daniel 12:7" href="/Daniel.12.7"><i>Dan.</i> 12:7</a>.</i>: “He lifted his right hand and his left hand to the Heavens and swore by the Eternally Living.”

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Not profane I shall not eat from yours, not usable, not pure, pure, or impure, leftover, or mushy, is forbidden. Like a sheep, like the storage places, like wood, like altar gifts, like the altar, like the Temple, like Jerusalem. If one made a vow by one of the serving pieces of the altar even though he did not mention “sacrifice”, it is as if he made a vow by the sacrifice. Rebbi Jehudah says, he who says “Jerusalem” did not say anything<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah enumerates substitute terms for <i>qorbān</i> in making an oath which forbids the use of certain things to the maker of the vow. Most details are explained in the Halakhah. “Leftover” are the leftovers of a valid sacrifice after the time allotted for its consumption; this is forbidden both as a sacrifice and as leftover. “Mushy” is the biblical term for a sacrifice which was brought from the start with the idea that it should be eaten out of its time and/or its place. This also is doubly forbidden.<br>An extended list of substitute expressions is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 1:3" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).1.3">Tosephta, 1:3</a> (partially quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 13a" href="/Nedarim.13a">Babli, 13a</a>).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Not profane I shall not eat from yours,” etc. That which I would eat from you shall not be profane but a sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">The same explanation in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 11a" href="/Nedarim.11a">Babli, 11a</a>.</i>.
“Not usable” for me but it is for the altar.
“Not pure,” for me but it is for the altar.
“Pure,” for the altar but not for me.
“Impure,” for me but [pure] for the altar. 
“Mushy”. There is no difference whether mushy for an elevation offering or mushy for a well-being offering<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">The elevation offering is totally burned on the altar; no part of it is eaten. Nevertheless, there is a time limit on the sacrifice; it must be burned on the altar during the day it was slaughtered and the following night. If the blood was received by the officiating Cohen with the idea of burning the sacrifice the next day, the sacrifice itself becomes forbidden as mushy. The well-being offering can be eaten for two days and the intervening night or, for a thanksgiving offering, the day and the following night; but if its blood is brought to the altar with the idea of eating from the sacrifice when mushy, immediately it is permanently forbidden.</i>. The leftover of an elevation offering [never]<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that the scribe switched “had” and “did not have” between elevation and well-being offerings. Since the elevation offering never was permitted to anybody, it was forbidden to use it while it was a sacrifice and it remained forbidden as leftover after its time had expired. Therefore, “leftover from an elevation sacrifice” is a thing which is permanently forbidden. Used in a vow formula it implies permanent prohibition.</i> had a time when it was usable, the leftover of an elevation offering (never) had a time when it was usable<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Since a well-being offering can be eaten for some time by the offerer and his family and the prohibition of “leftover” is one after the fact, if somebody refers to food as “leftover from a well-being offering” it is unclear whether one refers to the time it was permitted or when it was forbidden. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 12a" href="/Nedarim.12a">Babli (12a</a>) takes it for granted that “leftover” as object of a vow can only refer to leftover from an elevation offering, implicitly answering the open question of the Yerushalmi.</i>. If he said, this is for me as a leftover of a well-being offering, what is it?
“Like a sheep.” Rebbi Joḥanan said, like the sheep of the daily offering<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">The most common sacrifice, a natural substitute for <i>qorbān.</i> In that case, one should read כָּאִימְרָא. The statements are quoted in <i>Gen. rabba</i>56(14).</i>. There, they say, like the young of a purification offering<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">The purification offering of a common person is a female animal. If she gives birth after dedication, the lamb is totally forbidden; it cannot be used for anything and nothing can be done for it. What the rabbis there (Babylonia) assert is that a substitute for <i>qorbān</i> can be something which is forbidden even to the altar (and so could be considered as the opposite of <i>qorbān</i>). This opinion is not mentioned in the Babli.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, like our father Abraham’s ram<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ber.</i> 22:13. This was a sacrifice on an altar; one reads כְּאִימְרָא.</i>. What Rebbi Ḥiyya<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Gen. rabba,</i> this is attributed to Bar Qappara. In one opinion, the ram was created at the end of the Sixth Day of Creation [Mishnah <i>Abot</i> 5(9)].</i> stated supports Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: Like a sheep which never suckled. Following the opinions of the rabbis there, one could state: Like the Temple courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">This also is forbidden for profane use and is not a sacrifice.</i>. Rebbi Abin said, following the opinion of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish one could state: Like the sacrifices on the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">See next paragraph.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Abun said, following Rebbi Joḥanan one could state: Like the cleansing of the inner altar and the candelabra<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">This really refers to the statement that one may make a vow by referring to the serving pieces of the altar. R. Joḥanan prefers that one make the vow by something which is served daily.</i>.
“Like the storage places,” like the storage places of wood, like the storage places of sacrificial animals<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">The storage place for altar wood was in the Temple courtyard, where wood was checked for worms (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Middot 2:5" href="/Mishnah_Middot.2.5">Mishnah <i>Middot</i> 2:5</a>). The holding area for the animals was outside the Temple on the Temple Mount, where animals were checked for blemishes.</i>. “Like wood,” like the two logs<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">The logs needed to burn on them the daily sacrifice, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 2:4:1-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.2.4.1-4">Mishnah <i>Yoma</i> 2:5</a>.</i>. “Like altar gifts,” like the flame of the fire<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Everywhere in talmudic literature, אִשֶּׁה is derived from the root אֵשׁ “fire”. But fire is not anything a vow can fall on. The same problem arises with the last three terms which cannot represent <i>qorbān</i>, but only the place of the <i>qorbān</i>.</i>. “Like the altar,” like the sacrifices on the altar. “Like the Temple hall,” like the sacrifices in the Temple hall<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">The daily incense and the blood of those special sacrifices whose blood has to be sprinkled on the incense altar.</i>. Like Jerusalem,” like the sacrifices in Jerusalem.
“If one made a vow by one of the serving pieces of the altar,” e. g., cup<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">To catch the blood at the moment of slaughter.</i>, shovel<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">For incense.</i>, and pouring cup.
“Rebbi Jehudah says, he who says ‘like Jerusalem’<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">This is a reformulation of the Mishnah. According to the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 11a" href="/Nedarim.11a">Babli, 11a</a>, according to the Tanna of the Mishnah, R. Jehudah would accept “like Jerusalem” as a <i>qorbān</i> formula. There is no hint of this in the Yerushalmi.</i> did not say anything,” since this one intended to refer only to its wood and stones<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Which are permitted.</i>. “Like the Torah,” that is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 1:4" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).1.4">Tosephta 1:4</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 14b" href="/Nedarim.14b">Babli 14b</a>.</i>. “Like the sanctity of the Torah, like what is written in it,” that is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 1:4" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).1.4">Tosephta 1:4</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 14b" href="/Nedarim.14b">Babli 14b</a>.</i> like the sacrifices written in it. There are Tannaїm who state, “like the Torah and what is written in it,” is permitted. Rebbi Abin bar Cahana said, that is Rebbi Simeon’s, as Rebbi Joḥanan said, by the words of Rebbi Simeon, if it turns out that he had no wheat, he is free also for the rest<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the discussion in <i>Šebuot</i> 5:4. A (who has no witnesses and no documents to prove his case) says to B, give me the wheat, barley, and spelt which you hold for me. B says, I have nothing of yours. A says to B, swear that you have no wheat, barley, and spelt of mine, and B says Amen. If it turns out that he swore falsely, he is guilty of one crime. R. Simeon says, if he swore correctly about the wheat, he cannot be prosecuted for swearing falsely about barley and spelt since an oath (and a vow) which is partially voided is totally voided.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, it is everybody’s opinion. “Like the Torah”, like the sanctity of the Torah. “Like what is written in it,” like the sanctity of the verses<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">Since the second opinion is treated second and at length, it is the opinion accepted in the Yerushalmi, in contrast to the Babli.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody says sacrifice, elevation offering, or flour offering, purification offering, or well-being offering, that I will not eat with you, he is forbidden; Rebbi Jehudah permits<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jehudah requires that the object to which the vow refers be mentioned. Therefore, if he had said “like a sacrifice, like an offering” it would be a valid vow (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 1:2" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).1.2">Tosephta 1:2</a>).</i>. The sacrifice, like a sacrifice, sacrifice if I would eat with you, he is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">Since in this case one might read the sentence as: Any food I would eat from you should be a (the, like a) sacrifice.</i>. It is for sacrifice what I will not eat with you, Rebbi Meїr forbids. If somebody says to another person, a <i>qônām</i> that my mouth speaks with you, that my hand works with you, that my foot goes with you, he is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">At first glance it seems that these vows are not object-oriented since speech, working, and walking are no concrete objects. But since mouth, hand, and foot are concrete objects, the vow can refer to them and then this person’s mouth, hands, and feet are forbidden any contact with the other person, which can be realized only if the person swearing the oath refrains from doing anything for the person intended by the vow.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody says sacrifice, elevation offering, or flour offering,” etc. Everybody agrees <i>this</i> sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">הָא קָרְבָּן “this is a sacrifice” is not an expression of referral.</i> is permitted, <i>like a</i> sacrifice is forbidden. Where do they disagree? Sacrifice. Rebbi Jehudah says, he who says ‘sacrifice’ is as if he said ‘<i>this</i> sacrifice’ and is permitted; but the rabbis say, he who says ‘sacrifice’ is as if he said ‘<i>like a</i> sacrifice’ and is forbidden. Rebbi Jehudah said to them: Do you not agree that one who said “oath” is like one who said “this oath” and is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">If he would say “like an oath” it would be a denial of oath and would be invalid, but “like a sacrifice” is the essential formula of a vow.</i>? And here he who says ‘sacrifice’ is as if he said ‘<i>this</i> sacrifice’ and is permitted! But the rabbis throw restrictions on it<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">In matters of vows and oaths, the translation of the vernacular into the formal language of the law always must follow the restrictive interpretation (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.2.4.1">Mishnah 2:4</a>).</i>. One who said “oath” is like one who said “this oath” and is forbidden. But here, he who says ‘sacrifice’ is as if he said ‘<i>like a</i> sacrifice’ and is forbidden.
Fife, the fife, with a fife, either ‘that I shall eat with you’ or ‘that I shall not eat with you’, is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ḥālîl</i> “fife” is derived from the root חלל II, Arabic خلّ “to pierce, make a hole” (among other meanings). Nobody takes this as close to <i>ḥûlîn</i> “profane”, derived from חלל I, Arabic حلّ “being permitted, not holy” (among other meanings) or חָלִילָה “desecration, God forbid!”. The expression cannot be interpreted as a vow.</i>. No profane food<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">Read לָא חוּלִין.</i> that I would eat with you, forbidden. Profane food I would eat with you, permitted.
Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Are there words of Rebbi Meїr that from a negative one understands a positive? There is no sacrifice from this. What I shall not eat from you. Therefore, what I shall eat from you is profane, it is not qorbān<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah about לָא קָרְבָּן mentions only R. Meїr but does not mention the anonymous majority which must hold that no vow is intended. It is generally accepted that R. Meїr does not accept to derive a positive statement from the inversion of a negative (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 11a" href="/Nedarim.11a">Babli 11a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 13b" href="/Nedarim.13b">13b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 36a" href="/Shevuot.36a"><i>Šebuot</i> 36a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 17a" href="/Sotah.17a"><i>Soṭah</i> 17a</a>; Yerushalmi <i>Erubin</i> 3, 21b 1. 24, <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:3 64a 1. 16, <i>Šebuot</i> 4:14 35d 1. 66, 7:1 37c 1. 45). It is obvious that the first sentence is a rhetorical question.</i>.
‘My going [is forbidden] to you, my speech [is forbidden] to you,’ he said nothing. Why?
