Because he referred the vow to something immaterial. “Vow, vow”<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">This is an application of the second hermeneutical rule <i>gezerah šawah</i> (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 1:1:20" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.1.1.20"><i>Berakhot</i> Chapter 1, Note 70</a>) that in legal texts, a word has only one definite meaning. The word “vow” is used in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:2" href="/Leviticus.27.2"><i>Lev.</i> 27:2</a> to describe the pledge of a predetermined sum of money, which is material. Therefore in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:3" href="/Numbers.30.3"><i>Num</i>. 30:3</a>, the word “vow” also must refer to something material.</i>. Just as “vow” which was said there refers to something (im-)material<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">The context requires that both times the expression שֶׁאֵין בּוֹ מַמָּשׁ “immaterial” must be emended to שֶׁיֵּשׁ בּוֹ מַמָּשׁ “material”. Unfortunately, there are no parallels to this argument in talmudic literature.</i>, so “vow” said here refers to something (im)material. ‘My eye [is forbidden] to see for you, my ear [is forbidden] to hear you,’ he is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">Since now the vow is referred to something material.</i>. You say, ‘my hand [is forbidden] to work with you,’ he is forbidden. If he ploughed with him<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">If the worker makes a vow not to enjoy anything from his employer, can he work for him since the transaction is a simple exchange of work for money, or is he forbidden to work in case his work causes a permanent improvement of the employer’s property? The question is not answered.</i> on the ground, what is forbidden to him? Up to the amount of his wages or any improvement of the ground? If he fenced in<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">Some commentators and editors want to emend here either to גרר  “to grate”, or גדד “to cut”. There seems to be no reason for emendation.</i> an oven with him, what is forbidden to him? Up to the amount of his wages or any improvement of the oven?

Chapter 2



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The following vows are permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Even though they sound like vows, they are not vows.</i>: Profane I would eat with you<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Since he vows to eat what is permitted, there is nothing to it.</i>; like pork<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">As the Halakhah explains, a vow must refer to something which can be a sacrifice. Since swine cannot be sacrificial animals, if he says “what I would eat from you is like pork”, there is no vow and no legal consequence. If he would say, a <i>qônām</i> that what I would eat from you is like pork, he would be forbidden since he referred to the sacrifice.</i>, like idolatry, like carcasses<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Forbidden food, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:21" href="/Deuteronomy.14.21"><i>Deut</i>. 14:21</a>.</i>, like meat from a torn animal<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Forbidden food, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:30" href="/Exodus.22.30"><i>Ex</i>. 22:30</a>.</i>, like abominations<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Forbidden food, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:29" href="/Leviticus.11.29"><i>Lev</i>. 11:29</a> ff.</i>, like crawling things<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Forbidden food, anything not on the list of permitted animals. These are explicitly permitted to Gentiles, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 9:3" href="/Genesis.9.3"><i>Gen</i>. 9:3</a>.</i>, like Aaron’s <i>ḥallah</i> and his heave<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Heave and <i>ḥallah</i>, the heave from dough, are holy. Sacrifices can only be brought from sources that are profane before dedication. Therefore, heave and <i>ḥallah</i> cannot become sacrifices. As long as the person making the vow does not mention <i>qorbān</i> or any of its substitutes, there is no vow.</i>, are permitted. One who says to his wife, you are for me like my mother<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">He says that sexual relations with his wife should be forbidden as they are with his mother. Since his mother cannot be a sacrifice, there is no vow but the rabbi is not permitted to tell him this but must find another reason (e. g., do you realize how much money a divorce would cost you?) to find a cause to annul the vow because people should be made uncomfortable vowing. If the vow is not dissolved within one week, the wife can ask the court to force a divorce; the husband being the guilty party (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.6.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 5:6</a>). (Cf. Qor‘an 58:2.)</i>, one finds for him an opening from another place that he should not be flippant about this. A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not sleep, that I shall not speak, that I shall not walk, or one who says to his wife, a <i>qônām</i> that I shall not sleep with you, he is under the obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:3" href="/Numbers.30.3"><i>Num</i>. 30:3</a>. As long as <i>qorbān</i> or one of its substitutes is not mentioned, sleeping, speaking, and walking are all immaterial and there really is no vow. But since there was intent to make a vow, the verse may be applied (at least by rabbinic standards).</i> “he shall not profanate his word.” An oath that I shall not sleep, that I shall not speak, that I shall not walk, he is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Since an oath does not depend on any material substrate (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.1.1">Chapter 1, Note 4</a>).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The following vows are permitted,” etc. “To the Eternal<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:3" href="/Numbers.30.3"><i>Num</i>. 30.3</a>.</i>”, nobody can forbid anything on himself unless it could be given to the Eternal<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The comparison used in a vow must refer either to a sacrifice or to something that could be a sacrifice or a <i>ḥerem.</i> In the language of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 14a" href="/Nedarim.14a">Babli, 14a</a>, “there is no vow unless it relates to something that can be vowed.” A lenghty paraphrase in <i>Sifry Num</i>. 153.</i>. What is stated in the House of Rav disagrees: “From where that one may not make and dissolve vows which are dissolved for you from Heaven but people consider them as binding? The verse says<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:3" href="/Numbers.30.3"><i>Num</i>. 30.3</a>.</i>, ‘he shall not profanate his word.’ He should not make his words profane.” They wanted to say, for example using “<i>qorbān</i>” or “oath”; but in all other respects it would be permitted. It comes to tell you, also all other respects<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The opinion of R.Yose, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.1.8">Chapter 1, Notes 64</a> ff.</i>. “To prohibit a prohibition on himself<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:3" href="/Numbers.30.3"><i>Num</i>. 30.3</a>.</i>”. There are Tannaїm who state: on himself, not on others. There are Tannaїm who state: even on others. They wanted to say that he who says on himself, not on others, to forbid others’ property<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence has been badly distorted by editors and commentators of recent editions of the Yerushalmi. The original scribe of the ms. wrote in both cases על אחדים. The corrector changed the first occurence into של אחרים. It seems that the correct version is על של אחרים “[to forbid his own property] to others.” It is clear that in both opinions, a person may forbid his own property (permanently) on another (in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 47a" href="/Nedarim.47a">Babli, 47a</a>, compared to the power of a father to disinherit a son), but only according to the second opinion one may not forbid the property of others, over which he has no control, to himself. The second opinion has no parallel in either Talmud.<br>In <i>Sifry Num</i>. 153 one reads: “‘To prohibit a prohibition on himself’, he prohibits for himself but not for others.” This is opposed to both Talmudim.</i>, but he who says even on others, to forbid his own property for others, but not the property of others for himself.
“One who says to his wife, you are for me like my mother”. Your cohabitation is for me like cohabitation with my mother, like my mother’s flesh; he did not say anything<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The translation and interpretation of these sentences are uncertain. Another approach to the text, based on a different reading of the meaning of the periods, would be: “One who says to his wife, you are for me like my mother, your cohabitation is for me like cohabitation with my mother. Like my mother’s flesh, he did not say anything.” In that case, the first example is an explanation of the Mishnah, the second is a separate statement which might not conflict with the Mishnah. This is the approach of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 14a" href="/Nedarim.14a">Babli, 14a</a>. If the translation in the text is chosen, the requirement that a formal annulment of the vow is required, is restricted to the wording in the Mishnah, where it is not so clear even to the unlearned that the vow is null and void.</i>. If he said, this loaf is for me like cohabitation with my mother, what<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">What are the rules?</i>? Let us hear from the following: A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not sleep with you, Rav says, he is forbidden, Samuel says he is permitted. How does Samuel uphold “he shall not profanate his word”? As if he shall not profanate his word<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of the Mishnah is purely rabbinical; it cannot possibly have a biblical basis.</i>. Rav sticks with his opinion<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">From the previous paragraph.</i>: The Tannaїm in the House of Rav disagree: “From where that one may not make and dissolve vows which are dissolved for you from Heaven but people consider them as binding? The verse says<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:3" href="/Numbers.30.3"><i>Num</i>. 30.3</a>.</i>, ‘he shall not profanate his word.’ He should not make his words profane.”
“An oath that I shall not sleep for three days,” one whips him and he can sleep immediately<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Since nobody can go without sleep for three days, this is a vain oath forbidden by the Third Commandment. For the transgression he is sentenced to whipping; the oath itself is void. A similar statement, attributed to R. Joḥanan, appears in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 15a" href="/Nedarim.15a">Babli, 15a</a>.</i>. “An oath that I shall not eat for three days,” one waits until he eats and then whips him<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Since people can live for three days without food, the oath is not in vain and he can be punished only if he transgresses his oath.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <i>Qorbān</i> [shall be] what I shall not eat at your place<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Since he forbids only what he did not eat but nobody can forbid another person’s property for other people, he did not say anything.</i>, or <i>qorbān</i> [shall be] what I shall eat from you<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">This clause is extremely difficult to explain since it is formulated exactly the standard way in which a person can prohibit another’s food for himself. The Babli reads הָקָרְבָּן, an expression which had been determined not to be an oath (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.4.2">Chapter 1, Note 153</a>). It is clear in all three cases that the same vow declared as <i>qônām</i> or any other substitute would be valid and the person making the vow would be forbidden to eat from the other person since then the intent would be clear. It is only the use of the word which refers directly to sacrifice which makes the vow invalid.</i>, no <i>qorbān</i> [shall be] what I shall not eat from you<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">This clearly is idle talk.</i>; he is permitted. An oath that I shall not eat at your place, an oath that I shall eat from you, no oath that I shall not eat from you<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">For a person who holds that a double negation is a positive this is an oath that he will eat; this excludes R. Meїr who does not accept that argument, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.4.4">Chapter 1, Note 158</a>.</i>; he is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">He is bound by his oath for negative or positive.</i>. That is more restrictive for oaths than for vows. What is more restrictive for vows than for oaths? If he said <i>qônām</i> that I shall not make a <i>sukkah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:42" href="/Leviticus.23.42"><i>Lev</i>. 23:42</a>. The hut must at least get a new roof each year, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.1.1">Mishnah <i>Sukkah</i> 1:1</a>.</i>, that I shall not take a <i>lulab</i><sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:40" href="/Leviticus.23.40"><i>Lev</i>. 23:40</a>, the palm branch of the “4 kinds”.</i>, that I shall not put on phylacteries<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:9" href="/Exodus.13.9"><i>Ex</i>. 13:9</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:16" href="/Exodus.13.16">16</a>.</i>, as a vow he is forbidden, as an oath he is permitted since one cannot swear to transgress commandments<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">This is a principle accepted by all Jewish groups: <i>Philo, The Special Laws</i> II, 12–15; <i>Damascus Document CD</i> XIV 9.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “<i>Qorbān</i> [shall be] what I shall not eat at your place,” etc. Why did we not state: An oath that I shall eat from you, he is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">He not only is permitted, he is obligated to eat at the other person’s place. whereas in the case of a vow he would be free to eat or not to eat.</i>? Only because we stated: “That is more restrictive for oaths than for vows.” We wanted to say about “an oath that I shall not eat of yours,” that the words of the Sages are: he is forbidden. Therefore it was not stated.
“That is a sacrifice, that is for a sacrifice, that is like a sacrifice,” is permitted. “That is an oath, that is for an oath, that is like an oath,” is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">In all cases quoted in the Mishnah, it makes no difference whether <i>qorbān</i> is stated alone or modified by one of the inseparable prepositions. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 15a" href="/Nedarim.15a">Babli, 15a</a>, it is held that R. Meїr would not accept this statement since he holds that לַקָּרְבָּן might be understood as a negation לָא קָרְבָּן = לָקָרְבָּן.</i>.
“That is more restrictive for oaths than for vows. What is more restrictive for vows than for oaths?” “Or a person who swears to lightly express with his lips for bad or for good<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:4" href="/Leviticus.5.4"><i>Lev</i>. 5:4</a>.</i>.” Just as doing good is a matter of choice, so doing bad is a matter of choice; that excludes any prohibition which is a commandment<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 16b-17a" href="/Nedarim.16b-17a">Babli, 16b/17a</a>, this verse, dealing with the obligation of a sacrifice for an oath inadvertently not kept, only frees the maker of an oath to violate some commandment from the obligation to bring a sacrifice.</i>. They wanted to say, inadvertently, but not intentionally. It was found stated: Rebbi Ismael says, “<i>anything</i> that comes out of <i>his</i> mouth he shall do,<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:3" href="/Numbers.30.3"><i>Num</i>. 30:3</a>. In the Babli, this verse frees him from the sin of not keeping his vow. In the Yerushalmi, it extends the rule to <i>any</i> oath, including an intentional one.</i>” not what comes out of Heaven’s mouth. Should we say that this is the same for vows of sanctification? Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Hila: For a man may vow his <i>sukkah</i> to Heaven<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Temurah 7:2" href="/Mishnah_Temurah.7.2">Mishnah <i>Temurah</i> 7:2</a>: Anything whatsoever can be vowed to support the Temple.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A vow inside a vow is possible, an oath inside an oath is impossible. For example, if one said, I shall be a <i>nazir</i> if I would eat, I shall be a <i>nazir</i> if I would eat, if he eats he is obligated for each pronouncement separately<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">If he drinks wine after eating, he can be sentenced to two whippings. If he keeps his vow, he has to observe two 30-day periods of <i>nezirut</i> and bring two sets of sacrifices.</i>. An oath that I will not eat, an oath that I will not eat, if he eats he is guilty only once<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">If he breaks his oath, he can be punished only once.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A vow inside a vow is possible,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The entire Halakhah is also in <i>Šebuot</i> 3:8.</i> Rebbi Yose asked: An oath, an oath, an oath that I shall not eat, but he ate? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Šebuot</i> 3:8.</i>: “An oath that I shall not eat this loaf, an oath that I shall not eat, an oath that I shall not eat. If he ate, he is guilty only once.” Because he said <i>this</i>. Therefore, if he had not said <i>this</i>, he would be guilty for each of the oaths<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph admits of two quite different interpretations. The first, by R. David Fraenckel, notes that in the question, the three oaths are mentioned before the object of the oath is specified, but in the Mishnah the object is specified before the second oath is pronounced. In the Mishnah, the second and third oaths are void since the loaf is already forbidden. But in R. Yose’s question, nothing is forbidden when the second and third oaths are pronounced; therefore, they all are valid and with one act the person who then eats the loaf commits three sins in one act.<br>The second, not very likely, explanation, by R. Moses Margalit, assumes that many loaves are before the person when he makes the multiple oath and one may assume that each single oath refers to a different loaf. Therefore, if he eats all of them he is guilty for each loaf separately since the Mishnah states only that if he expressly refers to the same loaf each time he swears, he is guilty only once.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">There is a parallel in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 28b-29a" href="/Shevuot.28b-29a">Babli, <i>Šebuot</i> 28b/29a</a>.</i> Avime the brother of Ḥefa<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, he is called ‘Epha. In the Babli, where Avime is called “his brother”, Avime is the superior scholar. In the Munich ms., the notation “his brother” is missing.</i> said, I studied vows, I studied oaths. Ḥefa wanted to examine him: There were five loaves before him and he said, an oath that I shall not eat this loaf. Then he said, these two loaves. Then he said, these three loaves. Then he said, these four loaves. Then he said, these five loaves. Then he ate the first, what are the rules? He said to him, he is guilty for each [oath] separately<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Assuming that the first loaf was included in every subsequent oath.</i>. He said to him, he is guilty only once. From the moment he mentioned “oath” about it, he made it like carcass meat<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Forbidden food, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:21" href="/Deuteronomy.14.21"><i>Deut</i>. 14:21</a>.</i>. From there on he is as if he wanted to apply oaths to prohibitions, but no oath can be applied to prohibitions. He examined him a second time. There were five loaves and he said, an oath about these five loaves. Then he said, these four. Then he said, these three. Then he said, these two. Then he said, this single one. Then he ate all of them? He said to him, he is guilty only once. He said to him, he is guilty for each [oath] separately. If somebody had said, an oath that I shall not eat five, and he ate four, would he be free from punishment<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Since he did not keep his oath. But in Avime’s opinion, the first oath voids all the subsequent ones.</i>? Rebbi Yose said, it is reasonable following Ḥefa’s brother in the last case, following Ḥefa in the first<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">In no case can an oath be added to an oath.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Indeterminate oaths are interpreted restrictively and explicit ones liberally. How is this? If one said, this is for me like salted meat<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., a sacrifice (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 2:13" href="/Leviticus.2.13"><i>Lev</i>. 2:13</a>).</i>, like libation wine<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:1-13" href="/Numbers.15.1-13"><i>Num</i>. 15:1–13</a>.</i>, if his intent was for Heaven, he is forbidden, if for idolatry, he is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Since everything destined for idolatrous worship is forbidden for any use, it cannot become <i>qorbān</i>.</i>, if indeterminate<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">If the person making the vow had nothing special in mind, his words have to be interpreted as referring to an instance which makes his vow valid.</i> he is forbidden. This is for me like a ban, if by a ban for Heaven<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">A gift to the Temple which is forbidden for any use until redeemed from the Temple treasury.</i>, he is forbidden, if priest’s<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Bans given to priests become their fully profane private property, not restricted in any way. A referral to such bans cannot prohibit.</i>, he is permitted, if indeterminate he is forbidden. This is for me like tithe, if animal tithe, he is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Animal tithe is automatically a sacrifice which does not need dedication (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:32" href="/Leviticus.27.32"><i>Lev</i>. 27:32</a>); it is <i>qorbān</i>.</i>, if from grain, he is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Tithe from which its heave was separated is totally profane in the Levite’s hand.</i>, if indeterminate he is forbidden. This is for me like heave, if Temple heave<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The half-šeqel yearly Temple tax which was used to buy public sacrifices; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 3:1:1-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.3.1.1-4">Mishnah <i>Šeqalim</i> 3:1</a>.</i>, he is forbidden, if heave from the threshing floor, he is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Since heave is private property of the receiving Cohen; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.2.1.1">Note 8</a>.</i>, if indeterminate he is forbidden, the words of Rebbi Meїr. But the Sages say, indeterminate heave in Judea is prohibited, in Galilee permitted, since Galileans do not know of Temple heave<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The Temple tax there was never known as “Temple heave” but as “half-<i>šeqel</i>.</i>. Indeterminate bans in Judea are permitted, in Galilee prohibited, since Galileans do not know of priest’s bans<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">They know bans only as expression of total prohibitions for everybody.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Indeterminate oaths are interpreted restrictively,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is a copy of the parallel in Halakhah 6:3, where the question is what a person forbids to himself if he vows not to eat salted meat.</i> They wanted to say, salted for preservation<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Salted meat than can be stored a long time (without refrigeration).</i>, not short-term salted. Rebbi Yudan said, since we have stated: “this is for me like salted meat<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the <i>baraita,</i> that is meat dipped twice in salt.</i>, like libation wine,” this imples that short-term salted is called “salted”. What is short-term salted? As it was stated: “He puts the limbs on the salt and turns them over.” Rebbi Abba Mari said, there is a difference, for if he leaves them there, they become salted meat<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Taking a piece of meat, putting it into a large vessel full of salt, turning it over there, and not washing the salt off, makes the meat much more salty than salting for immediate consumption. In the parallel in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 21a" href="/Menachot.21a">Babli, <i>Menaḥot</i> 21a</a>: “He puts salt on the limb, turns it over, and puts salt on the other side”.</i>. But which text says it? As Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, “one who takes olives from the vat dips each single one in salt and eats it<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ma‘serot</i> 4:3 (Notes 50,51) speaking about the agricultural worker who may eat untithed olives during the harvest.</i>.” That implies that short-term salted is called “salted”.
“This is for me like heave, if Temple heave, he is forbidden,” therefore ‘heave of thanksgiving’ is permitted. “If heave from the threshing floor, he is permitted,” therefore ‘heave of thanksgiving’ is forbidden. Here you say, forbidden; there, you say, permitted. Let us hear from the following: “Like Aaron’s <i>ḥallah</i> and his heave” is permitted, therefore ‘heave of thanksgiving’ is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">A thanksgiving sacrifice (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:11-14" href="/Leviticus.7.11-14"><i>Lev</i>. 7:11–14</a>) needs a concurrent gift of 40 loaves, of which 4 are given to the officiating priest as heave (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:14" href="/Leviticus.7.14">v. 14</a>). This is a heave not mentioned in the Mishnah. Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.2.1.1">Mishnah 1</a> notes only agricultural heave as not subject to vows, it follows that any accessory to a sacrifice must be vowed together with the sacrifice and, therefore, can represent a vow. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 12" href="/Nedarim.12">Babli, 12a/b</a>, comes to the same conclusion by a very involved argument.</i>.
“But the Sages say, indeterminate heave in Judea is prohibited, in Galilee permitted, since Galileans do not know of Temple heave.” But if they did know it, the indeterminate would be forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning of vows has to be determined by popular usage. Since learned people are not supposed to make vows, it is clear that the interpretation of vows depends on the meaning of the words in the vernacular, independent of the place where the vow was made.</i>. “Indeterminate bans in Judea are permitted, in Galilee prohibited, since Galileans do not know of priest’s bans.” But if they did know it, the indeterminate would be permitted. Here you say permitted, but there you say forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">This is an attempt to explain the rules in legal terminology, without appeal to the vernacular. The indeterminate vow would be a case of doubt; in one case one rules that doubt removes the vow, in the other that it confirms the vow. This is the attitude of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 19b" href="/Nedarim.19b">Babli, 19b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar says, these are statements of two Tannaïm<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">This is the solution of the Babli and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 1:6" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).1.6">Tosephta 1:6</a> which ascribe the statement on bans to R. Eleazar ben R. Ṣadoq. This solution is appropriate for the Yerushalmi, the Munich and Cambridge mss. of the Babli, and the quotes in many Medieval authors, which ascribe both statements to “the Sages” who would be free to rule in one case with R. Jehudah and in the other with R. Eleazar ben R. Ṣadoq. But in the printed Babli which, together with most independent Mishnah mss., attributes the statement of the Sages to R. Jehudah, the two-Tanna solution, rejected in the Yerushalmi but given in the Babli in the name of the Fourth Century Amora Abbai, is rather forced.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah said, all is from one Tanna, following the one who said<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Arakhin 8:6" href="/Mishnah_Arakhin.8.6">Mishnah <i>Arakhin</i> 8:6</a>, opinion of R. Jehudah ben Bathyra.</i>: “indeterminate bans are for the upkeep of the Temple.” But for the one who said<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">The anonymous Sages. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Arakhin 29a" href="/Arakhin.29a">Babli, <i>Arakhin</i> 29a</a>, states that in the absence of a Temple, bans for the upkeep of the Temple can be redeemed by pennies.</i>, “indeterminate bans are for priests,” even in Galilee they would be permitted. In Galilee where they are used to invoke Akhan’s ban<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 7:25-26" href="/Joshua.7.25-26"><i>Jos.</i> 7:25–26</a>, the expression of an absolute and irredeemable prohibition of all use.</i>, you have to say forbidden; in Judea where they are not used to invoke Akhan’s ban, you have to say permitted.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody made a vow of <i>ḥerem</i> and said, I referred the vow only to mariner’s nets<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">A homonym of חרם “ban”, derived from חרם II, Arabic خرم “to break, to make holes”, as a net is composed of holes. “Ban” is from חרם I, Arabic حرم “prohibit, excommunicate”.</i>, or made a vow of <i>qorbān</i> and said, I referred the vow only to the gifts for kings<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Crown money, the “voluntary” tax exacted at the accession of an emperor.</i>, or said, my bone<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">“My bone” means “myself” in biblical and rabbinic Hebrew.</i> is <i>qorbān</i> and said, I referred the vow only to the bone that I keep in my house for purposes of vows, [or said] a <i>qônām</i> that my wife may not have any use from me and said, I referred the vow only to my first wife whom I divorced, all these do not need a question<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously these are no vows and they are invalid in themselves.</i>. If they do question<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">If an uneducated person has qualms about such a vow it shows that he considers it as a vow and, therefore, it must be considered a vow even if the language does not qualify it as such.</i>, one punishes them and makes it difficult for them, the words of Rebbi Meïr, but the Sages say, one finds for them an opening from another place<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">One finds a reason that he should repent having made the vow and permits it; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.2.1.1">Note 9</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 3:1:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.3.1.2-5">Halakhah 3:1</a>.</i> and teaches them that they should not be flippant about vows.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody made a vow of <i>ḥerem</i>,” etc. A person made a vow by one of these expressions. He came before Rebbi Meїr who sent him to Rebbi Jehudah and told him, inform him that you came to me and I sent you to him. He came before Rebbi Jehudah who sent him to Rebbi Yose and told him, inform him that you came to Rebbi Meïr who sent you to me and I sent you to him. He came to Rebbi Yose, who told him, if the first one does not permit it, nobody else can permit it to you, for he holds that “if they do question, one punishes them and makes it difficult for them.” He returned before Rebbi Meïr and said to him, since you knew that, why did you not tell me from the start? He said to him, I saw you flippant and made it difficult for you.

Chapter 3



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Four kinds of vows did the Sages dissolve: Speeding-up vows, exaggeration vows, vows in error, and vows about acts of God<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">These will all be explained in the Mishnah.</i>. What are speeding-up vows? One who has something to sell says, a <i>qônām</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">In this form, the vow is void and does not have to be dissolved since it is not indicated to what it refers. One has to assume that the vendor says the money received for the sale shall be <i>qônām</i> if I sell for less.</i> that I shall not reduce the price below a tetradrachma, and the other one says, a <i>qônām</i> that I shall not increase my bid to more than a sheqel; both of them intend three denarii<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">A tetradrachma is 4 denarii, a sheqel 2. They split the difference.</i>. Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob says, also one who wants by a vow to get his neighbor to eat at his place. If he says to himself<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah in the Babli יאמר instead of ואמר. This requires a reformulation of what R. Eliezer ben Jacob said, 23b. The second statement of R. Eliezer ben Jacob is not connected to the first which simply extends the definition of speeding-up vows.</i>: any vow which I make in the future shall be void, [it is void] if he remembers at the moment he makes the vow.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Four kinds of vows did the Sages dissolve,” etc. Do not the Sages permit all kinds of vows? It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:2" href="/Numbers.30.2"><i>Num.</i> 30:2</a>.</i>: “Moses spoke to the heads of the tribes.” He referred the paragraph to the heads of the tribes that they should dissolve the people’s vows. Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:3" href="/Numbers.30.3"><i>Num.</i> 30:3</a>. The same argument in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chagigah 10a" href="/Chagigah.10a">Babli, <i>Ḥagigah</i> 10a</a>, in response to Mishnah 1:8: Dissolutions of vows “hang in the air” (have no biblical basis). This is the only argument accepted in the Babli.</i>: “He shall not profane his word.” <i>He</i> cannot profane his word; this implies that others can profane his words. Who is that? That is the Sage who dissolves the vow. Ḥananiah the nephew of Rebbi Joshua said<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 119:106" href="/Psalms.119.106"><i>Ps.</i> 119:106</a>. Also in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chagigah 10a" href="/Chagigah.10a">Babli, <i>Ḥagigah</i> 10a</a>. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 8a" href="/Nedarim.8a"><i>Nedarim</i> 8a</a>, the verse is interpreted to authorize vows to keep commandments of the Torah. In Sadducee theory (Damascus Document CD XVI 7–8), the consequence of such a vow is that the corresponding commandment cannot be broken even under extreme pain, which in pharisaic theory applies only to idolatry, incest and adultery, and murder.</i>, “I swore and I shall keep it.” Sometimes I keep it. Rebbi Joshua said<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 95:11" href="/Psalms.95.11"><i>Ps.</i> 95:11</a>. Also in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chagigah 10a" href="/Chagigah.10a">Babli, <i>Ḥagigah</i> 10a</a>.</i>, “What I swore in my rage:” In my rage I swore; I change my mind.
Do the Sages permit all kinds of vows? “Four kinds of vows did the Sages dissolve”! Rebbi Eleazar in the name of the elder Rebbi Ḥiyya: Those do not need the permission of a Sage<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, this is the authoritative opinion of Samuel, 21b.</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Rosh (R. Asher ben Ieḥiel (~ 1250 Germany - 1327 Toledo) quotes (3,1) a different text: רִבִּי שִׁמעוֹן בַר רַבָּא בְשֵׁם רִבִּי יוֹחָנָן אֵילּוּ אֵין צְרִיכִין הֵתֵר חָכָם. רַב וּשְׁמוּאֵל דְּאָֽמְרֵי תְּרַװַייהוּ. אֵין צְרִיכִין הֵיתֵר חָכָם. “Rebbi Simeon ben Rebbi Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: <i>these</i> need the permission of a Sage. Rav and Samuel both say: they do not need the permission of a Sage.” It is obvious that this cannot be the Yerushalmi text since דְּאָֽמְרֵי תְּרַװַייהוּ is pure Babylonian Aramaic.</i> Rav and Samuel both say that these need the permission of a Sage<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, this is the rejected opinion of Rav Assi (Issy).</i>. Issy objected: Did we not find an opening for vows from there only<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">This is confirmed in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 21a" href="/Nedarim.21a">Babli, 21a</a>, where Rav Assi holds that a vow can be dissolved by a Sage (an ordained rabbi) only if its invalidity is obvious, not because the person making the vow repents his action.</i>? Samuel said to him, bind a rope on it<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">It is not quite clear what this expression means. It might be that this kind of obviously void vow is the “rope with which one lifts other kinds of vows” to dissolution.</i>. A Mishnah disagrees with Issy<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 8:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.8.6.1">Mishnah 8:7</a>, speaking of a person who threatens to forbid to himself all intercourse with another if the other refuses to take gifts from him.</i>: “This one also can dissolve his vow without asking a Sage.” He explains it, that all vows need an opening from another place but these have their openings in themselves<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">It still needs to be dissolved by a Sage but the dissolution is granted automatically.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira said, that is, if they do not insist<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">If the person never had intended that the offered price should be the final price. R. Nissim Gerondi (“Ran”), in quoting this Yerushalmi (Commentary to the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 21a" href="/Nedarim.21a">Babli, 21a</a>), holds that the three sheqels quoted in the Mishnah are only an example and that any price between 2 and 4 denarii is acceptable but that the seller may not sell for 4 and the buyer not buy for 2 denarii without confirming their vows as real and requiring the counsel of a Sage.</i>. But if they did insist they need the permission of a Sage. If one did insist but the other did not insist, since this one’s is invalid, the other one’s also should be invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">If, e. g., the seller makes the same vow for two customers and he intends to insist on the full price for one but not for the other, his vow for the first is void since it is void for the second. (Concurrent explanation of Ran and Rashba.)</i>. A student of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Julianus said [after] Rebbi Yudan: If one asked a tetradrachma, one was ready to give him three<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Denarii.</i>, and another one said a sheqel. (Does he insist?)<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">This is missing in the text quoted by Rashba (Novellae, Chapter 3, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 20b" href="/Nedarim.20b">20b</a>), probably correctly. (This text not quoted by Ran.)</i> Since he renounces one denar for this one he may renounce another one for the other one and give it to him for two<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">It would be incorrect to read the Mishnah as not permitting the seller to lower the price more than half the original difference. (Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 3:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.3.1.3">Note 17</a>.)</i>.
Rebbi Immi said, who is the Tanna of speeding-up vows? Rebbi Tarphon<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">He says in Mishnah and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 5:3:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.5.3.2-4">Halakhah <i>Nazir</i> 5:5</a>, and in Tosephta <i>Nazir</i> 3:19 R. Jehudah in his name (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Tahorot 4:12" href="/Mishnah_Tahorot.4.12">Mishnah <i>Ṭahorot</i> 4:12</a> in his own name), that a vow of <i>nezirut</i> is only valid if it is unquestionable. One then assumes that all other vows follow the same rule. (The Tosephta is quoted another 6 times in the Babli.)<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 21a" href="/Nedarim.21a">Babli, 21a</a>, this is quoted by R. Ammi in the name of R. Jehudah Neśia.</i>! For he explains it for those who insist. Rebbi Abba said, explain it according to everybody for those who do not insist<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the preceding paragraph.</i>. If you say for those who insist, one should have stated that Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Who in the Mishnah permits self help in the dissolution of vows.</i> and Rebbi Tarphon say the same thing. Some Tannaїm say, “one who wants”; some Tannaïm say, “also one who wants”<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">In the Mishnah. In the first version, R. Eliezer ben Jacob’s statement is not a continuation of the first part of the Mishnah and does not necessarily deal with the same situation. In the second version, the one of our Mishnah, R. Eliezer ben Jacob’s first statement continues the Mishnah and, if the first part follows R. Tarphon, he does the same.</i>. He who says “one who wants” supports Rebbi Abba<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The first part refers to people who intend to change their mind but it implies nothing for R. Eliezer ben Jacob.</i>; he who says “also one who wants”, supports Rebbi Immi. Rebbi Pedat in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: who is the Tanna of speeding-up vows? Rebbi Tarphon<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">R. Pedat disputes the originality of R. Immi’s statement and claims priority for the latter’s teacher.</i>!
So is the Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">This language is somewhat unexpected since it usually introduces an emendation of the Mishnah.</i>: “Any vow which I make in the future shall be void, [it is void] if he remembers at the moment he makes the vow,” if he did not stipulate a reservation<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">If a person stipulates expressly that his vows shall be void even if he does not remember that stipulation at the moment of the vow, the stipulation is valid and active.</i>. But if he stipulated a reservation, then even if he does not remember. That is for vows; but for oaths? There are reservations for vows, there are no reservations for oaths, as it was stated about oaths imposed by judges<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Text from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 7:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.7.1.7"><i>Soṭah</i> 7:1, Note 27</a>.</i>: “According to our understanding, not according to your understanding.” Rebbi Jehudah said, he makes him swear according to his understanding. Why does he stipulate with him? Because of the uneducated, lest they say that there may be mental reservations for oaths.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Exaggeration vows: If he said<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">In all these cases, it is understood that he says such and such should be <i>qônām</i> for me if …</i>, if I did not see on this road [crowds] like those who left Egypt<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">About 600‘000 men; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:37" href="/Exodus.12.37"><i>Ex</i>. 12:37</a>.</i>; if I did not see a snake [as thick] as the beam of the olive press. Vows in error how? If he said, if I ate or drank, and he remembers<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">He remembers later but at the moment of his vow he was oblivious of the fact.</i> that he had eaten or drunk, that I shall not eat or shall not drink, and he forgot and ate or drank. If he said, a <i>qônām</i> that my wife cannot enjoy anything from me because she stole my wallet or hit my son<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">From another wife.</i>, and it turns out that she did not hit him or became known that she did not steal. If he saw people eating [his] figs and said to them, these are for you as <i>qorbān</i><sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">It is to be assumed that people were not afraid to steal but would never break a vow.</i>, but the persons turned out to be his father or brothers with others. The House of Shammai say, these are permitted, those with them forbidden. But the House of Hillel say, these and those are permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Since a vow which is partially voided is completely voided; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 9:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.9.6.1">Mishnah 9:6</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Vows of exaggeration,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">From here on, most of the Halakhah is also in <i>Šebuot</i> 3:9–11.</i> Is it impossible that crowds like those who left Egypt went by? But we are dealing with one look. Was it not the case that when Emperor Julianus went there<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The Babylonian campaign against Sapor II in the year 383 C. E.</i>, 1‘200‘000 [men] went with him? But we must be dealing with one look.
Did not the snake of king Sapor swallow camels and carts<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">A toned down version of King Sapor’s snake is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 25a" href="/Nedarim.25a">Babli, 25a</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 29a" href="/Shevuot.29a"><i>Šebuot</i> 29a</a>. There, the story of the snake is discussed by Samuel, which shows that the reference is to Sapor I.</i>? When they wanted to kill it, they filled camel bags<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 24:9" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.24.9">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i> 24:9</a> פָּחֲלָץ.</i> with straw and put glowing coals inside; it swallowed them and died. Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi said, I saw a snake skin which filled<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Reading עומני from Arabic عمن “to stay”. Cf. also Note 40.</i> the space between two columns. Rebbi Samuel bar Jacob said, I saw a snake skin which covered the genii<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Possibly statues of emperors who after death became <i>divi</i>. But cf. Note 41.</i> of government. Samuel said, square<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The snake was perhaps not particularly large but it had the form of a beam with square cross-section.</i>. Could you say it was not square<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Šebuot</i> וְאִם בִּמְרוּבַּע “if square, why must it be large?”</i>, why must it be large? Even if it was small! Rebbi Mattaniah said, it is the way of this Tanna to speak only about large things. You should know that it is so, for we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Šebuot</i> 3:8, example of something impossible.</i>: “A flying camel”. Could he not have stated “a flying rat”?
<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">This text is from <i>Ma‘serot</i> 5:7, fol. 52a, with variants and commentary in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 3:6:1-2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.3.6.1-2">Notes 122–129</a>. The text is also in <i>Šebuot</i> 3:9 (fol. 34d).</i> It was stated: “Rabban Gamliel says, there is nothing square from the six days of Creation.” Rebbi Berekhiah objected: Did we not state: “The body of <i>baheret</i> is like a square Italian grit.” Rebbi Bisna said, that in itself says that there is no square. Why did we state that? That he should square it. But there are lice! They are full of knots. But there is the knee of a mountain goat! It is round below. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel spoke only about animals. It was stated so: There is square in foods, there is no square in animals.
“Vain” and “untruth” both were said together, which is impossible for the mouth to say and the ear to hear<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The two versions of the Ninth Commandment, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 20:16" href="/Exodus.20.16"><i>Ex</i>. 20:16</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 5:17" href="/Deuteronomy.5.17"><i>Deut</i>. 5:17</a>, both are Sinaitic versions which had been said in parallel and were written serially.</i>. “Remember” and “keep”<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The two versions of the Fourth Commandment, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 20:8" href="/Exodus.20.8"><i>Ex</i>. 20:8</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 5:12" href="/Deuteronomy.5.12"><i>Deut</i>. 5:12</a>. The first two examples are also in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 20b" href="/Shevuot.20b">Babli, <i>Šebuot</i> 20b</a>, this example only in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rosh Hashanah 27a" href="/Rosh_Hashanah.27a">Babli <i>Roš Haššanah</i> 27a</a>, <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon ben Ioḥai</i> 20:5 (p. 148). A parallel text from here to the end of the Halakhah is <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 233; <i>Midrash Tannaïm</i> p. 138 (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:11" href="/Deuteronomy.22.11"><i>Midrash Haggadol Deut.</i> 22:11</a>), <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Yitro</i> Chap. 7.</i> both were said together, which is impossible for the mouth to say and the ear to hear. “Its desecrator shall be put to death” and “two unblemished one year old sheep<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the paragraph one speaks of laws that contradict one another, not different parallel texts. Another approach to the problem of contradiction in pentateuchal legislation is in <i>Sifry Num</i>. 3; a radically different one in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 109b" href="/Chullin.109b">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i>109b</a>, where Yalta, Rav Naḥman’s wife, holds that all of Sinaitic legislation has exceptions (in contrast to the commandments given to Noah which represent Natural Law and are without exceptions).</i>” both were said together<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The first verse is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 31:14" href="/Exodus.31.14"><i>Ex</i>. 31:14</a> on the observation of the Sabbath, the second is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:9" href="/Numbers.28.9"><i>Num</i>. 28:9</a> on the Sabbath sacrifice which requires many actions which if performed outside the Temple on a Sabbath would be capital crimes.</i>, which is impossible for the mouth to say and the ear to hear. “The nakedness of your brother’s wife you shall not uncover<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 18:16" href="/Leviticus.18.16"><i>Lev</i>. 18:16</a>.</i>”, “her brother-in-law shall come to her<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:5" href="/Deuteronomy.25.5"><i>Deut</i>. 25:5</a>; cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Yebamot</i>.</i>”, both were said together. “You shall not move real property from one tribe to another,” “any daughter inheriting real property,” both together<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">On the face of it, both verses, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 36:8-9" href="/Numbers.36.8-9"><i>Num</i>. 36:8–9</a>, say the same, viz., that an heiress may marry only a man of her own tribe. The explanation is given in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 111b" href="/Bava_Batra.111b">Babli <i>Baba batra</i> 111b</a>, where it is shown that the entire chapter 36 only refers to the daughters of Ṣelofḥad; for all others only the rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 27:7-11" href="/Numbers.27.7-11"><i>Num</i>. 27:7–11</a> are applicable without restrictions. In this case, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 36:8-9" href="/Numbers.36.8-9"><i>Num</i>. 36:8–9</a> is the exception from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 27:8" href="/Numbers.27.8"><i>Num.</i> 27:8</a>.</i>. “Fringes you shall make for yourself,” “do not wear <i>sha‘ṭnez</i>”, both were said together<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:11" href="/Deuteronomy.22.11"><i>Deut</i>. 22:11</a>, wearing wool and linen together is forbidden. In v. 12, <i>any</i> garment, including linen ones, is required to have fringes, which according to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:38" href="/Numbers.15.38"><i>Num</i>. 15:38</a> must contain a dark blue woolen thread.</i>. And so it says<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 62:12" href="/Psalms.62.12"><i>Ps</i>. 62:12</a>.</i>, “God spoke once, two I heard from this.” And it is written: “Is not my word like fire, says the Eternal, and like a hammer which shatters a rock.”
A vain oath, if one swears to change what is known to men; a false oath if he swears and changes<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">He changes what is true. This and the following paragraphs refer to Mishnah <i>Šebuot</i> 3:8.</i>. What is vain and what is false? Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If it is known to two persons, that is a false oath, to three, it is vain. Rebbi Hila in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: If it is known to two persons and another one at the end of the earth knows about it, that is a vain oath<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Since according to R. Eleazar the three people who know the truth do not have to be at the same place, it follows that for R. Joḥanan they have to be at the same place. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 29a" href="/Shevuot.29a">Babli, <i>Šebuot</i> 29a</a>, Ulla (= R. Hila) simply states that 3 people have to know to make an oath vain, without discussion whether they have to be together or not.</i>. What is the difference between them? If he was untrue before two [persons], threw it<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">The object about which he swore, e. g., (Mishnah <i>Šebuot</i> 3:8) of a stone that it was gold.</i> into the sea, and they had warned him because of a vain oath<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">To establish criminal intent, essential for criminal conviction; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 8:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.8.1.4"><i>Kilaim</i> 8:1, Note 9</a>.</i>, in the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan he is not whipped, in the opinion of Rebbi Eleazar he is whipped<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Since certainly some other person at another place knew the truth.</i>. If they had warned him because of a false oath, in the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan he is whipped, in the opinion of Rebbi Eleazar he is not whipped. Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Jehudah: Even an egg and a pearl<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">If he swears about an egg that it is a pearl (R. David Fraenckel) or that he had seen a pearl the size of an egg (R. Moshe Margalit). In connection with Mishnah <i>Šebuot</i> 3:8, the first explanation is more likely to be the correct one.</i>. What means <i>even</i>? But, <i>for example</i> an egg and a pearl.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 2:1" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).2.1">Tosephta 2:1</a>.</i>: “Just as vows of exaggeration are permitted so oaths of exaggeration are permitted.” But was it not stated: Vows of exaggeration are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 24b" href="/Nedarim.24b">Babli 24b</a>.</i>? Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Pedat: Here about those who insist<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">They show that their oaths should not be considered as exaggerations. Then they are bound by them.</i>, there about those who do not insist. Rebbi Abba said, you may even say in either case if they insist or if they do not insist; here about one who intends that his oath should fall on his property, “an oath that my property [should be forbidden] to me.” Then his property is forbidden [to him]. But in the matter of whipping, he cannot be whipped<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Both Tosephta and <i>baraita</i> deal with the same case; “permitted” means that the maker of such a vow, even if he uttered it after due warning, cannot be prosecuted; but what he forbade on himself remains forbidden. As noted by Rashba (<i>Novellae</i> to 24b), this contradicts the position of the Babli in the reading of most mss.</i>. Just as speeding-up vows are permitted so speeding-up oaths are permitted. It is the same case; if he intends that his oath should fall on his property then his property is forbidden [to him]. But concerning the matter of whipping, he cannot be whipped.
<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">A somewhat different text is in <i>Pesiqta rabbati</i> 22 (ed. M. Friedmann p. 113a).</i>Ḥizqiah said, he who swears that two are two is whipped for a vain oath. Rebbi Ḥaggai in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: One who saw rain falling and said: “O Lord, inundate with much water<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Šebuot</i>, the reading is קוּרִי פָּלֻּי בּרֶיכּסוֹן which N. Brüll (Jahrbuch für jüdische Geschichte und Literatur 1, p. 130) reads as κύριε πολὺ βρέξον “o Lord, give abundant water”, as first verse of a prayer for rain. S. Lieberman, in a full discussion of this text (יונית ויונות בארץ ישראל, Jerusalem 1962, pp. 25–27), points out that the invocation of God’s name in a vain prayer is forbidden by the Third Commandment and that the characterization as “vow” is only a reference to that Commandment.</i>”, is whipped for a vain oath. Rebbi Onias, Rebbi Jacob bar Abun in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman: 24 city councils<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Greek βουλή.</i> were in Judea and they all were destroyed because of true vain oaths, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 2:30" href="/Jeremiah.2.30"><i>Jer</i>. 2:30</a>. Another version in <i>Tanḥuma Maṭṭot</i> 1, <i>Vayiqra</i> 7.</i>: “For the vain did I hit your sons.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The first part of this paragraph has a parallel in <i>Ṡebuot</i> 3:11 but it is not a copy.</i> There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">This Mishnah, <i>Šebuot</i> 3:7, reads: “An oath that I shall not eat this loaf, an oath that I shall not eat it, an oath that I shall not eat it, he is guilty only once” if he ate the loaf. This parallels <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.2.3.1">Mishnah <i>Nedarim</i> 2:3</a> which explains that repeated vows are separate vows but repeated oaths are one and the same oath.</i>: “An oath that I shall not eat this loaf.” Something which would be forbidden for vows and is permitted for oaths<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Repeated oaths are not permitted but they do not add prohibitions to a simple oath.</i>. Not on that it was said but on the following<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah, <i>Šebuot</i> 3:5. An oath can refer to others; e. g., he swears to give a certain sum to another person.</i>: “Whether it was for himself or for others.” Rebbi Abba said in Samuel’s name: An oath that X gave a mina to Y. If it turns out that he had not given [a mina], since it is not in his hand for the future, it is not in his hand for the past<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Since X cannot swear that Y will give a mina to Z, having no power over Y’s actions, if he swears that Y gave to Z it is an invalid oath, neither vain not false.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Here the text in <i>Šebuot</i> is no longer a parallel.</i> It was found stated about both cases<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">About vows and oaths.</i>: It is more restrictive for oaths than for vows that an erroneous oath is forbidden but an erroneous vow is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 25b" href="/Nedarim.25b">Babli, 25b</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 28b" href="/Shevuot.28b"><i>Šebuot</i> 28b</a>, states the opposite: Both erroneous vows and oaths are permitted.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish asked before Rebbi [ ]<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">It is clear that something must be missing since R. Simeon ben Laqish could have asked Rebbi only as a very little boy but this is improbable since he was a wild youth not interested in studies. The commentaries all assume that the original text was בְּעָא קוֹמֵי רִבִּי [בָּא מַה] בָּא רִבִּי עֲקִיבָה and the copyist mistook the name בָּא for the verb בָּא: “Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish asked before Rebbi <i>Abba, what</i> does Rebbi Aqiba add to the words of the House of Hillel?” However this is not more than a weak conjecture since R. Abba I belongs to the generation of the students of R. Joḥanan. Probably the question was asked before R. Yannai, as most of R. Simeon ben Laqish’s other questions.</i>, does Rebbi Aqiba add to the words of the House of Hillel? As we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 9:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.9.6.1">Mishnah 9:6</a>.</i>, “until Rebbi Aqiba came and taught that a vow of which any part was permitted is totally permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">R. Aqiba adds much in that Mishnah, that a vow which is voided because the maker of the vow did not consider that it would deprive him of the enjoyment of sabbaths or holidays. Before him, the vow was lifted only for sabbaths or holidays. But in the Mishnah here, the House of Hillel lifts the vow for all people who were eating the figs when his vow was lifted as an erroneous vow only for his father and brothers. So why is the teaching of Mishnah 9:6 not credited to the House of Hillel, who are not mentioned in the Mishnah?</i>” He said to him, if you say that there, in the case of a vow which has to be investigated by a Sage, you say that a vow of which any part was invalidated is totally invalidated, here, where it does not need investigation by a Sage, so much more<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">From 9:6 one can infer the last statement of 3:2 but not vice-versa; the House of Hillel held that the principle does not apply to vows which have to be investigated by a Sage.</i>! Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Hila: Because of an erroneous vow; if I had known that my father and my brothers were there I would not have made the vow<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">R. Hila holds that in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.3.2.1">Mishnah 3:2</a> the vow is not partially but totally in error; the farmer would not have made the vow at all had he known that his father was there, and did not object to the presence of others. One has no information about the views of the House of Hillel in the matter of partially voided vows.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Vows in connection with acts of God: His friend made him vow that he would eat with him but he or his child fell sick<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">And he has to stay home to treat him.</i> or a river prevented him<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">By a flood.</i>; these are vows in connection with acts of God<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Since none of the parties had intended that the vow should include the case that the invited party was prevented by an act of God.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Vows in connection with acts of God: His friend made him vow,” etc. because he fell ill. Therefore, if he did not fall ill, no<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">It is a valid vow which must be kept.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah said, this is Rebbi Meïr’s, since Rebbi Meïr said, “he is forbidden until he gives<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 8:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.8.7.1">Mishnah 8:11</a>: “If somebody says to his friend, a <i>qônām</i> that I shall not have anything from you unless you come and take so and so much grain and wine for your son, he can annul his vow alone by saying, I said that only for my honor and that is my honor. But one who says, a <i>qônām</i> that you will not have anything from me unless you give so and so much grain and wine to my son, <i>R. Meïr says he is forbidden until he gives</i>. But the Sages say, this one also can annul his vow alone by saying, it is as if I received it.”</i>.” Rebbi Yose said, why do we not explain it according to everybody<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Since the entire vow falls under the category of speeding-up vows. But in the case of 8:11 there is another principle involved.</i>? As my teacher said<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ze‘ira, in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 8:7:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.8.7.2-3">Halakhah 8:11</a>, explaining the difference between R. Meïr and the Sages.</i>, they disagree when it was indeterminate. Where do we hold? If each one of them said, because of my honor, everybody agrees that he is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Both parties agree that they meant the vow to mean what it said; the vow is valid.</i>. If one of them said, because of my honor, and the other said, I said it in your honor, everybody agrees he is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Since the vow never was accepted by the other party.</i>. Rebbi Hila said, is it the way of people to say χαρίζεσθαι βίᾳ<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">An interpretation by N. Brüll (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 3:2:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.3.2.8">Note 70, p. 138</a>), erroneously ascribed to Jastrow by S. Lieberman (p. 34). Lieberman reads כורוסתי instead of בורוסתי of the Leiden ms., כורוסתי of the <i>editio princeps</i>.<br>As Lieberman explains, in Egyptian legal papyri one frequently finds the formula ὁμολογῶ χαρίζεσθαί σοι “I agree that I gave you”. R. Hila asked, would anybody say ὁμολογῶ χαρίζεσθαί σοι βίᾳ “I agree that I gave you by force?” A gift can be given only with the consent of the recipient; in the case of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 8:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.8.7.1">Mishnah 8:11</a>, a vow involving a gift can be valid only with the agreement of both parties.</i>?

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One makes a vow to tax assessors<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Reading with the Vienna ms. of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 2:2" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).2.2">Tosephta 2:2</a> edited by S. Lieberman: חָרָג, “fiscal agent” [Y. Kutscher, לשוננו 27 (1963), p.35], as in Arabic خراج “real estate tax, revenue.” This refers to the periodical assessment of wealth by the Roman government known as <i>indiction</i> (Roman 15 year periods, Byzantine 5 year periods). This reading is confirmed as that of the Babylonian Mishnah by Ravad (R. Abraham ben David of Posquières, <i>Temim De‘im</i> No. 59). Earlier commentators explain הרגים as criminal gangs who kill people unless they deliver to them all their money and/or valuables.</i> and to people who condemn property<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Government fiscal agents acting on an arbitrary decree (in the way it was Roman practice, introduced by Augustus, to condemn any inherited property if the emperor was not named principal heir for a third of the property, with the real heirs being reduced to the status of co-heirs to the emperor.)</i> and to toll collectors<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Since tolls were always farmed out, the tax farmer had to try to collect more than the legal rate in order to make money on the transaction, and the toll collector may be presumed to have asked for more than he would deliver to the tax farmer. These practices are classified as robbery; cf. Mishnah <i>Baba qama</i> 10:1–2, where toll collectors are classified with robbers.</i> that it is heave even though it is not heave<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">A Jewish killer, robber, or toll collector would be much too superstitious to touch heave.</i>, that it is king’s family’s property even though it is not theirs<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Property of a member of the emperor’s <i>familia</i>, his slaves and freedmen, whom he uses to administer the empire. Their property would be exempt of taxes and shielded from confiscation. This vow would be valuable also against Gentile claimants.</i>. The House of Shammai say, one vows anything except an oath, but the House of Hillel say, even an oath. The House of Shammai say, one may not offer a vow<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">One may make such a false vow only if required to do so by the extortionist who does not believe his victim’s assertions.</i>, but the House of Hillel say, one may offer. The House of Shammai say, only what he prescribes as vow, but the House of Hillel say, even what he does not prescribe.<br>How is this? They said to him, say: a <i>qônām</i> that my wife cannot have any use from me<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">That the <i>qônām</i> be valid if anything he said was untrue.</i>, and he said: a <i>qônām</i> that my wife and my children cannot have any use from me; the House of Shammai say, his wife is permitted but his children are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Since that part of the vow was not imposed on him.</i>; but the House of Hillel say, both are permitted.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One makes a vow to assessors and to people who condemn property,” etc. To assessors in time of <i>indiction</i><sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Reading with the Vienna ms. of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 2:2" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).2.2">Tosephta 2:2</a> edited by S. Lieberman: חָרָג, “fiscal agent” [Y. Kutscher, לשוננו 27 (1963), p.35], as in Arabic خراج “real estate tax, revenue.” This refers to the periodical assessment of wealth by the Roman government known as <i>indiction</i> (Roman 15 year periods, Byzantine 5 year periods). This reading is confirmed as that of the Babylonian Mishnah by Ravad (R. Abraham ben David of Posquières, <i>Temim De‘im</i> No. 59). Earlier commentators explain הרגים as criminal gangs who kill people unless they deliver to them all their money and/or valuables.</i>; to condemners in times of expropriations. Rebbi Jehudah ben Pazi stated in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If it was a dangerous situation, it is permitted. Rebbi Aḥa, Rebbi Ḥinena in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If it was civilized, it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">If the tolls were collected according to a published tariff, or a condemnation was collected pursuant to a court judgment. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 28a" href="/Nedarim.28a">Babli, 28a</a>, the Babylonian opinion is quoted that one may lie to the toll collector if he does not work following an official tariff, and the Galilean that one may not lie to a toll collector appointed by the government.</i>. It was stated: One hangs on Gentiles and government but not on Jews<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">If a person frees himself from paying tolls, he may claim that certain of his properties belong to other people. But he should not claim that they were another Jew’s property since the other Jew may sue him for damages once the rule of law has been re-established.</i>, because the strong-armed will fall and he should not risk that the prior [obligations] fall on him.
If he transgressed and offered [a vow], how is that in the opinion of the House of Shammai? Let us hear from the following: “The House of Shammai say, his wife is permitted but his children are forbidden.” That means, if he transgressed and offered [a vow], in the opinion of the House of Shammai he is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that since his wife is permitted even following his voluntary addition of his children, that this addition has no influence on the rest of the vow. (R. David Fraenckel emends the text from “permitted” to “prohibited”.)</i>. They wanted to say, vows but not oaths. It was found stated: Rebbi Ismael says, “<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Lev. 19:12. It seems that the argument is based on the second half of the verse, not quoted in the text: "for you would desecrate your God's Name, I am the Eternal". It is general rabbinic doctrine that the note "I am the Eternal" at the end of a verse indicates that a transgression depends on the intention, that God will judge whether a person intended evil or not (Sifra Qedošim Parašah 2(14). This means that when clearly no desecration of the Name is intended, the oath cannot be forbidden.</i>do not swear falsely in My Name,” you may swear to assessors and to people who condemn property and to toll collectors.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> These orchard trees shall be <i>qorbān</i> if they are not cut, this garment be <i>qorbān</i> if it is not burned; these have redemption<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">The circumstances of the vow are explained in the Halakhah. Since neither trees nor garments can be sacrifices, by dedication they become Temple property and can be bought back from the Temple, i.e., redeemed.</i>. These orchard trees shall be <i>qorbān</i> until they are cut, this garment be <i>qorbān</i> until it is burned; these have no redemption<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">All Temple property other than sacrificial animals can be bought back from the Temple administration. But in this case, buying the property back would have no effect; immediately it would be rededicated if the trees were not cut or the garment burned.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “These orchard trees shall be <i>qorbān</i>,” etc. If he saw the king’s cutting crew coming near<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">They will cut trees for public works without paying for them. The owner promises money to Heaven if he will be protected.</i>, if he saw fire coming near, and he said: These orchard trees shall be <i>qorbān</i> if they are not cut, this garment shall be <i>qorbān</i> if it is not burned or torn. Were they sanctified retroactively or only for the future<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">If it turns out that his property was saved, what is the exact moment when his vow becomes active. If one says from the moment he made the vow, that point in time is well defined. If one says, from the moment it is clear that his property will be spared, that moment is difficult to pin down. The question is not answered.</i>? What is the difference? If he used them. If you say that they are sanctified retroactively, he committed larceny. If you say for the future, he did not commit larceny.
<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is copied from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:5:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.5.2-7"><i>Qiddušin</i> 3:5</a> (64a, 1. 72). Therefore “there” means here and “here” the text in <i>Qiddušin</i>.</i>Ḥizqiah said, if he redeemed them they revert to their sanctity<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the second part of the Mishnah, where the orchard was vowed for the time before it was cut.
