The opinion of Ḥizqiah is that described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 3:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.3.5.1">Note 111</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 28b" href="/Nedarim.28b">Babli, 28b</a>, the opinion is ascribed to bar Pada, a contemporary of Ḥizqiah.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, if he redeemed them they are redeemed<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 28b" href="/Nedarim.28b">Babli, 28b</a>, this is the opinion of Ulla (R. Hila).</i>. The Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan: “These have no redemption.” He explains thus: After they have been cut they do not need redemption<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">In general, we say that anything sanctified cannot become profane without some form of redemption. But in this case, the cutting of the trees or the burning of the garment is the act of profanation.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, what Ḥizqiah said refers to the case that he himself redeemed them<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">Because he himself stated that his orchard be holy until cut down. In the Babli, 130a, this is the opinion of R. Joḥanan.</i>. But if another person redeemed them, the sanctity is removed from them<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">For a third party the orchard is Temple property and becomes profane by payment of the redemption price.</i>. The argument of Rebbi Yose is switched: There, he says that if [another] redeemed them, the sanctity is removed from them, but here<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:5:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.5.2-7"><i>Qiddušin</i> 3:5</a>, where the question was raised, what is the status of a woman whose husband gives her some valuables and says that these should serve as <i>qiddušin</i> (preliminary marriage, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:1:15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.1.15"><i>Yebamot</i> 1:1, Note 63</a>) after he will have divorced her. It is obvious that if another man gives <i>qiddušin</i> to a married woman to be valid once she will be divorced, those <i>qiddušin</i> are invalid (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.5.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i>3:5</a>) since the divorce depends on a third party (the husband). R. Yose holds that the husband himself can give <i>qiddušin</i> for after a divorce since no third party is involved. Therefore, there is no contradiction in R. Yose’s position here and there.</i>, he says that if she was married to another man the <i>qiddušin</i> [of the first] were not removed! All that is questionable for him is if he gave her two <i>peruṭot</i>, one for the moment and one for after he has divorced her, what is the situation<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 30a" href="/Nedarim.30a">Babli, 30a</a>, this is a question of R. Hoshaia.</i>?

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who makes a vow<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">A “vow away from X” is always a vow that all of X’s property should be considered <i>qorbān</i> by the maker of the vow, who then is forbidden any use of X’s property and may not receive any favors from him.</i> away from the seafaring men is permitted the dwellers on dry land; from the dwellers on dry land he is forbidden the seafaring men<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., also the seafaring men.</i> because the seafaring men are part of the dwellers on dry land. [One speaks] not of those who go from Acco to Jaffa but those who frequently take to the sea.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who makes a vow away from the seafaring men,” etc. That you should not say, not from those who go from Acco to Jaffa but those who frequently take to the sea. “From the dwellers on dry land he is forbidden the seafaring men,” not only those who go from Acco to Jaffa but even those who frequently take to the sea. But did he not make a vow regarding the dwellers on dry land? This implies that the seafaring men are part of the dwellers on dry land<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">In the words of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 30b" href="/Nedarim.30b">Babli, 30b</a>, “every seafaring man will eventually reach land.”</i>. If he made a vow away from the seafaring men after thirty days but [a certain mariner] became a land dweller, there is a dispute between Rebbi Ismael and Rebbi Aqiba<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">The problem is compared to that of Halakhah 11:9 (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 89a" href="/Nedarim.89a">Babli 89a</a>) dealing with a widow who vowed to be a <i>nezirah</i> after 30 days and before the 30 days elapsed she married. R. Ismael holds that the time of the vow is determining, and since she made the vow while on her own, the husband cannot dissolve her vow even though it will start to become active only during marriage. He will hold that any mariner who became a land dweller within 30 days of the vow is forbidden since he was a mariner at the moment the vow was made. R. Aqiba holds that the moment the vow becomes active is determining. Therefore, he holds that the husband can dissolve his wife’s vow and in the case of a vow against mariners, the person who became a dweller on dry land before the vow was activated is permitted.</i>. For Rebbi Ismael says, after the vow, and Rebbi Aqiba says, after the prohibition. And about children it is a problem<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">All commentators identify טלייתא with תלייתא “people going on pleasure trips.” This procedure has no basis. טליא only means “boy, young man”. Since minors are subject to their parents’ will, is a boy who is taken on a sea trip automatically a mariner or becomes a mariner in <i>halakhic</i> sense only when he enters a seagoing vessel of his own will?</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who makes a vow away from those who see the sun is also forbidden the blind since he intended only that seen by the sun<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 7:11" href="/Ecclesiastes.7.11"><i>Eccl</i>. 7:11</a>.</i>.<br>One who makes a vow away from those who see the sun is also forbidden the blind since he intended only that seen by the sun<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 7:11" href="/Ecclesiastes.7.11"><i>Eccl</i>. 7:11</a>.</i>.<br>One who makes a vow away from those who will be born<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 30b" href="/Nedarim.30b">Babli, 30b</a>, switches the meanings between ילודים and נולדים. However, the Halakhah here confirms the translation as given; which is also confirmed by biblical usage; in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 1:22" href="/Exodus.1.22"><i>Ex</i>. 1:22</a> יִלּוֹד clearly means “will be born”.</i> is permitted those who are already born. From those who are born, Rebbi Meïr permits even those who will be born, but the Sages say, he intended only those in whose nature it is to be born.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who makes a vow away from those who will be born,” etc. What is Rebbi Meïr’s reason? “And now your two sons born to you<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 48:5" href="/Genesis.48.5"><i>Gen</i>. 48:5</a>.</i>”, and they already were born. What is the Sages’ reason? “Lo, a son is born to the House of David, Josiah his name,<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 13:2" href="/I_Kings.13.2"><i>1K</i>. 13:2</a>.</i>” and he was not yet born.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who makes a vow away from those who keep the Sabbath is forbidden Jews and Samaritans<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">But Sabbatist Christians who do not accept the obligation to keep all laws of the Pentateuch are not included in the prohibition.</i>, from garlic eaters<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">In general use as fertility medication.</i> is forbidden Jews and Samaritans, from those who make pilgrimage to Jerusalem is forbidden Jews but permitted Samaritans<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">For their pilgrimages are to Mount Gerizim.</i>.<br>A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not enjoy from the descendants of Noah; he is permitted Jews and forbidden Gentiles<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Even though Jews also are descendants of Noah, the technical term “descendants of Noah” applies only to those outside the Sinaitic covenant, who are only bound by the natural law given to Adam and Noah.</i>. That I shall not enjoy from the descendants of Abraham; he is forbidden Jews and permitted Gentiles<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">Including the descendants of Hagar and Qeturah since only Isaac is called “Abraham’s descendant” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 21:12" href="/Genesis.21.12"><i>Gen</i>. 21:12</a>).</i>. That I shall not enjoy from Jews; he buys higher and sells lower<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Than the going market rate. In that case, he is guaranteed a loss in any commercial transaction; he may trade without violating his vow.</i>. That Jews shall not enjoy from me; he buys lower and sells higher, if they agree<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">He would have to tell his customers that he was overcharging them.</i>. That I shall not enjoy from them nor they from me; he should deal with Gentiles.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not enjoy from the descendants of Noah; he is permitted Jews and forbidden Gentiles<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Even though Jews also are descendants of Noah, the technical term “descendants of Noah” applies only to those outside the Sinaitic covenant, who are only bound by the natural law given to Adam and Noah.</i>. That I shall not enjoy from the descendants of Abraham; he is forbidden Jews and permitted Gentiles,” etc. Is Ismael not part of Abraham’s seed? “<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 21:12" href="/Genesis.21.12"><i>Gen</i>. 21:12</a>. The same in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 31a" href="/Nedarim.31a">Babli 31a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 59b" href="/Sanhedrin.59b"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 59b</a>..</i> For in Isaac will be called your seed.” And is not Esaw part of Isaac’s seed? Rebbi Yudan bar Shalom said, “in Isaac,” in part of Isaac. Rebbi Huna said, “ב” are two<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">Following the Alexandrian practice to denote numbers by letters.</i>, the son who will be inheriting two worlds, this world and the world to come. Rebbi Gershom in the name of Rebbi Aḥa, “<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 24:17" href="/Numbers.24.17"><i>Num</i>. 24:17</a>.</i> a star did step out from Jacob,” from whom did a star step out and will appear in the future? From Jacob! Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Ḥuna: In the future world, evil Esaw will put on his prayer shawl and sit with the Just in Paradise, but the Holy One, Praise to Him, draws him away. Why? “<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Obadiah 1:4" href="/Obadiah.1.4"><i>Ob</i>. 1:4</a>.</i> If you climb as high as an eagle or if you would make your nest between stars, from there I would take you down, says the Lord.” But “stars” are only the Just. As you say<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Daniel 12:3" href="/Daniel.12.3"><i>Dan</i>. 12:3</a>.</i>, “those who justify the many are like stars, eternally.”

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not enjoy from the uncircumcized, he is permitted uncircumcized Jews and forbidden circumcized peoples of the world. That I shall not enjoy from the circumcized, he is forbidden uncircumcized Jews and permitted circumcized peoples of the world, since the prepuce is simply a name for Gentiles as it was said<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 9:25" href="/Jeremiah.9.25"><i>Jer</i>. 9:25</a>.</i>: “For all Gentiles are uncircumcized and all the House of Israel are uncircumcised of heart.”<br>Rebbi Ismael says, circumcision is great, for its covenant was concluded thirteenfold. Rebbi Yose says, circumcision is great, for it pushes aside the stringencies of the Sabbath. Rebbi Joshua ben Qorḥa says, circumcision is great, for it was not suspended even one hour for Moses the Just. Rebbi Neḥemiah says, Rebbi Yose says, circumcision is great, for it pushes aside skin disease<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">The Halakhah will explain the Mishnah.</i>.<br>Rebbi says, circumcision is great, for with all the commandments which Abraham fulfilled he was not called whole until he circumcized himself as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 17:1" href="/Genesis.17.1"><i>Gen</i>. 17:1</a>.</i>: “Walk before me and be perfect.”<br>Another word: Circumcision is great, for without it the Holy One, praise to Him, would not have created his world as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 33:25" href="/Jeremiah.33.25"><i>Jer</i>. 33:25</a>. In the Babli, this is mostly explained as referring to the Torah (which has to be studied day and night, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 1:8" href="/Joshua.1.8"><i>Jos</i>. 1:8</a>) as blueprint for the world; 32a, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 68b" href="/Pesachim.68b"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 68b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 3a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.3a"><i>Avodah zarah</i>3a</a>. A similar text is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 2:7" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).2.7">Tosephta 2:7</a>.</i>: “So says the Eternal, if it were not for my covenant day and night, I would not have put in place the laws of heaven and earth.” <sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">This addition is not in the Babylonian Mishnah; it is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 2:6" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).2.6">Tosephta 2:6</a>.</i> Another word: Circumcision is great, for it is weighty corresponding to all other commandments of the Torah, as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 24:8" href="/Exodus.24.8"><i>Ex</i>. 24:8</a>. This verse speaks about the Sinaitic covenant; it is used here because in rabbinic and modern Hebrew the word בְּרִית “covenant” is synonymous with “circumcision”.</i>: “Behold the blood of the covenant which the Eternal concluded with you about <i>all</i> these words.”
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Yoḥanan bar Marya said, it is written: “On that day, the Eternal concluded a covenant with Abram as follows,” etc., up to “but my covenant I shall affirm with Isaac,” etc., thirteen covenants<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 17:2-21" href="/Genesis.17.2-21"><i>Gen</i>. 17:2–21</a> the word “covenant” appears 12 times. Therefore, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 15:18" href="/Genesis.15.18"><i>Gen</i>. 15:18</a> is added to reach the lucky number 13, the number of God’s attributes of mercy (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 34:6-7" href="/Exodus.34.6-7"><i>Ex</i>. 34:6–7</a>) even though it speaks of a prior covenant; cf. the preceding Note.</i>.
“On the eighth day one shall circumcize<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 12:2" href="/Leviticus.12.2"><i>Lev</i>. 12:2</a>.</i>,” even on the Sabbath. How can I confirm “its desecrator shall certainly die<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 31:14" href="/Exodus.31.14"><i>Ex</i>. 31:14</a>.</i>?” Even for circumcision? How can I confirm “on the eighth day one shall circumcize”? Even on the Sabbath? The verse says “<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 12:2" href="/Leviticus.12.2"><i>Lev</i>. 12:2</a>.</i><i>But</i> on the eighth day one shall circumcize,” even on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babylonian versions [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 132a" href="/Shabbat.132a"><i>Šabbat</i> 132a</a>, <i>Sifra Tazria‘ Pereq</i> 1(4)] the argument for performing circumcision on the Sabbath is based on the expression “on the Sabbath day” and not simply “on the Sabbath”.</i>. In Torah, Prophets, and Hagiographs we find that the Sabbath is as weighty as all other commandments of the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 1:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.1.5.3"><i>Berakhot</i> 1:8, Note 207</a>. The text is copied in <i>Ex. rabba</i> 25(16).</i> [taken together]. In the Torah, “until when will you refuse to keep My Commandments and Teachings<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ex</i>. 15:28.</i>,” and it is written: “See that the Eternal gave you the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ex</i>. 15:29. The Sabbath is called “commandments and teachings” when no other prohibitions were yet given to the Israelites.</i>.” In Prophets, as is written<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 20:21" href="/Ezekiel.20.21"><i>Ez</i>. 20:21</a>.</i>: “But the sons rebelled against Me; My Laws they did not follow” etc., and it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 20:13" href="/Ezekiel.20.13"><i>Ez</i>. 20:13</a>. In <i>Ex. rabba</i> the quote is instead from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 20:21" href="/Ezekiel.20.21"><i>Ez</i>. 20:21</a>, where the Sabbath is the only commandment singled out of all “laws and rules”. The word מאד “much” probably should be deleted.</i>: “My Sabbaths they much desecrated.” In Hagiographs, as it is written: “On Mount Sinai You descended<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Nehemiah 9:13" href="/Nehemiah.9.13"><i>Neh</i>. 9:13</a>.</i>,” and it is written: “And about Your holy Sabbath You informed them<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Nehemiah 9:14" href="/Nehemiah.9.14"><i>Neh</i>. 9:14</a>; again the Sabbath is the only commandment singled out of all “commandments and laws”.</i>,” etc. Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Abuna said, <i>commandment</i> of Sabbath is written <i>plene</i><sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">In all other occurrences in the Hebrew Bible the spelling is מִצְוֹת, only at this place it is מִצְווֹת.</i>, to inform you that it is as weighty as all other commandments of the Torah. A parable of two ladies who meet and you do not know which one is of higher standing. If one makes room for the other, you know that the other is of higher standing.
Idolatry is worse than all others: “But if you should err and not fulfill all these commandments.<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:22" href="/Numbers.15.22"><i>Num</i>. 15:22</a>. The rules for purification offerings are given in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4-5" href="/Leviticus.4-5"><i>Lev</i>. 4–5</a>. The appearance of another set of purification offerings in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:22-28" href="/Numbers.15.22-28"><i>Num</i>. 15:22–28</a> is explained by the rule that these offerings are only for expiation of inadvertent idolatry (<i>Sifry Num</i>. 111). The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 69b" href="/Eruvin.69b">Babli, <i>Erubin</i> 69b</a>, equates desecration of the Sabbath and idolatry in severity.</i>” Rebbi Judah bar Pazi said, the desecration of the Name is worse than all others. That is what is written<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 20:39" href="/Ezekiel.20.39">Truncated from <i>Ez</i>. 20:39</a>.</i>: “But to you, the House of Israel, so says the Eternal, Israel’s God, go and worship everybody his abominations but My holy Name do not desecrate, etc.
Because Moses was lazy for circumcision, the angel tried to kill kim. That is what is written<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 4:24" href="/Exodus.4.24"><i>Ex</i>. 4:24</a>.</i>: “The Eternal met him and wanted to kill him.” Rebbi Yose said, far be the thought that Moses was lazy for circumcision but he argued on his own: To perform circumcision and leave would be dangerous<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">Acute danger to a person’s life suspends all commandments and prohibitions (except those of murder, incest and adultery, and idolatry). In practice, this means that it is forbidden to circumcize a sick baby.</i>. To wait<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">To wait in Midyan until the baby had recovered. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 31b" href="/Nedarim.31b">Babli, 31b</a>, this argument is attributed to Rebbi. The argument of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel is quoted in his name, 32a.</i>, the Holy One, praise to Him, told him: “Go, return to Egypt.” But because he was lazy in preparing for the overnight stay before circumcision; that is what is written<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 4:24" href="/Exodus.4.24"><i>Ex</i>. 4:24</a>.</i>: “It was on the way, at the overnight stay.” Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, far be the thought that the angel wanted to kill Moses; it was the baby. Come and see, who is called <i>ḥātān</i><sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">Since Ṣippora, being a Midyanite Arab, spoke Arabic, the root ختن can mean either to circumcize or to become a relative through one’s wife. Therefore, the word is appropriate both for the boy and his father.</i>? Moses or the baby? There are Tannaïm who state that Moses is called <i>ḥātān</i>. There are Tannaïm who state that the baby is called <i>ḥātān</i>. He who said that Moses is called <i>ḥātān: Ḥātān</i>, blood is required from you<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">Explanations of the expression חתן דמים.</i>. And he said that the baby is called <i>ḥātān: Ḥātān</i>, in blood you are preserved for me. “Ṣippora took a flintstone and cut her son’s prepuce and touched his feet.<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 4:25" href="/Exodus.4.25"><i>Ex</i>. 4:25</a>.</i>” Rebbi Jehudah, Rebbi Neḥemiah, and the rabbis. One said, Moses’s feet. Another said, the angel’s feet. Another said, the baby’s feet. He who said Moses’s feet: Here I cut your obligation for you. He who said, the angel’s feet: Here your mission was accomplished. He who said the baby’s feet, she touched the baby’s body. “He left off from him; then she said, a blood <i>ḥātān</i> for circumcisions<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 4:26" href="/Exodus.4.26"><i>Ex</i>. 4:26</a>.</i>.” From here that there are two circumcisions; one for baring the gland and one for the fibers<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">A circumcision is not valid unless the gland is exposed and the wound is smooth; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 71b" href="/Yevamot.71b">Babli <i>Yebamot</i> 71b</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is in <i>Sifra Tazria‘ Pereq</i> 1(4), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 132b" href="/Shabbat.132b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 132b</a>.</i>“The flesh of his prepuce should be circumcized.<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 12:3" href="/Leviticus.12.3"><i>Lev</i>. 12:3</a>.</i>” Even if it has white skin disease<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:2-28" href="/Leviticus.13.2-28"><i>Lev</i>. 13:2–28</a>.</i>. How can I confirm: “Beware of skin disease to watch it carefully and to do …<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:8" href="/Deuteronomy.24.8"><i>Deut</i>. 24:8</a>. The injunction to follow all instructions of the priests in matters of impure skin disease is interpreted to mean that it is forbidden to cut out the diseased part of the skin. Then if a baby is born with diseased prepuce, how could it be circumcised?</i>”? Also at a circumcision. Then how can I confirm: “The flesh of his prepuce should be circumcized”? If there is no white skin disease? The verse says, “the flesh<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">Since the intent is to cut the flesh, cutting the skin is only incidental and is not in the mind of the person who does the circumcision.</i>”, even if it has white skin disease. <sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">The disagreement between rabbis Jonah and Yose is discussed in <i>Ḥallah</i> 2:1, Notes 9,10.</i> This is simple in the opinion of Rebbi Jonah, who says that a positive commandment supersedes a prohibition even if they are not written together. But in the opinion of Rebbi Yose, who says that a positive commandment supersedes a prohibition only if they are written together? Since it is written “the flesh of his prepuce,” it is as if it were written together<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">The prohibition is irrelevant, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 3:9:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.3.9.6">Note 186</a>.</i>.

Chapter 4



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The only difference between one who is under a vow [not to have] usufruct from another<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">B made a vow that A may have no usufruct from him.. If A makes a vow that B shall have no usufruct from him, the expression is in the active mode, הַמּוֹדֵר בְּ.</i> and one who is under a vow about use of food is passing through his real estate<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Setting his foot on the other’s property.</i> and vessels that cannot be used to prepare food. One who is under a vow about use<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The word הניית is not in the Babli and most independent Mishnah mss. From the Halakhah it will be seen that it was not in the original version of the Mishnah.</i> of food should not borrow from him a fine or coarse sieve, a grindstone, or an oven. But he may borrow from him a robe or a stole, nose rings and finger rings, and anything that is not used to prepare food. At a place where one rents out any of these it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Since he could buy food for the money saved by not renting; this is counted as usufruct used for food.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The entire paragraph is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:7:2-8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.7.2-8.1"><i>Megillah</i> 1:9</a>, 71a 1. 73.</i>“The only difference between one who is under a vow [not to have] usufruct from another,” etc. This means that vessels used to prepare food are forbidden. But did he not make the vow about food? Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, so is the Mishnah: The only difference between one who is under a vow [not to have] usufruct from another and one who is under a vow about use for food. In the House of Rebbi it was stated thus: “One who is under a vow about use<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The word הניית is not in the Babli and most independent Mishnah mss. From the Halakhah it will be seen that it was not in the original version of the Mishnah.</i> of food should not borrow from him a fine or coarse sieve, a grindstone, or an oven<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">This is the language of the Yerushalmi Mishnah. This and the explanation of R. Simeon ben Laqish shows that the Mishnah text was that of the Babli and most independent Mishnah mss: הַמּוּדָּר מַאֲכָל מֵחֲבֵירוֹ and that the Yerushalmi text is the corrected one. Question and answer are also in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 33a" href="/Nedarim.33a">Babli, 33a</a>.</i>.” It was stated: But he may borrow from him cups and bowls and fruit bowls, since these are not used to prepare food, only to serve food. Pans<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Greek; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 7:3:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.7.3.7"><i>Peah</i> 7:4, Note 69</a>.</i> and pots are forbidden. To mill and to press is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Milling grain and pressing grapes.</i>. Cutting is a problem, harvesting<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Cutting grains and harvesting grapes.</i> is a problem. What kind of usufruct? It was stated: But he may borrow a spade from him. We wanted to say, a spade of a digger. Explain it by a spade for weeding, and you cannot infer anything. Rebbi Abuna said, that <i>ysṭ’</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Megillah</i> זוֹסְטָא. Mussaphia sees Greek ζωστήρ -ῆρος, ὁ “belt” in the <i>Megillah</i> form; but is is difficult to separate coarse from fine flour by using a belt. {Cf. Latin <i>haustrum</i> (also <i>austrum</i>) from <i>haurire</i> “to draw (water), to pluck, take, etc.,” and gloss <i>hauritorium</i> (Greek ἀντλητήριος, adj., “for drawing up”), “a bucket” (Lewis and Short) (E. G.).}</i>, since one removes and separates the bran from flour with it, it is forbidden to borrow from him.
Rebbi Abba in the name of Rebbi Ze‘ira: Because it is as if he gave him money to buy a loaf with<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Since he could buy food for the money saved by not renting; this is counted as usufruct used for food.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked: Then is it forbidden to borrow money from him, for it is as if he gave him money to buy a loaf with? Rebbi Abina said, Rebbi Ze‘ira asked: That jewellery<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">J. Levy in his Dictionary refers to κοσμίδιον (κόσμιον) “jewellery” in Du Cange’s Greek glossary. S. Lieberman (הירושלמי כפשוטו<sup>2</sup>, New York -Jerusalem 1995, p. ח) thinks that this refers to professional dancers at weddings.</i> on them, is it forbidden to lend to them?

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one is under a vow [not to have] usufruct from another, [the other] may give his <i>šeqel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">If A has vowed that B should have nothing from him, A may pay B’s Temple tax of half a <i>šeqel</i> due every year. Since the money is paid to the Temple, B does not receive anything.</i>, pay his debt<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">As long as the debt is not due, A simply restrains the creditor from asking money from B.</i> and return what he has lost<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Since he returns B’s own property, B receives nothing from A.</i>. At a place where one takes a fee for this, the gain should be given to the sacred fund<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The Temple, or in its absence the communal welfare fund.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If one is under a vow [not to have] usufruct from another,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The entire Halakhah is found also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 13:2:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.13.2.2-7"><i>Ketubot</i> 13:2</a> (35d line 30 ff.)</i> Rebbi Abba bar Mamal asked: If somebody pays off somebody else’s debt without the latter’s knowledge, is that the disagreement of Ḥanan and the High Priests’ sons<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 13:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.13.2.1"><i>Ketubot</i> Mishnah 13:2</a>: “If somebody went overseas and another person paid for the upkeep of his wife (without a court order or a contract with the wife), Ḥanan said, that person lost his money. The High Priest’s sons disagreed with him and said, he shall swear how much he spent and collect it.”</i>? Rebbi Yose said, there the reason of the High Priest’s sons is that nobody expects his wife to die from hunger. But here, [the debtor could say:] “I could negotiate with him and he would forgive some.” Think of it, if [the loan] was on a pledge! “I could negotiate with him and he would return my pledge.” So far about a creditor who does not push [for repayment]. Even for a creditor who pushes, we can hear from the following: “He may give his <i>šeqel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">If A has vowed that B should have nothing from him, A may pay B’s Temple tax of half a <i>šeqel</i> due every year. Since the money is paid to the Temple, B does not receive anything.</i>. If somebody does not pay his <i>šeqel</i>, does one not take a pledge from him? This says, even for a creditor who pushes. You should know that this is so, as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>, a continuation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.2.1">Mishnah 2</a> about the person to whom usufruct from another was forbidden.</i>: “He can bring for him nests for males or females suffering from genital discharges<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">“Nest” is the technical term for a couple of birds from the pigeon family, prescribed sacrifice for the person healed from genital discharge <i>before</i> he could enter the Temple. For a male, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:14" href="/Leviticus.15.14"><i>Lev</i>. 15:14</a>. For a female, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:29" href="/Leviticus.15.29"><i>Lev</i>. 15:29</a>.</i>, nests for childbirth<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Due before the mother could enter the Temple, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 12:8" href="/Leviticus.12.8"><i>Lev</i>. 12:8</a>, for the wife of a man who could not afford a sheep, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 12:6" href="/Leviticus.12.6"><i>Lev</i>. 12:6</a>.</i>, purification and reparation offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:27-5:26" href="/Leviticus.4.27-5.26"><i>Lev</i>. 4:27–5:26</a>. These sacrifices are in part burned on the altar, in part eaten by the priests; nothing is given to the offerer and his family, in contrast to well-being offerings.</i>” since nothing of these comes to [the other person’s] hand. And here<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of a debt paid by the third party, the deal has to be structured so that nothing ever comes into the possession of the debtor.</i> also, that nothing should come into his hand.
Rebbi Joshua ben Levi says, nobody is held responsible for his neighbor except for <i>annona</i><sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The requisition for the Roman army, the amounts being determined in advance every year. It seems from this statement that both <i>annona</i> and the head-tax were imposed as communal obligations. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.1.3"><i>Demay</i> 2, Note 12</a>.</i> and head-tax. Rav said, anything for which one is held responsible for one’s neighbor has to be repaid. The strength of Rav is from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 10:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.10.6.1">Mishnah <i>Baba Qama</i> 10:6</a>: “He who appropriated a field and oppressors took it, if the entire country is hit, he may say to him: Here is yours before you. But if it was because of the robber, he is obligated to give him another field. If a river washed it away, he may say to him: Here is yours before you.”<br>Somebody took real estate from another by threats and/or force. In general, we say that “real estate cannot be stolen” since the original owner can prove his claim in court once anarchy has been suppressed. But here we deal with the case that something happened before the original owner could sue the robber and the field is no longer available. If the field was taken by an “oppressor”, a Roman official against whom there is no recourse in the courts then, if everybody’s field was taken, the original owner has no claim since the field would have been taken even if it had not been stolen. But if the field was taken because the robber had a run-in with the law, the robber must pay for a replacement even though legally the field was still the original owner’s property when it was taken. The Yerushalmi (<i>Neziqin</i>, 7c, line 20) notes that even if the oppressors took the field from the robber because of the robbed, the robbed could claim replacement from the robber since he might say: Give me what is mine and let any other person deal with me. On this, R. Joshua ben Levi and Rav disagree there as they disagree here.</i>: “He who appropriated a field and oppressors took it.” He did not hear that Rebbi Joḥanan said<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 10:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.10.6.2"><i>Baba Qama</i> 10:6</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 116b-117a" href="/Bava_Kamma.116b-117a">Babli, <i>Baba Qama</i> 116b/117a</a>, it remains a question whether the rule represents a fine or a generally valid legal principle.</i>, they exacted a fine from a robber. Rebbi Abin<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">R. Abin II.</i> asked, (who was the father of) Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun, and Rebbi Ḥiyya ben Julianus both said in the name of Samuel; one said, it is like <i>annona</i> and head-tax<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">If a Roman official took the field illegally it nevertheless has the status of <i>annona</i> or head-tax. This supports R. Joḥanan that the rule represents a fine. The opposite opinion supports Rav against R. Joḥanan.</i>; the other one said, it is not like <i>annona</i> and head-tax.
There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>. Personal healing is healing a human, which is a biblical commandment (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:2" href="/Deuteronomy.22.2"><i>Deut</i>. 22:2</a>) and cannot be abolished by a vow. Financial healing is veterinary medicine. The question is, if he is required to return what the other one had lost as required in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>, why can he not heal his cow which is lost without medical help?</i>: “He heals him in the sense of personal healing but not in the sense of financial healing.” Rebbi Judan and Rebbi Yose. One of them said, here if the vow refers to his body, there if the vow refers to his property<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.2.1">Mishnah 2</a> refers to a vow that B cannot personally have any gain from A; therefore A may return what B lost. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.3.1">Mishnah 3</a> deals with the case that B cannot have any material advantage from A; therefore A cannot heal B’s animal.</i>. But the other one said, here if he has somebody else who can heal him, there if there is nobody else who can heal him. If he has somebody else who can heal him, he should not be able to heal him personally! Not by everybody is a person successfully healed<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The personal skills of a medical man are not transferable.</i>.
Is not the property of the finder forbidden to the owner of the cow<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">If A’s property was forbidden to B and A returned a stray cow to B, why should the finder’s fee go to charity and not to A? It must be that B’s property was forbidden to A.</i>? Ulla bar Ismael in the name of Rebbi Isaac: If each man’s property was forbidden to the other.
Who is the Tanna of “the gain should be given to the sacred fund”? Rebbi Meïr! For Rebbi Meïr said, one commits sacrilege with prohibitions<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">If somebody forbids something for himself by the language of <i>qorbān</i> or any of its equivalents, the rules of sacrifices apply and any unauthorized use is sacrilege subject to the penalties described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:14-16" href="/Leviticus.5.14-16"><i>Lev</i>. 5:14–16</a>: A sacrifice in the value of one Temple <i>šeqel</i> and restitution of 125% of the value taken.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked: If somebody made a vow [to forbid] a loaf to himself, can he use it to warm his hands<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">If the loaf is fresh from the oven.</i>? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 2:9" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).2.9">Tosephta 2:9</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 35a" href="/Nedarim.35a">Babli 35a</a>, <i>Šebuot</i>22a/23b, in the name of R. Meïr. In all Babli sources, R. Simeon dissents.</i>: “If he said, this loaf shall be dedicated, if he or another person ate it, they committed sacrilege<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">It follows all the rules of a sacrifice since it is called <i>qorbān</i>. But since a loaf of bread cannot be a sacrifice, it can be redeemed (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:11-14" href="/Leviticus.27.11-14"><i>Lev</i>. 27:11–14</a>.)</i>; therefore, it can be redeemed. But if he said, it is for me [as if dedicated], if he ate it he committed sacrilege on goodwill<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Since he cannot eat the loaf or use it commercially, the only use open to him is to donate the loaf to somebody. The monetary value of the goodwill acquired in this act is the basis for computing the restitution (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:2:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.2.6">Note 38</a>).</i> but others would not commit sacrilege.” He said only, if he ate it. Therefore, to warm his hands is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Even for R. Meïr.</i>. The students of Rebbi Jonah in the name of Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya: That is if he said I shall not eat it nor taste it; if he forbade only eating to himself. But the question was, if somebody made a vow [to forbid] a loaf to himself, can he use it to warm his hands<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">For an unspecified vow and use which cannot be quantified in monetary terms. The question remains open.</i>?

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Also he<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The person who made a vow that the other shall have no profit from him. Heave and tithes diminish the farmer’s wealth; they do not increase it.</i> can separate his heave and tithes with the other’s knowledge, and can bring for him nests for males or females suffering from genital discharges<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">“Nest” is the technical term for a couple of birds from the pigeon family, prescribed sacrifice for the person healed from genital discharge <i>before</i> he could enter the Temple. For a male, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:14" href="/Leviticus.15.14"><i>Lev</i>. 15:14</a>. For a female, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:29" href="/Leviticus.15.29"><i>Lev</i>. 15:29</a>.</i>, nests for childbirth<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Due before the mother could enter the Temple, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 12:8" href="/Leviticus.12.8"><i>Lev</i>. 12:8</a>, for the wife of a man who could not afford a sheep, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 12:6" href="/Leviticus.12.6"><i>Lev</i>. 12:6</a>.</i>, purification and reparation offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:27-5:26" href="/Leviticus.4.27-5.26"><i>Lev</i>. 4:27–5:26</a>. These sacrifices are in part burned on the altar, in part eaten by the priests; nothing is given to the offerer and his family, in contrast to well-being offerings.</i> and teach him interpretations<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbinic interpretations of biblical verses, of the kind preserved in the <i>Mekhiltot, Sifra,</i> and <i>Sifry</i>.</i>, religious rules, and sermons<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">As the Halakhah explains, in imitation of Moses who taught the Children of Israel without taking money, one may not take money for teaching the Oral Law. (In rabbinic practice, one may take money for teaching during regular business hours to make up for the loss incurred by not being in business at that time.)</i>; but he may not teach him Bible; he may teach Bible to his sons and daughters<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Since Moses wrote the Torah only at the end of his life, he never taught it. Therefore, one may take money to teach to read, sing, and understand the biblical text. This anonymous Mishnah proves that practice should not follow R. Eliezer in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Sotah 3:4" href="/Mishnah_Sotah.3.4">Mishnah <i>Soṭah</i> 3:4, Note 85</a>.</i>. Also, he can feed his wife and children<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">If they are needy, since charity is a universal obligation.</i> even though the other is obligated for their upkeep. But he cannot feed his animals<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Since this would increase the animal’s worth; i. e., the other man’s property.</i>, whether pure or impure; Rebbi Eliezer says, he may feed his impure<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Those which cannot be eaten.</i> animals but not the pure ones. They asked him, what is the difference between pure and impure animals? He said to them, the pure animal’s soul is Heaven’s but its body is his<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">As food.</i>, but the impure’s soul and body is Heaven’s. They said to him, also the impure animal’s soul is Heaven’s but its body is his, since he could sell it to Gentiles<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Who may eat any kind of animal; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 9:3" href="/Genesis.9.3"><i>Gen</i>. 9:3</a>.</i> or feed it to the dogs.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Also he can separate his heave” etc. If somebody fixes the harvest of another person without the latter’s knowledge, who receives the goodwill<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">That value is estimated by what a third party Israel would be willing to pay to the farmer to have the latter give his heave to his grandson, the son of his daughter and his Cohen son-in-law, or the tithes to a levitic grandson. “To fix” means to separate heave and tithes, to prepare the harvest for use and sale.</i> from the tithes? Rebbi Abbahu says, the one who does the fixing<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Since he uses his own produce to make the other person’s produce permitted for profane use.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira says, the owner of the produce. Rebbi Ze‘ira is consistent since Rebbi Ze‘ira said in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 36b" href="/Nedarim.36b">Babli, 36b</a> (and also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 50b" href="/Yoma.50b"><i>Yoma</i>50b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 6a" href="/Zevachim.6a"><i>Zebaḥim</i> 6a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 2b" href="/Temurah.2b"><i>Temurah</i> 2b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 10b" href="/Temurah.10b">10b</a>), a statement of R. Abbahu in the name of R. Joḥanan states that the person purged only can make the exchange but the goodwill belongs to the person who gives the produce. The Babli also quotes R. Ze‘ira in the same sense as the Yerushalmi but clearly prefers R. Abbahu over R. Ze‘ira against the Yerushalmi.</i>, if somebody gave the sacrifice of a <i>nazir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:14-15" href="/Numbers.6.14-15"><i>Num</i>. 6:14–15</a>.</i> or of a [healed] person afflicted with skin disease<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:10" href="/Leviticus.14.10"><i>Lev</i>. 14:10</a>.</i>, the person purged makes (heave)<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">This word, found in the ms. and the <i>editio princeps</i>, makes no sense since the sacrifices are given to the Cohen who alone directs the purging ceremony. With all commentaries one has to read תְמוּרָה “substitution” instead of תְרוּמָה “heave”, a simple metathesis. It is asserted that if somebody dedicates an animal as somebody else’s obligatory sacrifice, then only the person for whom the sacrifice is destined may substitute another animal (which action is sinful, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:9-10" href="/Leviticus.27.9-10"><i>Lev</i>. 27:9–10</a>). If the donor would substitute another animal after dedication, the action would be invalid and the substitute profane as before. In analogy, R. Simeon ben Laqish must hold that sanctified food can be disposed of only by the person whose obligation is satisfied by the sanctification.</i>. The Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: “Also he can separate his heave and tithes with his knowledge.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">In that case, A would make a donation of the value of the goodwill to B while B is forbidden to receive any gain from A!</i>” Explain it, that [it was stipulated] that the other should not get the goodwill<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">This stipulation overrides any general rule and makes the transaction legal.</i>.
It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 4:5" href="/Deuteronomy.4.5"><i>Deut</i>. 4:5</a>. The same argument in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 37a" href="/Nedarim.37a">Babli, 37a</a>.</i>: “Behold, I taught you laws and rules.” Just as I did it for free, so you have to do it for free. One could think the same is true for Bible and translations? The verse says laws and rules. You have to teach laws and rules<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">In their practical rabbinic form.</i> for free; you do not have to teach Bible and translations for free<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Elementary school teachers have to be paid well. The text is quoted by Ran, commentary to 37a.</i>. But do we not see that the <i>baraita</i> teachers take their fees? Rebbi Yudan, the son of Rebbi Ismael, said: They take payment for lost time.
Rebbi Ze‘ira said, from their words [we can deduce] whether he<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">A, the maker of the vow.</i> may feed his slaves or may not feed his<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">B, the person forbidden profit from A.</i> slave<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">The inconsistencies in number and gender are in the text.</i>. They wanted to say that he who says<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">R. Eliezer.</i> “the pure animal’s soul is Heaven’s but its body is his,” and this one, because her<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">The inconsistencies in number and gender are in the text.</i> body and soul is Heaven’s, he<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">A, the maker of the vow.</i> may feed his<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">B, the person forbidden profit from A.</i> slave. And he who says<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">The Sages.</i>, since he could sell it to Gentiles or feed it to the dogs, since this one’s body is his<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">B, the person forbidden profit from A.</i> but he cannot sell her to Gentiles<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Since a circumcized slave cannot be sold to Gentiles and an uncircumcized slave cannot be kept in the household.</i> nor feed her to the dogs, he may feed his slave. It was found stated: He may feed his slave<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 38b" href="/Nedarim.38b">Babli, 38b</a>, a named <i>baraita</i>. The reason is given that slaves are not bought for fattening.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody is forbidden by a vow any profit from another person and that one comes to make a visit, he may stand but not sit down<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">If B is forbidden any profit from A it is obvious that B cannot sit on any of A’s chairs. But here it is stated that A cannot sit in B’s chair even if he makes a medical visit.</i>. He heals him in the sense of personal healing but not in the sense of financial healing<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>. Personal healing is healing a human, which is a biblical commandment (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:2" href="/Deuteronomy.22.2"><i>Deut</i>. 22:2</a>) and cannot be abolished by a vow. Financial healing is veterinary medicine. The question is, if he is required to return what the other one had lost as required in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>, why can he not heal his cow which is lost without medical help?</i>, and may bathe with him in a large pool but not in a small one<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">If the pool is so small that one additional person visibly raises the water level, this would be a profit and is forbidden. (In modern Hebrew, the word means “bathtub”. The root ابط means “hollow”).</i>, and he may sleep with him on the same couch. Rebbi Jehudah says, in summer but not in the rainy season because he would profit. He can lie with him [at a dinner] on the same couch and eat with him at one table but not from the same pot<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">One is afraid that he purposely would take less food so the other man would have more.</i>. But he may eat with him from a returning pot<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the Halakhah, a pot so full that in any case it will not be emptied by the guests.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody is forbidden by a vow any profit from another person,” etc. Rebbi Simeon ben Yaqim said, that he should not stay long<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">There is no intrinsic reason why A cannot sit in B’s chair (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.4.1">Note 72</a>). The rule is simply a precaution that A should not come to violate his own vow. The same tradition is mentioned in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 39a" href="/Nedarim.39a">Babli, 39a</a>, in the name of R. Simeon ben Elyaqim.</i>.
The Mishnah [deals with the case] that the healer forbade his property on the sick person<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">We did not say that the knowledge of the healer is his capital and he is forbidden to heal the sick.</i>. But if the sick person forbade his property on the healer he sinned against himself<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The healer is forbidden to treat the sick person since the sick forbade to himself to pay the healer or to show any recognition for the services received.</i>.
“But not in a small pool,” for he heats it. It was stated: He bathes with him in a small bathhouse, for he takes away heat.
It was stated: From the returning pot. What is a returning pot? There, they say, portions<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The pot contains equal portions for everybody; there is no possibility that one guest would take less so the other could have more. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 41b" href="/Nedarim.41b">Babli, 41b</a>, the explanation of the rabbis “here” (in Galilee) is accepted.</i>. The rabbis here say, a pot which contains [enough food] to eat, to be full, and to leave leftovers. It was stated: From the returning cup. What is a returning cup? The rabbis there say, spiced wine<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>conditum vinum</i> (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 6:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.6.5.2"><i>Berakhot</i> 6:5, Note 157</a>). This is drunk only in small portions.</i>. The rabbis here say, one fills a מיסורס, they draw, drink, and return<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">The vessel is newly filled for each guest; there is no fear of interference.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He should not eat with him from the common pot set before the workers<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">It belongs to the standard contract of day laborers that the employer has to feed them. This he usually does by preparing a pot from which everybody takes (literally “a trough”). This is then the same as a non-returning <i>tamḥui</i>.</i> nor work with him in a profession<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">This is the interpretation of the Yarushalmi. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 41b" href="/Nedarim.41b">Babli, 41b</a>, takes אומן to be a synonym of ניר “a furrow” and holds that both cannot work on the same field at the same time.</i>, the words of Rebbi Meïr. But the Sages say, he can work at a distance from him.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He should not eat,” etc. In the opinion of Rebbi Meïr he is forbidden to teach him a trade. In the opinion of Rebbi Meïr he is forbidden to act in his defense.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody is forbidden any profit from another person by a vow made before the Sabbatical year, he cannot enter that person’s field nor can he eat from the overhang<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Fruits of trees that hang over a public road, which do not belong to the tree’s owner but are ownerless.</i>. In the Sabbatical year, he cannot enter his field<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">As the Halakhah points out, if by biblical decree everybody is permitted to eat from the yield of the Sabbatical, it must be permitted to enter all agricultural property for the purpose of harvesting. But in our case, if the maker of the vow would stay one moment too long, he would be sinning.</i> but he can eat from the overhang. If he vowed not to eat from the other before the Sabbatical year, he can enter<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Since he did not forbid that for himself.</i> his field but he cannot eat of its fruits. During the Sabbatical year he enters and eats.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody is forbidden any profit from another person by a vow made before the Sabbatical year,” etc. Why can he not enter<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">As the Halakhah points out, if by biblical decree everybody is permitted to eat from the yield of the Sabbatical, it must be permitted to enter all agricultural property for the purpose of harvesting. But in our case, if the maker of the vow would stay one moment too long, he would be sinning.</i>? As Rebbi Simeon ben Yaqim said, that he should not stay long<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">The same in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 42b" href="/Nedarim.42b">Babli, 42b</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan explained the Mishnah: If somebody is forbidden any profit from another person by a vow made before the Sabbatical year, he cannot enter that person’s field nor can he eat from the overhang<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">While the fruits overhanging the public domain are ownerless, the tree belongs to the person he forbade to himself.</i>. In the Sabbatical year, he can enter his field and eat of the fruits<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">The reading here is uncertain. The text given is that of the first hand of the ms. The corrected version reads וּבַשְּׁבִיעִית יוֹרֵד לְתוֹךְ שָׂדֵהוּ וְאֵינוֹ אוֹכֵל מִן הַפֵּירוֹת “In the Sabbatical year, he can enter his field but <i>not</i> eat of the fruits.” This makes no sense. It seems that the text should be corrected to וּבַשְּׁבִיעִית אֵינוֹ יוֹרֵד לְתוֹךְ שָׂדֵהוּ וְאוֹכֵל מִן הַפֵּירוֹת “In the Sabbatical year, he can<i>not</i> enter his field but eat of the fruits” where the fruits are either the overhang or harvested by others on the field. This version is quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 42a" href="/Nedarim.42a">Babli, 42a</a>, as concurrent opinion of R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish. A similar statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 2:8" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).2.8">Tosephta 2:8</a>.</i>. If he vowed during the Sabbatical year, he enters and eats<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">This last clause of the Mishnah applies to both cases; there is no difference whether the vow refers to all property or only to food.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish explains the Mishnah: If somebody is forbidden any profit from another person by a vow made before the Sabbatical year, he cannot enter that person’s field nor can he eat of its fruits<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Including the overhang.</i>. If he made the vow in the Sabbatical year, he cannot enter his field but he can eat of the fruits<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">The reading here is uncertain. The text given is that of the first hand of the ms. The corrected version reads וּבַשְּׁבִיעִית יוֹרֵד לְתוֹךְ שָׂדֵהוּ וְאֵינוֹ אוֹכֵל מִן הַפֵּירוֹת “In the Sabbatical year, he can enter his field but <i>not</i> eat of the fruits.” This makes no sense. It seems that the text should be corrected to וּבַשְּׁבִיעִית אֵינוֹ יוֹרֵד לְתוֹךְ שָׂדֵהוּ וְאוֹכֵל מִן הַפֵּירוֹת “In the Sabbatical year, he can<i>not</i> enter his field but eat of the fruits” where the fruits are either the overhang or harvested by others on the field. This version is quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 42a" href="/Nedarim.42a">Babli, 42a</a>, as concurrent opinion of R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish. A similar statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 2:8" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).2.8">Tosephta 2:8</a>.</i>. If he vowed not to eat from the other before the Sabbatical year, he can enter his field but he cannot eat of its fruits. If he made the vow during the Sabbatical year he enters and eats<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">This last clause of the Mishnah applies to both cases; there is no difference whether the vow refers to all property or only to food.</i>. That statement of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish follows Rebbi Yose, for Rebbi Yose said, for his vow preceded his abandoning, his abandoning his vow<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">These two words are probably superfluous; they are missing in the parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:10:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.10.2-4">Halakhah 10</a>. It is stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.10.1">Mishnah 10</a> that if A and B walk together and B has no food, then A may put some food on a rock and declare it ownerless, whereupon B can take it. R. Yose objects since A’s food remains forbidden to B if nobody else acquired it before it came to A. R. Yose [<i>Peah</i> 6:1 (fol. 19b), <i>Demay</i> 3:2 (fol. 23b), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:10:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.10.2-4"><i>Nedarim</i> 4:10</a>] rejects the concept of ownerless property and holds that the owner has full responsibility for his abandoned property until it is taken up and acquired by another person.</i>. Rebbi Jonah, Rebbi Abba bar Ḥiyya, in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Rebbi Yose agrees that he is permitted in the case of biblical abandoning<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Sabbatical produce is available for everybody by biblical decree, not by human action. Therefore, there is no comparison between <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.6.1">Mishnaiot 6</a> and 10.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody is forbidden any profit from another person by a vow, he should not borrow from him nor ask him for anything<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Tools or animals.</i>, should not give him a loan nor take a loan for him, should not sell to him nor buy from him. If he said, lend me your cow, he says: she is not available. If he said, a <i>qônam</i> that I shall never plough with her, if he usually ploughs, he is forbidden, the rest of the world is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">His agricultural workers may still use that cow to plough his field.</i>. If he does not usually plough, he and the rest of the world are forbidden.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody is forbidden any profit from another person by a vow, he should not lend him,” etc. If somebody says, a <i>qônām</i> that I shall never plough, it is the same as if he said, a <i>qônām</i> that my field should never be ploughed<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">The grocer understands that he has to acquire the money before he gives anything to X in order not to infringe on the vow.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody is forbidden any profit from another person by a vow and he has nothing to eat, the other person goes to the grocer and tells him, X is forbidden any profit from me by a vow and I do not know what to do<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">The grocer understands that he has to acquire the money before he gives anything to X in order not to infringe on the vow.</i>. Then he pays him and X comes and takes from him.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody is forbidden any profit from another person by a vow and he has nothing to eat,” etc. For he cannot recover from him in a court of law<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Neither the donor nor the recipient can go to court if the grocer takes the money and does not deliver since the donor cannot be more explicit if he does not want to violate his vow. Therefore, the money really becomes the grocer’s and this method is not circumventing the law.</i>.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he had his house to build, his fence to repair, his field to harvest, the other person goes to the journeymen and tells them, X is forbidden any profit from me by a vow and I do not know what to do. They work with him and come and take their wages from the other person.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If he had his house to build, his fence to repair,” etc. May he say, he who works will not lose<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">While clearly A cannot contract with the workers that they should work with X on his account, can he tell them that he will pay (in a way which would not be enforceable in court if they were not journeymen under special protection by biblical law and custom.)</i>? Let us hear from the following. <sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the paragraph, the text is also in <i>Šabbat</i> 16, fol. 15c 1. 35. It refers to Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 15:7: “If a Gentile comes to extinguish a fire, one does not tell him to extinguish or not extinguish since one is not responsible for his rest.” All fires mentioned here happened on a Sabbath.</i> In the days of Rebbi Immi there was a fire in the village. Rebbi Immi sent a crier to the markets of the Gentiles saying: “He who works will not lose.<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, in the case here one also is permitted to assure people of their wages. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 121a" href="/Shabbat.121a">Babli, <i>Šabbat</i> 121a</a>, R. Ammi (= Immi) says directly that suggesting payment is permitted in the case of a fire on the Sabbath.</i>” Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Yose said, that was danger to life. But if there was danger to life, Rebbi Immi himself should have fought the fire! Did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">Also in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 84b" href="/Yoma.84b">Babli, <i>Yoma</i> 84b</a>.</i>, in any case of danger to life one does not say that [the necessary work] be done by Gentiles or minors, but [it should be done] even by important Jewish persons. It happened that there was a fire in the courtyard of Yose ben Simai in Šiḥin, and the garrison of the barracks<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>castra, -orum, n</i>. “military camp, barracks, fortress.”</i> of Sepphoris came to fight it but he did not let them fight it; he said, let the collector collect his due<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Since the fire was on a Sabbath, he took it as divine punishment.</i>. Immediately there formed a cloud, there was rain which extinguished it<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">As reward for his piety.</i>. After the Sabbath he sent to each of them a tetradrachma and to their commander<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἔπαρχος, equivalent of Latin <i>praefectus</i>(<i>castrorum</i>).</i> 50 denarii. Rebbi Ḥanina said, there was no need for him to do that<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 121a" href="/Shabbat.121a">Babli, <i>Šabbat</i> 121a</a>, this is the opinion of the Sages, based on the Mishnah (Note 104), while Yose ben Simai wanted to encourage them to fight Sabbath fires at Sepphoris.</i> A Samaritan was a neighbor of Rebbi Jonah. There was a fire in Rebbi Jonah’s neighborhood; he wanted to fight it but Rebbi Jonah did not let him<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Since as a Samaritan he has to keep the Sabbath and, therefore, might fight fires only if there is a danger to life, rather than to property.</i>. He said to him, because of my property! He said, yes<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jonah agreed to be responsible for the Samaritan’s loss if the fire reached his property.</i>. And everything was saved. Rebbi Jonah from Kefar-Immi spread his garment over the grain stack and the fire retreated from it.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If they were walking on the road and he had nothing to eat, the other gives something to a third person as a gift, and this one is permitted it. If nobody is with them, he puts it on a rock or a fence and says, this is ownerless for everyone to take and this one can come and eat, but Rebbi Yose forbids<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">These two words are probably superfluous; they are missing in the parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:10:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.10.2-4">Halakhah 10</a>. It is stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.10.1">Mishnah 10</a> that if A and B walk together and B has no food, then A may put some food on a rock and declare it ownerless, whereupon B can take it. R. Yose objects since A’s food remains forbidden to B if nobody else acquired it before it came to A. R. Yose [<i>Peah</i> 6:1 (fol. 19b), <i>Demay</i> 3:2 (fol. 23b), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:10:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.10.2-4"><i>Nedarim</i> 4:10</a>] rejects the concept of ownerless property and holds that the owner has full responsibility for his abandoned property until it is taken up and acquired by another person.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated: Rebbi Meïr says that as soon as a person abandons anything from his property, it is no longer in his possession. Rebbi Yose says, nothing may leave the hands of its owners except if it is taken up by another person<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">E. g., if somebody is injured on abandoned property which had not been taken up by another person, the original owner has to pay according to R. Yose but not according to R. Meïr.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah, Rebbi Abba bar Ḥiyya, both say, Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai: The words of Rebbi Yose [deal with the case] that a person gave a gift to ten people and this one was one of them<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose compares abandoning property to giving something to a group of people without specifying who gets what. In that case, the transfer of ownership rights certainly depends on the new owner taking possession. This is also R. Joḥanan’s position in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 43a" href="/Nedarim.43a">Babli, 43a</a>.</i>. If somebody abandoned his field, some Tannaïm say, he can retract<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">If nobody took the property in the meantime.</i>, but some Tannaïm say, he cannot retract. Rebbi Ḥizqiah, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: The one who says that he can retract follows Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">Since the property still is his to do with as he pleases.</i>. But the one who says that he cannot retract follows Rebbi Meïr. Does this mean that abandoned property in the sense of Rebbi Yose is subject to tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">Since a gift does not remove the obligation of heave and tithes; cf. <i>Ma‘serot</i> 1:1, Notes 18–23.</i>? What did he say? Abandoned property, not gift<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">Since the statement of R. Yose is about abandoned property; it cannot follow all the rules of gifts.</i>! Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa came in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: Abandoned property in the sense of Rebbi Yose is subject to tithes. There<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonia.</i>, they say that abandoned property in the sense of Rebbi Yose is not subject to tithes, <sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the paragraph, the text is from <i>Ma‘serot</i> 1:1, Note 20.</i> since Rebbi Joḥanan said in the name of Rebbi Yannai: <sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:29" href="/Deuteronomy.14.29"><i>Deut</i>. 14:29</a>.</i>“The Levite shall come,” etc. You are obliged to give him from what you have but he has not. This excludes abandoned property for which your and his hands are equal. Gleanings, forgotten sheaves, <i>peah</i>, and abandoned property are all equal.
<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">The entire paragraph is also in <i>Peah</i> 6:1, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.3.1">Notes 23–27</a>.</i> So far, if he abandoned it for a longer period of time. But if he abandoned only for a short time? Let us hear from the following: “If he abandoned his field, he may cancel his action during three days.” Rebbi Ze‘ira said, it says only “three days”. Therefore, after three days he may not cancel his action. Rebbi Simeon Dayana stated before Rebbi Ze‘ira: Even after three he may cancel his action. He said to him, since you say after three days, is it the same after three or after many? A <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Ze‘ira<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 44a" href="/Nedarim.44a">Babli 44a</a>.</i>: “About when is this said? If he abandoned in an unspecified way. But if he said: My field shall be abandoned one day, one week, one month, one year, a sabbatical period, as long as nobody took it over, either he or another person, he may cancel. But after somebody acquired it, either he or somebody else, he cannot cancel.” This means that short or long periods are the same. It also means that they were not worried about dishonesty. This means that a person may abandon and reacquire. That obviously answers Rebbi Zeïra’s question, since Rebbi Zeira had said, are short and long times the same?
“This one can come and eat, but Rebbi Yose forbids.” What is Rebbi Yose’s reason? Because his vow preceded his abandonment. Therefore, not if his abandonment preceded his vow. They asked before Rebbi Yose: What is the difference if his vow preceded his abandonment<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">Why does R. Yose accept abandonment before the vow as valid when he holds that everything remains the person’s own until it is picked up by somebody else?</i>? He said to them, it did not occur to him to forbid what he abandoned<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">It has nothing to do with property rights. A vow cannot refer to anything that was not in the vower’s mind.</i>. Rebbi Jonah said, so they asked before him: That means, not if it applies to a single person<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">The reference is to the second half of the paragraph where it is stated that for everybody abandonment declared before three adult witnesses is immediately valid since it is a public act; even for R.
