Meïr an act before less than three witnesses is not valid. But in the Mishnah it is stated that no witnesses are present and nevertheless R. Meïr holds that in respect to the vow the abandonment is valid.</i>? But did we not state: “This one can come and eat, but Rebbi Yose forbids”? He said to them, it did not occur to him to forbid what he abandoned. Rebbi Jonah, Rebbi Abba, Rebbi Ḥiyya, in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Joṣadaq: If somebody abandons his field to ten persons he cannot retract<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 45a" href="/Nedarim.45a">Babli, 45a</a>, three people. (R. Joshua ben Levi holds that this requirement is rabbinic.)</i>. Rebbi Yose said, that follows Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Mana asked: When you said, “to three,” [did you not mean] “in the presence of three”? Instead of asking about Rebbi Meïr, ask about Rebbi Yose! When you said, “to ten,” [did you not mean] “in the presence of ten”? He said to him, if he abandoned it before two persons, he can retract and it remains subject to tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">If any of the persons knowing of the abandonment takes the property for himself, he cannot prove the title if the original owner reneges on his abandonment since he can support his claim only by one witness, which is not enough. Therefore, if the owner abandons the real estate and then reclaims it, it never left his potential possession and all produce grown on his field was never totally abandoned; the duty of tithing was never disestablished..</i>. If he abandoned it before three persons, he cannot retract and it is free from tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Any one of the three can take the real estate and has two witnesses to back up his claim. If the original owner would retake it when nobody else had taken it, he takes genuinely abandoned property and no tithes are due (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:9:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.9.2">Note 106</a>).</i>.

Chapter 5



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Partners<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">People who own houses built around a common courtyard. They are partners in the courtyard. One exits from the houses to the street only through the courtyard.</i> who mutually made vows not to have usufruct from one another are forbidden to enter the courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">For the anonymous Tanna, the entire courtyard is common property; nobody can enter the courtyard without stepping into the property of both of them. Therefore, neither one can enter his courtyard without profiting from the other’s property.</i>. Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob says, each one enters into what is his<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.1.4.1">Mishnah <i>Baba batra</i> 1:6</a> explains that the parties of a common courtyard can force a partition if the area of the courtyard exceeds a certain minimum. As the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 46b" href="/Nedarim.46b">Babli explains, 46b</a>, R. Eliezer ben Jacob refers only to a courtyard larger than the minimum. He holds that each square inch of the courtyard is potentially the property of one owner, only it was not determined who the actual owner is. Therefore, each of the partners can claim that he temporarily steps only onto his own property. It must be that R. Eliezer ben Jacob permits a straight walk from the entrance gate to the house door; this would be the domain belonging to the person’s house in a separation of properties.</i>. Both are forbidden to put up there a grindstone or an oven, or to raise chickens there<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Everybody agrees that the common courtyard can be used for private purposes only by the consent of all owners. If the person forbidden by a vow did not object to the other using the courtyard for his private purpose, he would make him a present of monetary value.</i>. If one of them made a vow not to have usufruct from the other, he cannot enter the courtyard. Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob says, one can say to the other, I enter into my property, I do not enter into yours. One forces the one who made the vow to sell his part<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">According to the Babli, this statement refers to the majority position. According to the Yerushalmi, this is part of R. Eliezer ben Jacob’s statement and is explained in the Halakhah.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Partners who mutually made vows not to have usufruct from one another,” etc. The rabbis say, every single [square] hand-breadth is common property of the partners. Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob says, each one enters into what is his. If the courtyard was divided by pebbles<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ψῆφος. If at some time they decide to mark the borders between the domains belonging to the different houses by rows of pebbles cemented into the ground, this amounts to a division of the common property and the rabbis will not hold that a formal contract is needed to disestablish the former common domain. (In modern Hebrew, the word means “mosaic”.)</i>, even the rabbis agree. If one of them went and sold his part, even Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob agrees<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">If one of the partners sells his part, he can no longer claim to enter into what is his and even according to R. Eliezer ben Jacob he is forbidden to set foot in the courtyard.</i>. If they contracted between themselves to concede<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">They split the common courtyard as outlined in <i>Baba batra</i> but in the contract agreed that they would not insist on excluding the other party from entering the separate domain of the other party. Then for the rabbis the split did not change anything.</i>, it is still in the hands of the partners. It is only needed if they did not contract between themselves to concede; what is the position of Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob? And did we not state there, “he buys higher and sells lower<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 3:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.3.8.1">Chapter 3, Note 147</a>. Does R. Eliezer ben Jacob permit transactions at the regular price?</i>”? What is the position of Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob? And did we not state there, “he should not lend him nor ask him for anything<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.7.1">Chapter 4, Note 99</a>. Since no transfer of property is involved, does R. Eliezer ben Jacob agree?</i>,”? What is the position of Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob? And what Rebbi Simeon ben Yaqim said, that he should not stay long<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.4.2">Chapter 4, Note 76</a>. Does R. Eliezer ben Jacob agree that the visits have to be as short as possible?</i>?
“Both are forbidden to put a grindstone or an oven up there, or to raise chickens there<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Everybody agrees that the common courtyard can be used for private purposes only by the consent of all owners. If the person forbidden by a vow did not object to the other using the courtyard for his private purpose, he would make him a present of monetary value.</i>.” This is needed for Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">While the rabbis agree, for them the statement is immaterial since both partners are forbidden entry into the courtyard.</i>, for what he states about “partners who mutually made vows not to have usufruct from one another.” Because they mutually made vows not to have usufruct from one another, does this imply that if they had not made vows not to have usufruct from one another, they would be presumed to concede one to the other<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">If somebody wants to put up an oven or a grindstone in the courtyard, can he presume to have the permission of all inhabitants of the courtyard without asking?</i>? Rebbi Jeremiah said, partners have the custom to concede one to the other in these matters. There<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 3:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.3.6.1">Mishnah <i>Baba batra</i> 3:6</a>. It is stated there that simply putting a stove, a grindstone, or chickens into a courtyard does not create a presumption of ownership, but building a foundation for the grindstone or an enclosure for the oven does.</i>, we have stated: “The following establish presumption of ownership.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">In the absence of documents, a testimony of three years of undisturbed ownership together with a claim of legal acquisition (by buying, inheritance, or gift) entitles the occupant to a documentary title; cf. <i>Yebamot</i> 12, Note 29 (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 3:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.3.4.1">Mishnah <i>Baba batra</i> 3:4</a>).</i>” Rebbi Eleazar said, if somebody raises chickens in a courtyard in which he does not dwell, this establishes presumption of ownership. Rebbi Yose said, this is correct. As you look at it, if he had the right to raise them, he raised them. If he had no right to raise them, he established a presumption of ownership. Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Benaiah: Partners can veto any activity of the other party in a courtyard except for washing<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">He disagrees with R. Jeremiah and the conclusion drawn from the formulation of the Mishnah. The same statement in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 57b" href="/Bava_Batra.57b">Babli, <i>Baba batra</i> 57b</a>. The statement of R. Jeremiah does not appear in the Babli.</i>, for the honor of the daughters of Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">It cannot be expected that a Jewish woman would go to the river to do her washing there and be seen by everybody without shoes and with uncovered arms.</i>. Rebbi Mattaniah said, that is, at a place where women wash, but not at a place where men wash<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">If men wash it is a commercial activity and that certainly needs the permission of the other dwellers in the courtyard.</i>. And what you say except for washing in the courtyard [applies to] the entire courtyard except for the four cubits of that party where one cannot hinder anything<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">A strip four cubits wide along the entire front of a house is private property of the owner of the house, not common property of the partners and, as a matter of principle, the owner of the house can do there anything he wants; the mistress of the house can wash there without asking anybody.</i>. But if the place was at an incline, one can veto even within the four cubits of another party since one might say to him, you pour out and it flows down to my place<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">That is not a matter of property rights but of torts; the injured party could claim damages.</i>. It was stated: The place of an oven or a hearth does not establish a presumption of ownership, but roofing of any size on top of them establishes a presumption of ownership<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 3:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.3.6.1">Mishnah <i>Baba batra</i> 3:6</a> it is asserted that only a wall of at least ten hand-breadths around an oven or a hearth does count. The text here is very close to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 2:13" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).2.13">Tosephta <i>Baba batra</i> 2:13</a>: Putting an oven or a hearth in a courtyard does not establish a presumption of ownership, but making a roof of any size does.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira said, only if the wall is useful for the oven.
Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai: Partners can acquire one from the other<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 8:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.8.1.4"><i>Ševi‘it</i> 8:1, Note 15</a>, rabbinic practice does not recognize transfer of property by payment, but only by delivery or a symbolic act indicating delivery. Also, since anything found on a person’s real estate is his, one can state that real estate acquires for its owner (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 5:6:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.5.6.3"><i>Peah</i> 5, Note 125</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 8:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.8.2.4"><i>Kilaim</i> 8, Note 46</a>). If there is only one owner, delivery of goods into the courtyard is legal delivery and transfer of ownership. R. Joḥanan asserts here that even among partners on their common real estate one may assume, following R. Eliezer ben Jacob, that the place on which some merchandise is put is at least temporarily owned by the acquiring party alone. This supports the opinion in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 46b" href="/Nedarim.46b">Babli, 46b</a>, that practice follows R. Eliezer ben Jacob. R. Abun bar Cahana follows the opinion of the anonymous majority, that common property is not divisible, and requires an action by the acquirer, either collecting the objects or at least moving them in a symbolic display of ownership rights.</i> and are responsible for damages inflicted by one on the other<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">This statement is taken in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 3:8:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.3.8.2"><i>Baba qama</i> 3:8</a> to show that an owner is liable for injuries on his property suffered by anybody being on the property by right or invitation.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Cahana said, if he said, collect and acquire. But if they were collected he does not acquire unless he moves.
“I enter my property, I do not enter yours.” And you say, one forces him<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">There seems to be no reason why R. Eliezer ben Jacob should require a sale of the property. It is clear that following the anonymous rabbis a person who by the vow of another is prevented to reach his own house can sue to remove this obstacle and force the maker of the vow to divest himself of the real estate.</i>? It was not said on this, but on<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 5:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.5.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>, speaking of a third person C visiting a partner B while partner A had forbidden him any use of his property.</i>: “I enter your partner’s property, I do not enter yours.” In that case one forces the [maker of the vow] to sell his part. If he says, you shall not have any usufruct from me. But not if he says, I shall not have any usufruct from you<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">There is trouble only if A forbids all his property to C but not if A forbids himself any of C’s properties. Unfortunately, the word מוּדָּר admits of both interpretations. If A makes the vow, מוּדָּר בּ- means that A takes nothing from C, מוּדָּר מ- that C may have nothing from A. The Babylonian Mishnah 2 follows the first alternative, the Yerushalmi one the second.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If anyone from outside was under a vow of no usufruct from one [of the partners], he should not enter the courtyard. Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob says, he can say to him, I enter your partner’s property, I do not enter yours.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If anyone from outside was,” etc. And we did not state “one forces”! Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: “If he was a frequent maker of vows, one forces him,” because the other [partner] can say to him, one came to me and you did such and such to him; a second person and you did such and such to him; the third time he tells him either you undo your vow or sell your part.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one was under a vow not to have usufruct from another and he owns a bathhouse or an olive press leased out in town; if he retains the grip of a hand<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">“Hand” here means “possession”, as in Middle High German <i>hant</i>, Swiss legal German <i>Hand</i>, French (for real estate) <i>main</i>.</i> on them, the other one is forbidden; if he retains no grip of a hand on them, the other one is permitted. If a person says to another, a <i>qônām</i> that I shall not enter your house, or that I shall not buy your field, if the owner died or sold it, the other one is permitted. That I shall not enter this house, or that I shall not buy this field, if the owner died or sold it, the other one is forbidden.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> What is the grip of a hand? One half, a third, or a quarter<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">If the lessee pays a percentage of revenue or gain (in the examples between 50% and 25%), the original owner is directly profiling from the other’s use of his facilities. But if the rent is a fixed sum, then no gain accrues directly to the building’s owner by people using bathhouse or olive press. The latter statement is explicit in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 46b" href="/Nedarim.46b">Babli, 46b</a>.</i>.
“This, your house,” because of what do you catch him? Because of “your house,” “this”<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Rashba (<i>Novellae</i> to 46b) has a slightly enlarged text: משום ביתך או משום זה “because of ‘your house’ or because of ‘this’?” The text as it stands is completely intelligible. A vow because of “your house” becomes void if it is no longer the other’s, because of “this” is permanent.</i>? If it collapsed and he himself rebuilt it, is it not “this”? Let us hear from the following: “If somebody said to his heirs, give a wedding house to my son or a widow’s house to my daughter, if it collapsed, the heirs are required to rebuild it.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Since the <i>baraita</i> does <i>not</i> say that if he said “this house” the heirs do not have to rebuild, it follows that “your house” is determining, not “this”. This means that if the house collapses and he rebuilds, it is forbidden, but if it is sold, it is permitted.</i>” <sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the paragraph there is an approximate parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 9:8:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.9.8.2"><i>Baba Qama</i> 9:14</a>, source of the Mishnah.</i> There, we have stated: “If somebody says to his son, ‘a <i>qônām</i> that you can not enjoy anything from me,’ if he dies, [the son] inherits<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Since he inherits by biblical decree (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 27:6-11" href="/Numbers.27.6-11"><i>Num</i>. 27:6–11</a>), not by his father’s will.</i>. ‘During my lifetime and after my death,’ if he dies, [the son] does not inherit.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 2:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.2.1.2">Chapter 2, Note 15</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 47a" href="/Nedarim.47a">Babli 47a</a>.</i>” [Did we not state, ‘during my lifetime’, [the son] inherits; ‘after my death’, [the son] inherits?] What is the difference between the one who says it one by one and the one who says it by twos<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">If he makes two separate vows, the first one, a <i>qônām</i> forbidding his property during his lifetime, is valid, but the second one for the time after his death is invalid since nobody can forbid to another person anything not in his possession. Why does it become valid if combined?</i>? <sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The text in <i>Baba Qama</i> switches the Mishnaiot quoted, respectively, by Rav Jeremiah and Rebbi Yose.</i> Rav Jeremiah and Rebbi Yose [ben Ḥanina] both say, ‘a <i>qônām</i> that I<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that instead of עלי “for me” one has to read עליו “for him”, as is clear from the rest of the sentence.</i> cannot enjoy these my properties during my lifetime and after my death,’ from the moment he said “these” he forbade to him during his lifetime and after his death<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Both of them disagree with the position taken earlier by the editors of the Yerushalmi and assert that “this” overrides “your house”. If the house collapses and he rebuilds, it is permitted, but if sold, it remains forbidden.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, we have stated in <i>Nedarim</i> what we did not state in <i>S̄ebuot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">There is no comparable Mishnah in Tractate <i>Šebuot</i>. In <i>Baba Qama</i>, the text is: “We have stated in <i>Neziqin</i> what we did not state in <i>Nedarim</i>,” but there, the reference is to a Mishnah in <i>Neziqin</i>, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 5:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.5.3.3">Note 39</a>. The difference is <i>not</i> a variant reading.</i>: “A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not enter your house, or that I shall not buy your field, if the owner died or sold it, the other is permitted. That I shall not enter this house, or that I shall not buy this field, if the owner died or sold it, the other is forbidden.” Because he said “this”. Therefore, if he had not said “this”, he would have<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that one has to read “had <i>not</i> forbidden”. In <i>Baba Qama</i> this last sentence is missing; the preceding one reads: “Because he said ‘these’, he forbade to him both during his lifetime and after his death.”</i> forbidden to him both during his lifetime and after his death.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “I am <i>ḥerein</i> for you<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ḥerem</i> is an expression for a gift to the Temple of offerings that cannot be redeemed, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:28" href="/Leviticus.27.28"><i>Lev</i>. 27:28</a>. As a term for a vow, it is equivalent to <i>qorbān</i>.</i>”, the addressee is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">To have any usufruct from the vower’s property.</i>. “You are for me <i>ḥerem</i>,” the vower is forbidden. “I am for you and you are for me,” both are forbidden. Both are permitted the institutions of the returnees from Babylonia<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">These are detailed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 5:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.5.5.1">Mishnah 5</a>. They were destined for public use and are immune against private vows.</i> and forbidden the institutions of their own town<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Since both of them are partners in the public institutions of the town; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 5:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.5.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “ “I am <i>ḥerem</i> for you”, etc. ‘A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not have any usufruct from you and anyone I would ask about you’, he has to ask about the first but not the second. Some Tannaïm state: He has to ask both about the first and the second<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">If a person vows not to have anything from a certain other person and any rabbi who would dissolve this vow, if he has second thoughts and asks a rabbi to disolve the vow, there are differing opinions on whether he then has to ask a second rabbi to dissolve his vow regarding the first rabbi. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 90" href="/Nedarim.90">Babli, 90a/b</a>, two questions are raised which are not found in the Yerushalmi: (1) What means first and second; maybe first is the rabbi whom he has to approach and second the object of the vow? (2) Is it clear whether a vow can be dissolved that has not yet led to any prohibition?</i>. Samuel the son of Rebbi Joseph ben Rebbi Abun said, that follows the one who said “after the prohibition”<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the disagreement of R. Ismael and R. Aqiba (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 3:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.3.6.2">Chapter 3, Note 128</a>; Halakhah 11:9, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 89a" href="/Nedarim.89a">Babli 89a</a>) whether the point in time which determines the rules is the moment the vow is made or the moment the prohibition is slated to begin. For R. Ismael, who holds that the moment of prohibition is determining, the second prohibition starts the moment the vower goes to see a rabbi. Therefore, that prohibition has to be dissolved also. But for R. Aqiba, who goes after the moment the vow was made, there is a vow only about one person and when that prohibition is lifted, the vow and the second case with it disappear.</i>. But for the one who said “after the vow”, a vow which is partially invalid is totally invalid<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 9:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.9.6.1">Mishnah 9:6</a>, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 3:2:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.3.2.10">Chapter 3, Note 84</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 2:10" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).2.10">Tosephta 2:10</a>, a slightly different version in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 8a" href="/Bava_Batra.8a">Babli, <i>Baba Batra</i> 8a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 112a" href="/Sanhedrin.112a"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 112a</a>.</i>“A person who makes a vow ‘to forbid himself the people of the town,’ if another person came and dwelled there for thirty days, [the vower] is permitted to deal with him. ‘From the inhabitants of the town,’ if another person came and dwelled there for thirty days, [the vower] is prohibited from dealing with him.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">“People of the town” are its taxpayers. The obligation to pay local taxes starts only with a residence of 12 months. “Town dwellers” are those who have to contribute to the local welfare fund. That obligation starts after thirty days.</i>” ‘A <i>qônām</i> that the people of my town cannot have any usufruct from me,’ he cannot ask the local rabbi<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Since he is an interested party.</i>. ‘The usufruct of the people of my town [is forbidden] to me’, he can ask the local rabbi. Some Tannaïm state, even in the first case he can ask, for it does not mean that [the rabbi] would dissolve his own vow<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The rabbi does not have to disqualify himself since formally he is only forbidden to dissolve his own vows and it is probably embarrassing to him to deal with a person who makes that kind of vow.</i>. A vow of the community cannot be dissolved<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">This is generally agreed on (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 36a" href="/Gittin.36a">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i> 36a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Makkot 16a" href="/Makkot.16a"><i>Makkot</i> 16a</a>); since voter rolls change continuously, it is impossible to get all people who voted for the vow together to ask for dissolution.</i>. A vow made in public cannot be dissolved<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 36a" href="/Gittin.36a">Babli, <i>Giṭṭin</i> 36a</a>, decides against this.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 2:10" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).2.10">Tosephta 2:10</a>.</i>“If somebody made a vow that another should not have usufruct from him, if the vow was made in the other’s presence, he can request the vow to be dissolved only in the other’s presence; if the vow was not made in the other’s presence, he can request the dissolution either in the other’s presence or in his absence.” Rebbi Joḥanan said, because of the shame; Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, because of the suspicion<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan feels that the maker of an inappropriate vow should be shamed, R. Joshua ben Levi requires the other party to be present lest he thought that the vower broke his vow. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 65a" href="/Nedarim.65a">Babli, 65a</a>, disagrees and holds that the rule is biblical. Since Moses had sworn to Reuel to stay in Midian (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 2:21" href="/Exodus.2.21"><i>Ex</i>. 2:21</a>), God dissolved his vow not at the Senna-bush but in Midian, with the consent of his family (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 3:18" href="/Exodus.3.18"><i>Ex</i>. 3:18</a>).</i>.
It turns out that one disagreement is like an other other disagreement, as it was stated: On the Day of Atonement, one has to detail one’s deeds, the words of Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra. Rebbi Aqiba says, one does not have to detail the sin<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yoma</i>, Yerushalmi 1:9 (45c, 1. 48); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 86b" href="/Nedarim.86b">Babli 86b</a>. [There, the first author is R. Jehudah ben Baba. In the first hand of the Munich ms. the second author is R. Jehudah; this seems to have been the reading of Maimonides (<i>Tešubah</i> 2:3) and <i>Tosafot Yešenim ad loc</i>.]</i>. Some Tannïm state, one has to detail the vow; some Tannïm state, one does not have to detail the vow. A person made a vow not to earn money. He came before Rebbi Yudan bar Shalom. He asked him, what did you forswear to yourself? He said to him, not to earn money. Rebbi Yudan said to him, is there anyone doing that? He said to him, as a gambler<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Perhaps Greek κυβευτής, ὁ “gambler”. (In rabbinic practice, a professional gambler is inadmissible as a witness.)</i>. He said, praise to Him Who chose the Torah and the Sages who said, one has to detail the vow<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">If he had not asked, he would have dissolved the vow which did not warrant dissolution.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> What are the institutions of the returnees from Babylonia? For example, the Temple Mount, the courtyards<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">The Temple courtyards.</i>, and the cistern in the middle of the road<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Traditionally made for pilgrims.</i>. What are the institutions of that town? For example, the town square, the bathhouse, the synagogue with the ark and the scrolls. And he writes his part to the Patriarch<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 5:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.5.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>, if each one writes over his part of the public institutions of the town to the Patriarch, both of them are permitted to walk in the town and to use its institutions.</i>.<br>Rebbi Jehudah says, one of them writes to the Patriarch and the other to a private person<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">This is a continuation of the previous Mishnah. R. Jehudah permits either of the parties to donate their usufruct of public property to a third party even though public property is not transferable in this way.</i>. What is the difference between him who writes to the Patriarch and him who writes to a private person? The one who writes to the Patriarch does not have to perform an act of delivery<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">In talmudic legal theory, a transfer of property is only valid if the point in time of the transfer of property rights or of claims to property is well defined. This usually is done by the buyer (or recipient) or his representative giving to the seller (or donor) or his representative a vessel (or edge of garment) to hold temporarily. In talmudic theory, Boaz took off his shoe to acquire the right to buy back Elimelekh’s property and with it Ruth (by preliminary marriage) from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ruth 4:8" href="/Ruth.4.8">Tob (<i>Ruth</i> 4:8</a>). Following the terminology of the Book of Ruth, in Babylonian/rabbinic terminology this act is called קִנְיָן “acquisition”.</i>, the one who writes to a private person has to perform an act of delivery. But the Sages say, in either case one has to perform an act of delivery. Rebbi Jehudah says, the people of Galilee do not have to write since their forefathers already wrote for them<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">They never recognized private rights of the citizens to public property. Therefore, the vower and the subject of his vow can use public property without formalities.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “What are the institutions of the returnees from Babylonia,” etc. So is the Mishnah: A town square which is crossed by a public road is as if from the returnees from Babylonia<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 2:10" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).2.10">Tosephta 2:10</a>. This is not an emendation of the Mishnah but an explanation: A town square which is crossed by an imperial highway is imperial, not local, property.</i>. A man used a Torah scroll for his preliminary marriage<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Where the man has to give his fiancee something of value; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 2:1:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.2.1.10"><i>Peah</i>2:3, Note 46</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 4:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.4.1.6"><i>Demay</i> 4:2, Note 19</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:1:15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.1.15"><i>Yebamot</i> 1:1, Note 63</a>.</i> to a woman. Rebbi Sabbatai and Rebbi Ḥasida brought the matter before Rebbi Yose who said, she is not married<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">A Torah scroll cannot serve as a marriage gift.</i>. Rebbi Ḥizqiah said, you read the letter<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Maybe it should be: I read the letter.</i> and there is written in it “not only”. Rebbi Mana said, I asked before Rebbi Ḥizqiah, what means “not only”? Did he marry with a Torah scroll which is private property<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Since a Torah scroll may be sold only if a person is in extreme difficulties (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 3:6:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.3.6.2-4"><i>Bikkurim</i> 3:7</a>, end), it is doubtful whether monetary value was transferred. (For that reason, a modern marriage is concluded by the gift of a gold ring without a stone, whose commercial value can be easily determined.)</i>? He said to us, she is not married. That comes to tell you, even if he used the public Torah scroll<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">In which every member of the synagogue has partial ownership. But since only the town council backed by a vote of the entire population could sell a Torah scroll, even if the man transferred his interest to his bride, he did not transfer disposable value.</i> to marry, that is “not only”. But that means, even if he used a privately owned Torah scroll to marry, that is “she is not married.”
“Rebbi Jehudah says, even if only one signs over his part to the Patriarch,” etc. So is the Mishnah: One of them has to sign over his part to the Patriarch<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Not both of them to ordinary citizens. Cf. J. N. Epstein, מבוא לנוסח המשנה <sup>2</sup>, Jerusalem-Tel Aviv 1964, p. 361, who raises the question whether the “Patriarch” is the head of the Academy or the head of the city administration.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a person who by a vow was forbidden usufruct from another has nothing to eat, the other donates [food] as a gift to a third party and the person is permitted it<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">This was already stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.10.1">Mishnah 4:10</a>; it is repeated as introduction to the definition of “gift”.</i>. It happened in Bet Ḥoron with a person whose father was by a vow forbidden usufruct from him; when he married off his son he said to a friend, here the courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">His own courtyard, where the wedding takes place and his father is forbidden entry.</i> and the meal are given to you as a gift and they shall be yours until my father has come and eaten with us at the [wedding] meal. He said to him, if they are mine, they are dedicated to Heaven<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">They are Temple property to be sold for the upkeep of the Temple.</i>. He said, I did not give you my property that you should dedicate it to Heaven. He said to him, you gave me your property only that you and your father should eat, drink, and be friendly with one another and let the sin hang on my head. When the case came before the Sages they said, any gift with the proviso that if [the recipient] dedicated, it was not sanctified, is no gift.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If a person who by a vow was forbidden usufruct from another,” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan said, it is obvious that this one was learned.
<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 28a" href="/Sukkah.28a">Babli <i>Sukkah</i> 28a</a>.</i> Hillel the Elder had eighty pairs of students. The greatest among them was Jonathan ben Uzziel, the least important Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai. Once he fell sick and all came to visit him. Rabban Joḥanan ben Zakkai waited in the courtyard. He said to them, where is the least important among you who is a head in wisdom and a head for generations? Unnecessary to speak of the greatest among you! They said, he is in the courtyard. He said to them, let him enter. When he entered, he said to them “to let my lovers inherit substance; their treasuries I shall fill.<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 8:21" href="/Proverbs.8.21"><i>Prov</i>. 8:21</a>. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oktzin 3:12" href="/Mishnah_Oktzin.3.12">Mishnah <i>Uqeṣin</i> 3:12</a>.</i>”
“Rabbi Joḥanan said, it is obvious that this one was learned.” Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, that is what happened<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">The background of the last Mishnah. A similar story, with Jonathan ben Uzziel in the role given here to Shammai, is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 133b" href="/Bava_Batra.133b">Babli, <i>Baba Batra</i> 133b</a> with Shammai trying and failing to attack the method.</i>: Jonathan ben Uzziel’s father vowed not to let him have any usufruct from him and in his will gave his<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Jonathan ben Uzziel.</i> part to Shammai. What did Shammai do? He sold some, gave some to the sacred fund, gave him<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Jonathan ben Uzziel.</i> the remainder as a gift, and said: He who wants to attack this gift<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">As circumventing the will of the donor.</i>, let him first get back [the merchandise] from the buyers and from the sacred fund; after that he can get [the remainder] back from this<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Jonathan ben Uzziel.</i> one<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">If somebody wants to shield himself from an accusation of “Beth Ḥoron gift”, he should give something away before giving to the person prohibited by the vow.</i>.
Rebbi Jeremiah asked: Does this mean that nobody can give a gift to a friend on condition that he not dedicate it to Heaven<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Does such a clause invalidate any gift even if given honestly and permanently?</i>? So is the Mishnah: Any gift similar to that of Beth Ḥoron, which was dishonest in that if [the recipient] dedicated, it was not sanctified, is no gift<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">In the language of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 48a" href="/Nedarim.48a">Babli, 48a</a>: If the end proved that the beginning was dishonest. The Babli prefers to emend the first, not the last, sentence of the Mishnah.</i>.

Chapter 6



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who makes a vow to abstain from cooked food is permitted roasted and scalded food<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In rabbinic tradition, based on the Babli, שלק means “preserved by prolonged cooking” so that the food can be kept for a long time without refrigeration. But Rashba (<i>Novellae ad loc</i>.) makes a convincing argument, based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 10:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.10.6.2"><i>Terumot</i> 10:8 (Notes 103–104</a>), that שלק is scalding (pouring hot water over the food), not cooking in a pot.</i>. If one said, a <i>qônām</i> that I will not taste a cooked dish, he is forbidden fine dishes<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Those which contain visible moisture.</i> and permitted thick ones<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">If there is no more visible moisture and it can be eaten without bread (or today, without a fork).</i>. Also he is permitted a soft boiled egg<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Galen VI, 769 distinguishes kinds of eggs: ἑφθά “hard boiled”, τρομητά “trembling”, i. e. with solid egg white but soft yolk, which tremble if shelled, ῥοφητά “soft”, which can be sipped. In contrast to the Yerushalmi which identifies the last two kinds, the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 50b" href="/Nedarim.50b">Babli (50b</a>) on the testimony of Samuel, a medical doctor, defines a <i>trometa</i> egg as a diagnostic egg made extra hard and indigestible by alternatingly cooking and freezing until it is small and hard, can be swallowed whole, and, when excreted, examined for traces of blood from stomach or intestines.</i> and ash-gourd<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Explained in the Halakhah.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who makes a vow to abstain from cooked food,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph and the next are also in <i>Erubin</i> 3:1 (20d 1. 21) and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:9:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.9.2-9"><i>Nazir</i> 6:11</a> (55c 1. 18). The argument of this paragraph is also in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 49a" href="/Nedarim.49a">Babli, 49a</a>, where the opinion of R. Joḥanan is declared to be that of the Tanna of the Mishnah.</i> A Mishnah states that scalding is called cooking, as we have stated: “If he cooked the well-being offering or scalded it<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.9.1">Mishnah <i>Nazir</i> 6:11</a>. This refers to the ram which is the well-being offering of the <i>nazir</i> at the end of his period of sanctification. The verse, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:18" href="/Numbers.6.18"><i>Num</i>. 6:18</a>, requires the Cohen to take a limb “from the cooked ram”. The Mishnah accepts שָׁלוּק as cooked. Since the meat of the ram must be eaten immediately, it is clear that שלק cannot mean “to preserve by prolonged cooking”.</i>.” A verse [states] that “roasted” is called “cooked”<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 35:13" href="/II_Chronicles.35.13"><i>2Chr</i>. 35:13</a>: “They cooked the <i>pesaḥ</i> in the fire as is the rule”; i. e., (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:9" href="/Exodus.12.9"><i>Ex</i>. 12:9</a>) “roasted in fire”. Cooked would have been “over the fire”.</i>: “They cooked the <i>pesaḥ</i>” etc. If you say, against the rules, Rebbi Jonah from Bostra said, “as is the rule”. A Mishnah states that scalded is called cooked, and a verse that roasted is called cooked; but did we not state: “One who makes a vow to abstain from cooked food is permitted roasted and scalded food”? Rebbi Joḥanan said, in matters of vows one follows common usage. Rebbi Joshia said, in matters of vows one follows biblical usage<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 49a" href="/Nedarim.49a">Babli, 49a</a>, states that R. Joshia disagrees with the Mishnah and forbids both roasted and preserved.</i>. What is the difference between them? ‘A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not taste wine on Tabernacles.’ In the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan he is forbidden on the last day of the holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The eighth day of the fall festival which in popular consciousness is part of Tabernacles but in biblical terminology (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 29:35" href="/Numbers.29.35"><i>Num</i>. 29:35</a>) is a separate holiday.</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Joshia, is he permitted? Also Rebbi Joshia agrees that he is prohibited. Rebbi Joshia said it only for restrictions<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">He requires that both in biblical Hebrew and in the vernacular the terms should denote different things.</i>.
Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, Rebbi Joḥanan ate bake-meats<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 1:3:14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.1.3.14"><i>Ḥallah</i> 1:5, Note 164</a>; baked goods, not bread, made in the market. An exaggerated parallel to this is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 44a" href="/Berakhot.44a">Babli, <i>Berakhot</i> 44a</a>.</i> and said, I did not taste food on that day. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Erubin</i> 3:1. That Mishnah contradicts R. Joḥanan.</i>: “He who made a vow not to eat food is permitted water and salt”? Explain it following Rebbi Joshia, who said, in matters of vows one follows biblical usage. And from where that everything is called food? Rebbi Aḥa bar Ulla said<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 45:23" href="/Genesis.45.23"><i>Gen</i>. 45:23</a>; the verse implies that “food” is different from grain and bread. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 35b" href="/Berakhot.35b">Babli, <i>Berakhot</i> 35b</a>, disagrees and defines as מזון any food made from grain.</i>: “And ten female donkeys carrying grain, bread, and food.” Why does the verse say, “and food”? From here that everything is called food.
May one who made a vow not to have anything cooked be permitted smoked? May he be permitted fried? May he be permitted anything cooked in the hot springs of Tiberias<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">If a pot is immersed in a hot spring, is the food cooked by human action? If it is not by human action, it cannot be sanctioned.</i>? The rabbis of Caesarea asked: Is anything smoked forbidden because of Gentile cooking<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">In the opinion of the Yerushalmi [<i>Šabbat</i> 1 (3c 1. 65), <i>Avodah zarah</i> 2:9 (41d 1. 35)] it is a biblical prohibition, based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 2:28" href="/Deuteronomy.2.28"><i>Deut</i>. 2:28</a> to eat Gentile food which is in a state different from raw and not prepared with the help of a Jew. In the opinion of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 37b-38a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.37b-38a">Babli, <i>Avodah zarah</i> 37b/38a</a>, this is a purely pharisaic-rabbinic restriction for which the verse serves as a crutch. The question now is whether hanging anything in a chimney over a smoky fire which was not lit for the purpose of cooking would be considered cooking anyhow.</i>? Is it forbidden because of cooking on the Sabbath? Is it forbidden because of meat and milk? Does it cause <i>ṭevel</i> for tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">This is always the same question. Cooking is forbidden on the Sabbath (Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 7:2) parallel to baking. Since baking cannot be replaced by smoking, there is a question. Similarly, it is forbidden to cook milk and meat together (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:19" href="/Exodus.23.19"><i>Ex</i>. 23:19</a>; 34:26; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:21" href="/Deuteronomy.14.21"><i>Deut</i>. 14:21</a>).<br>Cooking untithed food makes the food forbidden until heave and tithes have been taken. {While the Mishnah (<i>Ma‘serot</i> 4:1) only states that preserving by prolonged cooking creates the obligation, the formulation in the Halakhah is “fire creates the obligation”. In the first formulation, preserving by smoking might be excluded, in the second it is included.) The questions are unanswered since in matters of biblical prohibitions a doubt must be resolved in a restrictive decision.</i>? If somebody made a vow not to have anything smoked, is he permitted cooked? Rebbi Abba, Rav Jehudah in the name of the House of Rav Assi: <i>Ḥabiṣ</i><sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic خَبِيص “a delicacy made from dates, cream, and starch”.</i> is unproblematic as Gentile cooking and is acceptable as <i>eruv tavšīlīn</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rav Huna: Anything which can be eaten raw is unproblematic as Gentile cooking and is acceptable as <i>eruv tavšīlīn</i><sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 38a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.38a">Babli, <i>Avodah zarah</i> 38a</a>, in the name of Rav, Rav Huna’s teacher.</i>.
Reading of Rashba (<i>Novellae ad</i> 49a)<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">This is a coherent text which makes sense. However, the substitution of צלי for צלוי indicates that the text was copied without attention to the intricacies of Yerushalmi spelling. The deviations from the ms. text are indicated by a change of typeface. <br>הַנּוֹדֵר מִן הַמְבוּשָּׁל מָהוּ שֶׁיְּהֵא מוּתָּר בְּעָבֶה. נִישְׁמְעִינָהּ מִן הָדָא. אָסוּר בְמַעֲשֵׂה קְדֵירָה רַךְ וּמוּתָּר בְּעָבֶה. מָה אִם תַּבְשִׁיל שֶׁהוּא <i>אָסוּר בִּצְלִי</i> וּבְשָׁלוּק מוּתָּר <i>בְּמַעֲשֵׂה קְדֵרָה עָבֶה</i>. מְבוּשָּׁל שֶׁהוּא מוּתָּר <i>בִּצְלִי</i> וּבְשָׁלוּק אֵינוֹ דִין שֶׁיְּהֵא מוּתָּר בְּעָבֶה. וְיֵשׁ קַל וַחוֹמֶר בִּנְדָרִים. אֶלָּא כֵינִי. תַּבְשִׁיל שֶׁ<i>הוּא בִּצְלִי</i> וּבְשָׁלוּק מוּתָּר בְּעָבֶה. וּמְבוּשָּׁל שֶׁהוּא מוּתָּר <i>בִּצְלִי</i> וּבְשָׁלוּק מוּתָּר בְּעָבֶה.</i>:<br><sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The translation follows the Rashba text.</i> Is one who makes a vow to abstain from cooked food permitted a thick<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">If there is no more visible moisture and it can be eaten without bread (or today, without a fork).</i> dish? Let us hear from the following: “He is forbidden soft dishes<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Those which contain visible moisture.</i> and permitted thick ones<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">If there is no more visible moisture and it can be eaten without bread (or today, without a fork).</i>.” Since a dish which would be forbidden roasted or scalded is permitted as a thick dish, regarding “cooked food” which permits roasted and scalded, is it not logical that he be permitted the thick dish? Are there arguments <i>de minore ad majus</i> in matters of vows<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">According to R. Joḥanan, the meaning of vows can be ascertained only by analysis of the vernacular which is not subject to logical rules.</i>? But it must be so: Since a dish which is forbidden roasted or scalded is permitted as a thick dish, the cooked which is permitted roasted or scalded is permitted as a thick dish.
It was stated: He is forbidden soft cakes<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἴτριον, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 1:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.1.4.2">Ḥallah 1, Note 178</a>. The dough must still be so soft that it must be put into a pitta to be eaten.</i>, since the physician frequently eats his bread with them. Rav Ḥisda said, he is forbidden a roasted<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">This usage of גלגל “to roll, make round” is documented in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Oktzin 2:6" href="/Mishnah_Oktzin.2.6">Mishnah (<i>Uqeṣin</i> 2:6</a>) and Yerushalmi <i>Šabbat</i> 3:3 (6a 1. 18). The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 49a" href="/Nedarim.49a">Babli, 49a</a>, has a general formulation by Abai, a student of Rav Ḥisda: Anything eaten with bread is called a dish.</i> egg since a sick person frequently eats his bread with them.
“He is permitted a soft boiled egg,” a soft one<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Galen VI, 769 distinguishes kinds of eggs: ἑφθά “hard boiled”, τρομητά “trembling”, i. e. with solid egg white but soft yolk, which tremble if shelled, ῥοφητά “soft”, which can be sipped. In contrast to the Yerushalmi which identifies the last two kinds, the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 50b" href="/Nedarim.50b">Babli (50b</a>) on the testimony of Samuel, a medical doctor, defines a <i>trometa</i> egg as a diagnostic egg made extra hard and indigestible by alternatingly cooking and freezing until it is small and hard, can be swallowed whole, and, when excreted, examined for traces of blood from stomach or intestines.</i>’<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">It must be fluid if it cannot be eaten in a pitta, cf. the preceding Note.</i>. “And ash-gourd,” <sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">This is from <i>Kilaim</i> 1, end of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 6:2:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.6.2.2-3">Halakhah 2</a>, formulated in a slightly different way.</i> Rebbi Ḥinenah said, it is a kind of bitter gourd and they sweeten it in hot ashes.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody makes a vow to forbid himself anything made in a pot, he is forbidden only what is made in its heat<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The expression “made in a pot” is restricted to cereals; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 37a" href="/Berakhot.37a">Babli <i>Berakhot</i> 37a</a>.</i>. If he said, a <i>qônām</i> that I shall not taste anything going into the pot, he is forbidden anything that may be cooked in any pot<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Including vegetables and meat.</i>.<br>‘From the pickled’, he is forbidden only pickled vegetables<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Normally “pickling” only vegetables. In the second sentence, anything pickled is forbidden, whether it is of the kind usually covered by the verb or not. The other Mishnaiot are treated similarly.</i>. ‘That I shall not taste anything pickled’, he is forbidden everything pickled.<br>‘From the scalded’ he is forbidden only scalded meat, the words of Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babylonian Mishnah, this is missing and the entire Mishnah is anonymous. It is found in הילכות הרא״ש.</i>. ‘That I shall not taste anything scalded’, he is forbidden everything scalded.<br>‘From the roast’ he is forbidden only roasted meat, the words of Rebbi Jehudah. ‘That I shall not taste anything roasted’, he is forbidden everything roasted.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody makes a vow to forbid himself anything made in a pot,” etc. What is made in its heat? For example split spelt, porridge, barley gruel<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">For these three kinds of cereal, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 2:1:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.2.1.11"><i>Soṭa 2</i>, Notes 47–49</a>.</i>, farina, rice, groats<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">This translation is tentative; from זרד “making branches”.</i> and mashed cereal<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">This meaning is well established in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 47a" href="/Yoma.47a">Babli, <i>Yoma</i>47a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 41b" href="/Nedarim.41b"><i>Nedarim</i> 41b</a>. According to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rashi on Berakhot 37a" href="/Rashi_on_Berakhot.37a">Rashi (to <i>Berakhot</i> 37a</a>), made from cereal split into many small pieces.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 51a" href="/Nedarim.51a">Babli, 51a</a>.</i>[One who forbids himself] “anything going into the pot, is forbidden what goes into the frying pan<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Greek λοπάς; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 8:7:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.8.7.6"><i>Peah</i> 8:8, Note 128</a>.</i>. From anything going into the frying pan is permitted what goes into the pot.”<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 3:2" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).3.2">Tosephta 3:2</a>.</i> For there are things going into the frying pan which do not go into the pot. What do you have? For example, greasy<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">This translation is very tentative; it is based on Arabic خِلم “sheep’s fat”. It is difficult to read one word החלמן since חֲלִימָה is a plant (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 6:1:26" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.6.1.26"><i>Berakhot</i> 6:1, Note 112</a>) and חֶלְמוֹן is egg yolk.</i> fish. “One who makes a vow to forbid to himself what is baked in the oven, is forbidden only bread. From what is prepared in the oven, anything is forbiddden that may be made in an oven”<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 3:2" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).3.2">Tosephta 3:2</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> ‘From the salted’ he is forbidden only salted fish. ‘That I shall not taste anything salted’, he is forbidden everything salted.<br>‘That I shall not taste fish, fishes’, he is forbidden them either large or small, either salted or unsalted, either raw or cooked. But he is permitted fish cake<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">“Ground finely”.<br>In the opinion of <i>Arukh</i>(<i>s. v</i>. טרית) and Rashi in his commentary to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 6a" href="/Megillah.6a"><i>Megillah</i> 6a</a> the reference is to <i>triton</i>, a kind of tuna fish. This explanation is accepted by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ran on Nedarim 51b" href="/Ran_on_Nedarim.51b">Ran (commentary to <i>Nedarim</i> 51b</a>) who explains: “A large fish ground into fine cakes”. But in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 35b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.35b"><i>Avodah zarah</i> 35b</a>, Rashi explains “small salted fish” which Lewysohn explains as Greek θρίσσα (Attic θρίττα). According to the Halakhah, both explanations might be correct, depending on a person’s dialect. {טְרִית, if Greek, however, might be expected to be derived from a word beginning with <i>tau</i>, rather than <i>theta</i>. Compare Latin <i>tritus</i>, “rubbing, wearing”, from <i>tero</i> “rub to pieces” forming a double expression with Hebrew טְרוּפָה “ground, finely ground with “fish” understood (E. G.). For an example of a Greek-Hebrew double expression cf. E. and H. Guggenheimer, <i>Notes on the Talmudic Vocabulary 5</i>, לשוננו לז (1973), 23–26 (in Hebrew).}<br>A person vowing not to eat fish intends only food recognizable as such.</i> and fish fluid<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Fluid extracted from fish by pressing.</i>. If somebody vows not to eat anchovy<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic صَحْنَى “small salted fish”.</i> he is forbidden fish cake and permitted fish fluid and brine<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>muries</i>, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 1:3:16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.1.3.16"><i>Demay</i> 1:3, Note 156</a>.</i>. If somebody vows not to eat fish cake he is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli: “forbidden”. The Mishnah mss. in the Maimonides tradition follow the Yerushalmi.</i> fish fluid and brine.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “ ‘From the salted’ he is forbidden only salted fish,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">This is a copy from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 2:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.2.4.2">Chapter 2:4, Notes 59–63</a>.</i> They wanted to say, salted for preservation, not short-term salted. Rebbi Yudan said, since we have stated: “this is for me like salted meat, like libation wine,” this imples that short-term salted is called “salted”. What is short-term salted? As it was stated: “He puts the limbs on the salt and turns them over.” Rebbi Abba Mari said, there is a difference, for if he left them there, they would become salted meat. But which text says it? As Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Zabda said, “one who takes olives from the vat dips each single one in salt and eats it.” This implies that short-term salted is called “salted”.
It was stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">A different version in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 51b" href="/Nedarim.51b">Babli, 51b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 3:5" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).3.5">Tosephta 3:5</a>.</i> Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar says: If he said, ‘a <i>qônām</i> that I shall not taste a fish’, he is forbidden large ones and permitted small ones. ‘Fishes I shall not taste,’ he is forbidden large and small ones. ‘Fish<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">The collective for fish, meaning fish that appear in large swarms.</i> I shall not taste,’ he is forbidden only sardines<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Greek χαλκίς, -ίδος “pilchard, sardine”.</i>. What is small and what is large? This is as Rebbi Ze‘ira said, from any fish less than a <i>litra</i><sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">A Roman <i>libra</i>, 345 g.</i> which I eat, I am tasting sardines. Here, it is the same<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">A fish is small if it weighs less than a Roman 1b.</i>.
“He is permitted fish cake”; therefore not if it is not ground. Rebbi Jeremiah said, Ze‘ira<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Almost certainly, the scribe forgot the title “Rebbi”.</i> asked: Does this not disagree with Rebbi Joḥanan who said, in matters of vows one follows common usage<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 6:1:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.6.1.2-7">Halakhah 1</a>, first paragraph. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 30b" href="/Nedarim.30b">Babli 30b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 49a" href="/Nedarim.49a">49a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 51b" href="/Nedarim.51b">51b</a>, and 5 times in other tractates.</i> It is not the way of people that if a man says to his neighbor, sell me a fish, that he should sell him a sardine<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Unspecified “fish” does not mean sardine.</i>. Rebbi Abin understood it from the last clause, “if somebody vows not to eat fish cake he is permitted fish fluid and brine<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that the quote should be the sentence before the last, forbidding fish cake for the person who vows not to eat anchovy.</i>,” therefore not if it is not ground? Rebbi [Jeremiah]<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">In analogy to the first version, this is the name forgotten by the scribe.</i> said, Rebbi Ze‘ira asked: Does this not disagree with Rebbi Joḥanan who said, in matters of vows one follows common usage. It is not the way of people that if a man says to his neighbor, sell me a טְרִי, that he sells him a צַחְנָא. There<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonia. It is asserted that there one calls צחנא what in Galilee is called טרית. The Tanna of the Mishnah is Babylonian.</i>, they call צַחְנָה טְרִית.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody vows not to drink milk, he is permitted curd<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">This is the definition of the Yerushalmi. The definition of Rashi and the Arukh is: the serum remaining when curd is pressed into cheese.</i> but Rebbi Yose forbids<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that curd is colloidal milk, not cheese. But if somebody restricts his vow to curd, he certainly excludes fluid milk.</i>. But from curd, he is permitted milk. Abba Shaul says, if he vows not to have cheese, it is forbidden to him whether salted or unsalted<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Even though most cheese is salted.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody vows not to drink milk, he is permitted curd,” etc. What is curd? Curdled milk. What is the reason of Rebbi Yose? The name of its father is called over it<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Since its name still contains the word “milk”.</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Yose, is one who vows not to taste wine permitted cooked wine<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Which has lost all its alcohol by cooking. Since in the Aramaic vernacular it still is called wine it is forbidden.</i>? Cooked wine”.
<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is from <i>Ŝevi‘it</i>6:3, Notes 116–125; quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 57b-58a" href="/Nedarim.57b-58a">Babli 57b/58a</a>.</i>“This is the rule Rebbi Simeon declared in the name of Rebbi Joshua: For everything that may become permitted through some action, such as <i>ṭevel</i>, Second Tithe, donations to the Temple, and “new grain”, the Sages did not fix any limits, but a kind with its own is forbidden in the minutest amount, a kind with a different kind if it can be tasted. But for everything that cannot become permitted through any action, such as heave, <i>ḥallah, orlah</i>, and <i>kilaim</i> in a vineyard, the Sages did fix as limit both a kind with itself or with a different kind if it can be tasted.” How do you treat vows<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">If somebody vowed not to eat something which then became mixed with permitted food and is no longer recognizable. Is the mixture forbidden if it contains the most minute amount of forbidden material or does it become permitted if the forbidden food no langer can be tasted?</i>? As referring to something that can become permitted or to something that cannot become permitted? It seems reasonable that we treat them as referring to things that can become permitted, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 7:7:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.7.7.2-6"><i>Ketubot</i> 7:9</a>, in a discussion of the difference between the dissolution of a vow by a rabbi for cause compared to that by the father of the vow of his not quite adult daughter. One would have expected the text to read דתני as appropriate for a <i>baraita</i>, not דתנינן תמן used for a Mishnah.</i> “for the Elder uproots the vow from the start.” They said, he only uproots for the future<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">While the rabbi might annul the vow, he cannot annul the guilt incurred by violation of the vow before it was annulled.</i>. Certainly a Mishnah treats it as referring to something that cannot become permitted, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 9:2:1-2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.9.2.1-2"><i>Terumot</i> 9:4, Notes 57–64</a>; quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 60a" href="/Nedarim.60a">Babli, 60a</a>.</i>: “The growth from heave is heave; the growth from their growth is profane. But the growths from <i>ṭevel</i>, First Tithe, aftergrowth of the Sabbatical, heaves from outside the Land, <i>dema‘</i>, and First Fruits are profane as are growths from their growths. Growths from dedicated [seeds] and Second Tithe become profane; one redeems them corresponding to the time of sowing.” We have stated on this: When has this been said? Anything whose seeds disappear. But in produce whose seeds do not disappear, the growth from their growth is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Since the growth of the growth from seeds dedicated by a vow remains forbidden, a vow makes it something which admits of an act (a request for annulment) that makes it permitted.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ŝevi‘it</i> 6:3, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 6:8:13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.6.8.13">Note 126</a>. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 5:6:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.5.6.4"><i>Kilaim</i> 5:7, Note 76</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 7:3:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.7.3.6"><i>Terumot</i> 7:7, Note 122</a>; ‘<i>Orlah</i>1:1, Note 29; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 57b" href="/Nedarim.57b">Babli <i>Nedarim</i> 57b</a>).</i> There is a restriction on growth, as Rebbi Zeïra said in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: An onion of <i>kilaim</i> in a vineyard, taken out of the ground and replanted, stays forbidden even if it grows enormously because growth of something forbidden cannot neutralize the prohibition. But another Mishnah treats it as something that can become permitted, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 6:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.6.5.2"><i>Nedarim</i> 6:9</a>.</i>: “For if somebody vowed not to have a certain thing and it became mixed with something else, if it can be tasted it is forbidden.” This explains that it was mixed with another kind, following the rules of something that can become permitted.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody vows not to eat meat, he is permitted clear bouillon and coagulated fibers<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">The solid material which accumulates on the wall of the pot at the water’s edge, which usually is scrubbed off as waste.</i>, but Rebbi Jehudah forbids. Rebbi Jehudah said, it happened that Rebbi Ṭarphon forbade to me eggs that were cooked in it. They said to him, that is correct; when? If he would say, that piece of meat [is forbidden] to me<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">If somebody forbids for himself meat in general, food is judged by categories and eggs are not meat. But if somebody forbids a particular piece of meat, that piece is for him as if it were not kosher and it follows the rules of admixtures of forbidden ingredients as detailed in <i>Terumot</i> Chapter 10; or, as explained in the Halakhah, he forbids himself any usufruct, i. e., any taste of the forbidden food.</i>. In truth, if somebody forbids himself something by a vow and it became mixed with something else, if it can be tasted it is forbidden<br>If somebody vows not to drink wine he is permitted a dish prepared with wine. If he said, a <i>qônām</i> that I shall not taste this wine, if it fell into a dish and it can be tasted, that is forbidden.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody vows not to eat meat,” etc. Rebbi Hila said: The moment he said “this one”, he forbade for himself it and its usufruct.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody vows not to eat grapes, he is permitted wine; not to eat olives, he is permitted oil. If he said, a <i>qônām</i> that I shall not taste these olives or grapes, he is forbidden them and anything coming from them<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">These rules are all examples of the principle expounded in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 6:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.6.5.2">Halakhah 9</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody vows not to eat grapes,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 53a" href="/Nedarim.53a">Babli 53a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 3:3" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).3.3">Tosephta 3:3</a>.</i>: Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar said, a <i>qônām</i> for anything which usually is eaten and of which some derivative is eaten; if he forbade the thing to himself by a vow, the derivative is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">In Babli and Tosephta: Forbidden.</i>. What is an example? E. g., grapes and olives. And anything which usually is eaten but no derivative of which is eaten; if he forbade the thing to himself by a vow, the derivative is permitted. What is an example? E. g., mulberries. And anything which usually is not eaten and but a derivative is eaten; if he forbade the thing to himself by a vow, he intended the derivative. What is an example? E. g., garden seeds that are not eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 1:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.1.1.5"><i>Kilaim</i> 1:1, Note 21</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody vows not to eat dates, he is permitted date honey; from winter grapes<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">They are sour and can only be used for making vinegar.</i>, he is permitted winter grape vinegar. Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra says, if he vowed to forbid to himself anything which is called by the name of its derivative<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">For example dates which in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 8:8" href="/Deuteronomy.8.8"><i>Deut.</i> 8:8</a> are called “honey”. In the same verse, olives are referred to as “oil olives”.</i>, he also is forbidden the derivative, but the Sages permit it.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody vows not to eat dates,” etc. What is the reason of Rebbi Yose? The name of its father is called over it<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Since its name still contains the word “milk”.</i>. What is the reason of Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra? The name of its descendant is called over it. It is reasonable that Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra should agree with Rebbi Yose but Rebbi Yose would not agree with Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra. Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra should agree with Rebbi Yose: The name of its descendant, so certainly also the name of its father. Rebbi Yose would not agree with Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra: He says only, the name of its father. This implies: not the name of its descendant.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody vows not to use wine, he is permitted apple wine. Not oil, he is permitted sesame oil. Not honey, he is permitted date honey. Not vinegar, he is permitted winter grape vinegar. Not leeks, he is permitted field leeks<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">For the relationship between כרשין, <i>allium porrum,</i> and κεφαλωτόν, <i>allium capitatum,</i> cf. <i>Kilaim</i> 1:2, Notes 42,56.</i>. Of vegetables, he is permitted field vegetables<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Vegetables grown in extensive cultivation.</i>, because that is an accompanying name<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">In all examples of the Mishnah, the simple name does not cover the composite name as product of trade. For example, if somebody buys “wine”, the seller cannot fulfill his contract by delivering apple wine. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 53a" href="/Nedarim.53a">Babli, 53a</a>, points out that in Babylonia no olive trees grow and a standard contract for “oil” there is for delivery of sesame oil; there, a vow of abstention from oil permits the use of olive oil.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody vows not to use wine,” etc. The Mishnah speaks of a place where one does not call field leeks leeks. But not at a place where one calls field leeks leeks. Just in that case it is needed<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">The case of leeks is different from the other cases in the Mishnah since it is not question of a composite name but of Greek vs. Hebrew name. Therefore, at a place where <i>allium capitatum</i> is never called כרשין the assertion of the Mishnah is trivial and does not have to be stated. The Mishnah is only needed when in common speech Hebrew and Greek expressions are used interchangeably. Since this would not extend to commercial contracts, the rule of the Mishnah stands.</i>, even a place where one calls field leeks leeks: “Not leeks, he is permitted field leeks.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is from <i>Ševi‘it</i> 6:4, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 6:8:15-9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.6.8.15-9.1">Notes 140–144</a>. A different version is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 53" href="/Nedarim.53">Babli 53a/b</a>.</i>“Not vegetables, he is permitted field vegetables because this is an accompanying name.” We have stated on that: “He who makes a vow to abstain from vegetables in the Sabbatical is also forbidden field vegetables.” Rebbi Crispus stated the reason in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina ben Gamliel: That means, as long as Rebbi did not permit to import vegetables into the Land. But since Rebbi permitted to import into the Land there is no difference between the Sabbatical and the remaining years of the Sabbatical cycle.
