Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina says, endives are important enough to become impure as food in the Seventh year. That means, as long as Rebbi did not permit to import vegetables into the Land. But since Rebbi permitted to import vegetables into the Land there is no difference between the Sabbatical and the remaining years of the Sabbatical cycle.
<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the Halakhah, the text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 1:2:2-35" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.1.2.2-35"><i>Sanhedrin</i>1:2</a> (18d 1. 28). The tannaïtic statements are from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sanhedrin 2:5-7" href="/Tosefta_Sanhedrin.2.5-7">Tosephta <i>Sanhedrin</i> 2:9–13</a>. The entire piece is inserted here since this first paragraph refers to the preceding one. For this and the following paragraphs the reference in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 12a" href="/Sanhedrin.12a">Babli is <i>Sanhedrin</i> 12a</a>. In Babli sources, before Rabban Gamliel the observant never ate imported vegetables since those might still carry lumps of impure foreign soil.</i> It was stated: “One intercalates for a year neither in a Sabbatical nor in the year after the Sabbatical; but if they intercalated it is intercalated<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">The lunar/solar calendar of rabbinic Judaism requires 7 additional lunar months in a 19 year period. Before the publication of the computed calendar by the fifth generation Amora R. Yose, it was the privilege of the Synhedrion to declare the intercalation (which had to be the month before the spring equinox). One tried not to extend the Sabbatical during which agricultural work was forbidden. In the year following the Sabbatical, the use of new grain was forbidden until the first sheaf of newly cut barley was presented in the Temple during the festival of unleavened bread (the 16th of Nisan in Pharisaic theory, the Sunday of the festival in Boethusian-Sadducee, and the 22nd of Nisan in Jubilee-Sadducee theory), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:14" href="/Leviticus.23.14"><i>Lev.</i> 23:14</a>; Philo <i>The Special Laws</i> II, 162 ff. Therefore, one should try to have Nisan as early as possible in such a year.</i>.” Rebbi Ze‘ira in the name of Rebbi Abbahu said: That means, as long as Rebbi did not permit to buy vegetables for importation into the Land. But since Rebbi permitted to buy vegetables for importation into the Land there is no difference between the Sabbatical and the remaining years of the Sabbatical cycle<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, the calendar computation disregards the Sabbatical. {The calendar computation is explained in the Appendix to the author’s edition of <i>Seder Olam,</i> Northvale NJ 1998.}</i>.
“Rebbi Meïr used to teach: It says, ‘A man came from Baal-Shalishah and brought to the man of God bread from first grain, etc<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Kings 4:42" href="/II_Kings.4.42"><i>2K.</i> 4:42</a>. He brought 20 barley-grain breads. Baal-Shalisha seems to have been near Jericho.</i>.’ At no place in the Land of Israel does produce ripen earlier than at Baal-Shalishah, and only that kind ripened early.” ‘He brought to the man of God,’ when did he bring it? After the <i>‘Omer</i> ceremony<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">The presentation of new barley, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 6:8:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.6.8.4">Note 84</a>.</i>, since it is said: ‘Give to the people to eat.’ “But since the year was in need of an intercalation, why did Elisha not intercalate? This teaches you that the year was one of famine and everybody was jumping to the threshing floors<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">All grain was sold directly from the threshing floor because of its scarcity. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 11b-12a" href="/Sanhedrin.11b-12a">Babli, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 11b/12a</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sanhedrin 2:5" href="/Tosefta_Sanhedrin.2.5">Tosephta <i>Sanhedrin</i> 2:9</a> state outright that “one does not intercalate in a year of famine.” A minority of sources in the Babli attributes the argument to Rebbi.</i>.”
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sanhedrin 2:6" href="/Tosefta_Sanhedrin.2.6">Tosephta <i>Sanhedrin</i> 2:10–11</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 12a" href="/Sanhedrin.12a">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i> 12a/b</a>. Instead of the hapax <i>hip‘ll</i>הֶעֱשִׂי לְצִיבּוּר (confirmed by the Tosephta), the parallel in <i>Sanhedrin</i> has standard rabbinic <i>pi‘el</i> עִישֶּׂה “he forced”, which because of the disappearance of gutturals appears in the Babli as הֵשִׂיא “counseled” (which is a correct reference to <a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 30:2" href="/II_Chronicles.30.2"><i>2Chr.</i> 30:2</a>).</i>: “One does not intercalate for a year because of impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">One does not intercalate a 13th month to give people time to purify themselves in time for the <i>pesaḥ</i> sacrifice on the 14th of the first month.</i>; Rebbi Jehudah says, one does, since we find that Hezekia intercalated because of impurity, as it was said<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 30:18" href="/II_Chronicles.30.18"><i>2Chr.</i> 30:18</a>: Most of the people from the former Northern Kingdom were not pure (in the first month) so they ate the <i>pesaḥ</i> in the second month, not as is written.</i>: ‘For most of the people, etc.’ Rebbi Simeon said, even though they intercalated in Nisan, only Adar was intercalated<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon holds that the biblical reference in <a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 30" href="/II_Chronicles.30"><i>2Chr.</i> 30</a>, that after the fall of Samaria Hezekia celebrated the festival in the second month is really incorrect but that he decided in Nisan that more time was needed to prepare for the sacrifices and, what is not permitted by the calendar rules, retroactively turned Nisan into Second Adar and, therefore, the second month into the first. He prayed for divine indulgence for this breach of the rules.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Jehudah said in the name of Rebbi Simeon, Hezekia forced the multitude to celebrate the Second Passover<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">The substitute festival for people absent or impure in the first month, available for individuals only, not for a majority of the people (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 9:9-14" href="/Numbers.9.9-14"><i>Num.</i>9:9–14</a>).</i>.” Some Tannaïm state: One intercalates for the year because of impurity. Some Tannaïm state: One does not intercalate. He who says, one does not intercalate, from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 30:18" href="/II_Chronicles.30.18"><i>2Chr.</i> 30:18</a>: Most of the people from the former Northern Kingdom were not pure (in the first month) so they ate the <i>pesaḥ</i> in the second month, not as is written.</i>: “For they ate the <i>pesaḥ</i> not as it was written.” How does the one who says one does intercalate, explain “for they ate the <i>pesaḥ”</i>? They intercalated in Nisan but only Adar was intercalated. This follows what Rebbi Simon bar Zavdi said, they found the skull of Oman the Jebusite under the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">This explanation disagrees with R. Simeon ben Jehudah. They moved the month because the Temple was not usable in Nisan since the altar was found to be a “tent” over human bones and thus impure. The text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 5:2:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.5.2.9"><i>Soṭah</i> 5:2, Note 90</a>.</i>.
It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 30:19" href="/II_Chronicles.30.19"><i>2Chr.</i> 30:19</a>: “With all his heart he prepared himself to seek God, the Eternal, his fathers’ God, except for Temple purity.” Since one was discussing Hezekia, some related verses are explained.</i>: “With all his heart he prepared himself to seek God, his fathers’ [God], etc.” Rebbi Simon bar Zavdi and Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman. One of them said, with all he did to purity the Temple, he did not fully establish the purity of the Temple. The other said, with all the good works he did, he did not fully do his duty for the purity of the Temple. It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 29:17" href="/II_Chronicles.29.17"><i>2Chr.</i> 29:17</a>.</i>: “They started on the first of the first month.” And it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 29:18" href="/II_Chronicles.29.18"><i>2Chr.</i> 29:18</a>. They needed 7 days to cleanse the Temple courtyards and 8 for the Temple building.</i>: “On the sixteenth of the month they finished. Could they not have eliminated all idolatry from there in one day? Rebbi Idi said, because of Chaldean idols which were engraved.
<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>baraita</i> in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 56a" href="/Pesachim.56a">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 56a</a>; quoted <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 10b" href="/Berakhot.10b"><i>Berakhot</i> 10b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 47a" href="/Sanhedrin.47a"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 47a</a>; in most Mishnah mss. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 4:9:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.4.9.2-9"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 4:9</a>.</i> Six things did Ezekiah, the king of Judea, do. With three they<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">The rabbinic authorities of the Mishnaic period.</i> agreed, with three they did not agree. He dragged his father’s bones and they agreed<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">He buried him in an undignified way to atone for his bad ways. The basis is the note in <a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 28:27" href="/II_Chronicles.28.27"><i>2Chr.</i> 28:27</a> that Aḥaz was not buried in the kings’ graves, contradicting <a class="refLink" data-ref="II Kings 16:20" href="/II_Kings.16.20"><i>2K.</i> 16:20</a>.</i>. He smashed the bronze snake and they agreed<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Kings 18:4" href="/II_Kings.18.4"><i>2K.</i> 18:4</a>.</i>. He hid the table of medicines and they agreed<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">According to Maimonides (Commentary to <i>Pesaḥim</i> 4:9), a book of magical remedies.</i>. With three they disagreed. He closed the upper Giḥon spring and they disagreed<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that they did not connect <a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 32:30" href="/II_Chronicles.32.30"><i>2Chr.</i> 32:30</a> with <a class="refLink" data-ref="II Kings 20:20" href="/II_Kings.20.20"><i>2K.</i> 20:20</a>.</i>. He cut down the Temple doors<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Kings 18:16" href="/II_Kings.18.16"><i>2K.</i> 18:16</a>.</i> and they disagreed. He intercalated Nisan in Nisan<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the preceding paragraph, following R. Simeon.</i> and they disagreed.
One does not intercalate for a year before New Year’s Day; if they did intercalate it would be invalid. But for an urgent need one may intercalate immediately after New Year’s Day. Nevertheless, only Adar is intercalated. <sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">This and the following paragraphs are found in <i>Śevi‘it</i> 10:2, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 6:3:1-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.6.3.1-4">Notes 41–55</a>. The text is also in <i>Roš Haššanah</i> 3:1, 58c 1. 51, in addition to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 1:2:2-35" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.1.2.2-35"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 1:2</a>.</i> Rebbi says, Nisan never was lengthened. But did we not state: “If the New Moon appeared in time”? If it would appear, it did not appear. Rav said, Tishre was never lengthened. But did we not state: “If the month was long”? If it would be, it never was.
“When they sanctified the year at Usha, on the first day Rebbi Ismael, the son of Rebbi Joḥanan ben Baroqa, led and recited following the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, we did not follow this at Jabneh. On the second day, Rebbi Ḥananiah, the son of Rebbi Yose the Galilean led and recited following the opinion of Rebbi Aqiba. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, this we did follow at Jabneh.” But does this not mean that they sanctified it on the first and the second day? Rebbi Zeїra in the name of Rav Ḥisda: That year was disorganized. What is “the first, the second”? Rebbi Abun in the name of Rav: The first year, the second year<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 6:1:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.6.1.2-7">Halakhah 1</a>, first paragraph. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 30b" href="/Nedarim.30b">Babli 30b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 49a" href="/Nedarim.49a">49a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 51b" href="/Nedarim.51b">51b</a>, and 5 times in other tractates.</i>! But was it not stated: the first day, the second day?
If they sanctified it before its time or after its lengthening, should I assume it was lengthened? The verse says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:2" href="/Leviticus.23.2"><i>Lev</i>. 23:2</a>) “them”, “them”, “these are My holidays.” Before its time is not “My holidays.” Before its time, the 29<sup>th</sup> day, after its lengthening, the 32<sup>nd</sup> day. From where that one intercalates for the year because of the [men of the] diaspora who set out but did not yet arrive? The verse says (<i>Num</i>. 23:44), “Moses spoke about the holidays of the Eternal to the Children of Israel”. Make the holidays so they can be observed by all of Israel. Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman said, only if they had reached the river Euphrates.
“One intercalates for a year only in Judea, but if [a month] was intercalated in Galilee it is validly intercalated. Rebbi Ḥanania from Ono testified that, if it cannot be intercalated in Judea<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">Because of war or persecution, as in the war of Bar Kokhba and its aftermath.</i>, one intercalates in Galilee.<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sanhedrin 2:6-7" href="/Tosefta_Sanhedrin.2.6-7">Tosephta <i>Sanhedrin</i> 2:13</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 11b" href="/Sanhedrin.11b">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i> 11b</a>.</i>” One does not intercalate a year in Galilee, but if it was intercalated in Galilee it is validly intercalated. One does not intercalate a year outside the Land; if it was intercalated outside the Land it is not validly intercalated if it was possible to intercalate in the Land of Israel; but if one cannot intercalate in the Land of Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">Because of war or persecution, as in the war of Bar Kokhba and its aftermath.</i>, one intercalates outside the Land.
Jeremiah intercalated outside the Land. Ezechiel intercalated outside the Land. Baruch intercalated outside the Land<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">There is no biblical basis for these statements which are based on the hypothesis that the biblical calendar is identical with the rabbinical. In Talmudic tradition, Baruch ben Neriah did not follow Jeremiah to Egypt but went to Babylonia where he founded the first Babylonian academy.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 63" href="/Berakhot.63">Babli <i>Berakhot</i> 63a/b</a>; some details there seem to be more historical.</i>Ḥanania the nephew of Rebbi Joshua<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">His name and that of his father was Ḥananiah ben Ḥananiah, meaning that both were posthumous sons, a very unlucky reference which can be avoided by circumlocution. He supported his uncle’s determined opposition to any revolt against Rome and it seems that he left Palestine at the outbreak of the Bar Kokhba revolt and founded the Academy of Nahardea, later headed by his descendant Samuel.</i> intercalated outside the Land. Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, the identity of the head of the Academy who reestablished the authority of the Palestinian Academy in fixing the calendar is not mentioned. It is to be expected that this was Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel who became head after the Hadrianic decrees were abolished, perhaps by Septimius Severus. If it was Rebbi, Ḥananiah must have been a centenarian.</i> sent him three letters through Rebbi Isaac and Rebbi Nathan<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, two grandsons of priests serving in the Temple. The Tanna R. Nathan (the Babylonian) is to be dated to the time of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel.</i>. In one he wrote, to His holiness Ḥanania. And in one he wrote, the kid goats you left behind became rams. And in one he wrote, if you do not accept, go to the thistle desert, do slaughter and let Onias sprinkle<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">The blood of the sacrifice. A reference to the (illegitimate) Onias Temple in Egypt.</i>. After the first [letter], he honored them. After the second, he honored them. After the third, he wanted to disgrace them. They said to him, you cannot do that since you already did honor us. Rebbi Isaac rose and quoted, it is written in the Torah: These are the holidays of Ḥanania the nephew of Rebbi Joshua. They said to him, “the holdays of the Eternal<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:4" href="/Leviticus.23.4"><i>Lev</i>. 23:4</a>.</i>”. He said to them, that is with us<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">The correct verse only applies to the followers of the patriarchate in Galilee.</i>. Rebbi Nathan rose and finished: “For from Babylonia will go Torah forth and the Eternal’s word from Nahar-Peqod.” They said to him, “for from Zion will Torah go forth and the Eternal’s word from Jerusalem<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 2:3" href="/Isaiah.2.3"><i>Is</i>. 2:3</a>.</i>”. He said to them, that is with us. He went to complain about them to Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra at Nisibis<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">The representative of the autochthonous Babylonian Academy whose ancestors already were the leaders in Jerusalem 100 years before the destruction of the Temple.</i>. He<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jehudah ben Bathyra.</i> said to him<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Ḥanania.</i>, follow them, follow them. He said to him<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jehudah ben Bathyra.</i>, I do not know whom I left there. Who would tell me that they are wise to the computations as I am? If he<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Ḥanania.</i> had said, they are not wise, they should listen to him. Since they are wise to the computations as he is, he<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Ḥanania.</i> has to listen to them. He<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jehudah ben Bathyra.</i> got up and rode on a horse. Where he reached, he reached. Where he did not reach, they continued following the corrupt [calendar]<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">We do not know what the material difference was in the calendar computations. There is a slight possibility that it was in the rules of moving the date of the New Year, a difference that surfaced 700 years later in the quarrel between Saadya Gaon in Baghdad and the Patriarch Meїr in Jerusalem.</i>.
It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 29:1" href="/Jeremiah.29.1"><i>Jer</i>. 29:1</a>.</i>, “to the outstanding Elders of the diaspora”. The Holy One, praise to Him, said: The Elders of the diaspora are very dear to me. More beloved by me is a small group in the Land of Israel than a great Synhedrion outside the Land. It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="II Kings 24:16" href="/II_Kings.24.16"><i>2K</i>. 24:16</a>. It has been suggested from their association with the soldiers that these were the people trained in the production of weapons.</i>: “The craftsmen and the smiths one thousand,” and you say so? Rebbi Berekhiah in the name of Rebbi Ḥelbo and the rabbis. Rebbi Berekhiah said, one thousand craftsmen and one thousand smiths. But the rabbis say, together one thousand<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Modern Bible commentators follow the rabbis.</i>. Rebbi Berekhiah in the name of Rebbi Ḥelbo said, these are the fellows<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">The people strictly adhering to the rules of ritual purity, cf. <i>Introduction to Tractate Demay</i>, p. 349. If this is the correct interpretation, the question is who are the small group in the Land of Israel. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 88a" href="/Gittin.88a">Babli, <i>Giṭṭin</i> 88a</a>, this is the only opinion mentioned.</i>; but the rabbis say, these are the councilmen<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">Greek βουλευταί, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 1:1:45" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.1.1.45"><i>Peah</i> 1:1, Note 167</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Hoshaia, when he received witnesses at Kallirhoë<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote"><i>En Ṭab, Kallirhoë</i>(probably <i>Ḥamat Gader;</i> cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rosh Hashanah 25a" href="/Rosh_Hashanah.25a">Babli <i>Roš Haššanah</i>25a</a>) was the place chosen in the early Amoraic period to proclaim the days of the New Moon and the intercalation of a month in a year. The witnesses testify that they have seen the new moon.</i>, used to say to them: You should know the importance of the testimony that comes from your mouth, how much rent money depends on your mouths<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">Since rent usually is paid by the month or the year, it makes a difference whether a month has 29 or 30 days, or a year has 12 or 13 months.</i>. Rebbi Abuna said, if it is so, it is even a matter of criminal law. If somebody sleeps with a girl three years and one day old, he is stoned<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">If the girl was engaged to be married (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:23-26" href="/Deuteronomy.22.23-26"><i>Deut</i>. 22:23–26</a>), for which the minimal age of consent is 3 years and a day. The girl, as a minor, cannot be prosecuted. Any seduction of an underage girl is statutory rape, but talmudic law assumes that a girl who was raped at less than 3 years and one day is still a virgin since her hymen is supposed to repair itself. But if she was raped at the age of at least 3 years and one day she is no longer a virgin and cannot demand a virgin’s portion in any marriage settlement.</i>. The Court decided to lengthen<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">Either a month or a year. In the first case, assume the girl was born on the 30th of a certain month. After three years, if the same month has 30 days, she is only 3 years old. But if that month is declared to have only 29 days, the 30th is the first day of her fourth year and any man committing adultery with her is stoned. Similarly, if the girl was born in Nisan then the 3rd birthday is either 12 or 13 months after the 2nd; there is a full month where the decision of the Synhedrion makes a difference in her standing in criminal law. Obviously, the same problems appear with reaching adulthood; the example of the minor was chosen because of the sermon following.</i>, if he sleeps with her he is not stoned. Rebbi Abun said: <sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 57:3" href="/Psalms.57.3"><i>Ps</i>. 57:3</a>.</i>“I am calling to Almighty God, to the God who decides<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">גמר is used for the vote of the judges by which a case is decided. Nature, created by God, acts as if God had voted with the majority of the judges; cf. <i>Midrash Tehillim</i> 57[1].</i> with me.” If a girl is three years and one day old, if the Court decided to lengthen, her hymen repairs itself, otherwise it does not repair itself<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">If the girl was engaged to be married (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:23-26" href="/Deuteronomy.22.23-26"><i>Deut</i>. 22:23–26</a>), for which the minimal age of consent is 3 years and a day. The girl, as a minor, cannot be prosecuted. Any seduction of an underage girl is statutory rape, but talmudic law assumes that a girl who was raped at less than 3 years and one day is still a virgin since her hymen is supposed to repair itself. But if she was raped at the age of at least 3 years and one day she is no longer a virgin and cannot demand a virgin’s portion in any marriage settlement.</i>.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who makes a vow to abstain from cabbage is forbidden cabbage shoot<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἀσπαραγός “shoot of edible vegetable”, used here in the restricted sense of cabbage shoot.</i>, from cabbage shoot he is permitted cabbage<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">The shoot has no leaves yet; from cabbage one eats the leaves but not the shoot</i>. From groats, he is forbidden groat soup but Rebbi Yose permits it; from groat soup he is permitted groats<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">Dry groats.</i>. From soup he is forbidden garlic<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Which was always used to spice soup.</i> but Rebbi Yose permits it; from garlic he is permitted soup.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">In ms. and <i>editio princeps,</i> <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 6:9:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.6.9.2-3">Halakhah 14</a> is the next paragraph.</i>: What is Rebbi Yose’s reason? Is its father’s name called upon it<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Since the name “cabbage” does not appear in <i>asparagos,</i> the vow concerning cabbage cannot extend to <i>as paragos</i>.</i>? In the opinion of Rebbi Yose, if somebody makes a vow to abstain from wine, he is permitted spiced wine<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">Latin condition (<i>vinum</i>), cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 6:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.6.5.2"><i>Berakhot</i> 6:5, Note 157</a>.</i>.
“From cabbage, he is forbidden cabbage shoot,” etc. “From groats, he is forbidden groat soup,” only if most of it is groats. “From soup he is forbidden garlic,” only if most of it is garlic? In that case, you go after the taste, in the other after the main ingredient<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">Spices are forbidden as long as they can be tasted; bland food only as long as it is the main ingredient.</i>.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> From lentils, he is is forbidden <i>ašišim</i>, but Rebbi Yose permits. From <i>ašišim</i> he is permitted lentils.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Yasa went to Rebbi Yose who brought roasted lentils out for him, ground them, formed them into a dough with honey, and fried them. He said to him, these are the <i>ašišim</i> mentioned by the Sages.

Halakhah 11

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> ‘That I shall not taste wheat or wheats: he is forbidden both flour and bread<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">The interpretation of the Mishnah in the Halakhah and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 3:7" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).3.7">Tosephta (3.7</a>) in the name of R. Jehudah is the opposite of that given in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 53b" href="/Nedarim.53b">Babli (53b</a>) in the name of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel. Following R. Jehudah in <i>baraita</i>/Tosephta, חִטָּה is a singular and refers to single kernels for chewing; חִיטִּים as plural refers to material for baking. R. Jehudah in the Mishnah, and Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel in the Babli, hold that the collective חִטָּה means wheat bread but the plural חִטִּים means single kernels to be chewed. Similarly, for them the collective גְּרִיס means porridge, but the plural single kernels to be chewed. According to both Talmudim, in the first part of the Mishnah the person forbade to himself both the singular-collective and the plural. Therefore, R. Jehudah, who quotes only the singular in his statement, does not disagree with the anonymous majority.</i>. ‘That I shall not taste groat or groats: he is forbidden both raw and cooked. Rebbi Jehudah says, ‘a <i>qônām</i> that I shall not taste groat or wheat’, he is permitted to chew them raw.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> ‘That I shall not taste groat or groats, etc. It was stated: “Rebbi Jehudah says, ‘a <i>qônām</i> that I shall not taste a groat kernel,’ he is forbidden to chew and permitted soup. ‘That I shall not taste groats,’ he is forbidden soup and permitted to chew. ‘That I shall not taste a wheat kernel,’ he is forbidden to chew and permitted bread. ‘That I shall not taste wheats,’ he is forbidden bread and permitted to chew.
“Wheat” and you say so? Rebbi Yose said, so is the way of people, if they see white bread they say, blessed Who created this wheat<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">This is inconsistent with the preceding <i>baraita</i>; it refers to the statement of R. Jehudah in the Mishnah who sees in the collective חִיטָּה a synonym of “bread”.</i>.

Chapter 7



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who makes a vow to abstain from vegetables is permitted squash, but Rebbi Aqiba forbids it. They said to Rebbi Aqiba, does it not happen that a person says to his agent, buy vegetables for us, and he says, I found only squash<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">“Vegetables” are grown in a vegetable garden and eaten raw or as a side dish. Squash is produced without irrigation and is not eaten raw. The person sent to buy vegetables will not buy squash without separate instructions.</i>? He said to them, that is true. Would he ever say, I found only legumes<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Legumes (Arabic قُطْنِيَّة) are seeds, such as peas and lentils, to be ground into flour. As the last sentence of the Mishnah makes clear, legumes not ready to be ground into flour are vegetables. The person sent to buy vegetables will never ask for permission to buy legumes.</i>? But squash is contained in the notion of “vegetable”<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The Babylonian Mishnah adds: “But legumes are not contained in the notion of ‘vegetables’ ”.</i>. He is forbidden fresh Egyptian beans and permitted dried ones.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Does Rebbi Aqiba think “I found, I did not find”<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Does R. Aqiba hold that any usual substitute comes under the category of the original? The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 54a" href="/Nedarim.54a">Babli, 54a</a>, holds that this is R. Aqiba’s opinion. The Babli must hold that a person sent to buy meat will not suggest fish as a substitute.</i>? Then one who forbids meat to himself should be forbidden fish and grasshopper meat since, if a man says to another, buy meat for us, he will return and say, I found only fish<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Fish and grasshoppers do not come under the category of “meat” since they can be cooked with milk. (Yemenite Jews used to roast grasshoppers in clay pots used for neither meat nor milk).</i>! But Rebbi Aqiba must think that squash are vegetables, but the rabbis think that squash are not vegetables. That applies to legal situations. “The vegetables of this garden are sold;” if squash were there, they would be sold in the opinion of Rebbi Aqiba, not sold in the opinion of the rabbis. The same applies to declarations of abandonment<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">If somebody declares his vegetables as ownerless or dedicates them to the Temple, for the rabbis he does not include squash, for R. Aqiba he does.</i> or dedications.
Rebbi Jacob bar Idi, Rebbi Ḥiyya, in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: It turns out that the minority opinion here is the majority opinion there and vice-versa. As it was stated: “One who makes a vow to abstain from meat is forbidden all kinds of meat, is forbidden head, feet, neck, heart, and liver<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli adds, “and fowl”, cf. Note 7.</i>. But he is permitted fish meat and grasshoppers<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This statement also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Chullin 8:1" href="/Mishnah_Chullin.8.1">Mishnah <i>Ḥulin</i> 8:1</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Chullin 8:1" href="/Tosefta_Chullin.8.1">Tosephta <i>Ḥulin</i> 8:2</a>.</i>. In this vein, Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, intestines are not meat and those who eat them are not humans.”
Everybody agrees that a person who vows to forbid gourd to himself is permitted vegetables, as it was stated: A person who makes a vow to abstain from a main object is forbidden the peripherals; if he vows from the peripherals, he is permitted the main object. One who vows to abstain from meat is forbidden sinews, he who vows to abstain from sinews is permitted meat. Where do they disagree? About Egyptian gourd. But everybody agrees that Greek gourds<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 1:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.1.5.2"><i>Kilaim</i> 1:5</a>.</i> are vegetables. Rebbi Crispus in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: All sorts of gourd and cabbage which we eat are Greek gourds. Rebbi Jehudah bar Ṣeradia says, <i>qarmals</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Neither the family of plants אפומלייא nor the individual plants mentioned here have been determined, except for the last one. In Arabic, נבע is a tree from which arrows and bows are made. {Perhaps cf. Greek πωμάριον, τό, Latin <i>pomarium</i>, “orchard”, as a reference to “tree fruits”? (E. G.)}</i> are vegetables. Rebbi Jonah and Rebbi Yose asked, are <i>qarmals</i> subject to tithes? <sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">From <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 2:7:4-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.2.7.4-5"><i>Ševi‘it</i> 2:10, Notes 98–101</a>, dealing with the rules of the Sabbatical year. The statement of Bar Qappara there is attributed to R. Jonah.</i> Bar Qappara stated, <i>qarmals</i> are free from tithes. That is, as long as they did not form gourds; but when they formed gourds they are like vegetables. Rebbi Yose instructed that it is forbidden to sip water from colocasia leaves because they are food for deer.
Is one who makes a vow to abstain from vegetables permitted kinds of ‘<i>pvmlyy</i>’, as, for example, <i>nb‘h</i>, <i>msrvlh</i>, <i>plvlyh</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Neither the family of plants אפומלייא nor the individual plants mentioned here have been determined, except for the last one. In Arabic, נבע is a tree from which arrows and bows are made. {Perhaps cf. Greek πωμάριον, τό, Latin <i>pomarium</i>, “orchard”, as a reference to “tree fruits”? (E. G.)}</i>, and taro<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Colocasia, Greek κολοκασία, Arabic קֻלקָאס. Its root is used to produce a kind of flour in Africa; hence, it is an intermediate between a vegetable and a legume. The only problem is that of vows (e. g., if a person makes a vow to abstain from vegetables), since in matters of vows one does not follow technical usage but the meaning in local dialects. Hence, there may be places where taro is commonly subsumed under vegetables and other places where it is not.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Text from <i>Peah</i> 1:6, Note 273.</i> Rebbi Isaac ben Ḥaqolah and Rebbi Joshua ben Levi both say taro is like a vegetable for tithes, the sabbatical year, <i>peah</i>, and <i>kilaim</i>. For vows it is problematic.
If somebody makes a vow to abstain from vegetables, would he be permited dried ones? Let us hear from the following: “He is forbidden fresh Egyptian beans and permitted dried ones.” He mentioned only Egyptian beans, a kind which has a threshing floor<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., Egyptian beans are separated from their hulls and stored in quantity. A contract for delivery of Egyptian beans requires delivery of dried ones. A vegetable is permitted in dry form only if that dry form is traded as a separate kind.</i>. Therefore, anything which has no threshing floor is forbidden even if dried. <sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 4:3" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).4.3">Tosephta 4:3</a>.</i> If somebody makes a vow to abstain from bulbous plants<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">This translation follows S. Lieberman, <i>Tosefta ki-f shutaḥ Nedarim</i>, p. 455.</i> he is forbidden green melon<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.1.2.1"><i>Kilaim</i> 1:2, Notes 38–39</a>.</i>, squash, water melon, sweet melon<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.1.2.1"><i>Kilaim</i> 1:2, Notes 38–39</a>.</i> and all tree fruits. If somebody makes a vow to abstain from sheep meat, he is permitted lambs, pidgeon chicks<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">These have nothing to do with sheep; pigeons are only mentioned for inclusion in the next clause.</i>, and milk<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Sheep’s milk.</i>. But if he said, which grew this year, he is forbidden all of these. <sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 4:3" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).4.3">Tosephta 4:3</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 76b" href="/Yoma.76b">Babli <i>Yoma</i> 76b</a>.</i> If somebody makes a vow to abstain from cider, he is forbidden everything sweet and permitted wine. That is, following those who say that vows are interpreted in the vernacular<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 6:1:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.6.1.2-7">Halakhah 6:1</a>; everybody’s opinion in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 76b" href="/Yoma.76b">Babli, <i>Yoma</i> 76b</a>. It seems that in Mishnaic times, the sweetness of the cider was the determinig factor.</i>. But following those who say, vows are interpreted in biblical Hebrew<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joshiah, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 6:1:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.6.1.2-7">Halakhah 6:1</a>.</i>, the Torah used “cider” as an expression for wine; “your cider”<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:17" href="/Deuteronomy.12.17"><i>Deut.</i> 12:17</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:23" href="/Deuteronomy.14.23">14:23</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 18:4" href="/Deuteronomy.18.4">18:4</a>.</i>, that is wine.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who makes a vow to abstain from flour is forbidden dry Egyptian beans<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Not only Egyptian beans but all legumes (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 7:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.7.1.1">Note 2</a>) ground into flour, as explained at the end. Egyptian beans are only mentioned because of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 7:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.7.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>.</i>, the words of Rebbi Meїr. But the Sages say, he is forbidden only the Five Kinds<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Wheat, barley, spelt, foxtail, and oats; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 6:1:22" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.6.1.22"><i>Berakhot</i> 6:1 (Note 89</a>), <i>Kilaim</i> 1:1 (Notes 2,3), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.1.1.1"><i>Ḥallah</i> 1:1 (Note 1</a>). Only these contain gluten which produces sour dough.</i>. Rebbi Meїr says, one who makes a vow to abstain from produce<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">In Biblical language, תבואה is all agricultural produce. In rabbinic language, the word is used only for cereals.</i> is forbidden only the Five Kinds but he who makes a vow to abstain from flour is forbidden everything<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Not everything but every cereal and legume.</i>, but permitted tree fruits<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Even carob fruit which sometimes is ground into a kind of flour.</i> and vegetables.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who makes a vow,” etc. What is Rebbi Meїr’s reason? What the rain produces from the earth<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic دجز “to be dark, rainy”.</i>. What is the reason of the rabbis? Flour is עבור<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The expression עבור is used in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 5:12" href="/Joshua.5.12"><i>Jos</i>. 5:12</a> to indicate grains used to make bread.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The quote is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.1.2.1">Mishnah <i>Ḥallaḥ</i> 1:2</a>, the remainder from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Challah 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Challah.1.2.1">Halakhah <i>Ḥallah</i> 1:3, Notes 105</a>, 110–112 Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 4:3" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).4.3">Tosephta <i>Nedarim</i> 4:3</a>.</i> He who makes a vow not to use bread or produce is forbidden them, the words of Rebbi Meїr. Therefore, is he who makes a vow not to use bread or produce forbidden everything according to the rabbis? Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, so is the Mishnah: “He who makes a vow not to use flour is only forbidden these.” How do we hold? If he uses “bread” in the biblical sense, then also if he says “produce” it is meant in the biblical sense. He should be forbidden everything since it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:9" href="/Deuteronomy.22.9"><i>Deut</i>. 22:9</a>): “The produce of the vineyard.” If he simply says “bread”; only from wheat or barley is it simply called “bread”. Rebbi Yose said, I confirmed it, at a place where one eats bread from all [kinds], only from the five kinds it is simply called “bread”.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who made a vow to abstain from garments is permitted sack-cloth, carpets, and goat’s hair cloth. If he said, a <i>qônām</i> that wool shall not come onto me, he is permitted to cover himself with shorn wool; that linen should not come upon me, he is permitted to cover himself with linen fibers<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">It is assumed that “wool” and “linen” refer to garments made from these materials, not to the materials in themselves. R. Jehudah disagrees and describes a scenario where the opposite would be true.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, everything refers to the vow. If he was carrying<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">A load of raw wool or linen.</i> and sweating and smelling badly, when he said, a <i>qônām</i> that no wool or flax should be on me, he is permitted to wear but forbidden to carry on his back.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who made a vow to abstain from garments,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 4:3" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).4.3">Tosephta 4:3</a>; a longer list in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 55b" href="/Nedarim.55b">Babli, 55b</a>.</i> One who made a vow to abstain from clothing is permitted sack-cloth, carpet, and goat’s hair cloth, leather apron<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>scortea, sc. vestis.</i></i>, and bandages<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">By consensus of the moderns, from Buxtorf to Lieberman, this is Latin <i>feminalia, -ium, n.,</i> which Lewis and Short translate by “bandages for upper thighs”. {Compare also <i>femorale, -is, n.,</i> “covering for the thigh” (E. G.)} <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 27:6" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.27.6">Maimonides (Commentary to <i>Kelim</i>27:6</a>) defines פמליא as a “muslin headscarf”.</i>. He is forbidden fascia<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><i>fascia, -ae, f.</i> “band, bandage, breast band, diadem” (Lewis and Short).</i> and belt<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>funda,</i> cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 9:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.9.5.1"><i>Berakhot</i> 9:7, Note 258</a>.</i>. One who vows to abstain from clothing is forbidden all kinds of clothing but permitted these. Rebbi Jeremiah said, Rebbi Ze‘ira asked: If one said, a <i>qônām,</i> that I shall not wear a garment, that I shall not cover myself with clothing<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Is anything permitted to a person abstaining from garments and clothing? The question, more typical for R. Jeremiah than R. Ze‘ira (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 10:6:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.10.6.5"><i>Terumot</i> 10, Note 110</a>, <i>Ma‘serot</i> 3, Note 156), is not answered.</i>? Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar said, if he said, a <i>qônām</i> for anything that is generally used to cover oneself and a derivative of it is generally used to cover oneself; generally<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Usually amended to: If he vowed to abstain from the material. The emendation seems to be a corruption; the text requires an investigation of the vower’s intention.</i> he is permitted the derivative; if he made a vow to abstain from the derivative he is permitted the material itself. What is an example? For example, sheepskin<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Sheepskin can be made into coats but wool is textile material in its own right.</i>. For anything that is generally used to cover oneself but no derivative of it is generally used to cover oneself; if he vowed about it, he is permitted the derivative; if he made a vow to abstain from the derivative he is forbidden the material itself. What is an example? For example, goatskin<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Goat’s hair is used for doormats but not usually for garments.</i>. And anything that is generally not used to cover oneself but a derivative of it is generally used to cover oneself; if he vowed about it, he intended only the derivative. What is an example? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, for example raw cotton<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">This is not usable unless freed from wood particles.</i>.
So is the Mishnah: If he was carrying and sweating; when he said, a <i>qônām</i> that no wool or flax should be on me, he is forbidden to wear them but permitted to carry them on his back<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">This text seems to imply that in popular usage, עוֹלה על was used only for garments, not for loads. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 4:4" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).4.4">Tosephta (4:4</a>) and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 55b" href="/Nedarim.55b">Babli (55b</a>), the text is: “If he was wearing wool, was uncomfortable, and said, a <i>qônām</i> that wool should be on me, he is forbidden to wear but permitted to carry it. If he was carrying wool and sweating; when he said, a <i>qônām</i> that no wool should be on me, he is permitted to wear it but forbidden to carry it on his back.” In that version, the meaning of עוֹלה על has to be determined by the context.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who vows not to use the house is permitted the upper floor, the words of Rebbi Meïr; but the Sages say that the upper floor is part of the house. One who vows not to use the upper floor is permitted the house.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who vows not to use the house,” etc. The opinion of Rebbi Meïr is reasonable for a city dweller<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">In rabbinic (and also modern) Hebrew, בַּיִת has two different meanings. It may mean “building” in a general sense; but applied to dwellings it usually means “appartment”; in talmudic Hebrew often “ground floor one-room appartment”. In most cases in a city, two different families dwell in the “house”, the ground floor, and in the upper floor. But a farmer uses the ground floor as a dwelling and the upper floor for storage; for him, “house” and upper floor form a unit. Therefore, practice has to follow R. Meïr in a city and the Sages in the countryside.<br>On the other hand, a mention of the upper floor certainly excludes the ground floor.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who vows not to use the bed<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Again, “bed” can mean any appliance used to sleep on, or the particular implement called “bed” in the trade. R. Meïr assumes that the narrow meaning is understood since this is the most frequent use, but the Sages include all lexical meanings of the word.</i> is permitted the couch, the words of Rebbi Meïr, but the Sages say, a couch is included in the notion of bed. One who vows not to use the couch is permitted the bed.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who vows not to use the bed,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph and the next are also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 3:1:2-27" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.3.1.2-27"><i>Berakhot</i> 3:1</a> (5d 1. 70) and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Moed Katan 3:5:2-28" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Moed_Katan.3.5.2-28"><i>Mo‘ed Qaṭan</i> 3:5</a>, 83a 1. 11; explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 3:1:7-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.3.1.7-8"><i>Berakhot</i> 3, Notes 22–35</a> (pp. 261–264). Both paragraphs are paralleled in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 56" href="/Nedarim.56">Babli, 56a/b</a>.</i> It was stated: “A <i>dargesh</i> is put upright and is not turned over. Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar says, he removes its <i>qlwnṭryn</i> and that is enough.” Rebbi Yosa in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: practice follows Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Yose, if a couch has posts which are upright and removed together with it, he takes them off and that is enough.
What is a couch and what is a <i>dargesh</i>? Rebbi Jeremiah said, one that one plaits on its body is a couch and one that one does not plait on its body is a <i>dargesh.</i> But have we not stated (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 16:1" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.16.1"><i>Kelim</i> 16:1</a>) “Bed and crib after he rubs them with fish skin.” If it is plaited on its body, why does he rub? Rebbi Eleazar said, explain it with those Caesarean cribs that have holes.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who vows not to use a town is permitted to enter its domain<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The domain of a city is a rectangular area whose boundaries are 2000 cubits (one mile) from the last house in Northern, Southern, Eastern, and Western directions. A town dweller is permitted to walk in this domain on the Sabbath. Cf. <i>Soṭah</i> 5:4.</i> but forbidden to enter its suburbs<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">A house is defined as suburban if it is outside the city wall but connected to the wall by a row of houses where no two houses are more than 70 cubits apart. This also implies that the person making the vow may not approach a suburban house closer than 70 cubits.</i>. But one who vows not to use a house is forbidden inwards from the doorpost<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">While a house opening onto a common courtyard has the 4 cubits in front of its door as its proper domain which no other dweller of the courtyard may use to deposit his wares, the maker of the vow is not excluded from stepping on that domain.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who vows not to use a town,” etc. From where that the suburbs of a town are like the town? It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 5:13" href="/Joshua.5.13"><i>Jos.</i> 5:13</a>; this is written before the description of the conquest of Jericho. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 56b" href="/Nedarim.56b">Babli, 56b</a>, the argument is in the name of R. Joḥanan. Since for the Sages, the biblical meaning of a word has precedence over the meaning in the vernacular, one tries to have a biblical reference for the Sages’ opinion.</i>, “it happened when Joshua was in Jericho.” How could he have been in Jericho? Is it not written that<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 6:1" href="/Joshua.6.1"><i>Jos.</i> 6:1</a>: “Jericho was closed and besieged because of the Children of Israel; no one left and <i>no one entered.</i>”</i> “Jericho was closed and besieged,” and you say so? Rebbi Judan bar Shalom said, he was in a suburb. Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Aḥa: It happened in Jericho<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The argument does not prove anything; nobody can be sure that the editor of the book of Joshua follows a chronological order; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 8:3:12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.8.3.12"><i>Soṭa</i> 8, Note 127</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Mana asked, does this not disagree with Rebbi Joḥanan, since Rebbi Joḥanan said, in matters of vows they follow the vernacular<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Even the Sages should not be interested in the biblical acceptation of a word if it is a matter of interpretation of a vow.</i>? Is it not the way of a person who sees another one outside the gate to say, I saw him in Tiberias<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">The common acceptation of the word is identical with the biblical.</i>?

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> ‘These fruits shall be <i>qônām</i> for me, a <i>qônām</i> they shall be for my mouth’, he is forbidden what is exchanged for them or what grows from them<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Since he forbade himself any usufruct.</i>. ‘That I shall not eat, that I shall not taste,’ he is permitted what is exchanged for them, or what grows from them if the seed disappears. But if the seed does not disappear<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Defined in <i>Terumot</i> 9:6 as arum, garlic, and onion.</i>, even second generation growth is forbidden.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> ‘These fruits shall be <i>qônām</i> for me,’ etc. How far<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">How far is the growth of replanted garlic and onions forbidden if there is a vow not to eat from them. The reference is to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 9:2:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.9.2.6"><i>Terumot</i> 9:7, Notes 82–83</a>, referring to growth from garlic and onions forbidden as <i>ṭevel.</i> The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 57b" href="/Nedarim.57b">Babli, 57b</a>, refers to a more lenient opinion discussed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 7:3:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.7.3.6"><i>Terumot</i> 7:7, Note 121</a>.</i>? It comes, as Rebbi Jacob bar Idi said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: It is forbidden for three harvests, but the fourth is permitted. Here, it is the same.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one says to his wife, a <i>qônām</i> shall be anything you work for, a <i>qônām</i> shall it be for my mouth, a <i>qônām</i> shall it be to my mouth; he is forbidden what is exchanged for it<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">If the wife earned money, any usufruct of that money is forbidden to her husband.</i> or what grows from it<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">If the wife planted a tree or grew agricultural produce, fruit and produce are forbidden to her husband.</i>. ‘That I shall not eat, that I shall not taste,’ he is permitted what is exchanged for them, or what grows from them if the seed disappears. But if the seed does not disappear<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Defined in <i>Terumot</i> 9:6 as arum, garlic, and onion.</i>, even second generation growth is forbidden.<br>‘What you prepare I would eat until Passover<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">In all the following statements it is understood that the husband said, “it shall be a <i>qônām</i> if …”. The Babli and most independent Mishnah mss. read אֵינִי “I do not” for Yerushalmi אֲנִי “I would”; the meaning is the same.</i>, what you make I would wear until Passover,’ if she made before Passover, he may eat or wear after Passover.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If one says to his wife,” etc. So is the Mishnah: “What you prepare until Passover I shall not eat, what you make until Passover I shall not wear”.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> ‘What you prepare until Passover I would eat what you make until Passover I would wear if she made before Passover, he is forbidden to eat or wear after Passover.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “What you prepare,” etc. So is the Mishnah: ‘What you make until Passover I shall wear’<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that the text intends to read <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 7:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.7.9.1">Mishnah 10</a> before <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 7:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.7.8.1">Mishnah 9</a> and to emphasize the positive statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 7:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.7.8.1">Mishnah 9</a>. These and the following two Mishnaiot emphasize that word order does count.</i>.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> ‘That you provide me with usufruct until Passover if you would go to your father’s house until Tabernacles<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">In the interpretation of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 15" href="/Nedarim.15">Babli, 15 a/b</a>, the husband after Tabernacles makes a vow of <i>qônām</i> that his wife cannot have anything from him until Passover (in the spring) if she goes to her father’s house before Tabernacles (next fall). It is obvious that if the wife goes before Passover, she is forbidden until Passover (and she should not have any usufruct from her husband now since maybe she will go afterwards). But if she goes during the six months between Passover and Tabernacles, any usufruct she had before Passover would be retroactively forbidden; therefore, he would transgress the commandment not to profane vows (by formulating his vow in a way which invites violating it) and she could be punished for violating his vow.<br>While this interpretation seems to fit the language of the Mishnah (and is accepted as obvious by Maimonides in his Commentary), the Yerushalmi in the Halakhah rejects the idea that the wife could be guilty because of the husband’s vow and reads the Mishnah as given in the translation, that the husband forbids himself any usufruct from his wife. Therefore, he cannot have any usufruct now since she later might defy his instructions. There is no reason to think that the text of the Halakhah be corrupt.</i>.’ If she went before Passover, she is forbidden to deliver usufruct to him until Passover, after Passover “he should not profane his word<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:3" href="/Numbers.30.3"><i>Num</i>. 30:3</a>.</i>.”
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “That you would have usufruct from me,” etc. <i>He</i> is forbidden to have usufruct from <i>her</i> immediately, for maybe she would go after Passover and it would turn out that his having usufruct would be retroactively [forbidden].

Halakhah 11

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> ‘That you provide me with usufruct from you until Tabernacles if you would go to your father’s house until Passover.’ If she went before Passover, she is forbidden to deliver usufruct to him until Tabernacles; but she is permitted to go after Passover.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “That you would have usufruct from me until Tabernacles,” etc. <i>He</i> is forbidden to have usufruct from <i>her</i> immediately, for maybe she would go after Passover and it would turn out that his having usufruct would be retroactively [forbidden].

Chapter 8



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> ‘A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not taste wine today,’ he is forbidden only until nightfall<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The Tanna will not include the night with the day.</i>. ‘This week’, he is forbidden the entire week; the Sabbath belongs to the past<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">If he makes a vow not to drink wine this week, he is forbidden until after the coming Sabbath day since the Sabbath is the end of the week.</i>. ‘This month’, he is forbidden the entire month; the day of the New Moon belongs to the future<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">If he makes a vow not to drink wine this month he is permitted wine the first day of the New Moon.</i>. ‘This year’, he is forbidden the entire year; New Year’s Day belongs to the future. ‘This Sabbatical period’, he is forbidden the entire Sabbatical period<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The period of 7 years including the next Sabbatical year.</i>; the Sabbatical year belongs to the past.<br>But if he said, one day, one week, one month, one year, he is forbidden from day.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">If he vows to abstain from wine for one day, he has to abstain for 24 hours from the moment of the vow. One week is counted as 7 times 24 hours from the moment of the vow, a year is from day and hour of the vow to the same day, same hour, of the next year.</i>
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “ ‘A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not taste wine today,’ ” etc. This implies that he is permitted at nightfall<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This seems to be obvious from the Mishnah. But in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 60a" href="/Nedarim.60a">Babli, 60a</a>, Rav Jeremiah bar Abba requires that a rabbi give permission to drink wine after nightfall. The statement here has to be read as rejection of that opinion.</i>. Does this not disagree with Rebbi Joḥanan, since Rebbi Joḥanan said, in matters of vows one follows common usage? It is not usual that a man should say to another in the evening, I did not eat until evening. Would he say, yesterday<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">In the evening, he does not refer to the daylight hours as “yesterday”. Therefore, he should be forbidden wine until he goes to sleep.</i>? Refer to the following and it does disagree: It is not usual that a man should say to another in the morning, I did not eat in the evening. Would he say, the same day<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">This is the same argument in the reverse. A person before sunrise will refer to the evening hours of the same night as “yesterday”. Therefore, the evening hours of the preceding day belong to that day in popular conscience.</i>? Does the following not disagree? This day from this week, or this week from this day<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Since everybody agrees that a week consists of 7 times 24 hours, and it consists of 7 days, it should be obvious that a day in vows should be 24 hours and the distinction made between <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 8:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.8.1.1">Mishnaiot 1</a> and 2 seems to have no basis.</i>? What is the difference between “this day” and “today”? That is, following the opinion that in matters of vows one follows common usage. But here, one follows the opinion that in matters of vows one follows biblical usage<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Which in the story of Creation defines a day by a night followed by daylight.</i>. Rebbi Jonah from Bostra said, it is the way of people to say to another, bear with me this day<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">He asks him to work with him during daytime only. There is an acceptation of “day” in the vernacular which refers to daytime only.<br>For this meaning of the root סבר cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 2:1:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.2.1.2-4"><i>Baba Batra</i> 2:1</a> (13b line 31).</i>.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 8:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.8.6.1">Mishnah 7</a>: “ ‘A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not taste wine this year,’ he is forbidden the year and [an eventual] intercalary month. ‘Until the beginning of Adar,’ until the beginning of the First Adar. ‘Until the end of Adar,’ until the end of the First Adar.” The main argument is about the first part of the Mishnah.</i>, we have stated: “Until the beginning of Adar, until the beginning of the First Adar. Until the end of Adar, until the end of the First Adar<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Even in an intercalary year, when the Second Adar is added as the 13th month, “Adar” without a qualifier means only the month of First Adar which is part of every year. [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 8:5:4-6:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.8.5.4-6.3">Halakhah 7</a> and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 63" href="/Nedarim.63">Babli (63a/b</a>), supported by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 4:7" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).4.7">Tosephta (4:7</a>), qualify this statement to apply only if the person making the vow did not know that the year would have a second Adar. If he knew, the vow would extend to Second Adar.]</i>.” Does this imply that Nisan is the beginning of the year as far as vows are concerned<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Rosh Hashanah 1:1:1-16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Rosh_Hashanah.1.1.1-16">Mishnah <i>Roš Haššanah</i> 1:1</a>. Nisan is biblically counted as the first month and Tishre as the seventh, but in Second Temple practice the year always started in the fall with the first day of Tishre. One does not discuss here the problem of the several calendars of the monarchical period.<br>Since the Mishnah states that “he is forbidden during the year and its intercalary month”, it seems that the year must be counted from Nisan since, if it were counted from Tishre, the intercalary month would be in the middle of the year and it should be obvious that the person imposes a continuous prohibition on himself.</i>? Tishre is the beginning of the year as far as vows are concerned. That you should not say, the beginning of Adar should compensate for Ellul and he would be permitted in Ellul<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">If a person said, I shall not drink wine for a year, it would imply a prohibition for 12 months. But since he said, <i>this</i> year, the prohibition lasts either 12 or 13 months, as the case may be. The statement of the intercalary month is made to underline the difference between “a year” of twelve months and “this year” of possibly 13 months. If somebody says on New Year’s Day of an intercalary year that he will not drink wine for <i>a</i> year, he will in effect be permitted to drink wine on the first of the coming Ellul. But for <i>this</i> year, he has to observe the additional intercalary month without compensation.</i>; therefore, it was necessary to say that “he is forbidden it and its intercalary [month]”.
“If he said, one day, one week, one month, one year, one Sabbatical period, he is forbidden from day to day.” From hour to hour<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">To the same time on the next day (or day of the next week, next month, next year). It then is stated that a <i>baraita</i> parallels the Mishnah, explicitly mentioning “from hour to hour.”</i>. It was stated, from hour to hour.
<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">This text is from <i>Ta‘aniot</i> 3:13.</i> Rebbi Aḥa, Rebbi Abbahu, in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: On the Sabbath, a person is forbidden to fast until the sixth hour<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Since it says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 58:13" href="/Isaiah.58.13"><i>Is</i>. 58:13</a>): “You shall call the Sabbath a delight”, one is forbidden to fast on the Sabbath (except if the Sabbath is preempted by the Day of Atonement). As R. Yose states, the Mishnah in <i>Ta‘aniot</i> (3:13) states that a person who fasted for more than half the day is required to fast the entire day. The day between sunrise and sunset is divided into 12 equal hours with noon being the end of the sixth hour (disregarding the equation of time). At the equinox, these hours are the constant hours, l/24th of a day. Since a person fasting until noon then would have to fast the entire day, he may not fast until noon on the Sabbath.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, a Mishnah states this<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ta‘aniot</i> 3:13, speaking of a fastday proclaimed in a year of draught, position of R. Eliezer. (The anonymous majority requires a full day of a fast for rain if the rain started to fall during daytime.)</i>: “If they fasted when it started to rain, before noon they should not finish,” because until then it is morning; “after noon they should finish,” since most of the day already was spent in holiness.
The words of Rebbi Joḥanan imply that one may fast for hours<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">In both Talmudim, not eating is counted as a private fast only if the person fasting undertook the fast by a vow or its equivalent. From the example of R. Joḥanan it follows that the Yerushalmi accepts as valid a vow to fast a certain time, even if the person did eat before and will eat after the fast during daylight. It is possible that R. Jonah held that a fast can be vowed in the middle of the day, on condition that one abstain from food for the remainder of the day. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 11b-12a" href="/Taanit.11b-12a">Babli (<i>Ta‘anit</i> 11b/12a</a>) seems to accept only fasts that cover the entire daylight hours of a day. The only “fast by hours” accepted in the Babli is one where a person did not eat breakfast and in the middle of the day decides to vow to spend the remainder of the day fasting (cf. Maimonides <i>Ta‘aniot</i> 1:13 and its commentaries.)</i>, for Rebbi Joḥanan said, I shall fast until I finish studying this chapter, until I finish studying this text. The words of Rebbi Jonah imply that one may fast for hours, for Rebbi Jonah was in Tyre when he heard that Rebbi Yose’s son had died. Even though he had eaten cheese and drunk water, he finished that day fasting. The words of Rav imply that one may fast for hours, for Rav said, one may borrow his fast day and pay back<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 12b" href="/Taanit.12b">Babli, <i>Ta‘anit</i> 12b</a>, this refers only to fasting full days, that one may transfer the obligation of a private fast to another date.</i>. Samuel said to him, is that a vow<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">It depends on the terms of the vow. If “a day” of fast was vowed, Samuel will agree with Rav. If “this day” was vowed and the person can no longer stand his hunger, Samuel will send him to a rabbi to dissolve the vow. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 12b" href="/Taanit.12b">Babli, <i>Ta‘anit</i> 12b</a>, Samuel agrees with Rav in the case the person fasting can no longer stand his hunger. [The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Taanit 12b" href="/Taanit.12b">Babli, (<i>Ta‘anit</i> 12b</a>, <i>S̄abbat</i> 11a), disallows the statement of Rav in case of a fast to avert the consequences of a bad dream.]</i>? If he had vowed to fast but forgot and ate the volume of an olive, he lost his fast day. Rebbi Abba in the name of the rabbis there<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonia.</i>: That is, if he said simply “a day”. But if he said “this day”, he has to continue fasting until the end. He spoke only of eating; that excludes tasting<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Consuming less than the volume of an average olive is not called “eating”.</i>. Rebbi Abba Ḥasida in the name of Rebbi Ze‘ira: Tasting does not create obligations of benediction<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">There is no obligation of a benediction for tasting a small amount; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 6:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.6.1.2"><i>Berakhot</i> 6:1, Note 5</a>. A shortened version of the statement is a <i>baraita</i> in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 14a" href="/Berakhot.14a">Babli, <i>Berakhot</i> 14a</a>.</i>, robbery<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Taking such a small amount from somebody else is not robbery. (Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 3:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.3.2.4"><i>Demay</i> 3:2, Note 56</a>).</i>, nor of <i>demay</i><sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Even the most scrupulous may taste food from a person suspected of not tithing.</i>, nor of the interruption of a fast day.
A private person who committed himself for a fast day<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">By making the vow on the day before (commonly after the afternoon prayer). A common fast is only during daytime.</i>, eats and drinks after nightfall. But if he said a public fast day<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Following the rules of the Day of Atonement which runs from evening to evening (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:32" href="/Leviticus.23.32"><i>Lev</i>. 23:32</a>).</i>, he eats and drinks only during daytime. If he made a vow to fast<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">And indicated the dates of the days he wanted to fast.</i> and it turned out to fall on Sabbath days or holidays, he is whipped but does not need permission from a Sage<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Since festive meals are required on the Sabbath or any holiday of pilgrimage by biblical law, it is sinful to make a vow to break a biblical law (which subjects to vower to punishment) even though the vow is invalid (and does not need invalidation by a rabbi).</i>. If he made a vow to fast and it turned out to fall on days written in the Scroll of Fasts<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The collection of <i>beraitot</i> describing the days on which in Pharisaic practice fasts are forbidden. These include the days before and after holidays, Ḥanukka (a popular holiday having no biblical source since the books of Maccabees are disavowed in Jewish tradition), Purim (a biblical holiday based on the book of Esther which has no status of holiness), and a great number of days recording the ascent of the pharisaic sect to dominance in Jewish public life, in particular Temple practice. These later dates would have lost their significance with the destruction of the Temple. The last event mentioned in the Scroll of Fasts is the revocation of Hadrian’s decrees by Antoninus Pius. This shows that the Scroll of Fasts is tannaїtic; its abolition was an amoraitic problem.</i>, Rebbi Ḥizqia and Rebbi Yudan in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba: One said, he fasts but does not finish, but the other one said, he is whipped but does not need permission from a Sage. That is, you say that before the scroll of fasts was abolished. But when the Scroll of Fasts was abolished, all this was abolished. Rebbi Ḥanania<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Probably a scribal error for “R. Ḥanina”.</i> and Rebbi Joḥanan both say that the Scroll of Fasts was abolished<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rosh Hashanah 18b" href="/Rosh_Hashanah.18b">Babli, <i>Roš Haššanah</i> 18b</a>, R. Ḥanina holds that the Scroll of Fasts was abolished, R. Joḥanan that it was not abolished (since usually Rabbis Ḥanina and Joḥanan are opposed to one another).</i>. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, the Scroll of Fasts was abolished. Rebbi Joḥanan said, yesterday I was sitting and stating: “It happened that they decreed a fast day at Lod during Ḥanukka. Rebbi Eliezer went and had a haircut, Rebbi Joshua went and took a hot bath. Rebbi Joshua said to them, go and fast for what you fasted.” And you say, the Scroll of Fasts was abolished? Rebbi Abba said, even though you say that the Scroll of Fasts was abolished, Ḥanukka and Purim were not abolished<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rosh Hashanah 19b" href="/Rosh_Hashanah.19b">Babli agrees,<i>Roš Haššanah</i> 19b</a>.</i>. The acts of the rabbis imply that the Scroll of Fasts was abolished: Rebbi Jonathan fasted on the day before New Year’s Day. Rebbi Abin fasted on the day before Tabernacles. Rebbi Ze‘ira fasted 300 fast days, and some say 900 fast days and did not care about the Scroll of Fasts.
