Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa told the scribes: If a woman comes to ask you, tell her that one may fast any day except Sabbaths, holidays, New Moons, the intermediary days of holidays, Ḥanukka, and Purim.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> ‘Until Passover’<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">“A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not … until Passover”.</i>, he is forbidden until it comes, ‘until it be’, he is forbidden until it is passed<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">This follows the vernacular since in popular language “Passover” means “the holiday of unleaved bread” (Nisan 15–21), whereas in biblical language “Passover” is the day of slaughter of the Passover sacrifice (Nisan 14).</i>. ‘Until before Passover’, Rebbi Meїr says, until it comes, Rebbi Yose says, until it passed<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The Halakhah explains that this refers to biblical language; the difference between the two opinions is whether the prohibition ends at nightfall of Nisan 13 or 14.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “ ‘Until Passover’, he is forbidden,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">A slightly longer parallel to this paragraph is in <i>Qiddušin</i> 3:11. It is difficult to decide which version is original.</i> Rebbi Jeremiah asked before Rebbi Ze‘ira: The opinion of Rebbi Yose seems to be inverted. There<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Qiddušin</i> Mishnah 3:9–10. A father, who has the right to marry off his underage daughter without asking her consent, has two sets of daughters from two wives. He marries off an older daughter (whom the bridegroom has not yet seen) but does not remember which one. Rebbi Meїr says, all his daughters are forbidden to marry except the youngest of the younger group, since any other daughter either is married and forbidden to every man except the one with whom the father contracted, or she is the sister of the married one and forbidden to the man with whom the father contracted. Rebbi Yose holds that all are permitted to other men except the oldest of the older group, who is married to the chosen groom. Similarly, if he married off the younger one, R. Meїr forbids all but the oldest of the older group while R. Joshua declares the youngest of the younger group to be married. It follows that R. Meїr interprets common language to mean the largest possible set whereas R. Yose holds that people always speak as definitely as possible. Therefore, in the Mishnah here, R. Yose should exclude the day of Passover and R. Meїr should include it.</i>, he says “until all the elder possibilities are exhausted, until all the younger possibilities are exhausted,” and here, he says so? He said to him: Since Ben Azai and Ben Zoma died, the perseverers<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 9:16:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.9.16.1"><i>Soṭa</i>9, Note 271</a>.</i> disappeared; no perseverer was there until Jeremiah appeared. Rebbi Abba, son of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba, said, why does he needle him<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 2:3:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.2.3.6"><i>Berakhot</i> 2:3, Note 96</a>.</i>? Did not Rebbi Eleazar already ask before Rebbi Joḥanan, the opinion of Rebbi Yose seems to be inverted? He said to him, it is not inverted, the Mishnah is inverted, for in the House of Rebbi they stated<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The same in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 61b" href="/Nedarim.61b">Babli, 61b</a>, in the name of Rav.</i>: “ ‘Until before Passover’, Rebbi Meїr says, until it passed, Rebbi Yose says, until it comes.” We ask “until before”, and you say so<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Who could ever think that “until before” could mean “until after”?</i>? He said to him, this is a Nabatean expression, “much<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., the festival of unleavened bread; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 8:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.8.2.2">Note 39</a>.</i> before Passover”. Rebbi Abin said, everybody agrees that he is permitted on Passover<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Holidays which are fixed in the calendar.</i>. Where do they disagree? The day before Passover. One of them says, until it comes, the other until it passed.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> ‘Until the grain harvest, the grape harvest, the olive harvest’, he is forbidden only until their time arrives. That is the principle: Everything that has a fixed time<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Holidays which are fixed in the calendar.</i>, if he said ‘until it arrives’, he is forbidden until it arrives; if he said ‘until it shall be’, he is forbidden until it passed. But everthing that does not have a fixed time<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Harvests which depend on the weather.</i>, whether he said ‘until it arrives’ or ‘until it shall be’, he is forbidden only until it arrives.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> If one fixed the time for his son’s wedding and said, a <i>qônām</i> that I shall not taste wine until the wedding<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The problem really would be if he said “until the wedding takes place”.</i>, is that as if the time was fixed? Or, since he could move the time to a later date, is it as if the time was not fixed<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The question is not answered. In the opinion of R. Nissim Gerondi (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 61b" href="/Nedarim.61b">Babli 61b</a>), this means an automatic restriction; if he said “until the wedding takes place”, he is forbidden to drink wine at the wedding.</i>?

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> ‘Until the fig harvest<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">This is the conclusion of the Halakhah. Biblically, קַיִץ, from קצה “to cut”, is the harvest of any fruit ripening in the summer which is cut from the tree by a knife (and also means “summer”).</i>, until there be fig harvest’, until people start to bring in baskets. ‘Until the fig harvest is over’, until people fold their knives. ‘Until the grain harvest’, until people start to cut wheat but not barley<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Which is harvested in early spring.</i>; everything follows the place of the vow, if made on the hills, on the hills, if made in the plain, in the plain.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> ‘Until the fig harvest, until there be fig harvest’, etc. “baskets”. Baskets of figs or baskets of raisins<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Raisins are transported in baskets in contrast to grapes which are harvested into vats, so as not to lose the juice flowing from injured grape berries. (Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 61a" href="/Nedarim.61a">Babli 61a</a>).</i>? Let us hear from this: “Until they fold the knives”. You have to say, knives for figs, not knives for raisins<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Raisins do not need knives.</i>. Let us hear from the following: <sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">This text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.1"><i>Demay</i> 2:1, Notes 105–107</a>.</i> A load of raisins came up to Tiberias. Gamliel the twin asked Rebbi Abba bar Zavda. He said to him: All of the Land of Israel does not produce a load of raisins. <sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">In the text of <i>Demay</i>, there follows a rhetorical question: Does the entire Land of Israel not produce a load of raisins?</i> But so he said to him: No single place in the land of Israel produces a load of raisins<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, the Mishnah must speak of the fig harvest.</i>.
“Until the grain harvest”. It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ruth 2:23" href="/Ruth.2.23"><i>Ruth</i> 2:23</a>. It is clear from the narrative that there was one continuous harvest activity, the wheat harvest following the barley harvest immediately. Therefore, the Mishnah seems unjustified in denying that “harvest” cannot refer to barley harvest.</i>, “until the end of the barley harvest and the wheat harvest”. That verse speaks of the South<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Bethlehem.</i>, the Mishnah of Galilee.
If he made a vow “until the fig harvest” in Galilee and descended into the valleys. Even though the fig harvest did start in the valleys, he is forbidden until it starts in Galilee<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 4:7" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).4.7">Tosephta 4:7</a>, Babli (as tannaїtic statement) 63a.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> ‘Until the rains, until there be rain,’ until the second rainy spell<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">A similar statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Sheviit 9:7" href="/Mishnah_Sheviit.9.7">Mishnah <i>Ševi‘it</i> 9:7, Note 93</a>. A rainy spell is called רביע “fertilizing” if the total amount of water is one handbreadth for R. Jehudah, 3 for R. Meїr. The time of the expected second rainy spell is given in <i>Ta‘aniot</i> 1:3 (64a, 1. 64), Tosephta <i>Ta‘aniot</i> 1:3, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 63a" href="/Nedarim.63a">Babli <i>Nedarim</i> 63a</a>, as <i>Marḥešwan</i> 7 for R. Meїr, 17 for R. Jehudah, 23 for R. Yose.</i>; Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, until the time of the second rainy spell. ‘Until the rains stop’, until the end of the Month of Nisan, the words of Rebbi Meїr; Rebbi Jehudah says, until after Passover<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">The evening after Nisan 21.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “ ‘Until the rains,’ ” etc. Rebbi Ze‘ira asked: If one said ‘until the rain’, is he forbidden until another rainfall came down<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 62b" href="/Nedarim.62b">Babli, 62b</a>, R. Ze‘ira is quoted to declare that the singular implies that one fertilizing rain only is meant.</i>? There<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>S̄eqalim</i> 6:6, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 106b" href="/Menachot.106b"><i>Menaḥot</i> 106b</a>.</i>, we have stated: “One who said, I take upon me [the obligation to bring] wood<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">A plural.</i>, should not bring less that two cut logs<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">For the altar in the Temple. The logs used for the altar had to be prepared so that all branches were cut off and all worms taken out. This is the emphasis on <i>cut</i> logs.</i>.” Rebbi Yose the important said that Rebbi Abba bar Mamai asked, if he said, I take upon me [the obligation to bring] to bring wood<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Singular.</i>, does he bring one cut log? Rebbi Eleazar said, a Mishnah explains that each one is a separate sacrifice, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 2:4:1-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.2.4.1-4">Mishnah <i>Yoma</i> 2:5</a>. While for the daily morning sacrifice only one Cohen brought wood to the altar, for the evening sacrifice there were two. R. Eleazar read the Mishnah to imply that each Cohen took only one log to the altar. This would imply that a single log is an acceptable gift to the altar.</i>: “Two in whose hands are two cut logs.” In order to increase the number of logs<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">There were four logs brought to the altar, two by each Cohen. This certainly implies that a person vowing “a log” has in fact to bring two, and probably that the person who vows not to taste something until the rains, is forbidden until the second batch of rain showers. (The Babli seems to disagree, 63a).</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Ta‘aniot</i> 1:4 (in the Erfurt ms.) The statements are switched in the Vienna ms. and <i>editio princeps</i>. The paragraph is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 9:5:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.9.5.5"><i>Ševi‘it</i> 9:7, Notes 98–101</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Yose said, anything depending on the fertilizing rain<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The vow mentions דביעה, the fertilizing aspect.</i>, until the second fertilizing rain comes. And anything not depending on the fertilizing rain<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">The vow mentions simply גשם “rain”.</i>, until the time of the second fertilizing rain. It was stated: Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, if rain came down for seven days without interruption, that includes the second fertilizing rain<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">That is enough for two fertilizing periods; quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 63a" href="/Nedarim.63a">Babli 63a</a>.</i>.” Why is it called fertilizing? Because it fertilizes the land.
“A <i>qônām</i> wine,” etc. Rebbi Meїr follows his opinion and Rebbi Jehudah follows his opinion, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ta‘aniot</i> 1:2.</i>: “Until when does one pray for rain? Rebbi Jehudah says, until after Passover; Rebbi Meїr says until the end of the Month of Nisan.”

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> ‘A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not taste wine this year’, if the year became intercalary he is forbidden it and its intercalary month. ‘Until the start of Adar’, until the first of First Adar; ‘until the end of Adar’, until the end of First Adar<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.1.2">Halakhah 1, Note 12</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">This is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 8:1:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.8.1.2-7">Halakhah 1</a> and refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 8:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.8.6.1">Mishnah 7</a>.</i> Does this imply that Nisan is the beginning of the year as far as vows are concerned<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Rosh Hashanah 1:1:1-16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Rosh_Hashanah.1.1.1-16">Mishnah <i>Roš Haššanah</i> 1:1</a>. Nisan is biblically counted as the first month and Tishre as the seventh, but in Second Temple practice the year always started in the fall with the first day of Tishre. One does not discuss here the problem of the several calendars of the monarchical period.<br>Since the Mishnah states that “he is forbidden during the year and its intercalary month”, it seems that the year must be counted from Nisan since, if it were counted from Tishre, the intercalary month would be in the middle of the year and it should be obvious that the person imposes a continuous prohibition on himself.</i>? Tishre is is the beginning of the year as far as vows are concerned. That you should not say, the beginning of Adar should compensate for Ellul and he would be permitted in Ellul<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">If a person said, I shall not drink wine for a year, it would imply a prohibition for 12 months. But since he said, <i>this</i> year, the prohibition lasts either 12 or 13 months, as the case may be. The statement of the intercalary month is made to underline the difference between “a year” of twelve months and “this year” of possibly 13 months. If somebody says on New Year’s Day of an intercalary year that he will not drink wine for <i>a</i> year, he will in effect be permitted to drink wine on the first of the coming Ellul. But for <i>this</i> year, he has to observe the additional intercalary month without compensation.</i>; therefore, it was necessary to say that “he is forbidden it and its intercalary [month]”.
Rebbi Abin in the name of Rebbi Hila: That is only if he vowed before they intercalated. But if they intercalated and then he vowed, that is not so<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">If he knew that there would be a Second Adar and he vowed until the end of Adar, that means the end of the Second Adar. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 63" href="/Nedarim.63">Babli, 63a/b</a>, differentiates between whether the vower knew that the year would have an intercalary month or whether he did not know (in the absence of a published calendar).</i>. Is it no different for rent of houses<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">If the house was let for a year. Does this mean 12 months if at the time of the contract it was not declared that the next year would have 13 months?</i>? If one said, the First Adar, and the other one says, the Second Adar, they should split the intercalary month<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">The parties come before the court in matters of a lease that was not supported by a written contract. The owner claims to have leased the house for 12 months, the renter claims it was for a year. Monetary disputes which cannot be decided because there are no documents are resolved by splitting the difference, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.1.1.1">Mishnah <i>Baba Meṣi‘a</i> 1:1</a>.</i>. Come and see, for vows you have no problem but for money matters you have a problem? Rebbi Hila said, that is, if they intercalated and after that he leased. But if he leased and after that they intercalated, that is not so<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The standard lease contract for rental property is one year. In the absence of proof to the contrary, one follows the standard contract.</i>. And in matters of documents one writes First Adar, Second Adar, only that for Second Adar one writes תניין<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">The Aramaic version; the Babli agrees in the name of R. Jehudah (63a). According to R. Jehudah, a mention of אדר ת̇ is documentary proof of a reference to Second Adar. The latter statement is missing in the Babli.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, for Second Adar one writes ת̇ and that is enough.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Jehudah says, if one said ‘a <i>qônām</i> that I shall not taste wine until Passover has come’, he is forbidden only until the night of Passover since he intended only until the time everybody drinks wine<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">While the essence of a festival of pilgrimage is the holiday sacrifice which is possible only during daylight hours, the holiday starts in the evening and today “Passover” means the holiday starting with sunset on Nisan 14. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 8:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.8.2.1">Note 37</a> and the author’s <i>The Scholar’s</i> <i>Haggadah</i> (Northvale NJ 1995), pp. 185–191.</i>.<br>If one said, “a <i>qônām</i> that I shall not taste meat until the fast<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">“Fast” without qualification always refers to the Day of Atonement, from the evening of Tishre 9 to the evening of Tishre 10. Since he is obligated to eat in the later hours of the afternoon of Tishre 9 to prepare for the fast, he intended the vow to terminate then.</i>,’ he is forbidden only until the evening before the fast since he intended only until the time everybody eats meat.<br>His son Rebbi Yose said, if one said ‘a <i>qônām</i> that I shall not taste garlic until the Sabbath,’ he is forbidden only until Friday evening, since he intended only until the time everybody eats garlic<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 3:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.3.7.1">Mishnah 3:10</a>.</i>.<br>If one says to his friend: A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not have any usufruct from you if you do not come and take for your children a <i>kor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">A volume of 30 <i>se’ah,</i> equal to Ezechiel’s <i>bat;</i> 384 liter.</i> of wheat and two amphoras of wine, he<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">The recipient can declare that it is as if he had received the amount stipulated and by this free the vower from his vow.</i> can undo his vow without referring to a Sage by saying, you did that only to honor me, that is my honor. Similarly, if one says to his friend: A <i>qônām</i> that you shall not have any usufruct from me if you do not come and give to my child a <i>kor</i> of wheat and two amphoras of wine. Rebbi Meïr says, he is forbidden until he gives, but the Sages say, this one also can undo his<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">In this case it is his own vow.</i> vow without referring to a Sage by saying, it is as if I received it.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The first case also is in dispute<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">R. Meїr disagrees also in the first case and requires the vow to be fulfilled.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira said, they disagree when it was undetermined. <sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Most of this text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 8:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.8.3">Halakhah 3:3, Notes 94–95</a>.</i> Where do we hold? If each one of them said, because of my honor, everybody agrees that he is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">They cannot agree on the essence of the vow; each one insists it is made in his own honor. In that case, what either one of them says is irrelevant for the other.</i>. If one of them said, because of my honor, and the other said, I said it in your honor, everybody agrees he is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">This is the first case of the Mishnah, and R. Meїr will agree that there is no vow.</i>. But we hold if it is undetermined<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">The maker of the vow does not indicate whether he agrees or disagrees with the interpretation of the vow by his friend. Then R. Meїr will hold that what the other party says has no influence on the vower.</i>. Rebbi says, undetermined is as if [every] one said, because of my honor. But the Sages say, undetermined is as if one said, because of my honor, and the other said, I said it in your honor.
Rebbi Yasa asked before Rebbi Joḥanan: Is our Mishnah from Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel, since Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, any obstacle that is not from her side does not invalidate the bill of divorce<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 7:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.7.6.1">Mishnah <i>Giṭṭin</i> 7:6</a>. A man gives his wife (who desires a divorce) a bill of divorce under the condition that she nurse his child for two years or that she serve his father for two years. If the child dies in the meantime or his father refuses to be served by her while she did nothing to make him angry, the anonymous majority holds that the condition was not satisfied and the bill of divorce invalid. But Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel holds that if she fulfilled the condition to the best of her ability, the divorce is valid. R. Yasa wanted to say, that Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel also will hold that a vow can be considered fulfilled if the second party refuses to accept the service.</i>? He said to him, here it is different because of opening the vow<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah starts the topic of the next Chapter, the conditions under which a vow can be invalidated. The main condition is that one find “an opening of remorse”, that a person would not have made the vow had he considered certain implications of it. These considerations are irrelevant for bills of divorce.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, but do we not deal with vows which do not need dissolution by a Sage<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">The topic discussed here is not that of the next Chapter.</i>? Rebbi Jonah said, we deal not only with one who made his vow dependent on something<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">This goes back to the previous paragraph. One can extend the argument given for the first case of the Mishnah to that of the second case and support the argument of R. Ze‘ira that R. Meїr and the Sages both hold that the two cases are parallel.</i>. So far it is if he wants to give him something. But in the case that he does not want to give to him<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">The second case of the Mishnah, since the person from whom the other vowed usufruct is providing the argument that the intention of the vow has been fulfilled. A different interpretation is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 24a" href="/Nedarim.24a">Babli, 24a</a>.</i>, he can say, I wanted only to examine you; but since I saw that it gives you pain, it is as if I had received.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If they<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">His family.</i> were importuning him to marry his sister’s daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Which is considered a good deed; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 62b-63a" href="/Yevamot.62b-63a">Babli <i>Yebamot</i> 62b/63a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 76b" href="/Sanhedrin.76b"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 76b</a>, based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 58:7" href="/Isaiah.58.7"><i>Is.</i> 58:7</a>.</i> and he said, a <i>qônām</i> if she ever has any usufruct from me, and similarly, a man who was about to divorce his wife and said, a <i>qônām</i> if my wife ever has any usufruct from me, they may have usufruct from him<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">The man who is about to divorce his wife cannot deprive her of the payments due to her as consequence of the divorce. He only forbade to himself any future sexual relations with his wife, implying that he could not remarry her after divorce.</i> since he intended only about matters of marriage.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If they were importuning him to marry his sister’s daughter,” etc. Rebbi Yose said, this is Rebbi Jehudah’s, since Rebbi Jehudah said<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 7:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.7.3.1">Mishnah 7:3</a>. The state of mind of the vower decides the meaning of his vow.</i>, “everything refers to the vow.” “And similarly, a man who was about to divorce his wife.” Rebbi Yose said, this is Rebbi Jehudah’s, since Rebbi Jehudah said<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 7:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.7.3.1">Mishnah 7:3</a>. The state of mind of the vower decides the meaning of his vow.</i>, “everything refers to the vow.”

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody was importuning his neighbor that he should eat at his place and that one said, a <i>qônām</i> that I shall not enter your house nor taste a drop of cold water from you, he is permitted to enter his house and to drink cold water there since he intended only about eating and drinking.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody was importuning his neighbor that he should eat at his place,” etc. Rebbi Yose said, this implies that if one brings his neighbor to swear that he will not eat, this one may eat of that neighbor<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">He may eat at his place after the dinner to which he was invited had passed without his participation. The interpretation is that of S. Lieberman (<i>Tosefta kiFshutah Nedarim</i> p. 464) who follows R. Simḥa of Dwinsk in reading חבר for עבר because of the well-documented collapse of gutturals in Galilean speech. The restriction of the vow to the one intended formal dinner is also explicit in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 4:8-9" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).4.8-9">Tosephta (4:8–9</a>) (even though the scenario there is slightly different) and in Maimonides (<i>Hilkhot Nedarim</i> 8:10). The Babli does not discuss the Mishnah.</i>.

Chapter 9



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Eliezer says, one opens<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">If a person comes to a Sage to ask about a vow, the Sage may find him “an opening of remorse” by telling him that his parents must be ashamed that their son is one of the wicked who make vows (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.1.1">Chapter 1, Note 9</a>).</i> for a man by the honor of his father and mother, but the Sages forbid it. Rebbi Ṣadoq said, before one opens by the honor of his father and mother one should open by the honor of the Omnipresent<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Who in general does not approve of vows.</i>; then there are no vows<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">If the Sage said, did you really want to make a vow to be called a sinner before God, everybody would say No, and every vow would be dissolved.</i>. The Sages agree with Rebbi Eliezer that if was a matter between a man and his father and mother<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">For example, if the son forbade all usufruct from his property to his parents (<i>Mat.</i> 15:5), it is appropriate for the Sage to point out to the son that he sins against the commandment to honor father and mother.</i>, that one opens for him by the honor of his father and mother.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Rebbi Eliezer says, one opens for a man,” etc. The rabbis say, a man resists the honor of his father and mother<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">It seems to be confirmed by the third sentence that one has to read “a man <i>does not</i> resist the honor of his parents.” This means that if the vower is told that his vow infringes on his duty to honor his parents, he immediately will agree to rescind the vow even though he has no remorse for making the vow. Then the annulment of the vow would be illegal.</i>. Rebbi Eliezer says, sometimes he resists, sometimes he does not resist<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Then it is not sure that the avowed remorse is faked.</i>. Rebbi Eliezer agrees that after their death he does not resist<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The Sage certainly cannot invoke the memory of his parents or that of his teacher.</i>. Everybody agrees that nobody resists the honor of his teacher, as we stated<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Abot</i> 4:15.</i>: “And the fear of your teacher shall be like the fear of Heaven.”
“Then there are no vows.” Let there be no vows! But is it not written<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:2" href="/Numbers.30.2"><i>Num.</i> 30:2</a>, the start of the chapter on vows.</i>: “Moses spoke to the heads of the tribes.” He hung the chapter on the heads of the tribes, that they could dissolve their vows<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 3:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.3.1.2">Chapter 3:1, Note 6</a>.</i>. If you say so<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">That there should be no vows.</i>, it turns out that you uproot the chapter of vows from the Torah.
Rebbi Jeremiah asked: Since you say, one opens for him by the honor of his father and mother; in things between him and the Omnipresent, one does not open for him by the honor of the Omnipresent<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Since this is not mentioned in the Mishnah. Since “the Omnipresent” is a name of God, the scribe inserted an apostrophe to make a later correction or erasure possible.</i>. But since in matters between him and his father and mother one opens for him by the honor of his father and mother; similarly, in things between him and the Omnipresent should one not open for him by the honor of the Omnipresent? What is the honor of the Omnipresent? For example, that I shall not make a tabernacle<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:42" href="/Leviticus.23.42"><i>Lev.</i> 23:42</a>.</i>, that I shall not take a <i>lulab</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">One of the “four kinds”, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:40" href="/Leviticus.23.40"><i>Lev.</i> 23:40</a>.</i><i>,</i> that I shall not put on phylacteries<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:9" href="/Exodus.13.9"><i>Ex.</i> 13:9</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:16" href="/Exodus.13.16">16</a>.</i>. One understands that he does it for his own benefit. As in the following<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 35:7" href="/Job.35.7"><i>Job</i> 35:7</a>. The reward of good deeds is purely the benefit of the doer.</i>: “If you are just, what are you giving Him?” “If you sinned, what would you do to Him?<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 35:6" href="/Job.35.6"><i>Job</i> 35:6</a>. The mention of this verse and the sermon following are induced by the preceding quote.</i>” Rebbi Yannai said, one who listens to his urges is as if he worshipped idols. What is the reason? “In yourself there shall be no alien force; do not bow down to a foreign god.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 81:10" href="/Psalms.81.10"><i>Ps.</i> 81:10</a>. The alien forces are the evil urges in a person; they are put in parallel with idol worship. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 105b" href="/Shabbat.105b">Babli, <i>Šabbat</i> 105b</a>, the statement is attributed to R. Abin.</i>”
Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish provided an opening: If you had known that one who makes a vow is as if he put a neck-iron<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 7:3:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.7.3.9"><i>Berakhot</i> 7:5, Note 116</a>.</i> on his neck, would you have made the vow? It is as if a gang of prisoners<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Reading with Jastrow and Krauss קֻסְטוֹדִייָה for קֻסְטוֹרִייָה, Latin <i>custodia</i> “watch, guard; gang of prisoners”.</i> was passing by, he saw that there was one unused neck-iron and put his head into it! “To bind a prohibition onto himself<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:3" href="/Numbers.30.3"><i>Num.</i> 30:3</a>.</i>”, as you say<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 40:1" href="/Jeremiah.40.1"><i>Jer.</i> 40:1</a>.</i>, “he was bound with chains.” Rebbi Jonathan<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 22a" href="/Nedarim.22a">Babli, 22a</a>, R. Nathan.</i> provided an opening: If you had known that one who makes a vow is like one who builds an idolatrous altar and one who continues in it is like one who sacrifices there, would you have made the vow? That is difficult to understand. Idolatry is a capital crime but vows are a simple prohibition; how can you say that? You have only what Rebbi Yannai said, one who listens to his urges is as if he worshipped idols. Rebbi Isaac provided an opening: If you had known that one who makes a vow is like one who takes a sword and sticks it in his heart, would you have made the vow? “Some talk bluntly like sword piercings.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 12:18" href="/Proverbs.12.18"><i>Prov.</i> 12:18</a>.</i>” Rebbi Ḥanina from Sepphoris in the name of Rebbi Phineas: It does not say “piercing” but “sword piercings”. For example, one who made a vow not to eat a loaf. Woe if he eats, woe if he does not eat. If he eats he transgresses his vow. If he does not eat he sins against himself<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Since a person who mortifies himself is called a sinner, <i>Sifry Num.</i>30, <i>Nazir</i> 1:5 (51c, 1. 58).</i>. What can he do? He goes to a Sage who will dissolve his vow, “but the speech of Sages is healing<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 12:18" href="/Proverbs.12.18"><i>Prov.</i> 12:18</a>.</i>. Rebbi Eudaimon in the name of Rebbi Isaac: Is it not enough what the Torah forbade you that you want to forbid other things for yourself? “To forbid a prohibition<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:3" href="/Numbers.30.3"><i>Num.</i> 30:3</a>.</i>”.
Rebbi Joḥanan provided an opening by having second thoughts: “If it were not for that, would he have come<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan took the fact that a person came to him to ask about a vow as proof of repentance and dissolved the vow without further inquiry.</i>?” Are second thoughts not something new<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 9:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.9.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>, the Sages forbid to take future changes into consideration.</i>? Rebbi Hila said, second thoughts are a common occurrence<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">They are built into the original vow.</i>. In this kind, Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 22b" href="/Nedarim.22b">Babli, 22b</a>, R. Simeon ben Rebbi.</i> did not find an opening for his own vow until one of the Galilean Elders came; some say, that one was Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">One of the students of R. Meïr.</i>. He took him from here and put him there; he took him from here and put him there, until finally he put him into the sun and told him to check his garments for fleas. They said to him, if you had known that this old man would treat you in such a way, would you have made the vow? He said, No. He permitted him. They asked, from where do you have this? He said to them, I was Meïr’s servant both times when he fled<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Most commentators want to read שנית “the second time” instead of שנים “twice” since the first time he had to flee was when he received ordination after the Bar Kokhba war when giving or receiving ordination was an act of rebellion against the Roman government and a capital crime. The reason for his second exile is not known; it is known that he died in Asia Minor (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 9:3:15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.9.3.15"><i>Kilaim</i> 9:4, Note 128</a>). If he left Galilee because Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel had excluded him from the Academy it was not “fleeing”. It is unlikely that (1) R. Meïr had a student with him when he fled the first time, and (2) that the same student would have been with him both in his youth and in his old age.</i>, but some say, Rebbi Meïr’s walking stick was in my hand and it teaches me knowledge.
Rebbi Jeremiah had his vow dissolved but kept it. Whether it was because he did not trust him who dissolved it<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">He was worried that the opening which another rabbi found for him was really invalid and that the other invalidated the vow only to please the head of the Academy.</i>, we do not know. Whether it was because one has urges only for things forbidden to oneself<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The same argument in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 6:4:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.6.4.2-5"><i>Yoma</i> 6:4</a> (43c 1. 21).</i>, we do not know. When Rebbi Jeremiah was not in the mood to judge, he said, my eyes are weak. “Following their pronouncements shall be all quarrels and all disfiguration<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:5" href="/Deuteronomy.21.5"><i>Deut.</i> 21:5</a>.</i>.” This compares quarrels to disfigurations<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Different detailed discussions of this comparison are in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 34b" href="/Sanhedrin.34b">Babli, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 34b</a>, and <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 208.</i>. Since disfigurations are “for all the Cohen sees”<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Lev. 13:12.</i>, so also quarrels are “for all the Cohen sees”.
Rebbi Mana made a vow away from his father’s wine<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Not to have any benefit from his father’s wine. It seems that R. Mana here is R. Mana II, the colleague of R. Shammai. He was the son of R. Jonah; one has to wonder why the father appears without his name.</i>. His father came to him and said to him, would you have made that vow if you had known that I am hurt by it? He said, no. He dissolved for him. Where do we hold? If he said, my benefit [is forbidden] to my father; is that not what Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman said in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: One forces the son to support his father<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">This parallels Mishnah 12; a person cannot get rid of monetary obligations by making a vow. But even if R. Mana had forbidden his own wine on R. Jonah (which seems to be excluded by the formulation of the vow), the head of the Academy certainly could buy his own wine without taking from charity and forcing the overseers of charity to have regress on his son.</i>. But we must hold that he said, my father’s benefit [is forbidden] to me. Rebbi Mana made a vow and came to Rebbi Shammai, who said to him, would you have made that vow if you had known that people do not want to deal with you since you are a vower? He said, no, and he dissolved for him.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> In addition, Rebbi Eliezer said, one finds an opening in changed circumstances, but the Sages forbid it. How is this? If he said, a <i>qônām</i> that I shall not benefit from Mr. X, who then becomes a public scribe<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Or any public official whose services he might need.</i> or who marries off his son to one of [the vower’s] relatives<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">And he wants to go to the wedding feast which, according to the custom of the times, was paid for by the groom’s family.</i>, and he said, if I had known that he will become a public scribe or marry off his son to a relative, I would not have vowed; or if he said, a <i>qônām</i> that I shall not enter this house and it was turned into a synagogue and he said, if I had known that it would become a synagogue, I would not have vowed; Rebbi Eliezer permits but the Sages prohibit<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Since it could not have been in the vower’s mind at the moment he made the vow.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “In addition, Rebbi Eliezer said, one finds an opening in changed circumstances,” etc. Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 64b" href="/Nedarim.64b">Babli, 64b</a>, Rav Ḥisda</i>: Rebbi Eliezer learned from Moses, to whom the Holy One, praise to Him, provided an opening by changed circumstances. The Holy One, praise to Him, said to him: If you had known that “all the men who want to kill you have died,” would you have vowed<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The background is in <i>Tanḥuma Šemot</i> 12,<i>Tanḥuma Buber Šemot</i> 11, on <i>Ex</i>. 2:21, quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 65a" href="/Nedarim.65a">Babli 65a</a>: “Moses swore that he would dwell with the man” deriving וַיּוֹאֵל from אלה “to swear”, not from יאל “to agree, to decide”. (The problem naturally is that the form וַיּוֹאֵל is <i>qal</i> if derived from יאל but <i>hiph‘il</i> if derived from אלה and would have to be translated “he (Reuel) made Moses swear to dwell with the man,” which should require a <i>nota accusativi</i> before “Moses”.} Since Moses swore in Midyan that he would stay there when he thought that he never could return to Egypt, he had to return there to have his vow dissolved.</i>? But did they really die? Were they not Dathan and Abiram<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The identification of “the evil one” in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 2:13" href="/Exodus.2.13"><i>Ex.</i> 2:13</a> is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 64b" href="/Nedarim.64b">Babli, 64b</a>, and <i>Tanḥuma Šemot</i> 10; identifying all anonymous evildoers in <i>Ex.</i> and <i>Num.</i> with Dathan and Abiram who were called “evil ones” by Moses (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 16:26" href="/Numbers.16.26"><i>Num.</i>16:26</a>).</i>? Only, they became poor<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, nobody in government would listen to their calumnies. In the Babli, this is attributed to R. Simeon ben Laqish.</i>.
Rebbi Jeremiah said, what you say is only that there are changed circumstances before the matter is discussed<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">It always is possible by discussing the matter to find an aspect of changed circumstances which has not changed from the time the vow was made.</i>. The force of Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">It is not stated what R. Yose (the Amora’s) position is but the last sentence of the paragraph implies that R. Yose opposes R. Jeremiah.</i> is from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 5:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.5.4.1">Mishnah 5:4</a>. When a group of <i>nezirim</i> came from Babylonia to Palestine shortly after the destruction of the Temple, he asked them whether they would have vowed to be <i>nezirim</i> had they known that the Temple would be destroyed and they would permanently be forbidden wine and grapes and contact with corpses since the termination of the state of <i>nazir</i> depends on the Temple ceremony; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 6:20" href="/Numbers.6.20"><i>Num.</i> 6:20</a>.</i>: “That was the error of Naḥum the Mede.” What was his error? That he found them an opening by changed circumstances. “Naḥum the Mede said to them: ‘Would you have made a vow to become <i>nezirim</i> if you had known that the Temple would be destroyed at some future time?’ ” Rebbi Ze‘ira said, the following he should have said to them: Did you not know that the earlier prophets had prophesied while the Temple was standing that eventually it would be destroyed? Then there are no changed circumstances. Rebbi Hila said, still it is changed circumstances. They could have said to him, we knew it, but it seemed to us that this referred to the far future<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 12:27" href="/Ezekiel.12.27"><i>Ez.</i> 12:27</a>.</i>: “The vision he sees is for many years; for faraway times he prophesies.” It turns out that Rebbi Jeremiah parallels Rebbi Ze‘ira and Rebbi Yose parallels Rebbi Hila.
(As)<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that the additional ד is a scribal error since this paragraph has no connection with the previous one.</i> we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.3.3.1">Mishnah <i>Megillah</i> 3:3</a>. R. Meïr holds that a synagogue can be sold only with a clause that would let the community reacquire the building if it so chooses. The Sages permit the sale to be absolute as long as the building is not used for undignified purposes. (According to a popular tradition, the <i>Altneuschul</i> synagogue in Prague is <i>Al-Tnai-Schul</i>, a synagogue built <i>on condition</i> that on the arrival of the Messiah it could be sold without restrictions.}</i>: “One sells a synagogue only conditionally”. <sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">From here on, the text is from <i>Megillah</i> 3:1 (73b 1. 38).</i> So far if it was built as a synagogue. How is the situation if the building was first built for profane use and then dedicated? Let us hear from the following: “A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not enter this house and it was turned into a synagogue.” That implies that if it was built as a courtyard and afterwards was dedicated that it becomes holy. How? When does it become holy, immediately or when it is used? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Megillah (Lieberman) 2:13" href="/Tosefta_Megillah_(Lieberman).2.13">Tosephta <i>Megillah</i> 2:13</a>.</i>: “If somebody makes a chest for a Torah scroll, or wrappings for a Torah scroll, before they were used for a Torah scroll they may be used for private use; after they were used for a Torah scroll they may not be used for private use.” Since these were made for holy use but became holy only when used, that which was built as a courtyard not so much more<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The same conclusion in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 26b" href="/Megillah.26b">Babli, <i>Megillah</i> 26b</a>.</i>? What is the status of those if they were made for profane use and then dedicated? They become holy<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The text of <i>Megillah</i> is very much condensed here.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Meïr says, there are things like changed circumstances which are not really changed circumstances<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">There are changed circumstances which are admissible to annul a vow.</i>, and the Sagesagree with him<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">This is the reading in many Mishnah mss. and Gaonic and early rabbinic sources but not in the existing Babli mss. and the printed editions which read: The Sages do not agree with him. The <i>gemara</i> in the Babli seems to presuppose the Yerushalmi Mishnah. For details cf. <i>The Babylonian Talmud with variant readings, Nedarim II,</i> ed. M. Hershler, Jerusalem 1991, p. קנב, Note 49.</i>. How is this? He said, a <i>qônām</i> that I shall not marry this woman for her father is evil, and they told him that he died or that he repented; a <i>qônām</i> that I shall not enter this house because it has a bad dog inside, or a snake; they said to him the dog died, the snake was killed; these there are things like changed circumstances which are not changed circumstances, and the Sages agree with him.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Rebbi Meïr says, there are things like changed circumstances”. Samuel says, because of an erroneous vow; the dog was already dead, the snake had already been killed<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Samuel restricts the Mishnah to the case that the dog was already dead when the vow was made. Then it is obvious that the vow would not have been made had the vower known all the facts. But if the vow was justified at the moment it was made, Samuel would deny that the death of the dog was reason enough to nullify the vow for the future.</i>. Rebbi Hila in the name of Rebbi Eleazar, because he is like someone who makes his vow dependent on something<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">They hold that the vow can be nullified at the death of the dog because the reason for the vow had been stated at the beginning. As the example shows, if the vower had said, I shall not enter the house as long as there is a bad dog there, the vow would automatically be voided at the dog’s death. But because the reason was not formally stated as a condition, the vow mentioned in the Mishnah needs to be voided by a Sage.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 65a" href="/Nedarim.65a">Babli, 65a</a>, the opinion of Samuel is attributed to R. Joḥanan and that of R. Hila to Rav Huna, with the discussion supporting Rav Huna.</i>: A <i>qônām</i> that I shall have no benefit from this man as long as he wears black garments<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The sign of a person engaged in sexual misbehavior; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Moed Katan 17a" href="/Moed_Katan.17a">Babli <i>Mo‘ed Qaṭan</i> 17a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chagigah 16a" href="/Chagigah.16a"><i>Ḥagigah</i> 16a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 40a" href="/Kiddushin.40a"><i>Qiddušin</i> 40a</a>.</i>. If he wore white, he would be permitted to him. Rebbi Ze‘ira in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: That one does not need the permission of a Sage.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> In addition, Rebbi Meïr said, one opens for him with what is written in the Torah. One says to him<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">A person who vowed that another could not have any benefit from him.</i>, if you had realized that you sin against “you shall not take revenge<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:18" href="/Leviticus.19.18"><i>Lev</i>. 19:18</a>.</i>”, “you shall not nurse hatred<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:18" href="/Leviticus.19.18"><i>Lev</i>. 19:18</a>.</i>”, “you shall not hate your brother in your heart<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:17" href="/Leviticus.19.17"><i>Lev</i>. 19:17</a>.</i>, “you shall love your neighbor as yourself<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:18" href="/Leviticus.19.18"><i>Lev</i>. 19:18</a>.</i>”, “let your brother live with you<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:36" href="/Leviticus.25.36"><i>Lev</i>. 25:36</a>.</i>”, maybe he would become poor and you cannot provide for him! If he said, if I had realized this, I would not have vowed, he is permitted.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It is written: “You should not take revenge or nurse hatred against your fellow countrymen.” How is that? He was cutting meat and the knife fell down on his hand. Should he go and hit his hand<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Should he punish the hand which held the knife for injuring his other hand? Since all Jews are responsible for one another (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 27b" href="/Sanhedrin.27b">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i> 27b</a>), hitting one’s neighbor is like hitting himself.</i>?
“You shall love your neighbor as yourself<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:18" href="/Leviticus.19.18"><i>Lev</i>. 19:18</a>.</i>”. Rebbi Aqiba says, that is a great principle in the Torah. Ben Azzai says, “this is the book of the descent of man<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 5:1" href="/Genesis.5.1"><i>Gen</i>. 5:1</a>, which negates the role of race in the creation of man.</i>” is a more important principle<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifra Qedos̄im Pereq</i> 4(12). A fuller version is in <i>Gen. rabba</i> 24(8): Ben Azzai says, “this is the book of the descent of man” is a great principle in the Torah. Rebbi Aqiba says, “you shall love your neighbor as yourself” is a great principle in the Torah. That you should not say, since I was insulted, another should be insulted with me; since I was cursed, another should be cursed with me. Rebbi Tanḥuma {last generation of Galilean Amoraim} said, if you did this, know Whom you are insulting: “In God’s image He made him.”</i>.
“Maybe he would become poor”. Are these not changed circumstances<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">And therefore excluded as argument following <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 9:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.9.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>.</i>? Rebbi Ze‘ira said, poverty is frequent. As the following: A man had a lawsuit against a rich man. He wanted to be judged before Rav; Rav sent for [the rich man], who said: For this one I should come to court? If all the camels of Arabia came they could not carry all the leather sacks<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Aramaic plural of Greek κώρυκος “leather bag”.</i> containing my mortgages<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ὑποθήκη; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 7:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.7.1.4"><i>Yebamot</i> 7:1, Note 25</a>.</i>! [Rav] heard this and said, what does this one pride himself with what is not his! There should be depreciation of it! Immediately there came a royal decree<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κέλευσις “command, order”.</i> that he and his property should belong to the treasury<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ταμεῖον “treasure, storage”, used in the sense of the king’s treasury. The mixture of Aramaic and Greek in this popular tale may be an example of “hellenistic speech”; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 7:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.7.1.5"><i>Sota</i> 7:1, Note 22</a>.</i>. He [the rich man] came before Rav and said to him, pray for me that my personality<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Lest he become a slave of the treasury.</i> could be restored. He prayed, and it was restored to him.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One creates an opening for a man with his wife’s <i>ketubah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The marriage contract, here taken as technical term for the sum the groom promises to pay his wife in case of a divorce. The minimal sum for a first marriage of the woman is 200 <i>zuz</i>, defined in Mishnah <i>Peah</i> 8:8 as sufficient capital to lift its owner above the poverty level and disqualify him from public charity. The <i>zuz</i> is usually identified with the silver denarius.</i>. It happened that one vowed usufruct from his wife<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">This makes it impossible for him to live with his wife and forces him to divorce her.</i> whose <i>ketubah</i> was 400 denar. She<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babylonian Mishnah, he came before R. Aqiba.</i> came before Rebbi Aqiba who obliged him to give her her <i>ketubah</i>. He said, Rebbi, my father left 800 denar. My brother took 400 and I 400, would it not be enough if she take 200<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The minimum amount admissible as <i>ketubah</i>.</i> and I 200? Rebbi Aqiba told him, even if you have to sell the hair on your head, you will pay her <i>ketubah</i>. He said to him, if I had known that, I would not have vowed. Rebbi Aqiba freed him<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">From his vow so he could remain married. One might also translate: R. Aqiba voided it [the vow]. In the Babylonian Mishnah, R. Aqiba permitted her to remain with her husband.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One creates an opening for a man with his wife’s <i>ketubah</i>” etc. Does one collect from movables<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">It is talmudic theory that debts covered by a document (such as a <i>ketubah</i> or a mortgage) must be satisfied by foreclosing real estate (unless otherwise stated in the document of indebtedness). It was only after the Arab conquest, when the Jews in Babylonia had lost their real estate holdings, that the Gaonic authorities decreed that every <i>ketubah</i> must be paid in currency and/or movables. How could R. Aqiba order the man to cut off and sell his hair to pay his ex-wife since cut hair clearly is movable? (The Babli explains that the man should hand over all his real estate to his wife and sell his hair to buy food for himself.)</i>? Rebbi Abba said, even if one could say, one collects from movables, one tells him to pay<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that one has here a disagreement of the two Talmudim (explanation of I. Eisenstein in עמודי ירושלים <i>ad loc</i>.). The Babli sticks to its thesis that a <i>ketubah</i> is satisfied only by real estate and holds that a partially satisfied <i>ketubah</i> represents a title to all future property the ex-husband might acquire. The Yerushalmi holds that if the husband cannot satisfy his divorcee’s claim with real estate, one tells him to satisfy it with any other means available.</i>. Rebbi Manisha asked, could one tell the heirs to collect from pledged property<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">If this would refer to real estate, the question would be trivial. Mortgaged or otherwise pledged property is part of the inheritance. Standard mortgages (including <i>ketubah</i>) are not on a specified piece of land but on all holdings of the debtor. If the debtor dies, the entire property comes to the heirs from whom the mortgage holder can require satisfaction under the supervision of the court.<br>The quote with which the question is answered shows clearly that the issue is about movables. Then it cannot be a question of mortgages other than a <i>ketubah</i>. The question is whether the court can order the heirs to honor their father’s obligation to his ex-wife from movables, <i>to let her collect</i> from movables.</i>? Rebbi Abba Mari said, the Mishnah says that one does not say so, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 9:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.9.2.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 9:2</a>. In the Mishnah, R. Aqiba states that the heirs take all movables since any creditor would have to swear that his claim was not in any way satisfied by the deceased but the heirs take everything without oath. (R. Tarphon disagrees and would give the widow preference).</i>: “It should be given to the heirs since everybody has to swear but the heirs do not have to swear.” What does one make her swear? There, they say, from straw I did not collect, from his person I did collect<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">“There” is Babylonia. It is difficult to understand what is meant. It seems that she has to swear that her claim was not satisfied even in an indirect way.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One opens about festive days and Sabbaths<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">That the vower could not enjoy Sabbaths and holidays.</i>. In earlier times, they said that these days are permitted but the rest forbidden, until Rebbi Aqiba came and taught that a vow which was partially voided is totally voided.<br>How is this? ‘A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not benefit any one of you,’ if one was permitted, all are permitted. ‘That I shall not benefit this one and this one and this one,’ if the first one becomes permitted, all are permitted; if the last one becomes permitted, he is permitted and the others are prohibited<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>n</i>-th term in a sequence cannot appear before the (<i>n</i>-l)-th. If there is no (<i>n</i>-l)-th there can be no <i>n</i>-th. If he had said: all of you, <i>viz</i>., X and Y and Z, the order would have been irrelevant. Since he says, X and also Y and also Z, the later depends on the former. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 66a" href="/Nedarim.66a">Babli, 66a</a>, notes that only R. Simeon considers this three separate vows; the Yerushalmi, which quotes R. Simeon elsewhere [e. g., <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 4:3:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.4.3.2-6"><i>Nazir</i> 4:3</a> (53b)], does not mention it here but seemingly restricts the principle of R. Aqiba to vows declared to concern a group; cf. also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 9:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.9.6.2">Note 89</a>.</i>. ‘That I shall not benefit, a <i>qorbān</i> for this one, a <i>qorbān</i> for that one’; each single one needs a separate opening<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">While these statements all form one sentence, they represent two separate vows.</i>.<br>‘A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not taste wine, for wine is bad for the intestines.’ If they told him, but old [wine] is good for the intestines, old [wine] was permitted; not only old wine is permitted but all wines. ‘A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not taste onion, for onion is bad for the heart.’ If they told him, but the rural kind is good for the heart, rural [onion] was permitted; not only rural is permitted but all onions. It happened that Rebbi Meїr permitted all onions to him.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One opens about festive days and Sabbaths,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 5:1" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).5.1">Tosephta 5:1</a>, this entered some Mishnah texts in the Babli.</i>: “If one<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">In a series of people who should have no benefit.</i> was permitted, all subsequent ones are permitted, all preceding ones are forbidden.” It was stated in the name of Rebbi Nathan<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 5:1" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).5.1">Tosephta 5:1</a>, quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 26b-27a" href="/Nedarim.26b-27a">Babli, 26b/27a</a>. There, R. Aqiba disagrees and upholds his principle while the Yerushalmi docs not mention R. Aqiba and gives the impression that it accepts the position of R. Nathan.</i>: “There exists a type of vow which may be partly voided and the rest confirmed. How is this? One made a vow [not to eat] from a fig basket which contained <i>benot s̄eba</i>’<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Light green delicacy figs; <i>Ma‘serot</i> 2:8, Note 135.</i> figs. If he said, if I had known that it contained <i>benot s̄eba</i>’ figs, I would not have made the vow, he is permitted the <i>benot s̄eba</i>’ figs and not only these but all figs. But if he said, if I had known that it contained <i>benot s̄eba</i>’ figs, I would not have extended my vow to <i>benot s̄eba</i>’ figs, only <i>benot s̄eba</i>’ figs are permitted to him.”

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One finds an opening for a man with his own honor and that of his children. One tells him<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">A man who made a vow which forces a divorce.</i>, if you had known that tomorrow one will say of you, it is the habit of this man to divorce his wife, and about your daughters one will say, they are daughters of a divorcee, what did the mother of these do to get herself divorced? If he said, if I had known that it is so I would not have made the vow, then it is dissolved.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One finds an opening for a man about his own honor,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously, this is the Yerushalmi tradition. The (Babylonian) <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 5:6" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).5.6">Tosephta, 5:6</a> attributes the text of the Mishnah to R. Jehudah ben Bathyra.</i>: Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra said, one finds an opening for a man exclusively about his own honor. As the following? A woman vowed [not to have benefit] from her daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">She did not want to be supported by her daughter.</i>. She came to Rebbi Joḥanan who said to her, if you had known that your daughter gets a bad reputation<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">People will say that she does not care for her mother.</i>, would you have made the vow? She said, no, and he permitted her. Is that not the Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Why is it worthwhile to record a decision which follows the Mishnah closely? [In the Babli (a total of 24 times) R. Joḥanan is reported to hold that an anonymous Mishnah <i>always</i> represents practice; in the Yerushalmi he holds <i>in general</i> that practice follows an anonymous <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Yevamot 4:11" href="/Mishnah_Yevamot.4.11">Mishnah (<i>Yebamot</i> 4:11, Note 177</a>)].</i>: “One finds an opening for a man about his own honor and that of his children”? That you should not think one follows Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> ‘A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not marry the ugly Miss X, and she is beautiful, black and she is white, short and she is tall, he is permitted. Not because she was ugly and became beautiful<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">This would be a change of circumstances, not admissible in an annulment.</i>, black and she became white, short and she became tall, but because the vow was erroneous. It happened that one<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Being pressured by his family to marry his niece.</i> made a vow renouncing benefit from his sister’s daughter. They brought her to Rebbi Ismael’s house and gave her a beauty treatment. Rebbi Ismael asked him, did you make your vow about this one? He said no, and Rebbi Ismael dissolved it<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">The vow.</i>. At that moment, Rebbi Ismael cried and said, the daughters of Israel are beautiful, but poverty disfigures them.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “ ‘A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not marry the ugly Miss X,’ ” etc. He made her a golden eye, a golden tooth<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 66b" href="/Nedarim.66b">Babli, 66b</a>, notes only that she had an ugly artificial tooth and R. Ismael replaced it by a gold tooth for which he paid himself. In the opinion of the Babli, R. Ismael denies that changed circumstances are no cause for annulment of a vow.</i>. So he said to him, acquire what is on her<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 5:6" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).5.6">Tosephta, 5:6</a>: “acquire her and her dresses”.</i>.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> And when Rebbi Ismael died the daughters of Israel did sing a dirge and say, ‘Daughters of Israel, cry about Rebbi Ismael’, as it is said about Saul: ‘Daughters of Israel, cry about Saul, etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">“Who clothes you in crimson with ornaments, who puts a golden jewel on your garments”, <a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 1:24" href="/II_Samuel.1.24"><i>2S.</i> 1:24</a>.</i>’
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “And when Rebbi Ismael died,” etc. It is written, ““Daughters of Israel, cry about Saul, etc.” Rebbi Jehudah and Rebbi Neḥemiah. One said, really daughters of Israel. For their husbands went to war and he provided them with food. Why does the verse say “who puts a golden jewel on your garments”?<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Is it not forbidden to any man, including the king, to give jewels to otherwise married women?</i> Jewellery looks good only on a supple body<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Having enough subcutaneous fat.</i>. The other one said, “the daughters of Israel” means the builders of Israel, the councils of Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">He bends the verse to avoid the appearance of questionable behavior, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 9:9:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.9.9.2">Note 102</a>.</i>. When he saw a group of fellows<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Demay.</i></i>, he gave them to eat and drink. What does the verse mean by “who puts a golden jewel on your garments”? That he heard the reason for a practice from a Sage and praised him.

Chapter 10



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Father and husband jointly dissolve the vows of a preliminarily married adolescent girl<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">An underage girl can be married off by her father. A woman becomes an adult in two steps. At age 12 (if she had developed two pubic hairs) she becomes an adult before the law but the father retains the right to marry her off and the right to her earnings for an additional 6 months, when she is called נַעֲרָה “adolescent girl”. After these 6 months she becomes an adult, בּוֹגֶרֶת “ripe”, is totally independent from her father, and has to marry on her own. The vow of a minor below age 11 is void. If she made a vow after age 11 (for a male after age 12), one has to investigate whether she understands the meaning and implications of a vow. If the investigation has a positive result, her vows are valid and, as far as vows are concerned, she has acquired the status of adolescent, even though in other legal aspects she remains a minor.<br>The chapter on vows states clearly that the father can dissolve his dependent daughter’s vows (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:4-6" href="/Numbers.30.4-6"><i>Num.</i> 30:4–6</a>). Similarly, the husband can dissolve the vows of his wife if she lives “in his house” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:11-15" href="/Numbers.30.11-15">vv. 11–15</a>). A preliminarily married girl (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 6:2:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.6.2.6"><i>Peah</i> 6:2 Note 46</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 4:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.4.1.6"><i>Demay</i> 4:1, Note 19</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 8:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.8.1.2"><i>Terumot</i> 8:1, Note 9</a>) remains under her father’s jurisdiction until she is taken to her husband’s house in the final ceremony. It is asserted that during her status as preliminarily married wife, the father can dissolve her vows since she is “in his house” and the husband is given special authority over her vows in vv. 7–9 which, therefore, are not duplicated in vv. 11–15.<br>The power of dissolution in any case is restricted to the day after the father or husband first was informed of the vow (vv. 6,9,13,15). But if one of them agreed to the vow within the allotted period, he can no longer object after that.</i>. If the father dissolved but not the husband, or the husband but not the father, it is not dissolved; one does not have to mention whether one of them confirmed it.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “An adolescent girl,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">A similar argument in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 67" href="/Nedarim.67">Babli, 67a/b</a>. In both Talmudim, the argument is amoraic.</i> It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:7" href="/Numbers.30.7"><i>Num.</i> 30:7</a>. The masoretic text reads הָיוֹ.</i>, “if she should be a man’s”. What are we speaking about? If a married one, it already is written<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:11" href="/Numbers.30.11"><i>Num.</i> 30:11</a>.</i> “if she vowed in her husband’s house”. If about an unmarried one, it already is written<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:4" href="/Numbers.30.4"><i>Num.</i> 30:4</a>.</i> “if she vows a vow to the Eternal”. Why does the verse say<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:7" href="/Numbers.30.7"><i>Num.</i> 30:7</a>. The masoretic text reads הָיוֹ.</i>, ““if she should be a man’s with her vows on her”? That refers to the preliminarily married adolescent girl whose vows are dissolved by father and husband. So far for vows which she vowed after she was prelinimarily married. Vows which she vowed before she was prelinimarily married? “With her vows on her,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:7" href="/Numbers.30.7"><i>Num.</i> 30:7</a>. The masoretic text reads הָיוֹ.</i>” to include the vows which come with her from her father’s house.
It was stated in the name of Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">If the material in the first paragraph is Amoraic, R. Eleazar is the Amora, bar Pedat. If the argument is tannaїtic, he is the Tanna, ben Shamua. R. Eleazar’s argument is paralleled in <i>Sifry Num.</i> 153 by R. Joshia, student of R. Ismael. The final argument in this paragraph is attributed to R. Ismael in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 68a" href="/Nedarim.68a">Babli, 68a</a>.</i>: “If she should be a man’s”<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:7" href="/Numbers.30.7"><i>Num.</i> 30:7</a>. The masoretic text reads הָיוֹ.</i>, the verse speaks about a preliminarily married adult girl<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">At 12 years and 6 months (or 6 months after she developed two pubic hairs) she permanently leaves her father’s <i>potestas.</i> He reads the verse as dealing with a case where there is no residual power of the father. But since vv. 11 ff. speak of the married woman, he must find a case of a preliminarily married woman free from her father.</i>. The colleagues say, Rebbi Eleazar says it correctly<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">His interpretation follows the wording of the verse more closely.</i>. Is it not difficult for Rebbi Eleazar: Did she not leave her father’s power the moment she became an adult<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Vv. 7–8 give the husband the right to dissolve vows which preceded the marriage. But vv. 11 ff. restrict the right of the husband to vows made during the marriage (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 10:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.10.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>).
