For the rabbis, the right to dissolve prior vows depends on the father’s collaboration. But R. Eleazar denies any participation to the father; why should the power of the preliminarily married husband be greater than that of the fully married one?</i>? Who may dissolve the vows of an orphan whose father had died<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">If the father had arranged his daughter’s preliminary marriage and then had died, the husband cannot dissolve his wife’s prior vows (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 10:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.10.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>) and, after the definitive marriage ceremony, he cannot dissolve prior vows. (The existence of undisclosed prior vows might be grounds for divorce.) An underage girl orphaned from her father can be married off by her mother and brothers, but that is only a rabbinic institution (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.2.1"><i>Yebamot</i>1:2, Note 118</a>). The husband can dissolve her vows only after the definitive marriage or after she became an adult. The latter case is the one dealt with by R. Eleazar.</i>? The husband dissolves<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">But only the vows made after the preliminary marriage and only after she has reached adulthood.</i>. It is difficult for the rabbis: If he may dissolve before she entered his power, is it not obvious [that he may dissolve] after she entered<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Why are vv. 11 ff. needed after vv. 7–9? That is a rhetorical question since the two cases are not comparable. For the rabbis, the husband dissolves the vow of the preliminarily married minor only in conjunction with the father, but he also dissolves vows made before the marriage. Once the woman is emancipated from her father, either by completed marriage or by becoming of age, the husband dissolves alone but only vows made during the marriage.</i>? How do the rabbis explain “with her vows on her”? What are we talking about? If about vows which she made before she was preliminarily married and she became preliminarily married, already the father and the husband had the power to dissolve<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">If they do not exercise their powers, they get no second chance. If the girl became an adult between preliminary and definitive marriage, the father lost his right of dissolution and the husband lost it with him. If the father died between preliminary and definitive marriages, the husband alone cannot dissolve; therefore, he cannot dissolve her vow after the definitive marriage when he lives with her.</i>. But we must talk about a vow which she made before her father died, then the father died<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Before she was preliminarily married. Then the father never had any right concurrent with the husband; the husband is not hindered by the father’s power.</i> and she became an adult. From where does Rebbi Eleazar obtain: “The father and the husband dissolve the vows of a preliminarily married adolescent girl.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Since he rejects the references given in the first paragraph.</i>” “Between a man and his wife.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:17" href="/Numbers.30.17"><i>Num.</i> 30:17</a>.</i>” That deals with the husband. The father? “Between a father and his daughter.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:17" href="/Numbers.30.17"><i>Num.</i> 30:17</a>.</i>” How do the rabbis interpret “between a man and his wife”? Not what is between her and others. “Between a father and his daughter,” not what is between her and others. Does Rebbi Eleazar not agree with this? He agrees; he understands everything from there: “Between a man and his wife”, not what is between her and others<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">This is the topic of Chapter 11. The husband can only void vows that either involve the relations between husband and wife or “vows of deprivation” (v. 14); he can forbid his wife to mortify herself. As in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 11:12:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.11.12.1">Mishnah 11:13</a>, if she had vowed not to sleep with any man, he can void his part but she remains forbidden if she should become a widow or a divorcee.</i>; “between a father and his daughter,” not what is between her and others.
“One does not have to mention if one of them confirmed it.” Come and see; if the father dissolved, it is not dissolved, and you say so<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah stated that if only one of them dissolved, in the example given, the father, the vow is not dissolved. Why does one have to mention that it is not dissolved if one of them confirmed the vow, since confirmation implies non-dissolution!</i>? Not on that it was said, but on the following: If the father dissolved his part but the husband did not have time to dissolve before he died, the father dissolves the husband’s part<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">While the husband cannot dissolve after the father’s death (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 10:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.10.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>), the father can dissolve alone after the husband’s death (as long as the marriage is in the preliminary stage) since after the husband’s death the underage daughter returns to the father’s tutelage.</i>. Rebbi Nathan said, those are the words of the House of Shammai. But the statement of the Sages is that he does not need to dissolve<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Since the husband’s power has disappeared, the father’s prior dissolution is sufficient.<br>The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 69a" href="/Nedarim.69a">Babli (69a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 71a" href="/Nedarim.71a">71a</a>) and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 6:3" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).6.3">Tosephta 6:3</a> read: “[the father] <i>cannot</i> dissolve”. The Babli has a long discussion about the nature of the joint powers of dissolution. If the powers are parallel, then the dissolution by one of the parties has no influence on the other; the husband’s death leaves half of the vow undissolved and undissolvable. But if the powers are joint ones, then the husband’s death opens the way for the father to exercise his now unrestrained powers. There is no reason to think that the Yerushalmi makes a similar distinction.</i> if [the husband] did not confirm it. But if [the husband] confirmed, [the father] cannot void<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Since the affirmation cannot be undone.</i>.
You say that the father dissolves the husband’s part. If he wanted to confirm, he says, it is confirmed for you, without details, (it is confirmed for you, without details)<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that this is a case of dittography, even though one could read it as: If the father confirmed without indicating what he confirmed, then the vow is legally confirmed without indication whose part was confirmed (since the confirmation by one party is absolute.)</i>; the husband’s part is dissolved for you<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast, for the House of Shammai who hold that the father has to dissolve the late husband’s part, the dissolution is invalid unless the father explicitly declares the husband’s part dissolved.</i>. The following is obvious: If the father did not dissolve his part and she violated her vow, she is whipped. If the father dissolved but the husband did not, would she be whipped<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Since the father may dissolve after the husband’s death but the husband cannot dissolve after the father’s death, it is clear that the father’s power over his underage preliminarily married daugher is greater than the husband’s. Is the father’s power strong enough to eliminate the prohibition to violate one’s vow contained in 30:3?</i>? Or since if the husband died and his part became void in favor of the father, would she not be whipped? This follows him who said, death does not force [dissolution]<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that the word בַּהֲפָרָה is missing here; it was inserted in <i>editio princeps.</i><br>The argument follows the Sages in the previous paragraph, who hold that the husband’s death automatically voids the vow. For them, the power of the husband is secondary and the woman cannot be prosecuted.</i>. But following him who said, death does force dissolution<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The House of Shammai, who require the father explicitly to dissolve the late husband’s part, imply that the husband’s and the father’s powers are equal and that partial dissolution is of no legal value.</i>, and here since he did not dissolve for her and she violated her vow, she is whipped. It was stated: If the father dissolved his part but the husband died before he could dissolve<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">And she was preliminarily married to another man on the same day (since the period of dissolution is at most 24 hours after the first person knew of the vow.). <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 6:4" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).6.4">Tosephta 6:4</a>; there the re-marriage is noted.</i>, the last husband dissolves the part of the first one. Rebbi Yose said, a Mishnah says so: “Her father and her last husband dissolve her vows<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 10:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.10.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>.</i>”.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If the father died, his power is not voided in favor of the husband. If the husband died, his power is voided in favor of the father<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">While the husband cannot dissolve after the father’s death (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 10:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.10.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>), the father can dissolve alone after the husband’s death (as long as the marriage is in the preliminary stage) since after the husband’s death the underage daughter returns to the father’s tutelage.</i>. In this, He strengthened the father’s power over the husband. In another matter, He strengthened the husband’s power over the father since the husband dissolves in adulthood but the father does not dissolve in adulthood<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">An underage girl can be married off by her father. A woman becomes an adult in two steps. At age 12 (if she had developed two pubic hairs) she becomes an adult before the law but the father retains the right to marry her off and the right to her earnings for an additional 6 months, when she is called נַעֲרָה “adolescent girl”. After these 6 months she becomes an adult, בּוֹגֶרֶת “ripe”, is totally independent from her father, and has to marry on her own. The vow of a minor below age 11 is void. If she made a vow after age 11 (for a male after age 12), one has to investigate whether she understands the meaning and implications of a vow. If the investigation has a positive result, her vows are valid and, as far as vows are concerned, she has acquired the status of adolescent, even though in other legal aspects she remains a minor.<br>The chapter on vows states clearly that the father can dissolve his dependent daughter’s vows (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:4-6" href="/Numbers.30.4-6"><i>Num.</i> 30:4–6</a>). Similarly, the husband can dissolve the vows of his wife if she lives “in his house” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:11-15" href="/Numbers.30.11-15">vv. 11–15</a>). A preliminarily married girl (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 6:2:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.6.2.6"><i>Peah</i> 6:2 Note 46</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 4:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.4.1.6"><i>Demay</i> 4:1, Note 19</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 8:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.8.1.2"><i>Terumot</i> 8:1, Note 9</a>) remains under her father’s jurisdiction until she is taken to her husband’s house in the final ceremony. It is asserted that during her status as preliminarily married wife, the father can dissolve her vows since she is “in his house” and the husband is given special authority over her vows in vv. 7–9 which, therefore, are not duplicated in vv. 11–15.<br>The power of dissolution in any case is restricted to the day after the father or husband first was informed of the vow (vv. 6,9,13,15). But if one of them agreed to the vow within the allotted period, he can no longer object after that.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If the father died, his power is not voided in favor of the husband,” etc. They wanted to say that if the father had dissolved his part and died, his power is not voided in favor of the husband<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">If the girl is preliminarily married and the father dies after he had dissolved her vow but before the husband did, her vow cannot be dissolved.</i>. Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 10:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.10.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>. The quote does not prove anything; the proof is from the second part of the Mishnah which requires the husband to dissolve all prior vows of his bride prior to her entering his house, i. e., as long as she still is only preliminarily married. After she enters his house, he can no longer dissolve prior vows. Since the final marriage ceremony emancipated the girl (older than three years and one day) from her father and the father’s death does the same, it is concluded that the husband’s power over a preliminarily married girl endures only as long as she is under her father’s tutelage.</i>: “The way of learned people is that, before his daughter left his house, he told her: ‘Any vows which you had vowed in my house are dissolved’.” This implies that if the father had dissolved his part and died, his power is not voided in favor of the husband.
If the husband died, his power is voided in favor of the husband.” They wanted to say, after the husband had dissolved his part. But if the husband had not dissolved his part when he died, the power is not voided in favor of the father. Let us hear from the following: “If she made a vow while being preliminarily married. If she was divorced on the same day, preliminarily married on the same day, even a hundred times, her father and her last husband dissolve her vows<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 10:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.10.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>. Since the last husband has to dissolve, it follows that the first did not. Nevertheless, the father can dissolve her vows after the first husband was eliminated by divorce (or death).</i>.” That means, even if the husband had not dissolved his part and died, his power is voided in favor of the father.
“If the father died, his power is not voided in favor of the husband.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">As R. M. Margalit points out, one would expect the quote to read: “If the husband died, his power is voided in favor of the husband.”</i>” Rebbi Ḥaggai asked before Rebbi Yose. So far for vows she made after she was preliminarily married<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is a biblical decree that her vows can be voided only by the concurrent action of father and husband, we can understand that in the absence of the father, the husband has no authority. But the father was the only authority for vows preceding the preliminary marriage; why can we not say that at the father’s death the husband inherits his powers?</i>. Vows she made before she was preliminarily married; if the father died, was his power not voided in favor of the husband? He said to him, is it not true that vows she made before she was preliminarily married already could be dissolved by father and husband<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Since the husband in conjunction with the father can void vows that precede the preliminary marriage, the requirement of joint action is permanent.</i>? Why do you have to say, if the father died, his power is not voided in favor of the husband? That means, even vows she made after she was preliminarily married. Vows she made before she was preliminarily married, if the father died, was his power voided in favor of the husband?<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The argument is parallel to the preceding one, only this time to confirm that even for vows made after the preliminary marriage, the sole powers of dissolution belong to the father after the husband’s death.</i> He said to him, is it not true that vows she made before she was preliminarily married already could be dissolved by father and husband? You say, “if the husband died, his power is voided in favor of the father”! That means, even vows she made after she was preliminarily married, if the husband died, his power is voided in favor of the father!
“Since the husband dissolves in adulthood.” The Mishnah follows Rebbi Eleazar, since it was stated in the name of Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">If the material in the first paragraph is Amoraic, R. Eleazar is the Amora, bar Pedat. If the argument is tannaїtic, he is the Tanna, ben Shamua. R. Eleazar’s argument is paralleled in <i>Sifry Num.</i> 153 by R. Joshia, student of R. Ismael. The final argument in this paragraph is attributed to R. Ismael in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 68a" href="/Nedarim.68a">Babli, 68a</a>.</i>: “If she should be a man’s”<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:7" href="/Numbers.30.7"><i>Num.</i> 30:7</a>. The masoretic text reads הָיוֹ.</i>, the verse speaks about a preliminarily married adult girl<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">At 12 years and 6 months (or 6 months after she developed two pubic hairs) she permanently leaves her father’s <i>potestas.</i> He reads the verse as dealing with a case where there is no residual power of the father. But since vv. 11 ff. speak of the married woman, he must find a case of a preliminarily married woman free from her father.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If she made a vow while preliminarily married, was divorced on the same day<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The day she told of her vow either to her husband or to her father.</i>, preliminarily married on the same day, even to a hundred men<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Sequentially, each one after the previous one’s divorce.</i>, her father and her last husband dissolve her vows. That is the principle: as long as she did not leave her father’s power for one moment<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Either by definitive marriage or by becoming an adult.</i>, her father and her last husband dissolve her vows.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If she made a vow while preliminarily married,” etc. Rebbi Hila said, “being she will be”<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:7" href="/Numbers.30.7"><i>Num</i>. 30:7</a>. “To be” applied to a woman means “to be married,” <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 24:2" href="/Deuteronomy.24.2"><i>Deut</i>. 24:2</a>.</i>, even for a hundred beings, her father and her last husband dissolve her vows. Rebbi Yose said, a Mishnah says so: “Her father and her last husband dissolve her vows<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 10:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.10.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>.</i>”.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “The way of learned people is that, before his daughter left his house, he told her: ‘Any vows which you had vowed in my house are dissolved’. Similarly, the husband tells her before she enters his domain<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., before the final marriage ceremony.</i>: ‘Any vows which you had vowed before you enter my domain are dissolved,’ for after she enters his domain he cannot dissolve<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 10:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.10.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>. The quote does not prove anything; the proof is from the second part of the Mishnah which requires the husband to dissolve all prior vows of his bride prior to her entering his house, i. e., as long as she still is only preliminarily married. After she enters his house, he can no longer dissolve prior vows. Since the final marriage ceremony emancipated the girl (older than three years and one day) from her father and the father’s death does the same, it is concluded that the husband’s power over a preliminarily married girl endures only as long as she is under her father’s tutelage.</i>.<br>An adult girl and one who had waited<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">A different text of the Babylonian Mishnah is amended in the Halakhah there to the Yerushalmi text.</i> twelve months and a widow 30 days<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.2.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> <i>5:2</a></i> states that a girl who never was married before has 12 months after the preliminary marriage to prepare her trousseau and a widow 30 days. An adult girl, for whom the father no longer has responsibility, is supposed to have a trousseau by the twelfth month of her adulthood and can marry immediately (cf. the commentary ascribed to Rashi to the Babli, 73b, and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:3:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.3.2-4">Halakhah <i>Ketubot</i> 5:3</a>). If the groom does not bring her into his house by the appointed time (by means of the final marriage ceremony), he nevertheless becomes resposible for her upkeep as if he were completely married.</i>, Rebbi Eliezer says, since her husband is responsible for her upkeep he may dissolve but the Sages say that the husband dissolves only after she enters his domain.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “An adult girl and one who had waited twelve months,” etc. Rebbi Eliezer holds that the matter depends on the support. If he preliminarily married a woman on condition to support her<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">He agrees to support his wife from the time of the preliminary marriage.</i>, the husband dissolves for her<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Without the father’s concurrence.</i>. If he completely married a woman on condition that he<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The father.</i> support her, the father dissolves for her<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Without the husband’s concurrence. This paragraph has been emended by the standard commentaries to mean the opposite it says.</i>. The words of the Sages? Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The husband never dissolves<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Alone.</i> before she enters his domain.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a woman was waiting for her levir<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">She is a widow whose husband died without issue; she waits to be married to her husband’s brother; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.4.3.1"><i>Yebamot</i> 4:3, Note 84</a>. The Mishnah must speak about an underage girl who became a widow after a preliminary marriage only, and who still is under her father’s jurisdiction. The question is only whether the levir may dissolve her vows in conjuction with the father, since for a woman who is emancipated from her father, either by coming of age or by final marriage, the husband can dissolve only vows made after the final marriage act.</i>, whether it be one or two, Rebbi Eliezer says, he shall dissolve<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">All the ceremonies involved in a levirate marriage are rabbinical; by biblical law the childless sister-in-law is married only by actual intercourse (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 2:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.2.1.3"><i>Yebamot</i> 2:12, Note 12</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 6:1:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.6.1.2-7">6:1</a>). The rabbinic equivalent of the preliminary marriage is called “bespeaking” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.2.1.1"><i>Yebamot</i> 2:1, Note 6</a>). The legal power of “bespeaking” is a matter of controversy but it is certain that adultery with a “bespoken” woman is not a capital crime. While under the obligation to marry a levir, the widow by biblical law is unable to contract a valid marriage with any other man (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 1:1:21" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.1.1.21"><i>Yebamot</i> 1:1, Note 94</a>.) For R. Eliezer, the latter fact gives any levir the status of a husband in matters of vows.</i>. Rebbi Joshua says, for one but not for two<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joshua gives the right of the preliminary husband only to a single levir, even after “bespeaking”. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 74a" href="/Nedarim.74a">Babli, 74a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Aqiba says, neither for one nor for two<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">His position is explained in the following Mishnah.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If a woman was waiting for her levir,” etc. Following Rebbi Aqiba<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">For whom the right of dissolution depends on his relationship with the woman defining adultery, can the husband dissolve in questionable cases? The answers, which are not given, are obviously “no”.</i>, who can dissolve? For example, what Rebbi Abbahu said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, even a hundred <i>qiddušin</i> are valid for her<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 60a" href="/Kiddushin.60a">Babli <i>Qiddušin</i> 60a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yevamot 3:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yevamot.3.4.3">Yerushalmi <i>Yebamot</i> 3:4 (Notes 102–104</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 5:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.5.3.3">5:1 (Note 35</a>); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:1:2-13" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.1.2-13"><i>Qiddušin</i> 3:1</a>, (63c 1. 71). A man gives something of value to a woman to serve as <i>qiddušin</i> money for a preliminary marriage in a month’s time. If other men also give her deferred <i>qiddušin</i>, they all might end up preliminarily married to her and none of them can marry her. In that case, she is not bound to any one by marriage in the sense of R. Aqiba.</i>, who dissolves? And what Rebbi Jacob<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">In the source of this statement, <i>Qiddus̄in</i> 1:2, 59c 1. 8, one reads: “R. Joḥanan said.” Since there is no R. Jacob without father’s name among the students of R. Joḥanan (there are R. Jacob bar Aḥa and R. Jacob bar Idi), the name has to be considered a scribal error.</i> said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, he can allot her to his underage son<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the Hebrew slave girl (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:7-11" href="/Exodus.21.7-11"><i>Ex</i>. 21:7–11</a>), about a situation intrinsically connected to the Jubilee year which became void with the exile of the Ten Tribes. The verse gives the master the right to marry the girl by considering the price he paid for her as <i>qiddušin</i> money, or to give her to his son as wife without additional expenditure. If the son is a minor, she becomes his wife by biblical decree, But the wife of a minor cannot be prosecuted for adultery [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Kedoshim, Chapter 9 11" href="/Sifra,_Kedoshim,_Chapter_9.11"><i>Sifra Qedošim Pereq</i> 9(11</a>)]. Can the underage husband, biblically married to his underage wife, dissolve her vows?</i>, who dissolves? And what Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa sind in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, she does not even need a reparation offering for a possible sin<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">The wife of a deaf-mute or of an insane person (at the time of marriage) is not married by biblical standards and cannot commit adultery. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 113a" href="/Yevamot.113a"><i>Yebamot</i>, Babli 113a</a>, Yerushalmi 14:1 Note 7. (The rules of reparation offerings for possible sins are in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:17-19" href="/Leviticus.5.17-19"><i>Lev</i>. 5:17–19</a>).</i>, who dissolves?

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Rebbi Eliezer said, if he can dissolve vows for a wife which he himself acquired, so much more that he should be able to dissolve for a wife which Heaven acquired for him. Rebbi Aqiba answered him: No. What you say is about a wife which he himself acquired, where nobody else has any authority over her; what can you say about the wife which Heaven acquired for him, where others<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The other brothers of the deceased husband.</i> have authority over her? Rebbi Joshua said to him, Aqiba, your words apply to two levirs. What can you reply about one levir? He said to him, the sister-in-law does not belong completely to her man as the wife belongs completely to her husband<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The childless widow is unable to contract a marriage except with the levir; she does not commit adultery if she sleeps with another man before she is married by the levir. But a married woman who sleeps with another man commits a capital crime. For R. Aqiba, marriage is only a relationship whose violation is a capital crime.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Rebbi Eliezer said,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 6:5" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).6.5">Tosephta 6:5</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 74b" href="/Nedarim.74b">Babli 74b</a>.</i>“Rebbi Eliezer said, since a woman on whom I had no claim before she entered into my domain, becomes absolutely mine after she entered my domain, is it not logical that a woman on whom I had some claim before she entered my domain, shall become absolutely mine when she enters my domain<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">A man has no relation to an unrelated woman. After the preliminary marriage he has the exclusive right to her and dissolves her vows (together with the father). Since he has potential rights to his childless sister-in-law, should he not have the right to dissolve her vows (together with the father) after “bespeaking”?</i>? Rebbi Aqiba told him, no. If you speak about a woman on whom you had no claim before she entered into your domain, but after she entered your domain she became absolutely yours, then just as you had no part in her so no other man had any part in her. What can you conclude about a woman on whom you had some claim before she entered your domain, and after she entered your domain she became absolutely yours, but just as you had some rights to her so others had the same rights to her<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The other brothers.</i>! Rebbi Joshua told him, Aqiba, your argument holds for two levirs. What do you respond in the case of a single levir? He said to him, just as you make no difference in the rules of one waiting for one levir or for two levirs, whether he ‘bespoke’ her or did not ‘bespeak’ her, can it not be the same for vows and oaths? He said to him, that is true<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">The text in the Tosephta is different: אָמַר לוֹ. הֲבָל אִילּוּ הָיִיתָ בִימֵי רִבִּי לָעְזָר בֶּן עֲרָךְ וַהֲשֵׁבתָ תְשוּבָה זוֹ. “He said to him, I am sorry that you did not live in the time of R. Eleazar ben Arakh and give him this reply” (maybe he would have changed his mind).</i>. He said to him, if you had lived in the times of Rebbi Eleazar ben Arakh, he would have said that ‘bespeaking’ does not acquire completely since he would agree that he cannot dissolve until she enters his domain<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 6:5" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).6.5">Tosephta 6:5</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 74b" href="/Nedarim.74b">Babli 74b</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody says to his wife, all vows that you might vow from now until I shall return from place X shall be confirmed<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">In his absence. Everybody agrees that an as yet nonexistent vow cannot be confirmed.</i>, he did not say anything; [if he says] they shall be dissolved, Rebbi Eliezer says, they are dissolved, but the Sages say, they are not dissolved.<br>Rebbi Eliezer said, if he can dissolve vows that came under the category of prohibition<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Vows actually made by which the wife imposes a legally enforceable prohibition on herself.</i>, should he not be able to dissolve vows that did not yet come under the category of prohibition<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Vows not yet made.</i>? They said to him, it says<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:14" href="/Numbers.30.14"><i>Num</i>. 30:14</a>. The verse treats confirmation and voiding as parallels.</i> ‘her husband may confirm them and her husband may dissolve them”. What can be confirmed can be dissolved; what cannot be confirmed cannot be dissolved.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to Mishnaiot 8,9. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 6:5" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).6.5">Tosephta 6:5</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 75b" href="/Nedarim.75b">Babli 75b</a>.</i>“He<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel sources: They. This is no longer part of R. Aqiba’s argument, but belongs to the anonymous Sages.</i> answered to the words of Rebbi Eliezer: Let the <i>miqweh</i><sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">The ritual bath cleanses from most impurities. But a pure person cannot immerse himself to become immune to future impurities.</i> prove it! It frees the impure from their impurities but it cannot save pure ones. Rebbi Eliezer argued another track: Since in a situation where he cannot dissolve his own vows before he made them, he dissolves his wife’s vows before she made them, in a situation where he can dissolve his own vows after he made them, would it not be logical that he could dissolve his wife’s vows before she made them?<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">It is difficult to make sense of this text. In Tosephta and Babli one reads: מָה אִם בְּמָקוֹם שֶׁאֵינוֹ מֵפֵר נִדְרֵי עַצְמוֹ <b>מִשֶׁנָּדַר</b> הֲרֵי הוּא מֵיפֵר נִדְרֵי <b>עַצְמוֹ</b> עַד שֶׁלֹּא <b>יִדּוֹר</b>. מָקוֹם שֶׁמֵפֵר נִדְרֵי <b>אִשְׁתּוֹ מִשֶׁנָּֽדְרָה</b> אֵינוֹ דִין שֶׁיָּפֵר נִדְרֵי אִשְׁתּוֹ עַד שֶׁלֹּא תִדּוֹר. “Since he cannot dissolve his own vows after he vowed (for only a Sage can do that), but he can dissolve his own vows before he vows (by a declaration that all his future vows are null and void), then since he can dissolve his wife’s vows after she vowed it is only logical that he should be able to dissolve his wife’s vows before she vowed.” It seems that the Yerushalmi text should follow similar lines.</i> No! Why can he not dissolve his own vows before he made them<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">This text contradicts the next sentence. The Tosephta reads <b>אָֽמְרוּ</b> לוֹ וּמָה מֵיפֵר נִדְרֵי עַצְמוֹ עַד שֶׁלֹּא יִדּוֹר. “They said to him, <i>why is it that</i> he can dissolve his own vows before he made them?”</i>? For if he wants to confirm them, he confirms them. Could he dissolve his wife’s vows after she made them, when if he wanted to confirm them, he could not confirm them?<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">The Tosephta reads יָפֵר נִדְרֵי אִשְׁתּוֹ <b>עַד שֶׁלֹּא תִדּוֹר</b>. שֶׁכֵּן אִם רָצָה לְהָקֵם אֵינוֹ מוּקָם. “Could he dissolve his wife’s vows <i>before she made them</i> when if he wanted to confirm them, he could not confirm them?” (As R. Eliezer agrees in the Mishnah.)</i>” <sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 6:6" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).6.6">Tosephta 6:6</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 76b" href="/Nedarim.76b">Babli 76b</a>.</i>“But it says<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:14" href="/Numbers.30.14"><i>Num</i>. 30:14</a>. The verse treats confirmation and voiding as parallels.</i>, ‘her husband may confirm them and her husband may dissolve them’. What can be confirmed can be dissolved,; what cannot be confirmed cannot be dissolved.”

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The dissolution of vows may take place the entire day<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The day of Creation, the night followed by daylight.</i>; this can imply a lenient or a stringent implementation. How is that? If she made the vow Friday night<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">It could be any other night; the Mishnah informs us that dissolution of vows is permitted on the Sabbath.</i>, he may dissolve during the night and the next day until [the next] nightfall. If she made the vow shortly before nightfall, he dissolves until it becomes dark; for after dark he cannot dissolve.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The dissolution of vows may take place the entire day,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 76b" href="/Nedarim.76b">Babli 76b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 77a" href="/Nedarim.77a">77a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 157a" href="/Shabbat.157a"><i>Šabbat</i> 157a</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah and Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon say<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Sifry</i> <i>Num</i>. 156 this is attributed to R. Simeon himself.</i>, the dissolution of vows may take place from time to time<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">24 hours from the moment the husband is informed.</i>.” What is the reason of the rabbis? “From day to day<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:15" href="/Numbers.30.15"><i>Num</i>. 30:15</a>.</i>.” What is the reason of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah? “On the day of his hearing<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:6" href="/Numbers.30.6"><i>Num</i>. 30:6</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:8" href="/Numbers.30.8">8</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:13" href="/Numbers.30.13">13</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 76b" href="/Nedarim.76b">Babli, 76b</a>, and <i>Sifry</i> <i>Num</i>. 156, the arguments are switched: “On the <i>day</i> of his hearing” implies that at nightfall the time has run out, “from day to day” implies 24 hours.<br>It is not necessary to amend the Yerushalmi since Targum Pseudo-Jonathan and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:15" href="/Numbers.30.15">Rashi explain <i>Num</i>. 30:15</a> following the rabbis: “If her husband remains silent from the day he was informed to another day”, i. e., the next night. R. Yose ben R. Jehudah will explain that “the day of his hearing” starts only with his hearing.</i>.” How do the rabbis explain Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah’s reason, “on the day of his hearing”? Explain it that she made the vow on the start of the night<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">In that case, the husband has 24 hours even according to the rabbis.</i>. How does Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah explain the rabbis’ reason, “from day to day”? Explain it that she made the vow at the start of Friday night<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The mention of the <i>start</i> of Friday night is problematic. It is a correction by the scribe himself and probably an insertion at the wrong place.</i>, he became paralyzed<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">After he was informed of the vow.</i>, and then his power of speech returned<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Within 24 hours after he heard from his wife.</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah one gives him 24 hours. In the opinion of the rabbis he has only that day<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Until nightfall on Saturday, less than 24 hours.</i>. If he became paralyzed, and later<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Not on the same day.</i> his power of speech returned, in the opinion of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah one adds up to a total of 24 hours<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">The paralysis does not stop the clock. In this case, R. Yose ben R. Jehudah is more restrictive than the rabbis.</i>. In the opinion of the rabbis he always can dissolve when his speech returns. But if it happened one hour<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">“One hour” means “a short time”. If the husband was informed of the vow shortly before sundown and before he could dissolve the vow was paralyzed at sundown, for the rabbis he had his day and when he regains his speech cannot undo the vow.</i> before sundown, he can no longer dissolve.
“It shall be dissolved at the time of the afternoon prayer,” it is permanently dissolved<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">There can be no partial confirmation or dissolution. Since he did not mention confirmation, one does not take his word to mean that the vow shall be valid until the time of the afternoon prayer; it is dissolved immediately and permanently. The same holds in the following two cases.</i>. “It shall be confirmed at the time of the afternoon prayer,” it is permanently confirmed. “It shall be dissolved until the time of the afternoon prayer,” it is as if he said, “it shall be dissolved starting with the time of the afternoon prayer.”
There<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 24:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.24.5.1">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 24:5</a>.</i>, we have stated: “One dissolves vows on the Sabbath.” It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 77a" href="/Nedarim.77a">Babli, 77a</a>, this is the result of a discussion of late Amoraïm.</i>: Both vows that intrude on the Sabbath and vows that do not intrude on the Sabbath. “And one asks about vows that intrude on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 24:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.24.5.1">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 24:5</a>.</i>,” therefore not if there is no need for the Sabbath. The Elder can dissolve the next day<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">But the husband who was informed on the Sabbath must dissolve on the Sabbath or lose his right to dissolution.</i>. Then for Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah and Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon who say that the dissolution of vows is from time to time, he should not dissolve even vows that intrude on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">This argument is also made in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 77a" href="/Nedarim.77a">Babli, 77a</a>.</i>! Explain it according to everybody, if she made the vow at the start of Friday night.
<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 77b" href="/Nedarim.77b">Babli 77b</a>, with biblical support for the change in language between dissolution and permission.</i> Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The husband who said “there is no vow, there is no oath,” did not say anything. Also the Elder who said “it is dissolved for you, it is voided for you,” did not say anything. But everybody has to follow his own rules. The husband says “it is dissolved for you, it is voided for you,” and the Elder says, “there is no vow, there is no oath”.
Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">From Naḥmanides, missing in ms. and <i>editio princeps</i>; required by the general style of the Yerushalmi.</i> said, earlier generations were asking, may a man ask about his confirmation? How is that? If his wife made a vow, her husband heard it and did not dissolve for her<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">He did not dissolve in time and by inaction lost his veto power. The Yerushalmi seems to hold that a vow explicitly confirmed by the husband cannot be referred to an Elder for annulment, since it discusses only confirmation by default.</i>. It is obvious that he cannot dissolve as a husband. May he dissolve as an Elder? What are we talking about? If it is about vows between him and her, these are vows of himself<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">The husband can interfere with his wife’s vows only if it touches him personally or is a vow of mortification (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 10:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.10.1.3">Note 17</a>). If it touches him personally, he certainly cannot act as an Elder in his own behalf.</i>. But we must talk about vows between her and others. [Is that not a Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Nega‘im</i> 2:5: “A man [sitting as judge] can permit all vows except his own. R. Jehudah says, nor his wife’s vows concerning others.”</i>? “Not his wife’s vows between her and others!”] Is that not Rebbi Jehudah’s? It was stated in the name of Rebbi Judah; Rebbi Ḥiyya stated it in the name of the Sages<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ḥiyya [the older] is the great authority for the Mishnah text. According to him, one has to switch the attributions in the Mishnah and, therefore, practice has to follow what in the common Mishnah is attributed to R. Jehudah. Naḥmanides (11:1) holds that practice has to follow the Yerushalmi in this since the Babli does not discuss the theme; Maimonides (Commentary to <i>Nega‘im</i> 2:5; Code <i>Hilkhot Ševu‘ot</i> 6:6) disagrees and follows the majority opinion in the Mishnah.</i>.
May one permit<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ḥiyya [the older] is the great authority for the Mishnah text. According to him, one has to switch the attributions in the Mishnah and, therefore, practice has to follow what in the common Mishnah is attributed to R. Jehudah. Naḥmanides (11:1) holds that practice has to follow the Yerushalmi in this since the Babli does not discuss the theme; Maimonides (Commentary to <i>Nega‘im</i> 2:5; Code <i>Hilkhot Ševu‘ot</i> 6:6) disagrees and follows the majority opinion in the Mishnah.</i> vows in the night? Since vows under the husband’s jurisdiction, about which “on the <i>day</i>” is written<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:6" href="/Numbers.30.6"><i>Num</i>. 30:6</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:8" href="/Numbers.30.8">8</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:13" href="/Numbers.30.13">13</a>. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 76b" href="/Nedarim.76b">Babli, 76b</a>, and <i>Sifry</i> <i>Num</i>. 156, the arguments are switched: “On the <i>day</i> of his hearing” implies that at nightfall the time has run out, “from day to day” implies 24 hours.<br>It is not necessary to amend the Yerushalmi since Targum Pseudo-Jonathan and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:15" href="/Numbers.30.15">Rashi explain <i>Num</i>. 30:15</a> following the rabbis: “If her husband remains silent from the day he was informed to another day”, i. e., the next night. R. Yose ben R. Jehudah will explain that “the day of his hearing” starts only with his hearing.</i>, he may dissolve in the night, vows under the jurisdiction of the Elder, where “on the day” is <i>not</i> written, not so much more<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 77b" href="/Nedarim.77b">Babli agrees, 77b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 62b" href="/Eruvin.62b"><i>Eruvin</i> 62b</a>.</i>? Can one permit through an interpreter? Let us hear from the following: Rebbi Abba bar Ẓutra was made an interpreter for Rebbi Joḥanan in the case of a woman who did not know Syriac<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">She spoke only Greek. In general, a judge is only competent to sit in a case if he understands all witnesses since it says “by the mouth of two witnesses”, not the mouth of an interpreter (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 19:15" href="/Deuteronomy.19.15"><i>Deut</i>. 19:15</a>; <i>Makkot</i> 1:9. <i>Sifry</i> <i>Deut</i>. 188.)</i>.
It was stated: One is asked about vows only while sitting and wrapped<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">According to the rules of court proceedings. The judges have to be seated and wrapped in their judicial robes.</i>. The one who is asked must be sitting and the one who is asking is standing; from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 19:17" href="/Deuteronomy.19.17"><i>Deut.</i> 19:17</a>.</i>: “And the two people who have the dispute shall stand.” “Standing” means only “being judged”. That is for asking legal rules<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">The verse does not imply that the parties have to stand before the judges but that they have to remain there to be judged. The interpretation of the verse is in dispute, cf. <i>Ševu‘ot</i> 4:1 (35b, 1. 30 ff.), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 3:9:2-24" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.3.9.2-24"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 3:10</a> (21c 1. 15ff.), <i>Yoma</i> 6:1 (43b 1. 51 ff.); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 30a" href="/Shevuot.30a">Babli <i>Ševu‘ot</i> 30a</a>; <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 190.</i>. Asking about sermons from where? The verse says “shall stand, and shall stand”<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">The consecutive <i>vaw</i> is interpreted as sign of an addition; to answer anything of a theological nature, the Sage has to sit. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 77b" href="/Nedarim.77b">Babli disagrees, 77b</a>; the opinion of the Yerushalmi is quoted as that of Rabban Gamliel II.</i>. Rebbi Aḥa bar Pappus went to permit the vow of Rebbi Immi. He remained standing to say “there is no vow”<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Following the opinion of the Babylonian Amoraïm for the Babylonian Immi.</i>. Rebbi Mana went to permit the vow of the older Gamliel. He remained standing to say “there is no vow, there is no oath”. Rebbi Mana went to permit the vow of Gamliel the grandson. He said to him, do not treat me as you treated my grandfather, but sit down and I shall remain standing<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">He asked him to follow the Palestinian rules.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the Halakhah, the text also is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Chagigah 1:8:2-14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Chagigah.1.8.2-14"><i>Ḥagigah</i> 1:8</a>, 76c 1. 62.</i> Rebbi Ze‘ira, Rav Jehudah, Jeremiah bar Abba, in the name of Rebbi Samuel<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">Probably one should read: R. Ze‘ira, Rav Jehudah, Rav Jeremiah bar Aḥa in the name of Samuel.</i>: Three who know how to find an opening may permit like an Elder<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Persons who are not ordained but know the rules of invalidating vows explained in the preceding Chapters. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 78a" href="/Nedarim.78a">Babli, 78a</a>, R. Aḥa bar Jacob admits any three lay persons to invalidate vows (cf. R. Nissim Gerondi <i>ad loc</i>., <i>s. v</i>. אמר רב אחא בר יעקב.). In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 36b" href="/Bekhorot.36b"><i>Bekhorot</i> 36b</a>, the same rule is attributed to R. Ḥiyya bar Abin in the name of Rav Amram, a student of Samuel’s student Rav Naḥman.</i>. They thought, at a place where no Elder was available. The rabbis of Caesarea: Even at a place of an Elder<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Invalidation of a vow by three laymen is not infringing on the privileges of the rabbinate. The ordained rabbi has the privilege to invalidate a vow alone (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 78a" href="/Nedarim.78a">Babli, 78a</a>).</i>. They said before Rebbi Yasa: Rav Huna is “head of tribes”<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">Who was the undisputed head of the Babylonian rabbinate and was of the family of the davidic Head of the Diaspora. He certainly had the right to invalidate vows, being of the “heads of the tribes” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:2" href="/Numbers.30.2"><i>Num</i>. 30:2</a>).</i>. Who are the heads of tribes<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">Since they are mentioned in the plural, it appears that more than one person in a generation was empowered to invalidate vows.</i>? Rav Huna is head of the heads of tribes<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">He is the head of all ordained rabbis, even though his Babylonian ordination is not complete as explained in the next paragraph.</i>.
May one appoint Elders for selected topics<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">Is it possible to give ordination without conferring all rabbinical powers on the candidate?</i>? Let us hear from the following: Rebbi appointed Rav to invalidate vows and to see stains<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">To decide whether a female genital discharge was menstrual blood or not; i. e., whether the woman would be permitted to her husband or not. This is taken as example of his power to decide in matters of ritual prohibitions and includes permission to judge in all matters of such prohibitions.</i>. After his death, [Rav] asked his son for [permission to see] defects of firstlings. He said to him, I shall not add to what my father gave you. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, he gave him everything: To sit in judgment alone, to invalidate vows, to see stains, and to see outside blemishes<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">In the tradition of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 5a" href="/Sanhedrin.5a">Babli, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 5a</a>, Rav received full rabbinic powers except the right to decide anything in matters of firstlings. (A firstling of cattle, sheep, or goats must be sacrificed unless it has a blemish which disqualifies it as a sacrifice. In the absence of a Temple, the firstling must graze until it develops a blemish; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 15:19-23" href="/Deuteronomy.15.19-23"><i>Deut</i>. 15:19–23</a>.) The full ordination was refused to him since his intent was to return to Babylonia and Rebbi disapproved of that. After him, all rabbis with the limited diaspora ordination received the title of “Rav”.</i>. After his death, [Rav] asked his son for [permission to see] hidden defects. He said to him, I shall not add to what my father gave you. Even though you say, one appoints Elders for selected topics, only if he is competent for everything. As the following: Rebbi Joshua ben Levi ordained all his students, but he was sorry about one who had a defect in his eye and he could not ordain him<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Since skin lesions (wrongly translated as “leprosy”, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13-14" href="/Leviticus.13-14"><i>Lev</i>. 13–14</a>) must be seen with both eyes, a one-eyed or blind person could not be ordained to decide on their ritual purity.</i>; so he ordained him for selected topics<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">For everything except matters of impurity and duties as judge.</i>. That implies that one who is competent in one thing has to be competent in everything, and one who is not competent in everything cannot be declared competent in one thing<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">The difference between the titles of “Rav” and “Rebbi” is one of circumstances, not of quality.</i>.
May one appoint Elders for a fixed time? Let us hear from the following: Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba went to Rebbi Eleazar and asked him to intervene with the Patriarch Rebbi Jehudah, that the latter should write him a letter of recommendation for seeking a livelihood in a foreign country. He intervened, and [the Patriarch] wrote for him: Here we are sending you as our representative a great personality with all our powers until he shall return to us<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">He shall have full ordination until he returns to the Patriarch. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 5b" href="/Sanhedrin.5b">Babli, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 5b</a>, has a similar story involving R. Joḥanan who similarly gave conditional ordination to Rebbi Shimen.</i>. Rebbi Ḥizqiah, Rebbi Dositheus, Rebbi Abba bar Zamina in the name of the old Rebbi Dositheus: He wrote him the following. Here we are sending you a great personality who will not be ashamed to say “1 did not learn this”<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">In this version, which has no parallel in the Babli, the ordination was unconditional and permanent.</i>.
May one permit wearing a coat<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">Must the rabbi always be wrapped in his robe (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 10:8:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.10.8.8">Note 106</a>)? The word פלונס parallels Syriac פלימנא, פילמנא, Greek φαινόλης (φαιλόνης) παινόλης, Latin <i>paenula</i> “coat”.</i>? Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: One permits wearing a coat. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi permitted wearing a coat. Rebbi Huna in the name of Rebbi Jeremiah: At a place where one does not wear a toga. Rebbi Yose ben Abun said, for easy vows<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Those which can be permitted unquestionably, for which no argument is needed.</i>.

Chapter 11



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> These are the vows which he may dissolve: Matters connected with mortification<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In addition, he can dissolve vows impinging on their marital relations, as explained later.</i>. [E. g.], “if I wash, if I do not wash; if I wear jewels, if I do not wear jewels.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">These examples refer to conditional vows. One derives from the contract of the tribes of Gad and Reuben with Moses (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 32:29-30" href="/Numbers.32.29-30"><i>Num.</i> 32:29–30</a>) that a legally valid conditional contract must spell out the conditions both positively and negatively: If I do certain things, there is a stipulated consequence; if I do not, then the consequence is stipulated not to happen (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.3.3.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 3:3</a>). The vow is understood to be: A <i>qônām</i> should be a certain thing for me if I ever wash; if I do not wash, the thing shall not be <i>qônām</i>. Since not washing is mortification, the husband has the right to void the vow. If the vow had been unconditional, the husband would have no jurisdiction over it.</i>” Rebbi Yose said, these are not vows of mortification<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">His position is explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 11:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.11.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “These are the vows which he can dissolve,” etc. It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:14" href="/Numbers.30.14"><i>Num</i>. 30:14</a>. The verse ends: “Her husband shall confirm it or her husband shall dissolve it.”</i>: “Any vow and any oath of prohibition to mortify.” That covers only vows which contain mortification. Vows regarding the relations between him and her, from where? “Between a man and his wife<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:17" href="/Numbers.30.17"><i>Num</i>. 30:17</a>. One opinion in this Halakhah (Notes 11 ff.) and the consensus in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 79b" href="/Nedarim.79b">Babli (79b</a>) hold that the dissolution of vows of mortification is permanent since it is expressly sanctioned by the verse but that dissolution of a vow regarding marital relations, which is the result of an indirect inference, is valid only as long as the marriage continues. Such a dissolution would be automatically voided for the divorcee or widow. The Mishnah mentions only vows the husband can permanently dissolve.</i>.” So far the husband; the father from where? Since the husband can dissolve only vows of mortification and matters between him and her, so the father can dissolve only vows of mortification and matters between him and her<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This argument seems to be taken out of thin air. It is explained in <i>Sifry Num</i>. 155: V. 17 reads “These are the principles which the Eternal commanded to Moses between a husband and his wife, between a father and his daughter, in her adolescence, in her father’s house.” Now this verse is really an appendix to the laws governing the married wife. It is concluded that the restrictions which apply to the husband in relation with his wife in <i>his</i> house also apply to the father in relation to the adolescent daughter in <i>his</i> house.</i>.
Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish disagree. Rebbi Joḥanan said, the husband dissolves both vows and oaths<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The heading of the paragraph, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:2" href="/Numbers.30.2"><i>Num</i>. 30:2</a>, speaks of vows and oaths. (A vow refers to a thing, an oath implies the use of God’s name.)</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, he dissolves vows but not oaths<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Except for v. 2, oaths are never mentioned.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish also disagree about the vows submitted to the Elder. Rebbi Joḥanan said, the Elder permits both vows and oaths. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, the Elder permits vows, the Elder does not permit oaths. That of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish parallels that of Issi (Assi). A person came before Rebbi Yasa<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Since Yerushalmi אִיסִּי is the same as Babylonian אַסִּי, the formal name should not be יוֹסֵי but יָסָה.</i> to have his vow permitted. He asked him, what did you swear? He answered, ὢ πόποι<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Greek πόποι is classically an exclamation of surprise, anger, pain, etc.; it is later explained as “divinities, gods”. While the husband avoided using Hebrew or Aramaic expressions for God’s name, the Greek in this case has the meaning of “God of Israel”; this classifies the act as an oath, which R. Yasa refused to annul. The husband swore that his wife should no longer enter his house, i. e., he forced himself to divorce her and pay her the divorce settlement.</i> Israel, that she should not enter my house. He said to him, ὢ πόποι Israel, she shall not enter your house!
Rebbi Ze‘ira explained the Mishnah: “These are the vows which he can dissolve: Matters connected with mortification. [E. g.], ‘if I wash, if I do not wash; if I shall wear jewels, if I do not wear jewels.’ Rebbi Yose said, these are not vows of mortification” but vows between him and her. “The following are vows of mortification<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 11:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.11.2.1">Mishnah 11:2</a>. In this interpretation, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 11:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.11.2.1">Mishnah 2</a> is the continuation of the remark of R. Yose in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 11:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.11.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>.</i>” following Rebbi Yose. For example, “she said, all produce of the world is <i>qônām</i> for me, he may dissolve.” The rabbis say, if he dissolves vows of mortification, they are permanently dissolved. Vows between him and her are only dissolved as long as she is married to him. Rebbi Yose says, both vows of mortification and vows between him and her, if he dissolved them they are permanently dissolved<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Yose also agrees that the husband dissolves both vows of mortification and those between him and her, there must be a practical difference between the two classes of vows to make the classification important.</i>. That means, the rabbis say, if he dissolves vows of mortification, they are permanently dissolved. Rebi Yose says, if he dissolves vows between him and her, they are permanently dissolved. What is the difference between them? If she said, any benefit from me shall be <i>qônām</i> for you when I leave your domain. Why can he not dissolve that? Because she did not say, any benefit from my body shall be <i>qônām</i> for you when I leave your domain<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">A vow between him and her is only subject to the husband’s dissolution if it interferes with their marital relations. For R. Yose, he can dissolve a vow that would permanently forbid any sex with her after divorce; for the rabbis, he cannot dissolve the vow which comes into effect only after he will have lost the right of dissolution.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira and Rebbi Hila, both of them say: That is the essence of vows between him and her. In the opinion of Rebbi Ze‘ira, Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri said the same thing since we stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 11:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.11.4.1">Mishnah 11:4</a>, about a woman who vows not to do anything he tells her to do. The rabbis hold that he does not have to dissolve the vow since by law she is required to live with him and keep house for him, and nobody can abolish his duties under the law by making a vow. R. Aqiba holds that he must dissolve the vow since she might do more for him than is legally required and then would violate her vow. R. Joḥanan ben Nuri says that he would be well advised to dissolve the vow since she would not be able to do anything for him after a divorce. This implies that the husband may dissolve now the vow regarding matters between him and her which would become effective only after a future divorce.</i>: “Rebbi Joḥanan said, he shall dissolve it since maybe he would divorce her, then she would be forbidden to return to him.”
Rebbi Hila explained the Mishnah: “These are the vows which he can dissolve: Matters connected with mortification. [E. g.], ‘if I wash, if I do not wash; if I wear jewels, if I do not wear jewels.’ ” What means “Rebbi Yose said, these vows are not vows of mortification”? They are vows between him and her. “The following are vows of mortification” is everybody’s opinion<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 11:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.11.2.1">Mishnah 2</a> can be read as being accepted by everybody. This implies that R. Yose does not disagree with the anonymous majority that the husband’s powers of dissolution of vows between him and her are restricted to the time of their marriage. He simply disagrees with the classification of the two examples quoted in the Mishnah and holds that not washing or not wearing jewellery is not done as mortification but to spite the husband.</i>.
“If I wash, if I do not wash.” It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">In a <i>baraita</i> not otherwise recorded.</i>: “If I wash, if I do not wash; if I wear jewels, if I do not wear jewels”, these are vows of mortification. Both according to Rebbi Ze‘ira or according to Rebbi Hila, Rebbi Yose seems to contradict his own opinion, as <sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the next paragraph, the text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 8:5:4-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.8.5.4-5"><i>Ševi‘it</i> 8:5, Notes 77–84</a>.</i> it was stated: “A water source belonging to the townspeople, between them and outsiders, they have precedence over outsiders. Between outsiders and their animals, the outsiders have precedence over their animals. Their washing and the lives of outsiders, their washing has precedence over the lives of outsiders.” Rebbi Joḥanan said, who is the Tanna who said that washing is a necessity for survival? Rebbi Yose! As it was stated: “One may use it neither for steeping nor for washing. But Rebbi Yose permits it for washing.” The opinions of Rebbi Yose are contradictory. There he says, washing oneself is not a necessity of life<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">“There” is the Mishnah here, where R. Yose qualifies not washing as not being a mortification.</i>. And here, he says washing one’s garments is a necessity of life! Rebbi Mana said, a person might put off washing himself but nobody puts off washing his clothes.
<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the next paragraph, the text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 8:5:4-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.8.5.4-5"><i>Ševi‘it</i> 8:5, Notes 77–84</a>.</i> Jehudah from Ḥusa hid himself in a cave for three days because he wanted to find the reason why the necessities of life of one’s town have precedence over the necessities of life of another town. He came to Rabbi Yose bar Ḥalaphta and said, I was hiding in a cave for three days because I wanted to find the reason why the necessities of life of one’s town have precedence over the necessities of life of another town. He called his son Rebbi Abba and asked him about the reason why the necessities of life of one’s town have precedence over the necessities of life of another town. He said to him (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 35:15" href="/Numbers.35.15"><i>Num</i>. 35:15</a>): “These [six] cities shall be”, each town shall be, and only afterwards their surroundings around them. He [R. Yose] said to him [Jehudah from Huṣa], what did cause you [this emharassment]? That you did not study with your companions!
We understand “if I wash<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The discussion here is to understand R. Yose, who classifies the vows as being between him and her. He cannot accept the explanation given in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 11:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.11.1.1">Note 2</a> but it must somehow refer to marital relations. If she makes a vow not to have any relations with him if she washes, it is clear that he may dissolve the vow as one between him and her. But if she makes the vow not to have any relations with him if she does not wash, why can we not rely on her washing, as normal people do?</i>”. “If I do not wash”? Let her wash! Rebbi Mana said, if she said: “Any benefit from me shall be <i>qônām</i> for you after I shall have washed myself, if I ever wash myself.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The mention of “not washing” is not an independent vow. She makes the vow not to have any relations with him if she washes and then reinforces her statement by the assertion that she will not wash.</i>” Why should he not dissolve for her? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, only when she said, “any benefit from my body shall be <i>qônām</i> for you after I shall have washed myself.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">It is only a vow between him and her if she makes it clear that she refers to marital intercourse.</i>” Why can he not force? Did not Rebbi Huna say, [if she vowed] any benefit from me [shall be forbidden] to you, he forces her<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">He goes to court and has her declared an unruly wife; the court will deduct 7 denar from her marriage settlement per week and, if that settlement was reduced to zero, give the husband the right to claim 7 denar per week from any future inheritance or earnings (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.6.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 5:7</a>).</i> and sleeps with her. Any benefit from you [shall be forbidden] to me, he has to dissolve<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is in his hand to dissolve the vow, he cannot sleep with her as long as her vow is in existence since “one does not feed a person anything forbidden to him” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 81b" href="/Nedarim.81b">Babli 81b</a>).</i>. There is a difference because it<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Sexual relations.</i> is a benefit for him and her. Rebbi Abba Mari said, If I wash [today], I shall not wash forever. If I wear jewellery [today], I shall not wear jewellery forever<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">R. Abba Mari disagrees with the preceding and holds that the vow means what it says, that it is only about washing (or wearing jewellery) and not about sex. R. Yose holds that a person can go for a day without washing or wearing jewellery; therefore, the vow described in the Mishnah is neither a vow of mortification nor one between him and her; the husband has no way of interfering with that vow. It might become a vow between him and her if she should wash that day.</i>. The rabbis of Caesarea in the name of Rebbi Nasa: If I wash I shall not wear jewellery; if I wear jewellery I shall not wash.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The following are vows of mortification. If she said, all produce of the world is <i>qônām</i> for me, he may dissolve. The produce of this province [is <i>qônām</i>] for me, he can buy for her from another province. The produce of this grocery store [is <i>qônām</i>] for me, he cannot dissolve. If he can get the necessities of life only from that grocery<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">If that store is the only one which extends credit to him and he needs the credit, he can dissolve.</i>, he may dissolve, the words of Rebbi Yose.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Samuel said, if she said, all produce of the world is <i>qônām</i> for me; if there was grain heaped in the grain container<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The word is explained as a contraction from סִיטוֹדָקֵּי, Greek σιτοδόκη “granary”. The grocer sells grain from his container.</i>, he may buy for her from that<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Samuel holds that a vow is future directed unless otherwise stated; it does not cover produce already harvested.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, “the produce of the people of this province [is <i>qônām</i>] for me, if there were strangers<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ξένος “stranger, host”.</i> selling there, he can buy from them.
It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:14" href="/Numbers.30.14"><i>Num</i>. 30:14</a>.</i>: “Every vow and every oath of prohibition to mortify a person.” For example, [a <i>qônām</i>] if I would taste peppers, if I would taste rolls<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">In the opinion of S. Fraenkel κολλίκιον, diminutive of Greek κόλλιξ “large round bread”.</i>. Not only if that kind is available now; even if that kind is not available now, perhaps he might bring her some later on. Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai: If there were two pieces before them, one nice and one not nice. If she desired the nice one<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">There were two pieces of meat and she was afraid her husband would take the nicer (larger) one for himself. It is assumed that the servants cooked and brought the meal.</i> and made a vow to forbid herself the less nice one, he may dissolve that. Why can he dissolve? The colleagues say, because of her mortification<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">She might change her mind and opt for the smaller piece. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 82b" href="/Nedarim.82b">Babli, 82b</a>, holds that the husband cannot dissolve as long as she has enough not to be hungry. It does not discuss the case of a person becoming afraid of overeating.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira and Rebbi Hila both say, because of his mortification<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Since the verse speaks only of mortification whithout indicating to whom it refers, it is implied that the husband can dissolve his wife’s vow if it mortifies him.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira in the name of Samuel: A man can dissolve all vows except if she says, any benefit from me shall [be <i>qônām</i>] for X. That means he can dissolve: Any benefit from X shall [be <i>qônām</i>] for me<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 82a" href="/Nedarim.82a">Babli, 82a</a>, agrees with both statements.</i>. But did we not state: “The produce of this grocery store [is <i>qônām</i>] for me, he cannot dissolve.” Explain that following Rebbi Yose and they disagree with him, for we did state there<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Which is declared to be R.
