Yose’s opinion.</i>: “If he can get the necessities of life only from that grocer, he may dissolve.” Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The late Amora.</i> said, because he gives him credit, he delivers to him<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Since he would have to go hungry if he is temporarily out of cash and could not pay another grocer, this certainly is potential mortification for him.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, because he gives him good quality produce<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">R. Mana even considers it mortification if he had to take a lesser quality from another store.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> ‘A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not have benefit from people<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">“People” means everybody except her husband, who is considered to be identical with her.</i>’ he cannot dissolve, and she may benefit from gleanings, forgotten sheaves, and <i>peah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">As is pointed out in the Halakhah, these agricultural gifts to the poor, described in Tractate <i>Peah,</i> are abandoned by the farmer who has no right to give them to a poor person of his acquaintance. Therefore, the poor receive these gifts from God’s bounty, not from the farmer.</i>. ‘A <i>qônām</i> that priests and Levites can have no benefit from me’; they may take forcibly<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">If the person (male or female) has farming property, the vow cannot free them from the obligations which are liens on agricultural produce.</i>. ‘These priests and these Levites can have no benefit from me;’ others may take.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “ ‘A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not have benefit from people,’ etc. Rebbi Yoḥanan said, so is the Mishnah: “<i>And</i> she may benefit from gleanings, forgotten sheaves, and <i>peah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">This remark stands for a lenghty discussion in the Babli, whose Mishnah seems to read: “She may benefit from gleanings, forgotten sheaves, and peah.” (Cf. The Babylonian Talmud with Variant Readings, <i>Nedarim</i> vol. 2, p. 264, Note 15.) The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 83b" href="/Nedarim.83b">Babli, 83b</a>, notes that the first sentence of the Mishnah implies that the husband is not “people”. The second clause, in its version, seems to imply that the woman can eat only from gleanings etc., not from the husband, who would be part of “people” and as such entitled to dissolve the vow. R. Joḥanan points out that the second clause is part of the first sentence, not a stand-alone sentence: The woman may eat from her husband and, in addition, gleanings, abandoned sheaves, and <i>peah</i>.</i>”. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 7:4" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).7.4">Tosephta 7:4</a>. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 84" href="/Nedarim.84">Babli, 84a/b</a>, quotes verses which give the farmer the option either to distribute the tithe, then the woman is disqualified; or to abandon it on his threshing floor, then the woman may take it if she is poor.</i>: “And the tithe of the poor.” The tithe of the poor is not listed here. The tithe of the poor is given as acquisition; these by abandoning<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">As is pointed out in the Halakhah, these agricultural gifts to the poor, described in Tractate <i>Peah,</i> are abandoned by the farmer who has no right to give them to a poor person of his acquaintance. Therefore, the poor receive these gifts from God’s bounty, not from the farmer.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the Halakhah, the text (with minor variants) is from <i>Demay</i> 6:3, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 11:4:4-10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.11.4.4-10.1">Notes 59–76</a>.</i> Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina said, a person gives his tithes for the benefit of goodwill. Rebbi Joḥanan said, a person may not give his tithes for the benefit of goodwill. What is the reason of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:10" href="/Numbers.5.10"><i>Num</i>. 5:10</a>) “Everybody shall be the owner of his holy things.” Rebbi Joḥanan said “it shall not be his”. May he give them to whomever he likes?
A Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥaninah: “A vow that no Cohanim or Levites should have any advantage from me, they should take against his will.” He explains it about a person who says, I cannot possibly give them <i>any</i> gifts. You should know that this is so, since we have stated: “<i>These</i> Cohanim and Levites should [not] have any advantage from me, let others take.” A <i>baraita</i> disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan: “An Israel can say to another Israel, here you have a tetradrachma and give this firstling to my daughter’s son, a Cohen.” He explains, if he already wanted to give it to two [Cohanim] and that daughter’s son was one of them; then one said, here you have a tetradrachma and give it whole to my daughter’s son, a Cohen.
They asked before Rebbi Zeїra: a Cohen to an Israel, Rebbi forbids. What does Rebbi Yose say? Rebbi Ḥizqiah in the name of Rebbi Aḥa said, so he responded to them: In the opinion of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina, why is a Cohen to an Israel forbidden, not because it looks badly? Also Rebbi Joḥanan holds that from an Israel to an Israel it is forbidden because it looks badly. In addition, because of the following, as it was stated: “Cohanim and Levites who help at the threshing floor have no right either to heave or to tithe, and if the farmer gave, it is desecrated, as it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:15" href="/Leviticus.22.15"><i>Lev</i>. 22:15</a>): ‘They should not desecrate the sanctified things of the Children of Israel,’ but they desecrate them! In addition, they said that their heave is no heave, their tithes are no tithes, their dedications are no dedications, and about them the verse says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Micah 3:11" href="/Micah.3.11"><i>Micha</i> 3:11</a>): ‘Their heads judge for bribes, [their priests are for hire].’ The Omnipresent brings over them three catastrophies; that is what is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Micah 3:12" href="/Micah.3.12"><i>Micha</i> 3:12</a>): ‘Therefore, because of you Zion will be ploughed over as a field, Jerusalem will be desolate, and the Temple Mount a wooded hill.’
A Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan: “If somebody marries preliminarily giving heave, tithes, ‘gifts’, water for sprinkling, or ashes of the red heifer, she is married, even if the groom is an Israel.” He explains it with heave he inherited from his maternal grandfather, a Cohen.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> 4: ‘A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not work according to the wishes of my father, or your father, or my brother, or your brother,’ he cannot dissolve. ‘According to your wish,’ he does not have to dissolve<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Since she is obligated to keep house for him and work in the house, and nobody can annul his obligations by a vow.</i>. Rebbi Aqiba says, he has to dissolve, maybe she works more than the required minimum<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">She would be sinning if she did anything for him over the legal minimum stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.5.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 5:5</a>.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri said, he shall dissolve since maybe he would divorce her, then she would be forbidden to return to him.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “ ‘A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not work according to the wishes of my father,’ etc. There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.4.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 5:4</a>. The husband dedicated the wife’s income from her work to the Temple. He cannot dispose of what she needs for her livelihood. The question is only about what she earns in excess of her needs. The text of the first part of the paragraph is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:5:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.5.2-4"><i>Ketubot</i> 5:5</a> in slightly extended form; the parallel discussion in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 58b-59a" href="/Ketubot.58b-59a">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 58b–59a</a>.</i>: “If somebody dedicates his wife’s work, she works and eats. The excess? Rebbi Meїr says, it is dedicated; Rebbi Joḥanan the Alexandrian said, it is profane.” Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, they disagree about the excess over five tetradrachmas<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">It is stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.8.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i>5:9</a> that the husband has to give his wife a silver obolus every week as pocket money. If he does not give it, the Mishnah states that he has lost his claim to her earnings (and his dedication is void since nobody can dedicate what is not his.) He can also require her to spin wool in the weight of 5 Judean tetradrachmas.</i>. He explains it in the case of one who supports his wife with food but does not give her an obolus for her needs<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that R. Meїr, who is the presumed author of an anonymous Mishnah, states that spinning the 5 tetradrachmas is for the silver obolus she receives, and the excess of her work is for the food the husband provides. In the case considered here, the husband has no right to the value of the first 5 tetradrachma weights of her spinning but he can dedicate all the excess. R. Joḥanan the Alexandrian will hold that the 5 tetradrachmas will pay for the food and the excess for the pocket money, then the excess is the wife’s property and the husband has no right to dedicate it.</i>, as we stated: “If he does not give her an obolus for her needs, what she earns is hers”. Rebbi Joḥanan said, they disagree about the excess left after [the husband’s] death; for he explains it if he does not support her with food<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">He explains an arrangement that the wife takes neither food nor money from the husband and retains all her earnings for herself. In that case, the husband has no say about her earnings. But if she dies before him, he inherits from her and can dedicate what he wishes. (In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 58b" href="/Ketubot.58b">Babli, <i>Ketubot</i> 58b</a>, this is Samuel’s position.) R. Joḥanan the Alexandrian holds that nobody can dedicate anything not in his possession; therefore, any disposition of his future inheritance which he makes during her lifetime is void.</i>. But if he supports her with food, everybody agrees that it became dedicated<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan holds that the spinning of the first 5 tetradrachmas pays for food and pocket money. Therefore, anything in excess is unincumbered property of the husband.</i>. And here, he cannot dissolve! Rebbi Meїr says. he does not have to dissolve<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Since she is obligated, she cannot avoid an obligation by a vow.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan the Alexandrian said, hers. Rebbi Aqiba said, hers. Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri said, his<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">R Joḥanan the Alexandrian in <i>Ketubot</i> and R. Aqiba in <i>Nedarim</i> both hold that the excess work of the wife belongs to the wife. Therefore, R. Aqiba requires the husband to dissolve the vow lest the wife give to her husband from her earnings and break her vow. But R. Joḥanan ben Nuri holds that the excess is the husband’s; therefore he only counsels the husband to dissolve to permit him to take his wife back after a divorce.</i>.
“Rebbi Aqiba said, he has to dissolve.” Rebbi Abba said, if she forbids the yields of her work, [vowing] not to work, he can force her up to the weight of five tetradrachmas. More than that he cannot force, for he has to be afraid that she may work more and it turns out that she profits from what is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Since the excess is hers automatically.</i>. Therefore, Rebbi Aqiba says, he has to dissolve his part. Rebbi Hila said, it is impossible for her to make exactly the weight of five tetradrachmas. He could force her up to the weight of five tetradrachmas; more that that he cannot force. He has to be afraid that she might be over the limit by a minute amount; then he would profit from what is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">If she thinks that she spun the weight of five tetradrachmas but in fact she made a little more, then in accepting this he would be sinning inadvertently. It is in his interest to dissolve the vow. The smallest weight measurable in talmudic theory is the <i>peruṭa,</i> 1/32 of an obolus (about 4 mg in silver equivalent).</i>. Therefore …<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that one has to insert here the text from R. Abba’s argument.</i>.
“Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri said, he should dissolve.” Rebbi Hila said, the reason of Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri is that even if he knows that if he divorces her she would be forbidden to return to him, she needles him until he divorces her<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Joḥanan ben Nuri holds that the excess work is the husband’s and the vow would be active only after divorce, it seems that at this moment there is neither a vow of mortification nor one between him and her. How could the husband legally dissolve the vow? It seems R. Hila reads “she knows that if he divorces”. He argues that it is a vow between him and her since the wife, knowing that the husband would be extremely reluctant to divorce her, could be quite unpleasant with impunity and he would suffer.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If his wife made a vow and he was under the impression that it was his daughter, or his daughter made a vow and he was under this impression that it was his wife. If she vowed to be a <i>nazir</i> and he was under the impression that she vowed a sacrifice, or if she vowed a sacrifice and he was under the impression that she vowed to be a <i>nazir.</i> If she woved to abstain from figs and he was under the impression that it was from grapes, or if from grapes and he was under the impression that it was from figs, he shall dissolve a second time<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Since erroneous dissolutions or confirmations are invalid, the time limit for dissolution starts to run again the moment he is informed of his error.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If she said, a <i>qônām</i> that I shall not taste these figs and grapes,” etc. Even if he heard and remained silent, even if he heard and confirmed<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 11:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.11.5.1">Mishnah 5</a>. Even if he had erroneously confirmed the vow, once he is informed of the true situation, he may dissolve. This is explicit in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 7:4" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).7.4">Tosephta, 7:4</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If she said, a <i>qônām</i> that I shall not taste these figs and grapes, if he confirmed for the figs he confirmed everything. If he dissolved for figs it is not dissolved unless he also dissolves for grapes<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">A partially confirmed vow is confirmed; a partially dissolved vow is not dissolved (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 7:4" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).7.4">Tosephta 7:4</a>).</i>. If she said, a <i>qônām</i> that I shall not taste these figs, that I shall not taste these grapes; these are two vows.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Some Tannaïm state: “He shall confirm it<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 30:14" href="/Numbers.30.14"><i>Num.</i> 30: 14</a>: “Her husband may confirm it or her husband may dissolve it.”<br>The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 7:4" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).7.4">Tosephta (7:4</a>) has a fifth version: The husband may confirm one and dissolve the other of two vows made at the same time, i. e., it is applicable to the situation described at the end of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 11:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.11.6.1">Mishnah 6</a>.</i>”, even partially, “he shall dissolve it”, even partially. Some Tannaïm state: “He shall confirm it”, totally<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The husband has only the choice to accept or to reject the wife’s vow <i>in toto.</i></i>, “he shall dissolve it”, totally. Some Tannaïm state: “He shall confirm it”, even partially, “he shall dissolve it”, totally. Some Tannaïm state: “He shall confirm it”, totally, “he shall dissolve it”, even partially. The Mishnah follows him who said, “he shall confirm it”, even partially, “he shall dissolve it”, totally. As we have stated: “If he confirmed for the figs he confirmed everything. If he dissolved for figs it is not dissolved unless he also dissolves for grapes.” If she said, a <i>qônām</i> that I shall not taste a fig nor a grape berry, that is a matter of difference between Rebbi Ismael and Rebbi Aqiba since Rebbi Ismael says, the vow decides, and Rebbi Aqiba says, the prohibition decides<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">In Halakhah 9, R. Ismael says the determining factor is the vow. A multiplicity of vows made at the same time is treated as a multiplicity; there are two separate vows in our case. R. Aqiba says that a multiplicity of prohibitions imposed at the same time are one prohibition; there is only one vow.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> ‘I knew that there are vows but I did not know that they can be dissolved.’ ‘I knew that one can dissolve but I did not realize that this was a vow.’ Rebbi Meĩr says, he cannot dissolve, but the Sages say, he can dissolve<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">R. Meïr holds that he had his chance; if he did not use it because of his ignorance, it is his fault. The Sages hold that the time for dissolution starts only when he is instructed about the law. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 87b-88a" href="/Nedarim.87b-88a">Babli, 87b/88a</a>, R. Meïr’s position is explained by his interpretation of the biblical verses.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “ ‘T knew that there are vows,” etc. Rebbi Ze‘ira said, the reason of Rebbi Meïr: It is a subterfuge. He wants her to make vows so he can divorce her<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the second case, when he admits that he knew he could dissolve his wife’s vows. Then he should have dissolved the vow immediately even if it was a borderline case. If it was a vow, it would be dissolved; if it was not a vow, no harm would have been done. We suspect that he wants to encourage his wife to make vows so he could claim that she always makes vows and one cannot expect from him to live with such a wife; he asks permission from the court to divorce her without payment of the <i>ketubah</i> sum (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 7:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.7.6.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 7:6</a>).</i>. That is not so, he could have divorced her on the first occasion<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">This gives the position of the Sages who note that he could have started complaining at the first vow. Therefore, they accept his words at face value.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a person is by a vow prevented to benefit his son-in-law but wants to give money to his daughter, he says to her: These coins are given to you as a gift on condition that your husband shall have no claim to them, except what you trade for your needs.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If a person is by a vow prevented to benefit his son-in-law,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">In this version, the father cannot give his daughter an unrestricted gift. He must spell out a use; the daughter cannot save the money for an unspecified future.</i>: “Neither do you”. Rebbi said, who stated “neither do you”? Rebbi Meĩr, for Rebbi Meïr makes the hand of the slave the hand of his master<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Meïr, anything the slave acquires becomes automatically his master’s property. Therefore, no slave can buy his freedom from his master since he cannot have money that is not his master’s. Any money needed for his manumission must be in the hand of a third party [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 88b" href="/Nedarim.88b">Babli 88b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:3:2-14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.3.2-14"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:3</a> (60a 1. 26), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 23b" href="/Nedarim.23b">Babli 23b</a>; <i>Ma‘aser Šeni</i> 4:4, Notes 89,96; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 6:1:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.6.1.2-5"><i>Ketubot</i> 6:1</a> (30c 1. 46); <i>Eruvin</i> 7:6 (24c 1. 62)]. Similarly, R. Meïr gives the husband property rights in everything his wife acquires. He will accept a contract in which the wife retains all her earnings while renouncing all support from her husband as a contract in which the wife acts by the husband’s authorization. The majority accepts separate property both for the slave and the wife.</i>. Because he said “neither do you.” If he had not said “neither do you”, if the wife acquired, the husband would have acquired.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> “The vow of a widow or a divorcee, anything she forbids to herself shall be confirmed.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Meïr, anything the slave acquires becomes automatically his master’s property. Therefore, no slave can buy his freedom from his master since he cannot have money that is not his master’s. Any money needed for his manumission must be in the hand of a third party [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 88b" href="/Nedarim.88b">Babli 88b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:3:2-14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.3.2-14"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:3</a> (60a 1. 26), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 23b" href="/Nedarim.23b">Babli 23b</a>; <i>Ma‘aser Šeni</i> 4:4, Notes 89,96; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 6:1:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.6.1.2-5"><i>Ketubot</i> 6:1</a> (30c 1. 46); <i>Eruvin</i> 7:6 (24c 1. 62)]. Similarly, R. Meïr gives the husband property rights in everything his wife acquires. He will accept a contract in which the wife retains all her earnings while renouncing all support from her husband as a contract in which the wife acts by the husband’s authorization. The majority accepts separate property both for the slave and the wife.</i>” How is that? If she said, I shall be a <i>nazir</i> after thirty days, even if she married within these thirty days he cannot dissolve. If she made the vow under her husband’s authority and he dissolved it, if she had said, I shall be a <i>nazir</i> after thirty days, even if she should become a widow or a divorcee within the next 30 days, it remains dissolved.<br>If she made a vow, was on the same day divorced<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">She was definitively married or was an adult. By divorce she became an independent adult. If he remarries her, even on the same day, the vow precedes the marriage and is out of the husband’s power.</i>, and taken back, he cannot dissolve. This is the principle: He cannot dissolve for any one who was on her own for one moment.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The vow of a widow or a divorcee,” etc. This refers to what Rebbi Ismael says, the vow decides; Rebbi Aqiba says, prohibition decides<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 11:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.11.6.2">Note 65</a>. R. Aqiba says the moment she imposed the prohibition is the decisive one; since at that time she had no husband, the vow is not under the later husband’s jurisdiction. R. Ismael says that the point of activation of the vow determines; there is no vow until after 30 days and the husband then can dissolve the vow. The Mishnah follows R. Aqiba. This interpretation of the opinions of Rebbis Aqiba and Ismael is forced by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 7:6" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).7.6">Tosephta, 7:6</a>, and is interpreted in this way in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 89a" href="/Nedarim.89a">Babli, 89a</a>.</i>. If vow and prohibition came together<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">It is the normal case that a woman makes a vow for immediate validity. But we are looking for a case involving a divorcee.</i>? How is that? She said, I shall be a <i>nazir</i> in 30 days. Her husband heard it and did not dissolve<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Aqiba, he had his chance and missed it. For R. Ismael, his inaction is irrelevant since the vow is not yet activated.</i>. He divorced her and remarried her within the 30 days. Is that when vow and prohibition come together<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The Tosephta states: A widow who said: I shall be a <i>nazir</i> the moment I shall be married. R. Ismael says, the husband can dissolve (since the vow starts to be active in his domain, it is not a vow preceding the marriage). R. Aqiba says, he cannot dissolve (since the vow was made when she was a widow). In the case here, the remarriage was before the 30 days were over; for R. Ismael there is no question that the husband can dissolve the vow. For R. Aqiba the question is whether the divorce is counted to introduce a new domain, in which case he can dissolve, or is a continuation of the old, in which case he had his chance and did not use it. The question is not resolved.</i>?

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The vows of nine young women<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">In this introduction to the Mishnah, נערה is used in a nontechnical term “young female”, whether minor, adolescent, or of age. Later the same word is used in a more technical sense, “underage or adolescent.” The principle underlying the Mishnah is very simple: The vows of an unmarried girl are not subject to dissolution if either she has no father or she is emancipated from him either by coming of age or by having been definitively married and then divorced or widowed.</i> are confirmed: An adult who is an orphan, an adolescent who became an adult and is an orphan, an adolescent who did not become an adult but is an orphan. An adult whose father died, an adolescent who became an adult and her father died, an adolescent who did not become an adult and her father died. An adolescent whose father died and afterwards she became an adult, an adult whose father lives, an adolescent girl whose father lives. Rebbi Jehudah says, also if somebody marries off his underage daughter and she becomes widowed or divorced and returns to him, she still is an adolescent<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jehudah denies that an underage girl (not an adolescent) is emancipated from her father by marriage.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The vows of nine young women are confirmed,” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan said, there are two<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">One category is the orphan and the minor or adolescent emancipated by dissolution of her marriage; the other is the adult. For R. Jehudah, the widowed or divorced minor falls in a separate category. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 89b" href="/Nedarim.89b">Babli, 89b</a>, holds that the nine are R. Jehudah’s but that the majority recognizes the adult, the orphan, and the minor or adolescent emancipated by dissolution of her marriage.</i>. But why did we state “two”? To train the students. And according to Rebbi Jehudah, there are three.

Halakhah 11

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> ‘A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not take any benefit from my father, or your father, if I shall do anything for you.’ ‘[A <i>qônām]</i> that I shall not have any benefit from you if I shall do anything for my father, or for your father.’ These he can dissolve.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “ ‘A <i>qônām</i> that I shall not take any benefit from my father, or your father,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Nedarim (Lieberman) 7:7" href="/Tosefta_Nedarim_(Lieberman).7.7">Tosephta 7:7</a>; cf. <i>Tosefta ki-Fshutah</i> vol. 7, p. 501; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 89b" href="/Nedarim.89b">Babli 89b</a>.</i>: Rebbi Nathan says, he cannot dissolve<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">The reason seems to be that the vow is neither a mortification nor does it hinder marital relations. The Babli gives as reason that the husband cannot dissolve as long as his wife did not work for him because the prohibition did not start. There is no hint of that in the Yerushalmi.</i>, but the Sages say, he can dissolve. Why can he dissolve? The colleagues say, because of his mortification<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">He feels badly about the situation; that is reason enough.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira and Rebbi Hila say, because of her mortification<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">They hold that his being hurt can never be a reason. He gets the power of dissolution because she might be hurt by this in the future.</i>.

Halakhah 12

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Earlier they said, three categories of women have to be divorced and collect their <i>ketubah:</i> The one who says, I am impure for you<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The wife of a Cohen who declares that she was raped without witnesses. Since the rabbinically accepted definition of a זוֹנָה (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:7" href="/Leviticus.21.7"><i>Lev.</i> 21:7</a>) is a woman who had sexual contact with a man whom she could not legally marry, and a married woman cannot marry anybody, she is forbidden to her husband who has to divorce her. Since it is not her fault, he has to pay the entire <i>ketubah</i> sum.</i>, or Heaven is between you and me<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">She claims that he is infertile.</i>, or I am separated from the Jews<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">She made a vow not to sleep with any Jew. The earlier opinion was that a woman would not make such a vow unless she suffers from vaginism and sexual relations are painful for her. Since the vow is not frivolous, she can claim her <i>ketubah</i> payment.</i>. They changed to say that a woman should not be encouraged to want another man and cause trouble to her husband. If she says, I am impure for you, she should bring proof<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously not proof beyond a reasonable doubt but to make the claim plausible.</i>. Heaven is between you and me, they should try to mediate<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">As long as it is infertility, not impotence, she cannot force a divorce. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 65b" href="/Yevamot.65b">Babli, <i>Yebamot</i> 65b</a>, holds that the rabbi shall grant the divorce and not try to keep the wife in the marriage if she claims that their financial situation is such that she needs children to care for her in her old age and the husband cannot provide the children.</i>. I am separated from the Jews, he shall dissolve his part, she shall live with him and be separated from the Jews<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Since she made the vow only against sleeping with Jews, not with any man in the world, one assumes that she can enjoy intercourse and only prefers Gentiles.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Earlier they said,” etc. That is, if she cannot bring proof for her assertion, it is obvious that she is permitted to her house<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Since the later Mishnah states that she is not divorced, obviously she will be permitted to her husband.</i>. Rebbi Hila said, would it not be reasonable that a fellow should be apprehensive<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">A “fellow” follows the rules of purity strictly, cf. Introduction to Tractate <i>Demay.</i> R. Hila suggests that the strictly observant should follow the earlier Mishnah in this case.</i>, and if he was a Cohen that she should be forbidden to eat heave<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 90b" href="/Nedarim.90b">Babli, 90b</a>, this is the authoritative opinion of Rava. (There is internal and some ms. evidence that the author of this opinion is the less authoritative Rabba. In that case, the Babli would concur with the Yerushalmi that she may eat heave. But the legal tradition is to read Rava.)</i>? There came a case before Rebbi Ḥanina, the colleague of the rabbis, and he permitted her to eat heave. Rebbi Ḥaggai said, my father knew the first and the last case. Soldiers<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Greek στρατιώτης, “warrior, commander”.</i> entered the town. A woman came and said, a soldier<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Greek στρατιώτης, “warrior, commander”.</i> embraced me and ejaculated semen between my knees. He permitted her to eat heave<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Since we do not know of the incident from any other source, “the mouth which forbade” (she, who tells of the encounter with the soldier) “is the mouth which permitted” (that there was no penetration) (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 2:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.2.2.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i>2:2</a>).</i>. There came a case before Rebbi Isaac bar Tevele of a woman who said, my cowhand<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Reading בֵּיהָם “the one who cares for the cattle” for ביאם because of the disappearance of guttural sounds; already noted by David Fraenckel.</i> seduced<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">An adult woman is guilty of adultery if she lets herself be seduced and must be divorced without payment of the <i>ketubah.</i> (Seduction of a minor is treated as rape; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 1:2:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.1.2.5"><i>Soṭah</i> 1:1, Note 91</a>.)</i> me. He said to her, is the cowhand not forbidden? And he forbade her. Here, you say that he forbade her. There, you say that he permitted her. There, she came to forbid herself and he permitted her. But here, she came to permit herself and he forbade her.
“Heaven is between you and me”: as Heaven is far from earth, so this woman should be far from that man. “They should try to mediate.” Rav Huna said, they should make a dinner and they will get used to be with one another by the dinner.
<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">This is from Chapter 1, Note 74.</i> It was stated: “I am jailed away from you, I am separated away from you”. Rebbi Jeremiah asked, why did one not state “taken away from”? Rebbi Yose said, that was stated at the end: “I am taken away from the Jews.”
If she was divorced<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">The woman who vowed not to sleep with Jews.</i>, let her go and cling to the Arabs, for she loves them.
<sup class="footnote-marker">98“</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta Nezirut 3:12-13. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 71a" href="/Ketubot.71a">Babli, Ketubot 71a</a>, there is a lengthy discussion about the attribution of the statements to different authors.</i> If a woman made a vow to be a <i>nazir;</i> her husband heard and did not dissolve it: Rebbi Meïr and Rebbi Jehudah say, he put his finger between her teeth<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">He is to blame if she bites and hurts him.</i>, for if he wants to confirm, he can confirm. If he said, I cannot stand her being a <i>nazir,</i> he should divorce her and pay the <i>ketubah.</i> Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Simeon say, she put her finger between her teeth, for if he wants to confirm, he can confirm<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">She knows that she takes the risk that her husband would confirm her vow and she would be unable to serve him wine, or to cook dishes which require wine in the recipe, and that at the end she will be without her hair, so that the husband might get exasperated. (In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 7:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.7.2.2"><i>Ketubot</i> 7:2</a>, the Yerushalmi notes that if the husband by needling his wife pushes her to make the vow, Rebbis Yose and Simeon agree that he put his finger between her teeth.)</i>. If she said, I can stand it to be a <i>nazir,</i> he may divorce her<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">If he cannot stand living with a <i>nezirah.</i></i> without paying the <i>ketubah.</i>” Remove this, how can Rebbi Meïr and Rebbi Jehudah say so in the Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that the text here is somewhat defective, judging from the following parallel story. In any case, if he does not dissolve his wife’s vows, the husband is responsible for all consequences.</i>? Even in the later Mishnah, why did he not dissolve? Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Simeon follow the later Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">In requiring the woman to repeat and affirm her statement.</i>. But you might say, the earlier Mishnah. Why did she make the vow?
